[Page 1]

Investigations into Magic, an Edition and Translation of Martín Del Río\'s Disquisitionum magicarum libri sex

Volume 2

By Peter Maxwell-Stuart José Manuel García Valverde

Book Two

Magic Involving Evil Spirits

This is the most important subject for discussion in my work and so, in order to discuss it as lucidly as possible, let me \[divide it\] into several chapters or questions.

Book 2, Question 1

Is there such a thing as magic involving evil spirits?

Those who, along with the Sadducees, Democritus, Aristotle, Averroes, and Simplicius, deny the existence of evil spirits, also deny the existence of this kind of magic. Their opinion is irreligious and heretical because both evil spirits and magic involving evil spirits do exist. This \[magic\] does not rely upon the deliberate intention or the skill of human beings or natural causes, but upon some kind of non-material, independent power. That this is established fact is proved by the testimonies offered by Holy Scripture and the historical accounts and experiences of all ages which simply bring lighted lamps to the assistance of broad daylight. Along with St Augustine, all Catholic theologians and the majority of natural philosophers prove this point: Trismegistus and the Pythagoreans; the Platonists Psellus, Plotinus, Proclus, Iamblichus, Chalcidius, and Apuleius; and the Aristotelians Theophrastus, Ammonius, Philoponus, Avicenna, and Algazel.

Giving replies \[to questions\] about concealed thefts, and treasures: learning several branches of knowledge instantaneously without the bother of having to work at it: making an object disappear before the eyes of onlookers: and innumerable things of this kind---can they be traced back to any cause other than some kind of nature which is intelligent and different \[from that of everyone else\]? Finally, we know that, as recently as our grandfathers' memory, there were famous schools of this dark magic \[goetia\] in Salamanca and Toledo in Spain, at Lake Norcia in Italy, and in the Visignian cave.

Book 2, Question 2

From where does this magic come, and who invented it?

There are those who say that evil spirits exist but deny that this kind of magic has its origin in evil spirits or devils \[diaboli\]. They attribute its effects either to God\'s operating directly through this kind of magician, or to God\'s using good angels in order to produce them. The former say that certain graces, such as those of language and health, have been given freely, and that it is not unusual for these gifts to be granted, as in the case of Balaam and other wicked individuals.

First, I say that this opinion is entirely blasphemous because it attributes to magical practices something which belongs properly to graces freely given, and jumbles together conjuring-tricks and genuine miracles. Secondly, it makes God\'s free gifts subject to human artifice and certain ridiculous practices. Thirdly, \[it says\] that God would unfairly and in a spirit of envy prohibit something He Himself graciously bestows on human beings as a favour. Opposing arguments, which are found in well-known passages of Holy Scripture, prove only that effects such as these happen (up to a point) through magical practices and through graces freely given, and also that sometimes, for the propagation of the Faith as well as His own divine glory, God produces miracles through wicked people. The fact is that someone who operates through the gift of a grace freely given is not restricted to a particular time \[of day\], or occasion, or specific ceremonies, as is usually the case with magicians \[magi\] who accomplish nothing if they are found wanting in the slightest degree. Nor can gifts of this kind, freely given by God, be taught to others and passed on through instruction. Magicians, however, do hand on this magic to their pupils, and once those who listen to them have followed their teachers\' instructions, they do the same things \[as their teachers\].

Others say these effects \[are produced\] by good angels whom they call \'spirits\' \[spiritus\] because of their high status. This is what the celebrated modern magician, Scotus of Parma, used to boast. (He was the one who sent the apostate Bishop of Köln, Gebhard Truchsess \[von Waldeburg\], mad.) This is also the ancient fraudulent claim of Platonist magicians in particular Iamblichus, Porphyrius, Plotinus, Proclus, and Julian the Apostate. According to their tradition, all magic is divided into \'white\', which they think is lawful, and \'black\' which is unlawful. Hence, they call the white one Osoupyía \[theurgy\] and the black one yonteia \[goetia\]. Actually, the Arch-magician himself makes a good case for rejecting this plainly fictitious, foreign-based, theatrical \'theurgy\', when he testifies in clear terms that \'very often, under the names of God and angels, it is obligated to the wicked deceptions of evil spirits,\' and, \"although the greatest part of its ceremonies makes a show of cleanliness of mind, body, externals, and utensils, yet it searches out unclean spirits \[spiritus\] and deceiving Powers in order to worship them as gods.\' Later on he adds, \'the techniques \[known as\] \"Almadel\", \"notoria\", and \"Paulina\", the technique of making revelations, and more superstitions of this kind are types of this magic, and the more divine they seem to be to ignorant people\', (he says), \'the more destructive they are.\' Let those people take note of the opinion of their prince and standard-bearer on the subject of \'white\' magic-the simple truth from a liar. (St Augustine condemns this same theurgy in his book against the Manichaeans, The Advantage of Believing.) Add to these the fictitious planetary technique, inscribed on thin pieces of wood or metal, which was sent to Charlemagne by one of Bede\'s pupils-this is how these people lie and substitute Alkindi for Alcuin-useful (of course!) for obtaining a complete and more than natural understanding of the arts and sciences, and for achieving rank, riches, physical strength, enjoyment of love, etc.
[Page 2]

Secondly, in company with Catholics I say that God does not associate Himself with these magical operations, either on His own account or via good angels, any more than \[He does\] with anything else in which He engages as the universal cause. Consequently, I think that since evil spirits instituted all these \[operations\], they also carry them out and guide \[their performance\]. To think otherwise is an error. Proof of this lies first of all in case 26 in the various accounts of the canons provided by Gratian, and secondly in articles 9, 19, and 23 of the Articles of Paris. I quote: (i) \'That God is induced by magical practices and acts of harmful magic \[maleficia\] to force evil spirits to obey their incantations is an error;\' (ii) \'that good angels can be enclosed in stones, and that they can consecrate images or vestments, or do the other things involved in these practices, is an error and blasphemous;\' (iii) \'that some spirits are good and others omniscient and others are neither damned nor saved is an error.\"

Thirdly, \[my point\] is proved by these \[words\] of Arnobius in his Against the Heathen. \'Magicians not only have knowledge of evil spirits but accomplish whatever extraordinary thing they do by means of evil spirits. They perform conjuring-tricks because \[evil spirits\] breathe upon them and pour themselves into them,\' etc; and from Book 1, chapter 9 of The Frivolities of Courtiers by John of Salisbury, \'But long ago the holy Fathers ordered those who practise the more harmful conjuring-tricks and magical techniques, and different kinds of the disapproved \[form\] of astrology, to leave the church, because they knew that all these pieces of cunning, or rather noxious acts of harmful magic \[maleficia\] issued from an intimacy between evil spirits and human beings.\'

Next, it is proved by the argument that these spirits \[spiritus\] cannot be called \'good\' (i) because they order \[people\] to worship them as gods and make sacrifices to them, and this is an order good angels cannot give; (ii) these practices are directed as much to bringing death to others by means of robberies, murders, adulteries, etc. as to helping them; (iii) they blend together many lies and deceptions; (iv) the magicians are taught how to exercise command and compulsion over spirits, especially by making threats which are impossible and plainly ridiculous, (as Porphyrius says according to Eusebius), and none of this can apply to good angels at all. For this reason, it is clear Glykas should be read with care when he distinguishes magic from goetia on the grounds that magic comes from beneficent spirits \[genii\] and produces an outcome which is in some way good, while goetia comes from evil spirits and produces an outcome which is in some way bad.⁸ He has, you see, not only improperly made use of the generic term \'magic\' for the specific term \'theurgy\' but has also slipped into the error of those who support \'white\' magic and attribute the way it works to good angels. What is more, (let me issue this warning just once), this writer seems to attribute far too much to superstitious observations of portents, as do Constantine Manasses, \[Nicephorus\] Gregoras, and Nicetas \[Choniates\]. \'Palingenius Stellatus\' made the same mistake when, in Book 8 of his Zodiac which has been quite rightly proscribed, he thinks the air is full of good demons which he makes the originators of these practices and which, because he is too devoted to the lunacies of Plotinus and Iamblichus, he calls \'gods\'. He may not be a bad poet, but he is not a good Christian, in as much as he is more of a heretic than a Catholic, as his writings prove. Yet he ventured to dedicate that work to the Duke of Ferrara!⁹

A third error belongs to those who were of the opinion that magical effects take place through the agency of the souls of the dead. Catholic theologians have long exposed these people and have done so without difficulty because the reasons which are convincing in the case of good spirits \[spiritus\] are also convincing when it comes to the souls of the dead. Souls in Purgatory do not leave it except with God\'s permission to seek the prayers of the living. Finally, souls given over to everlasting torments cannot leave them by permission of licence or permission of evil spirits, in as much as by God\'s command they have been consigned to the very worst of prisons. It is therefore perfectly clear that the claims made by necromancers about summoning up souls with the help of a grimoire are fraudulent. If they did leave \[Hell\], they would do so at the command of the one God, but there is no reason God would order or permit this, and to think He would do so at the behest of magicians\' prayers and incantations is irreligious. So, the principal efficient cause of these practices is the Devil himself, and the second one is the human intelligence\'s innate quality which has been twisted out of shape as a result of the original Fall. Celio Calcagnini has described it to us very wittily, veiling \[his meaning\] with the garland of a merry fable in his Essay on Love Magic, using words I am not reluctant to write and, unless I am mistaken, you will take pleasure in reading.¹⁰ \'VAIN FUTILITY once conjoined with MARVEL in a furtive copulation and gave birth to a MAGIC with the name \'favourable\'. Dreading lest by chance TRUTH, (with which \[Vain Futility\] is perpetually engaged in hostilities, which she has also fought in close combat, and in which she has often been beaten), would kill off her offspring, handed it over, by conjugal agreement, to a married couple, SHAMELESSNESS and FEAR. But straight away they secretly laid it in the lap of INCOMPREHENSIBILITY to be brought up under the influence of the cradle-songs of CONJURING-TRICKS. \[Magic\] soon grew up and then, furnished with little wings, it flew off to the FORTRESS OF FOOLS, and there built a nest and gave birth to CREDULITY who, in gratitude for the kindnesses of her parent, nourishes \[Magic\] and loves her back\], as though \[Magic\] should shortly die if she did not.\'
[Page 3]

This is what he says, with equal acuity and truthfulness. Let us also proceed to fight on behalf of TRUTH and overcome VAIN FUTILITY and, once FEAR and SHAMELESSNESS have been put to flight, to measure the labyrinth of INCOMPREHENSIBILITY with the thread of real theology: and finally, once the cradlesongs of CONJURING-TRICKS have been brought to light, let us demolish the FORTRESS OF FOOLS and get rid of CREDULITY, who is so pernicious, from its nests: and may Almighty God grant us \[the ability\] to do this.

Book 2, Question 3

The different types of magic involving evil spirits, and the books which deal with it.

Since there is no such thing as theurgy or \'white\' magic, it follows that all this marvel-making magic is simply goetia and \'black\' \[magic\]. We usually call this \'Specific Magic\'. The Arch-magician divided it into two types, (a) the one whose practitioners, he says, surrender themselves to an evil spirit, sacrifice to him, and worship him, and (b) the one he would like to persuade us is free from a pact with evil spirits, but which gives them commands either by slight movements of the head and, through the power of divine names, binds them with an oath, then summons and constrains them \[to do the magician\'s will\]. He acknowledges that the former is forbidden by human and divine laws, is unspeakably wicked, and should be punished with every kind of fire; and he maintains that the latter does indeed accomplish what it claims to do, especially when it comes to constraining evil spirits, (which he later goes on to declare is an acceptable thing to do), but that it is exposed to the obvious dangers of delusion. His evidence in connection with the first is true. When it comes to the second, he lies: first when he denies that it depends on a covenant made with evil spirits, because in fact it is always made worse by an overt or tacit pact, and secondly when he maintains that evil spirits are constrained, (about which I shall speak later.)¹¹

People claim that the discoverer of this forbidden magic was one Mercurius, (which is contradicted by Symmachus, Prudentius, and \[John of Salisbury in Policraticus Book 1, chapter 9.) Others \[say it was\] someone called \'Zabulus\', but I think he was simply the evil spirit to whom St Cyprian and other Fathers applied this name. Then they praise a teacher, one Barnabas from Cyprus. (\[The Cypriots\] have fabricated him as an insult to St Barnabas the Apostle in order to flatter their ugly prostitute.) Likewise, people reveal, in an equally mendacious boast, that they have the books of Adam, Abel, Enoch, Abraham, Paul, Cyprian, Honorius, Albertus Magnus, and others, and in a blasphemous lie they say that the contents of these books were handed down by Adam\'s guardian angel, Raziel, and revealed by Raphael, Tobias\'s companion.¹³ They also pretend that Solomon was the author of The Little Key, (\[a claim\] which Battista Segni refutes very well in chapter 7 of his book Real Christian Concern), and thrust \[in our direction\] another huge volume divided into seven books, which is filled with sacrifices and incantations to evil spirits.

The Jews and Arabs in Spain used to leave this book to their descendants as an heirloom, and by its means used to effect a number of remarkable and incredible things. But Inquisitors of the Faith have quite justifiably burned as many copies as they have been able to find-and would they had been able to lay their hands on the last copy! I scarcely have any doubt that one fell into the hands of Johannes Trithemius and that it was from this that he got both the gestation of and material for his Secret Writing \[\'Steganographia\'\] which is crammed full of danger and superstition; and I suspect that the reputation of this work, which has not yet been added to \[the list of books prohibited by the Church, happened because while the book was still not yet in print it fell into the hands of \[only a\] few people. Moreover, when all books of magic were prohibited, this one which, among others, deludes the household \[of faith\], should have been considered as having been prohibited as well.
[Page 4]

I can say the same of Agrippa\'s Three Books of A Philosophy which has been kept Secret, and of the fourth Book, the most pernicious of all, which was added and published separately later: and about Thomas of York, Pietro d\'Abano, and the poisonous stuff \[produced\] by similar people, and a book on magical technique \[artis grimoyriae\] which teaches \[its readers\] how to evoke the dead, and was going the rounds in our fathers\' lifetime. I consider that all these people\'s wicked opinions, and also what they say about those older \[topics\], owe their value to the work of the Arch-magician, and one has to believe that his students cannot be allowed to escape the influence of their teacher in what they say about magic. This is why he writes as follows: \'These books openly publish, for the person who gives them careful attention, a list of their precepts, the ritual, the customary words and type of characters to be used, the way they should be put together, and the silly way \[the words\] should be spoken. They contain nothing but nonsense and deceit, and in later times have been assembled by the most desperate authors of \[people\'s\] ruination from a number of unholy practices mixed together with the ceremonies of our religion and sown with a good many unknown names and signs, in order to terrify the uneducated and the simple-minded. \[They are also aimed at\] those who have been rendered irrational by stupidity and those who have not had a decent education\'. The wretched man, while being aware of what is better clung, right up to the end of his life, to what was worse.

Book 2, Question 4

The basis of this kind of magic: a pact, whether explicit or implicit.

Conclusion 1: All magical working rests for its foundation on a pact made between the magicians and an evil spirit. Consequently, any time a magician wants to accomplish anything, he is constrained explicitly or implicitly by this prop to his craft to demand that the evil spirit abide by the contract. This is proved first of all by the authority of the \[Church\] Fathers. St Cyprian says that \[magicians\] have a covenant with an evil spirit. Gratian records the words of St Augustine in the text of his Decretals as follows. \'All practices of this kind, either of frivolous or harmful superstition, have been created from a noxious partnership, a pact so to speak of untrustworthy and deceitful friendship\'¹⁶

Secondly, it is proved by civil law in the form of an Imperial decree. \'Many people have used magical techniques to throw the elements into confusion. They do not hesitate to undermine the life of the innocent and dare to summon-this is the correct reading-¹⁷ the spirits of the dead and agitate them to that someone can destroy his enemies by means of their wicked practices. May a deadly pestilence annihilate these people, because they are alien to \[God\'s\] creation\'.

Thirdly, it is decreed as an item of belief by the Articles of the University of Paris in the following words: \[To say that\] entering into a tacit or overt pact with evil spirits is not idolatry or a kind of idolatry is an error, and we mean that an implicit pact exists in every superstitious practice for whose effect one cannot reasonably look to God or Nature.¹⁸

Fourthly, we are persuaded of its \[existence\] by reason because many wicked people desire this and so does the evil spirit, and \[people\'s\] strength does not overcome it. The prophet Isaiah tells us this. According to him, irreligious people say, \'We have struck a bargain with death and have made a pact with Hell\', \[28.15\]. St Thomas \[Aquinas\] says this probably refers to magicians, and so did Pope John XXII in his decretal against magicians, beginning \'On the subject of mirrors\', and Pope Sixtus V in his Bull against astrology. So, the wicked do this willingly, no less so than the evil spirit himself who dared offer Christ the Lord Himself the terms of a pact. \'All these things\', he said, \'I shall give you if you will prostrate yourself and worship me\', \[Matthew 4.9\]. Here one can see the pact belonging to an unspoken contract-I give on the understanding that you do. Hence nowadays the Devil says to magicians, \'If you devote yourself to me, I shall avenge you, I shall enrich you\', etc. which is the entire formula of a pact between \[sworn\] brothers.
[Page 5]

Fifthly, as I said, these things take place through the action of evil spirits, not the power of the magicians, and since human beings cannot compel the evil spirits to do them, it follows that the evil spirits make haste \[to do them\] because they want to. Evil spirits, however, pursue a human being out of sheer hatred, so one cannot imagine they make haste without wanting something in return. They exact some kind of price for their effort. Consequently, it is clear that this kind of covenant and association is like that between two brigands. One strolls about openly in a wood while the other lurks secretly in an ambush. The former attracts the attention of the latter with a special hiss or signal, and the latter treacherously shoots a traveller with an arrow. Often the former does not see this and does not know which weapon it was that inflicted death, and yet he agrees to the unfortunate \[traveller\'s\] murder. The magician is like the first robber, the evil spirit like the second. The situation is rather like that in which powerful individuals who are greedy for revenge attach to themselves men who are quick with a dagger. They give these men a pre-arranged nod as a signal of the person they want killed and when \[they want it done\].

Finally theologians, older as well as more recent, are in agreement on this point, as are legal experts and physicians, (\[Battista\] Codronchi, Illnesses caused by Poisons and Poisonous Magic, Book 1, and \[Andrea\] Cesalpino, A Peripatetic Investigation of Evil Spirits.) The matter is rendered absolutely certain by the remarkably unanimous confession of workers of harmful magic \[malefici\] and wise-women \[sagae\] throughout the whole of Europe and \[by the fact that\] every age is in agreement, and even though Lucan was a sceptic, he was aware of this kind of compact. But he was deceived by it, because he thought the evil spirit was bound by the pact.

\'Why is it the gods take this trouble to pay heed to chants and herbs, And are afraid to take no notice? Which pact Binds the gods under an obligation to its provisions? Must they obey, or do they take pleasure \[in obeying\]? Are they being rewarded simply because of a sense of duty about which we know nothing, Or have they exerted their influence because of silent threats? Do those \[threats\] Have authority over all the gods, or do these imperious chants Have one particular god who can compel the world By whatever kind of compulsion is exerted over him?\' \[Pharsalia 6.492499\].

Only the wise-women\'s \[sagarum\] protector \[Johann Weyer\] is resistant to this as he fills his pages with slight, worthless arguments, and in consequence I think they should be treated with contempt and consigned to silence rather than rebutted, most especially since some people have been more diligent than reluctant to do this.

Conclusion 2: The evil spirit is at liberty to fulfil this pact, or to cheat. This is proved by reason and experience. Reason tells, us that an evil spirit cannot be compelled by a human being to stand by his promises, and experience tells us he is a complete liar and very keen to deceive. Consequently, he rarely does what he has promised. But when he does stand by his promises, which he does occasionally, he does so not under compulsion but of his own accord, with the crafty intention (i) of keeping his hold over the magicians he has bound to him this way, and (ii) of enticing others and persuading them that the power they want lies in frivolities of this kind.

Conclusion 3: This kind of pact has two sides to it, the one explicit, the other implicit, but the indication of each of them is not the same in all circumstances. The following should be noted by way of explanation. To start with, one should be aware that the explicit pact works in three ways. The first involves various ritual observances during which loyalty and homage are promised to the evil spirit himself who appears visibly in some kind of physical shape in front of witnesses. Caesarius of Heisterbach tells the story of a nobleman from the territory of Liège who made a pact of this kind.²² The evil spirit, however, was heard speaking but was not seen, and at this point the young man, along with everything else he did, was ill-advised enough to deny his Creator with a gesture of renunciation and do homage to the devil. He, in his turn, promised \[the young man\] riches and honours. So because I feel friendly towards the reader, I also make a solemn promise that he will bring back abundant fruit from his taking the trouble to read this. On this subject, too, I should make reference to what Archdeacon Theophilus did, according to Sigebert. \'In order to recover his position, he sought help from the Prince of evil spirits, who ordered him to deny Christ, the Son of God, and His mother Mary, along with every tenet of Christianity, to confirm this denial in writing, to sign what he had written, to hand over the signed document to him, and to bind himself to his service\'. In the latter instance, the witness was a magician, in the former the Superintendent of Iniquity.²³
[Page 6]

Sometimes they do this via a little book of humble requests which they present to the Devil. In our own day, for example, magicians from Mantes-laJolie in Normandy, wrote a little book which they were going to offer to the Sibyls, the guardians of necromantic magic. Briefly, it said that (i) they begged the Ladies to deign to consecrate the \[magicians\'\] books of magic so that evil spirits \[spiritus\] would obey them quickly in every way in conformity with the formulae of invocation contained in the books, and that when they were invoked they would appear to them, without doing them harm, in the shape of a good-looking human being, and that they would not have to make any circle or enter houses or go into the fields; (ii) that the Sibyls would stamp their sign or character on the books, (there were three of them), so that the books would have more effectiveness and dominance over the foresaid spirits \[spiritus\]; (iii) that the petitioners were to be kept safe from judges\' traps and the punishment \[they inflict\]; (iv) that everything they started and their endeavours to get a friendly reception at the courts of princes were to be successful and prosper, and that they were to make money by gambling whenever they wanted; (v) that their enemies should not be able to do them harm. In return for these \[requests\], they promised in their turn that, on the very day their books were consecrated, they would regard the foresaid Sibyls as their mistresses and princes in perpetuity and offer them an individual soul every year for as long as they lived, (that is, they would murder and sacrifice a human being, or certainly initiate him into their ritual of unbelief.) But according to their binding agreement, the Sibyls were to provide these people in good faith with what they had demanded. (They were all burned in Paris, along with their books. \[Pierre\] Crespet tells this story in his book The Hatred of Satan, discourse 15.

The second way \[the pact works\] is like the first one in every regard except that it is done without witnesses. The third is carried out by a deputy, (that is, a magician acting as a third person), because the person making the covenant is too afraid to look at or converse with the evil spirit. \[Paolo\] Grillando, question 3, calls this third kind \'\[Tacit\', because although this declaration is made to someone other than the evil spirit, it is still clearly also made in the name of the evil spirit, as is obvious from what Grillando tells us in question 3, note 1 ff. Perhaps the less common form applies to the explicit pact in the example of a German woman who leaped backwards out of a large basin she was using as a bath and began to cry out that the distance she jumped away from Christ was the same distance she was approaching the evil spirit. (I shall speak about this at greater length in Book 5).

All these pacts have certain things in common. (i) There is a denial of the Faith and of Christianity, withdrawing one\'s obedience from God, repudiating the protection of the Blessed Virgin Mary, and on top of this attacking her loudly with insults. Blasphemously and contemptuously, they are accustomed to call her a big red-headed beanpole. So we have, \'The Devil comes and removes the word from their heart so that they may not be saved, (Luke 8.12), that is, he removes the true Faith and acknowledgement of it because he is canvassing for divine honours, (see St Augustine, The City of God, Book 10, chapter 20 and Against Faustus, Book 20, chapter 22.) This is why he forces \[people\] first of all to renounce the baptism whereby someone has been consecrated to the true God, and why, in his book on the destruction of the world, Hippolytus the Martyr writes that the evil spirit will force someone to say, \'I deny the Creator of heaven and earth. I deny baptism. I deny the worship I have been accustomed to offer God. I begin to cleave to you. I believe in you\'.²⁶ This is why the evil spirit jabs their forehead with his nail, pretending to scrape away the oil and destroy the mark of baptism. (ii) He also pretends to wash them with a new baptismal purification. Tertullian was not unaware of this.
[Page 7]

In his book on baptism he writes, \'Here, too, we recognise the Devil\'s eager exertions when, in emulation of God, he confers baptism on his \[adherents\]\'. (iii) So once the person has renounced his former name, he is given a new one, (as Della Rovere from Cuneo was called \'Goat-beard\'. Don\'t you recognise a figurative example in those limbs of Satan?)² The Egyptian Pharaoh changed Joseph\'s name (Genesis \[41\].45), but Holy Scripture never calls him by that name. Nebuchadnezzar imposed other names on Daniel and his companions (Daniel 3), but Daniel always calls himself \'Daniel\', and the three men in the fiery furnace, Hananiah, Azariah, and Mishael, sing \'You are blessed\' to the Lord, saying, \'Truly, 0 God, we are your soldiers and not those of an earthly king. We have given the name to you, not to a tyrant\'.

\(iv\) He compels them to renounce the god-fathers and god-mothers \[they had\] at baptism and confirmation, and assigns them other, new ones, because those who undertake this task behave like the sponsors of the Faith and, (as one has to believe), are required to teach their spiritual children.

\(v\) They give the Devil a piece torn from their clothing because the Devil is eager to lay claim to something which belongs to them personally-one of their spiritual gifts of grace, \[such as\] their faith and their baptism: a part of their body, \[such as\] their blood, as in the rites of Baal, (3 Kings 18.\[29\]): one of their possessions from Nature, \[such as\] their daughters, (as I shall point out in its place): one of their material possessions, \[such as\] a scrap of clothing, perhaps because they are accustomed to cast their clothes beneath the feet of their king as a recognition of his lordship? (4 Kings 9.\[13\] and Matthew 21. \[8\].)

\(vi\) They swear an oath to the evil spirit on a circle drawn on the ground. Is this because a circle, which lacks a beginning and end, is a symbol of the Godhead and the earth is God\'s footstool? Certainly \[the evil spirit\] would have them believe he is the lord of heaven and earth. Or is it because a circle, which lacks any angles at all, is the opposite \[shape\] of a cross, which is full of angles? Certainly \[the evil spirit\] hates the cross on which, he recalls, he was utterly defeated, and which gives him much grief. This is why he loves the opposite shape. You can find, among magical sigils, a cross placed in the middle of a circle, as though it were its prisoner. The Apostate, (as the theologian Gregory \[Naziazen\] indicates in his Against Julian), who was utterly devoted to magical things, used to interpret the sigil this way in order to presume that the sign of the cross should be, to put it plainly and briefly, trampled on and effaced.

\(vii\) They ask the evil spirit to delete them from the book of life and inscribe them in the book of death. For example, we read that the magicians from Avignon were inscribed in a pitch-black book. (viii) They promise \[to make\] sacrifices, as did \[the magicians\] from Mantes la Jolie about whom I spoke a little earlier, and certain witches \[striges\] promise to murder a little child every month or fortnight by making it lose strength, (that is, by sucking the life out of it.) See \[Bartolomeo della\] Spina, Night-Flying Witches, chapter 2.

\(ix\) They are obliged to pay something to their little masters, the evil spirits, every year so that they can either buy off their fear of being beaten or the distress of these payments to which they are liable under the terms of their pact. That \'something\', too, is not licit unless it is jet black.

(Nicolas Rémy proves this with many examples in his Worship of Evil Spirits, Book 1, chapter (x) \[The evil spirit\] usually imprints his brand or mark \[stigma vel characterem\] on some part of the body, sometimes in one place, sometimes in another, quite frequently hidden away, as though \[these people\] were runaway slaves whom he had bought and marked as his, this mark being insensible when pierced with a needle. You will not be surprised if you remember that in the last days Antichrist will imprint the Mark of the Beast (the Devil) on the hand or forehead of his partners, as St John bears witness in his revelation, (Apocalypse 13.16). This should be understood in the literal sense of the real character or mark of the Beast, as Primasius \[of Hadrumetum\] and \[Ambrosius\] Ansbertus say in their commentaries on the Apocalypse, and St Hippolytus in his book on the end of the world.³² The evil spirit has been accustomed to do this from the beginning of the world, because this unutterable wretch loves to imitate God who signs His people with the sign of circumcision in the Old Testament and with the sign of the cross in the New, a sign which \[Gregory\] Naziazen and St Jerome think was the successor to the sign of circumcision. Likewise, the Devil has signed with a particular sign, from their cradles, those heretics who are mixed up in magical practices.
[Page 8]

The authors who tell us this are well worth taking seriously. Irenaeus recorded, \'The followers of Carpocrates make use of magical techniques and incantations, potions and love spells, and familiar spirits who send dreams, and every other pieces of mischief, saying they have the power to lord it over the princes and structures of this world. On a mark which is branded \[on someone he adds, \'But others from among them brand their disciples on the back of the right ear-lobe\'³³ The evil spirit has usually marked \[people\] on the forehead, as Tertullian points out when he writes, Certain passages of Scripture are interpreted\] by the Devil, of course, whose business it is to falsify the truth. He even imitates the divine sacraments during secrets acts when idols are worshipped. He himself baptises certain people, especially those who believe in him and are his faithful \[adherents\] and promises them expiation of their sins. This is how he still consecrates people to Mithras. This is how he still marks his soldiers on their forehead.³⁴ Who were they? The followers of Basilides, who used to worship Abraxas. According to St Jerome, Abraxas is the same as Mithras, \[Commentary on Amos 5.9-10\], and so the origin of this mark is as old \[as that\]. I shall say more about this later.

\(xi\) Those who have been marked in this fashion make many promises very similar to those of Les Gueux of our times during their secret rites:³⁵ that they will never worship the Eucharist; that they will always be insulting and abusive to the Blessed Virgin and the relics of the saints, not only in word but in deed as well; that they will trample underfoot, spit upon, and break into pieces any relics and images of the saints; that they will refrain from making the sign of the cross, and using holy water, blessed salt, wax \[objects\] and relics blessed and consecrated by the Church; that they will never make a complete confession of their sins to a priest; that they will keep hidden, by a stubborn silence, their traffic with the evil spirit; that on the appointed days they will fly to meetings if they can; that they will not be sluggish in carrying out what has to be done there; and finally, that they will bins to the evil spirit\'s service as many people as they can. The Devil in his turn promises he will always be with them, will satisfy their desires in this world, and will bless them after death. These pacts do not bind both sides equally. The situation of the contracting parties is entirely unbalanced, because the humans are handing themselves over to eternal death and through sin are being made actual slaves of the Devil; and until such time as the demon-possessed come to God\'s grace again, they are subjected to the most hideous servitude.³⁶ Even so, humans acquire no right and no power over the evil spirit from this pact, and no \[special\] capability at all enters into the signs the pact obliges them to use. The crafty fellow pretends he is being compelled and that, of his own accord, he is carrying out whatever this kind of magic professes \[to do\].

The very serious mortal sin of idolatry takes place in these \[versions\] of the pact because it explicitly exhibits to created beings the cult of idol-worship. If magicians cast the Faith from their mind, they are apostates. If, on the other hand, they do not cast the Faith out entirely, but simply believe that they owe the Devil this worship, or that they can do what the Catholic faith says they cannot do, they are heretics; and if they \[merely\] pretend to do all these things, they are neither apostates nor heretics, but still commit a very grave mortal sin because they are committing an act of idolatry of their own free will and accord. Even if they have been forced by the evil spirit and have done this out of extreme fear, (as sometimes happens), they are still committing a less serious mortal sin against the profession of Faith. In these explicit pacts, which have to be distinguished from one another, one finds plain evidence from the solemn rituals \[these people use\]. \[Paolo\] Grillando has described these very clearly and, from the confession of a single witch \[strix\] has given very strong confirmation of what I have said, and I shall not hesitate to copy down what he says in \[his\] question.
[Page 9]

\'This woman, in hope of saving her life, gave an account of everything from beginning to end. This acknowledgement of her requires individual headings to express it all. First, she said that her \[Novice\] Mistress brought her in front of the judgement-seat of their prince, he being the Devil in the form of a king seated upon a throne of majesty. The \[Novice\] Mistress had instructed her beforehand what she should do, so she came forward and first was obliged to deny her baptism and abandon all the teachings of the Christian faith\'. (Here you have apostasy.) \'Then she had to cast aside all the Church\'s sacraments, and trample underfoot the cross and images of the Blessed Virgin Mary and other saints.\' (Here you have sacrilege.) \'They do not do these last in front of the Devil but do them elsewhere later on. It is enough that they promise to do them\'. (Here you have the profession). \'Then she made her vow by means of a solemn engagement, \[putting her hands\] in the prince\'s hands. She vowed and promised she would always be faithful to him, be obedient to him, and obey all his commandments\'. (Here you have the vow.)

\'Then, in the midst of her oath-taking, she touched \[some\] characters written on the outside of a large book containing pages which were hidden away, and did homage or perpetual vassalage\', (here you have homage,) \'\[saying\] she would never return to the faith of Christ, or keep God\'s commandments, but that she would pay attention only to those \[issued\] by the prince himself, and that in future she would be obedient to him and could come every time when summoned to \[his\] revels and assemblies. \[She also promised\] to do what the other women did during those \[assemblies\], and also that she would be present at their nocturnal sacrifices and would \[make\] the customary prayers and attend the ceremony of adoration\'. (Here you have idol-madness.) \'She would fulfil, to the best of her ability, whatever vows \[the prince\] might happen to put in front of her and bring as many people as she could to \[make\] this same profession\'. (Here you have the declaration.) \'On his part, however, the prince himself, with surpassing effrontery, promised to give this woman in front of him everlasting happiness, grandiose things, immeasurable things, and whatever pleasures she wanted to have in this world, and that at long last, after this life, she would obtain greater gifts \[than these\]\'. This is what Grillando says.

Jean del Vaulx from Stavelot, a seer \[sortiarius\], used to add further that the Devil had convinced him that after this life seers would be like evil spirits and would be changed into evil spirits and be given lordship in the air. Anyone easily realises that these things go back to the twaddle \[one finds\] in the works of Origen.

The implicit pact is of two kinds. First, when someone knowingly and willingly uses those superstitious signs usually employed by magicians, which he has got either from books or from conversations with magicians or other people. This, too, is a mortal sin, because in no way is it legitimate knowingly to enter into any fellowship, directly or indirectly, with vicious spirits \[spiritus\], as theologians and canon lawyers agree. If, however, anyone were to have got some natural remedy from the books of, or conversations with, magicians, was not party to any pact, he could legitimately use a remedy of this kind. This type of pact is easily recognised. The second type of implicit pact is when someone uses signs, not knowing they are magical, because he is unaware that these wicked things have been created by an evil spirit. This usually happens to those people who, in good faith, read superstitious books, thinking they are by reputable natural philosophers or physicians. The same applies when they are told about them by those who have a reputation of being good people and true to the Faith. In the case of private individuals, this is either not a sin at all or a very slight one, provided two conditions obtain: (i) if one can prove their ignorance \[of such things\]-for example, they are people who are not considered to have any knowledge of this as opposed to those who could be considered to be in possession of such knowledge, such as Church ministers, confessors, the heads of religious houses, and Doctors \[of Divinity or Canon Law\]. Those \[private\] individuals are, for the most part, labouring under a bone-idle, crass ignorance. Their sin, however, is less serious than that of those who knowingly make drawings \[of the signs\]; (ii) if the person who does not know what these things are is willing to give up this kind of superstition once he has been admonished, because after admonition he cannot plead ignorance. \[At that point\] he becomes contumacious, and because that is serious, the magnitude of the offence increases. This second kind of pact is very difficult to distinguish from an effect which is natural or miraculous, \[but\] I shall talk about this in the next question.
[Page 10]

Meanwhile, if you ask what persuades an evil spirit to \[make\] a pact of this kind and be obedient or submissive, it appears that workers of harmful magic \[malefici\] and the evil spirit have something in common which gives rise to a kind of intimacy rather like friendship. Little by little from this is born mutual trust. The trust of the lesser party creates a certain boldness and insolence, like that of imperial command, in the greater, and in the greater a willingness in some measure to do what his fellow-signatory commands. Thus, it turns out that the worker of harmful magic relies on the evil spirit when he thinks he is giving the orders, and the evil spirit pretends to recognise \[the magical worker\'s\] authority. Abbot Trithemius explains this very well at length in question 5 of his Book of Eight Questions, but in it he makes a different point, because he thinks that the evil spirit is willing to gratify his slaves by obeying them. Whatever the pact may be, he hates them with the utmost bitterness, and the consequence is that the more strongly and straitly he ensnares them, the more certainly and quickly he binds and destroys them.

Finally, one must understand most especially that it is a very serious sin to covenant with evil spirits by means of any pact, as I pointed out some time ago in my Admonitions. Here and now I shall simply insert and add many other points which, along with other people, Sébastien Michaëlis has written, most authoritatively, in chapter 7 of his Pneumologie, against those who make excuses for magicians, if they do no harm to others, on the grounds that such defenders of magicians maintain it is not wicked at all to use evil spirits\' help for good, especially when one is in command of them, as we read Christ and the Apostles were.

\[Michaëlis\] maintains the contrary, and is absolutely right to do so-that it is the height of wickedness to argue this way, and that this is the sign of the mind of a complete fool and someone who despises God, as Proverbs 18.3 says, \'When an irreligious person is deeply mired\' (in sin), \'he feels contempt, because in this state he mocks everything. If anyone says the punishments of Hell are to be feared and that the impenitent should be damned for eternity, \[the irreligious person\] laughs and says one should not be afraid of Hell at all, that God does not want the death of a sinner, and did not create a human being so that he should perish, etc. \[Michaëlis says\] that in Theodoret\'s opinion these people are undoubtedly worse than evil spirits themselves because \'evil spirits believe and tremble\', \[James 2.19\] \[whereas\] these people do not believe and do not tremble. They are also worse than heretics because when St Athanasius asked the chief of the heretics, Arius, what he thought-does someone who worships Satan do well or ill?-Arius replied that such a person is without religion and without God, has no common sense, and does not deserve to be called a human being. (St Athanasius during disputations at the Council of Nicaea.) This, spoken by a liar, is most accurately said and applies to seers \[sortiarios\] because these people worship Satan.

But Christ and the Apostles commanded Satan and told him what they wanted him to do. Why can we not do this as well? I could say we are not allowed to imitate whatever we like of what was done by the saints or God. When it comes to divine matters, you see, we must believe certain things, such as the Incarnation, transubstantiation, the Trinity, and Purgatory; we should honour certain similar things with our wonder, such as the miracles of Christ our Lord and holy people; and we should imitate certain things, such as those which are pertinent to the way we conduct our morals and our life, things which Christ commanded us to learn, such as gentleness, humility, and love. (On this point, see what St Paul said, \'Be imitators of me as I also am \[an imitator\] of Christ\', \[1 Corinthians n.1\]). A wish to imitate the wonderful things I mentioned is \[the mark\] of the very arrogant, stupid disposition of someone who wants and strives to be like God in omnipotence and omniscience. Such people imitate the Devil who wanted to be like the Most High, since they want to compel evil spirits through their command, as Christ did, \[and\] since such people want to learn secrets and future events from these \[evil spirits\], they are like Adam and Eve who sought a knowledge similar to that of God. God forbade this when He said, \'I shall not give my glory to someone else\', \[Isaiah 42.8\]. But such people say, \'I shall usurp it, even if you do not want me to\'. Christians in the old days used to exercise authority over evil spirits, (as Tertullian says), \'not by means of an operation to invite them in, but by dominating them in order to get rid of them\'.
[Page 11]

Therefore, my answer is that those who have a pact with evil spirits not only do not imitate the Lord and the Apostles, but clearly do the opposite, because \[the Lord and the Apostles\] were not in the habit of summoning evil spirits, did not consult them, and did not make use of their assistance. No, they would rebuke them, banish them, torture them, and even when \[the spirits\] spoke the truth would order them to fall silent. They would treatment just as though they were extremely hostile enemies, and they knew that they are our adversaries and range about like ravening lions, seeking out a soul they can devour. From the beginning of the world, God has set in place enmities between a human being and the Devil, (Genesis 3.15). These people enter into a treaty with God\'s foe and sworn enemy. After Job has spoken about the great malice of the evil spirit, he ends, Remember the war. Don\'t say anything further\', (Job 40.27), as if to say, \'Don\'t boast in vain or delude yourself about an armistice or striking a peace with him. Be on your guard, break your fast, stand ready for action, and await the signal for battle\'. God, as the one who ratifies treaties, has declared war.⁴⁶ There is perpetual warfare between God and the Devil because, \'The pride of those who hate God is always climbing higher\', \[Psalm 73.23\], and the Devil is correctly named \'Satan\', that is \'adversary\'. Consequently we, who are God\'s vassals, cannot make peace or have dealings with His enemy. St Paul declares, \'I do not want you to be the allies of evil spirits\', (1 Corinthians 10.21).

We made this promise during the sacrament of baptism, and are bound by this oath \[we took.\] The priest asks in the name of the Church and our godparents answer for us again and again, \'We renounce Satan\', (the adversary), \'and his vainglorious displays\'. Christians have been given the power to trample upon snakes and St Paul promises, \'God will grind down Satan beneath your feet\', \[Romans 16.20\]. Why do these people fall prostrate at \[Satan\'s\] feet? The Church has been promised the power to work miracles in the name of Christ and to cast out evil spirits. Why do \[these people\] seek refuge in the name and invocation of Beelzebub? So since this war was enjoined \[on us\] in the beginning, at the foundation of the world, and was renewed by the Apostles\' trumpet when the Christian Church began, and we swore a solemn oath and promised \[to continue it\] when we were regenerated in the Church, doesn\'t it follow that the person who makes any pact or cultivates intimate friendship with the enemies of the Church and of God is guilty of treachery, desertion, and apostasy no less than if a monks abandons his profession and his vows, a soldier deserts to the enemy, or a Christian takes himself off to Mahommedanism, Judaism, or some other heresy? All these are sins against the First Commandment, and therefore quite rightly I consider them to be very serious because that \[commandment\] is first in importance as well as in order. Because of their aim, the commandments of the first category are more important than those of the second, and among the three \[commandments\] of the first category, the first is superior to the others. The third \[cayegory\] deals principally with outward worship of God, the second with the words by which one must show Him honour, and the first with the love and inward fidelity one owes to God by virtue of religion-theosebeia or latria.⁴⁷ Consequently, since this virtue is the most acceptable of all to God, the sin which is its opposite must be particularly hateful \[to Him\], and so God added a penalty to this first commandment when He testifies that He is powerful and jealous, and that He will avenge this sin as far as the third and fourth generation, and calls those who transgress this commandment people who hate Him, (Exodus 20.5). So serious, then, is the sin of covenanting with an evil spirit.
[Page 12]

Moreover, such pacts are useless and worthless. The evil spirit never keeps his word and does not think he is bound by any promise. He dared to lie to Christ when he said, \'All this I shall give you if you prostrate yourself and worship me\', that is, revere yourself. Didn\'t another \[evil spirit\] promise Cyprian, the magician, that he would enjoy Justina? What did \[the spirit\] actually do?⁴⁸ What madness it is to expect the truth from the father of lying! Didn\'t Jesus say of him, \'There is no truth in him. When he speaks, he speaks a lie in accordance with his nature because he is a liar and the father \[of lying\]\'? (John 8.44). Therefore, you covenant with him in vain. He is a mixture, fused from lying and treachery. Believe \[St John\]. You are never going to find anyone \[the evil spirit\] has never deceived. In Book 8 of his Against Celsus, Origen shrewdly writes, \'One can assume that since evil spirits are wicked, they will not keep their word and pledge even to their own worshippers\'. Yes indeed, one can not only assume it, one can take for granted they will not keep \[their word\], because they are liars and pursue human beings with an irreconcilable hatred which they have never abated since the time of their fall, and never will diminish or abate. If God were to allow it, they would quickly destroy every mortal being. But because they see they are not allowed to do so, by using many different ways of alluring \[people\] into sin, they aim continually at the death of the soul, and this is how they try to make sure that human beings fall from blessedness.

The magnitude of their hatred is clear from the vehemence and bitterness of the tortures and hardships they have inflicted, and from the number of times God has given them limited permission \[to afflict Job, Sarah\'s husbands, St Antony, and countless others. Job tells us about the persistence of their hatred in 41.15 when he says, \'His heart will be hardened like a stone, and will have pain inflicted upon it, as though it were a smith\'s anvil, and in John 8.44 our Saviour explains the personal spirit \[genius\] of the father by reference to what his children do. \'You are \[the children\] of your father, the Devil, and you want to carry out your father\'s wishes. He was a murderer from the beginning\'. By these words He points out that this endeavour \[of the Devil\] is perpetual, and since he cannot \[achieve\] his end by overt force he proceeds by trickery and a semblance of kindliness, as St Athanasius, writing to the bishops of Egypt and Libya, very clearly tells us in his Against Arius, at the beginning of Oration 1. So those who trust \[an evil spirit\] are stupid and forget what the Holy Spirit warns us in Job 40.22-that it is impossible for anyone to submit himself to that lofty degree of arrogance. \'Will he multiply his prayers to you or speak soft \[words\] to you?\' that is, sincerely and with good faith to fulfil \[his promise to you. \'Will he make a covenant with you, and will you accept him as though he were going to be your slave for ever?\' \[40.23\]. He will dissemble and make promises but will not fulfil what he has promised. Therefore, this indicates that these \[promises\] should be regarded as áδúvata \[incapable of fulfilment\]. He cannot be tied to you by any bonds; he cannot be enclosed by any bars. \'Will you sport with him as \[you do with\] a bird, or will you hem him in with your slave-girls?\' \[40.24\]. That is to say, he will obey a human being who has preferred to do without eternal blessedness rather than obey God and worship the Word made human.
[Page 13]

Finally, these pacts and this intimacy with an evil spirit are not only useless and worthless. They are dangerous and baleful beyond measure. St Leo \[the Great says that evil spirits\' favours cause more hurt than any wounds and that it is best to merit their hatred than to be at peace with them, (Sermon 19 on the Lord\'s Passion). Reason confirms that nothing is more deadly than the death of the soul. They incur this by these pacts and this intimacy, and not only \[the death\] of the soul, but often of the body as well. I shall illustrate this now with one example, and with more on another occasion. In Book 1, chapter 9 of his Worship of Evil Spirits, Nicolas Rémy describes Théodore Maillot as follows.

\'When he was an old man, he obtained the governorship of a province in a huge kingdom. \[This province\] comes under the jurisdiction of Lorraine. While he was still a young man, he was desperate to marry a young woman from a very splendid family. All hope of his being able to achieve this had been torn from him and snatched away because he had no money and was of humble stock. He was an apprentice engaged in trade which, at that time, meant he was low-born and despised. So, he saw no sufficiently honourable way of even making his love known. Therefore, as people in hopeless situations, when expectation itself deserts them, easily turn to any kind of plan and embrace it without any thought whether they should shun it or follow it, Maillot went to a fellow-servant from Germany \[because\] he had been told that this man had an evil spirit who was prepared to be compliant in every respect. He opened up to him and told him what was making him ill and begged him not to begrudge him a remedy if he could provide him with one. His kindness, \[he said\], would not go unrewarded. The German willingly seized his opportunity because, in accordance with his pact, in a few days\' time he either had to free and disengage himself from the evil spirit by bringing him someone else who would transfer this debt to himself or incur another one, or be put to death and killed at the hands \[of the evil spirit\] who would twist and break his neck, (because this stipulation had been added to the pact.)

So, in order to carry out his plan, he agreed they should meet the following day at daybreak in an out-of-the-way room which would be locked and have its shutters drawn. They had scarcely got there when the doors suddenly opened and in came an elegant young woman with a generous figure. (The evil spirit wanted to show himself in this form first so that Maillot would not become terrified at his frightening appearance.) She announced she was the woman who would easily get him the marriage he wanted so badly, provided he was willing to follow the instructions she would give him. He was all agog and eagerly waited to hear whatever she would tell him \[to do\]. The first thing she started with was an instruction to abstain from theft, drunkenness, lust, blasphemy, and every other sin which defiles the soul. \[Then she said\] he should cultivate devout behaviour, support the needy from his resources, and weaken himself twice a week by fasting. He was not to neglect \[the Church\'s\] feast-days, he should pray every day, and he must unremittingly attend to all the things it was proper and fitting for a Christian to do. If he was willing to bind himself by a formal vow \[to do\] all this, the result would be that there would be no difficulty in his obtaining the marriage he wanted. Once she had said this and appointed a day by which she would receive his answer, she left hastily. Maillot, who could see that such holy and respectable conditions made a benefit of such importance to him obtainable, now began to think there was no point in delaying and that he should gratefully and willingly accept \[her proposal\]. But while he was giving the matter more and more thought and being driven hither and thither in different ways between hope and fear, a member of the household, who was a priest, guessed from the expression on his face that he was concealing something which was perplexing him. He approached him gently, found out what was troubling him, and with his advice prevailed on Maillot not to stoop to any further conversation with the evil spirit. The German was thus deceived in his expectation, and it was not long before he paid the penalty laid down in his pact, because not many days later, on a level open road, he tumbled from his horse, fell heavily to the ground, and was found to have died in an instant\'. This is what Rémy says.
[Page 14]

Book 2, Question 5

What are the signs by which one can recognise the effects of magic, which is dependent on a pact from effects which are natural, miraculous, and created by artifice.

When one does not find that a power is miraculous or natural or a clever piece of artifice, \[it means\] that a pact has intervened. Theologians have usually explained this over-obscure, brief dictum in broader terms. It applies to demonic magic (i) if the things which are done exceed their natural capability. This happens if it is explicitly established that what has been done does not have sufficient power to achieve this effect and that there is no good reason to ascribe it to God or good angels. On this point, \[Gregorio de\] Valencia says that the object employed for that purpose will fail to work if the distance \[at which it is meant to operate\] is too great, because there is general agreement that the cause \[of an action\] which is at a distance \[from its object\] cannot work unless the space between them is altered. Consequently, if that same cause did not work after it had been brought nearer or very close to \[its object\], but does work at a distance, Valencia thinks it is clear \[the action\] is not being done naturally. He says it would be another matter if a person of sound judgement were at least to confess he did not know the cause, because the power and comparative relationship of things in Nature is quite often hidden from us. It would also be another matter, in my opinion, if sensible people were to disagree with each other about whether such a thing can be done naturally or not-for example, if they were discussing an effect produced in something which was not far enough away \[from its cause\]. It is doubtful, you see, whether anything can act directly upon a passive object which is at a distance from it and have no effect at all on the space which is between them, and whether an active and a passive object have to be next to each another at the same time in accordance with the \[kind of\] contact known as \'quantitative\': or whether it is enough for them to touch in a manner known as \'virtual\' because its passive object exists inside the space to which the power of the active object can extend; because although in St Thomas \[Aquinas\]\'s opinion, not many people \[think this\] and it is not particularly likely, he does say it rests upon very plausible arguments and very serious-minded men have found it acceptable.

\(ii\) These same theologians think that there are three other sins attached to demonic magic. (a) If unknown, spurious, cryptic words are employed, \[words which\] are ridiculous, completely incoherent, or sacred but twisted to mean other things or to a meaning they were not designed to have; or if names of God, whose meaning one does not recognise, are adopted, or unknown names of good or bad angels, \[names\] the Church does not recognise. (Guillaume d\'Auvergne has discussed these very learnedly.)⁵¹ It is the same thing if certain characters or other figures are used, apart from the sign of the cross, or if that sign is not placed or fashioned as it should be, or placed where it should not be, or repeated unnecessarily in certain places a certain number of times; and finally, if some other object which has nothing to do with the effect intended by the operation is added. (b) When the effectiveness of the operation is attributed to certain ritual actions and practices peculiar to and especially devised for it-for example, that \[the operation\] be done on certain days or at certain hours, when the stars are in a particular position, on condition that there is a certain number of crosses or candles, or that an object is positioned in a particular way in such and such a drawing, and this it be of such and such a colour or shape. (c) When power is attributed to other practices which are clearly superfluous and neither good nor bad-for example, when, in order to drive out an evil spirit, someone is shaved so that he can be clothed in a piece of cloth which has never been washed, when he carries out the operation with one foot naked, without wearing a belt, with his hair dishevelled, and things of this kind.
[Page 15]

When it is said that all these, or one of them, is necessary for the operation to be successful, one is quite rightly more than suspicious, especially when two things happen at the same time, namely, one believes that what is done is not proportionate to the effect which is meant to be produced and is not being done with legitimate authority-that is, when the person who arranged and prescribed an object as a sign for such an effect was unable to make good a defect in Nature. (God does this in the sacraments, and the Church in her sacramental). Likewise when the effect itself cannot be produced except by means of a cause provided by the intellect and the required conditions do not seem to be of a kind which is appropriate or one which usually moves God or the angels to produce the effect, because under those circumstances it is clearer than daylight that the hoped-for effect can come only from an evil spirit: for example, if anyone demands that a statue tell him about things which are secret, any threats or prayers he might make would be absurd and ridiculous, as were those of the magicians Porphyrius tells us about, and the seal-rings of Lachesis made, according to Claudius Claudianus, for these people, perhaps in imitation of those \[secret things\].

On the other hand, when wonderful effects of this kind follow on from anything, and as often as this is done without ceremonies or ritual observances, we quite rightly come to the conclusion that the effect is produced by Nature. Here we must be careful to be on our guard with respect to the last two points \[I have made\] so that on the one hand during this investigation we are not rash enough to come to the hasty conclusion that all the effects are natural, because on many occasions an evil spirit adds a number of natural things to the mixture to make us think the whole thing is natural, and on the other we do not condemn them rashly because, (as St Augustine tells us), the natural properties of many things surpass and are hidden from our knowledge.⁵³ Things done by artifice can be detected with very little trouble if a demonstration of how they are done is given privately either to a magistrate, (whose job it is to examine these things judicially in open court, or to testify publicly to those who admire such things that \[their performers\] are licensed to do them), or to a confessor in the court of the confessional.

Book 2, Question 6

Does this magic produce real effects, and how can one recognise the pact which underlies them?

Anyone who maintains that all effects are the result of a conjuring-trick, or believes that they are all real, must be a water-melon, not a human being. Quite often an evil spirit deceives us because he is the father of lying. Therefore, he frequently blunts the eyes or dupes the other senses by casting a deceptive image in front of them. \[But\] not infrequently God obstructs him so that he cannot actually do something he would want or be able to do as a mark of favour to magicians, and when he realises this, he takes refuge in conjuring-tricks in order to hide his impotence. But when the evil spirit wants to produce a genuine effect and God allows him to do so, provided the \[effect\] does not go beyond the power he has, God does nothing to stop him from producing a real effect. Indeed, natural causes do give rise to a real effect, so the Devil can use them and connect active things with passive and therefore will be able to produce effects which are genuine. The minor proposition is proved because, according to the admirable author, Dionysius of Athens, \[the Devil\] lost none of his natural gifts by sinning and consequently it turns out that he also possesses a natural power which is very strong, a multiplicity of daily experience, and an exact knowledge of essential character and capabilities which are peculiar to each and every thing-so much so that he can very easily and accurately produce the effect he wants. This is why the majority of theologians cleverly recognise two magical effects-those which are real and those which are apparent. (This is why Tertullian calls \[magicians\] mountebanks and tricksters). I myself add a third type, a mixture of true and false effects, or ones which are merely apparent.
[Page 16]

I am referring to the effects of conjuring-tricksters\' \[apparatus\] which I reviewed in Book 1, chapter 4. Likewise, there is the story Philostratus wrote about a young man, Menippus, and all the furnishings and drinking-vessels at his wedding to a vampire witch \[lamia\], a story I have mentioned elsewhere, and Nicephorus\'s story about Empusa or \[Gelō\]-that when he was a child she often took \[the Emperor\] Maurice out of his bed, with no trouble at all, as though she intended to eat him directly. Secondly, there is the story about Pasetes whose habit was to use certain incantations to make it look as though a sumptuous banquet suddenly appeared. Then, whenever he wanted, everything would vanish again. It was also his custom to buy things and pay the price, but not long afterwards it would be seen that the money had secretly returned from the seller to the buyer. Thirdly, we read many such things about Simon Magus \[the Magician\] in St Clement of Rome \[who says\] he created a new human being out of air, became invisible to anyone he wanted, pierced rocks as though they were mud, brought statues to life, did not burn when he was placed on a fire, gave people to understand that, like Janus, he had two faces, changed himself into a sheep or a nanny-goat, rose into the air and flew, suddenly displayed a great deal of gold, was able to make kings and depose them. He told a scythe to come and reap, and it came and reaped ten times as much as any of the other \[scythes\]. Once, when a prostitute, Selene, was in a castle-tower and a huge crowd had hurried to see her and the tower was surrounded by a garland of bystanders, he made it look as though she was looking at the entire populace and leaning out of every window in the tower simultaneously. Finally, by means of earnest conjurations, he would evoke the soul of a child who had suffered a violent death, and with its help would do things of this kind. This is what Clement says.

Anastasius Nicenus adds several pieces of information. \'He used to make statues walk\', he says, \'and when he rolled himself round in a fire he would not be burned. He used to fly in the air and make bread from stones. He would turn into a snake and transform himself into many other creatures. He used to have two faces, change himself into gold, and cause all kinds of things to appear during banquets. He used to make the vessels in houses appear to move unseen, of their own accord, to perform the functions of those who were carrying them, and he would cause many ghosts to walk in front of him, which he said were the souls of dead people\'. The historian \[Michael\] Glykas\'s selection of these are along the same lines. Fourthly, Nicetas Choniates refers to this \[in his story\] about a magician by whose agency an oarsman started to break all the pottery he was carrying.

Fifthly, within living memory, there took place the conjuring tricks of Ziton in Bohemia, with whom I shall also deal in the final chapter of the next Book. \'As a display of his skill he would present himself to the King now in his own appearance and stature, now in those of someone else: likewise, wearing crimson and silk which were then suddenly wool and dirty rags. He would also steer towards someone walking on dry land as though he himself were on water. On several occasions he would have himself conveyed by cockerels tied to the epiredium⁵⁷ as his carriage-horses. As well as this, he used to play games on the King\'s dinnerguests in various ways, sometimes transforming their hands into cows\' \'feet so that they could not reach their plates, and sometimes into horses\' hooves. Several times he fastened very wide antlers on their forehead, and whenever they looked out of the windows at an unexpected sight, obviously they could not draw their head and face back to the table again; and to demonstrate that he could easily procure money for his own use, he fashioned thirty fat pigs from bundles of hay and drove them off as food for a rich miller who lived very close by. He offered them for sale at a price the miller was willing to pay but warned the buyer not to drive his new flock to the river. The miller ignored this warning, the pigs sank, and the miller saw bundles \[of hay\] floating in the river. So he looked for the seller for a long time and finally discovered him in a wine-shop, lying down on a bench with his feet stretched out. Filled with anger, he tried to seize hold of him by one of his feet and wake him up. Ziton shrieked loudly, got hold of the buyer by the throat, and dragged him off to ajudge. What was the miller to do, (since, so he thought, he had clearly been arrested), unless he were to add insult to injury with \[another\] injury and compound this injustice with Ziton further?\' Thus far Dubravius. Lastly, \[one should mention\] magicians\' boastful claims for certain herbs such as vervain, latace, sage, and carline thistle-for which see Pliny and Gellius.
[Page 17]

Finally, there are the kinds of things which (shame on them!) certain overcredulous, but in other respects devout princes used to allow to be done in their presence. \[A magician\] would place iron objects, without a magnet, silver dishes, and similar things of great weight on top of a table and draw them, jumping up and down as they went, from one end of the table to the other without the help of a cord, a hair, or any other device. He would get someone to pull out a playing-card from the rest of the pack without letting anyone else see it, and then change its face three times while the person who drew it still had the card in his hand. \[The magician\] would fashion mirrors which seemed to show things happening in places far away. He would make a real shrub grow in the space of three hours, a span\'s length from the table, and trees with leaves and fruit to spring up from another one he put in its place on the same spot. He would light a lantern with a special flame and make all the women present take off their clothes and reveal to the eyes those parts which Nature tells us should remain concealed; and while they were unclothed like this, they could not stop stamping their feet and jumping and dancing \[tripudiare\] as long as the lantern, which was hung up in the middle of the room, inscribed with certain characters, and filled with hare\'s fat, continued to burn. \[There are\] other stories of the same kind which apprentice entertainers gulp down when they read and learn from the writings of Battista della Porta, and since these do not offer either natural causes or satisfactory explanations involving skilful ingenuity to account for such things, one must ascribe them to conjuring-tricks.

According to most people, examples of genuine effects are (i) snake-charming, about which the prophet \[David\] says, \'Their frenzy is like that of a snake such as a deaf adder which refuses to listen and will not hear the voice of the enchanters and of the worker of poisonous magic while he is uttering his clever chants\', \[Psalm 58.4\]. (ii) Likewise the signs and wonders of the false prophets in St Matthew\'s Gospel, and (iii) those which, as I shall point out in their proper places, can be done and have been done. There are two \[examples\] in Holy Scripture about which it is not at all clear whether their effects were genuine or simply appeared to be so. The first is the resuscitation of Samuel by a female diviner \[pythonissa\].

Those who think it was not Samuel\'s soul which came, but an evil spirit under enchantment \[daemonem larvatum\] which came in his place, class this as an illusion, and perfectly serious writers have been of this opinion, too. In his book, Care for the Dead, however, St Augustine firmly maintains-as do \[Alonso\] Tostado and \[Nicholas\] of Lyra, and a good many modern \[writers\] I have praised elsewhere-that it really was Samuel\'s soul which appeared to Saul, not one evoked by the power of a magical chant. (Tertullian and those who followed him quite rightly rebut this.) But by God\'s command it came quickly in order to rebuke the irreligious king and call him to repentance for fear of his death which had been intimated to him. This opinion seems to me to be more true and more reliable because objectors do not yet offer a sufficiently satisfactory answer to the very clear testimony of Ecclesiasticus \[46.20\] where we read the following about Samuel. \'And after this he fell asleep\', (that is, he died and rested), \'and the King was told\', (which king, if not Saul, and when, if not on this occasion?), \'and Samuel showed him the end of his life\', (he foretold that Saul would die the next day), \'and he raised his voice from the earth in prophecy\'.
[Page 18]

What is this if not a coming forth from Hell which is in the middle of the earth? What is this if not someone speaking in his own voice and prophesying? Assuredly none other than he who had died and fallen asleep. The objection that he says, \'Tomorrow you will be with me\', Kings 28.19\] does not preclude this, because it cannot be said of Samuel\'s real soul. That was in Limbo, whereas Saul\'s was in Hell. How can a lie be attributed to the prophet? I am not saying this precludes \[the objection\] because those words, (as St Augustine says), can either be said of a state like that of death, as though he were saying, \"Tomorrow you will be with me, not as one of the living but as one of the dead. Tomorrow will be the last day which will dawn for you\': or we can say, in reference to the close resemblance of this place and habitation, that \[both Samuel and Saul\] are like someone who is in the same city, although not in the same house. The Limbo of the \[Church\] Fathers and the place \[called\] Gehenna belonging to the same Hell are different dwelling-places, like houses in a single town. (I shall say more on this subject later in this same Book in question 26, section 4.)
