The second \[example\], about the magicians in Egypt who vied with Moses in the performance of miracles, is open to uncertainty. It is written \[Exodus 7.11-12\] as follows: \'By means of incantations in Egyptian and certain secrets \[they had\], they did likewise and each man threw down his magic wand which turned into a snake\'. In many other places, too, they are later said \'to have done the same kind of thing as Moses\' and to have tried, in what turned out to be a fruitless attempt, to produce stinging insects, \[Exodus 8.8\]; and \'they could not do it\' is added. It is possible to defend the opinion of those who think that the magicians\' snakes and frogs were simply conjuring-tricks. Nevertheless, everyone else\'s opinion has always seemed to me to be more reliable and more likely because it is more consistent with Scripture and is not inconsistent with the known facts of natural philosophy. Doesn\'t it say that the magicians did just as Moses did? But he produced real snakes and real frogs. If those of the magicians were merely apparent, why didn\'t Moses reveal their fraud, or why did God allow him to be deceived in such a serious matter? Why are the magicians said to have tried \[to produce\] stinging insects and could not do so, if they did not actually try to produce real stinging insects?

There is still argument about the way in which they were produced. The more worthwhile argument comes from those who think the Devil stealthily withdrew the wands while the spectators were not paying attention, and substituted genuine snakes the same size as the wands in their place so quickly and so cleverly that the Egyptians who were watching were completely convinced that the appearance of the wands themselves had been changed this way: and because of this error of judgement by the spectators, when Scripture said the wands were changed into snakes, \[the change took place\] not in reality but in people\'s opinion. Consequently, they argue that \[both interpretations\] have something in common because, just as Moses\'s snake was real, the magicians\' snakes were real as well. However, just as snakes were produced from Moses\'s rod\'s acting as the final point of a change which had \[already\] started, snakes were produced from the magicians\' wands, too, but \[their wands were not the final point at which the change ceased to take place. I know why they say this and what their reasons are, but in my opinion there is nothing contrary to the principles of natural philosophy if we say, along with St Augustine and \[Cardinal\] Cajetan that real snakes and frogs came out of real wands which were acting as the source of change. Insects are living creatures and belong to a class of things which are not yet complete. The Devil could have produced them in a short space of time from wands which had become rotten, (something not beyond his capability), because what the sun would take many days to effect, he can do in a very short space of time by applying active \[agents\] which he knows are rather effective for this purpose.
[Page 19]

So, is there no difference between the magicians\' marvels and those of Moses? First, what Moses did was miraculous. What the magicians did was merely amazing. Secondly, Listen to \[Michael\] Glykas. \'They themselves did indeed turn their wands into snakes, but Moses\'s rod began to swallow their wands. They also turned water into blood but, once it had been changed, they could not turn it back into its former state. They also produced frogs but were unable to cleanse the Egyptians\' houses of them again. These \[creatures\] had the ability to cause annoyance to the Egyptians, but \[the magicians\] had no power to check the punishment which was being meted out to \[the Egyptians\]. Indeed, God even afflicted their bodies with pustules to a greater extent than He did to those of anyone else so that it would become clear from this not only that \[the magicians\] could not prevent the punishments they had provoked from God, but also that they \[would have to\] endure the same punishments as everyone else\'.

My opinion is that the example relating to Samuel\'s soul belongs entirely to the class of \[stories\] which are a mixture \[of truth and falsehood\] because, while the soul of Samuel which appeared was real, one detects falsehood or deception of the senses (a) in the fact that it seemed to obey the diviner\'s voice, and (b) in the fact that it did not seem to have brought \[Samuel\'s\] real body or his real shroud with it. The second example, relating to the Egyptian magicians, will also belong to the class of \'compounds\' if we follow the opinion of those who do not know that the snakes \[appeared as the result of\] a rapid substitution. On the other hand, if we agree with St Augustine\'s opinion, it will belong to the class of things which are \[entirely\] true. Let me also, with pleasure, number among \[examples\] of the \'compound\' type an extraordinary story by Robert Triezius from Lille about a contest between two magicians. One of them had seized an extraordinarily beautiful girl, set her on a wooden horse, and carried her off through the air. The other was presiding over a well-attended banquet in a castle in Burgundy and because the abductor was flying over this castle, he used spells \[carminibus\] to force the abductor to come down into the castle yard. He kept the crestfallen man there, unable to move, in front of everyone, along with his blushing prey. The abductor, however, was not slow to chant a spell secretly against the man who had got the better of him this way and who was not afraid of \[suffering\] anything similar. This man had thrust his head and neck through the bars of a window very high up \[in the wall\] while he was looking down \[into the yard\], and the abductor disrespected him by making huge horns suddenly sprout from his forehead, thus trapping and suspending him from the iron bars by the size of his branch-like extensions. As a result, he could not withdraw his head through the grating and, terrified by the dizzying height, he did not dare lower himself to the ground. The two men mutually agreed upon each other\'s safety, and then the one wearing the horns set the abductor free and sent him on his way with his prize. The abductor rose into the air once again, hidden in the fold of a cloud, and the other man got rid of his horns and, to the great delight of the spectators, withdrew his head safely into the dining-room. I believe the flying happened, but that the evil spirit\'s lifting the wooden\] foal \[into the air\] is not something peculiar to him, \[because lifting things into the air\] is something birds do. So, it is possible that the abductor and the girl he abducted were real, but I think the whole business of the horns was a conjuring-trick.
[Page 20]

So, now it remains to discuss how to distinguish genuine effects from counterfeit by looking at the pact involved. Marvels are not accomplished by magicians, but by the evil spirit himself, and therefore magicians will not be able, (as some people have written without due caution), to accomplish what an evil spirit cannot. But because of their pact, whatever he can do, magicians will be able to do as well. Once this basic point has been made, the following rules, like the pillars of the building, can be built on top of it. (i) If the magical effect is of the kind which exceeds the power exercised by an evil spirit-and powers of this kind are reserved to the omnipotence of God alone-then the effect must be considered a conjuring-trick. (ii) If it is agreed that the effect is not beyond the power of an evil spirit, the matter is plainly open to doubt and one must then take a closer look at the circumstances. The reason for doubt is that God does not always allow an evil spirit to do whatever he is capable of doing and will stop him from doing it; and an evil spirit does not always actually want to do what he is permitted to do but does take pleasure, as a conjuror, in deceiving human beings. But whether the effect exceeds the power of the evil spirit will be investigated in greater detail in what I am going to say next.

Book 2, Question 7

Can magicians do anything which is genuinely miraculous?

From what I said at the end of the preceding question, it is clear that the same question remains-can a magician do anything, and can an evil spirit do anything?-since the things a magician does are done by an evil spirit by virtue of their pact. This is why, once upon a time, magicians claimed they could perform miracles. According to Clement of Rome, Simon the Samaritan boasted he could do anything he wanted to do and that everything was obedient to him. In the chapter on the types of evil spirits in his book on sacrifices, Porphyrius writes That no matter how many times evil spirits have recourse to stratagems \[to produce\] their marvels, many of the things they do are quite contrary to the usual order of Nature. Consequently, Iamblichus calls them \'lackeys of the gods\' because they seem to follow the gods\' footsteps very closely when they do extraordinary things. The poets thought the same, \[although\] perhaps it will be more convenient to quote their words in their appropriate places. Some more recent \[authors\] appear to fall into this error when they say one should not pay attention to Nature\'s laws when it comes to the workings of magic. This I shall now look at more carefully.

I should like the reader to observe that in this treatise I do not use the word \'miracle\' to mean any kind of extraordinary effect, which is what secular authors do. I follow theologians who give it a more restricted definition, which is that of the Greek Tò Oépas, \'simply an effect produced beyond and above the power belonging to the natural order of created things\', (for example, restoring sight to someone born blind, or resurrecting the dead). But when the effect is consistent with the power belonging to the natural order of created things, (for example, the ability to stir up rain and winds, cure a fever, etc.), the Greek word is usually Tò θαûpa-not simply \'a miracle\', but \'a miracle in accordance with x\', or, \'as far as we are concerned, or \'a wonder\', or \'something extraordinary.⁶⁵ Again, these wonders \[mira\] are divided into (a) \'wonders done without any kind of restriction\' \[absolute\], which refers to something which would be worthy of astonishment of itself and in its own right, even if we knew why it was wonderful and how it was done, and (b) \'wonders in accordance with x\', or \'wonders as far as we are concerned, which refer to things which astonish us because we do not know why they are extraordinary, but will cease to find them so once we find out what has caused them.
[Page 21]

It occurs to me that there are various signs by which a wonder can be distinguished from a miracle. (i) If, as I said, the effect surpasses the power and natural capacity of a created thing, it will be a miracle. (ii) If the proposed aim of the effect is not good, (for example, spiritual or physical welfare), but simply bad, (for example, curiosity or something else which is repugnant to good morals or the true Faith), it is not a miracle. Under this heading, it is proved that the prodigies and \'miracles\' of Antichrist will not be true miracles, which is what St Paul wrote to the Thessalonians \[2 Thessalonians 2.9-10\]. From this it is clear what one must think about the cure of swollen glands done by that heretic prince Elizabeth, Queen of the English, if, as that parasite Tooker claims, she does it in confirmation of her faith. (iii) The wonders of evil spirits are often deficient in the smallest details, whereas miracles are perfect because they are works of God. (See Exodus 8 on the contest between the magicians and Moses.) (iv) The wonders of evil spirits vanish in the face of genuine miracles, just as the magicians\' wands were devoured by Moses\'s rod, and the oracles of evil spirits fell dumb in the presence of saintly people. (v) Finally, miracles happen as the result of open, earnest invocation of God. Magicians\' wonders always take place with a mixture of discordant, secret, meaningless, ridiculous, or superstitious prayers or signs.

First conclusion: No magicians can produce miracles in the proper sense of the word. It is the common opinion of theologians, (one which is proven), first that infinite power is required for working a miracle, and therefore \[such working\] is reserved to God, while the entire power of a created being is limited by bounds set by Nature. If it were otherwise, we should not have an effective argument to prove the truth of the Faith which the Church has always buttressed with miracles, in imitation of our Saviour who used this argument to prove that the unbelief of the Jews could not be excused because of the obvious miracles which had been performed in front of them. \'Had I not done things which no one else has done, they would not have had sin\', \[John 15.24\]. If you object that miracles have sometimes been performed by wicked people and people with a false notion of the Faith, and therefore miracles should not be considered an effective way of proving the Faith, I shall counter \[by saying\] that God is accustomed sometimes to extend this grace gratuitously even to people of that sort, but \[does so\] in proof of the true Faith, as when He gave a prophecy to Balaam and Caiaphas. He has never given confirmation of a false faith and cannot do so. God is no more capable of using miracles to confirm a lie than truth itself is capable of lying. Therefore, the genuine miracles of pseudo-prophets are done in Christ\'s name and provide confirmation of the true Faith. From this it is clear one must be careful to make a distinction, as in the following inference. \'X has performed a miracle, therefore his faith is true\', and \'X performed a genuine miracle to confirm the faith he was professing, therefore this faith of his is true\'. The first can be false because God is capable of working a miracle through infidels. The second is always true because God cannot work miracles through anyone to confirm a false faith. What Nicodemus said about Christ\'s miracles confirms my conclusion: \'No one can perform these wonders unless God is with him\', \[John 3.2\]. This is also the opinion of Origen, Against Celsus Book 2, St Augustine, The Trinity Book 3, chapter 8, and St Isidore, \[Differences\], the chapter \'not wonderful\', 26, question 2. Francisco Toledo says more about this in his learned comments on John, but when he says there that evil spirits sometimes do things which not only look like, but actually are miracles, he needs to be read with caution because he has stretched the word \'miracle\' to \[include\] any kind of marvel and spurious miracle, and this runs counter to the common meaning of the word \[as used\] by theologians.
[Page 22]

Second conclusion: Magicians cannot perform wonders without any kind of restriction, either. This is obvious because nothing actually done without any kind of restriction and which is wonderful in itself must be the same as a miracle, and this is what the prophet \[David\] means when he says, \'Blessed is the Lord God of Israel who alone does wonderful things\', and \'who alone performs great wonders.⁶⁷ Ignorant people usually think that certain things which have not risen from natural causes are wonderful in this unrestricted sense, an example being what the Kouropalates \[major-domo\] tells us about the dead Michael Cerulius\'s right hand which had taken the shape of a cross and stayed firmly in place like that. But in Volume XI of his Annals, for the year 1058, \[Cesare\] Baronio reveals a deception. While the parts of a dead person\'s body are still warm, someone who wants them to assume a \[particular\] shape and position \[finds\] that when they turn stiff and cold, they stay in that position and cannot be moved, and \[Baronio\] adds shrewdly that such things done by human craft are the \'miracles\' of schismatics, (I add \'of heretics as well\').

Third conclusion: With the assistance of an evil spirit, and if God allows it, magicians can produce many marvels \'in accordance with x\' or \'as far as we are concerned. One must look for the proof of this conclusion in the fact that there are very many wonderful and hidden effects in Nature, and the Devil knows all of them very well. He also knows all those which can be produced very quickly, although not instantaneously. Consequently, (as St Augustine said somewhere), it is very difficult for a human being to understand how far the Devil\'s power extends, and a saying of the prophet \[Job\] speaks about this, \'There is no power on earth which can compare with him who has been made so that he is not afraid of anyone\', \[Job 41.24\]. I do not want to accumulate a large number \[of examples\], so let one, told by Antonio de Torquemada, (which is full of surprise), do duty for all of them. In certain places in the Kingdom of Naples, when women give birth, just before the child is born, a single little frog or venomous toad, (or more than one), comes out first. If one of them touches the ground and immediately begins to jump about all over the place, the woman in labour dies at once. Consequently, the custom is to put straw mats all over the ground and cover the walls completely with tapestries so that while these ill-omened forerunners are falling or jumping out of the womb, they cannot reach the ground. This is why midwives usually have basins in the house, or another kind of vessel filled with water into which they can throw these little creatures. Then they cover it up thoroughly, take it down to a river, and drown \[the creatures\] in it. This is what Torquemada says, and a reliable tradition reports it as established fact. 69 If this does happen, I think it happens because of the cunning device of an evil spirit who counterfeits these frogs in answer to the requests of some worker of harmful magic \[malefici\] and kills the woman in labour, although God allows this to happen for good reasons. It is clear from this that without His permission, the evil spirit can do nothing, because not only could he not have harmed Job-something he very much wanted to do-on the strength of his own authority, but he could not have deceived the treacherous king, his slave, \[3 Kings 22.22 or indeed have gone into the pigs \[Matthew 8.31-32\]. Hence it happened that even though the Emperor Nero lacked neither intelligence, nor wealth, nor will, nor desire, nor tutors, the Devil would never be able to undertake a single trial of magic through him, and in consequence Nero would despise magic, \[Pliny, Inquiry into Nature 30.1415\]. Evidently the provident goodness of God did not allow this instrument, which was very ready to do harm, to come near the highest \[kind of\] power and the most corrupt \[kind of\] malice. (The extraordinary works of Antichrist will belong to this type of marvel.)
[Page 23]

Book 2, Question 8

How do magicians work these wonders with the help of the Devil?

You need to understand that all evil spirits operate either by unmediated action through local motion, or by mediated action, and this is done in two ways, either by deceiving the senses, or by applying active things to passive and so causing a genuine alteration. This is a teaching common to all theologians. Therefore, evil spirits operate through local motion and the alteration of things, or through deception.

With respect to local motion: inferior bodies obey the angels to such an extent that they can rotate the heavens. (I shall have to discuss elsewhere \[the question of\] whether such movers of the spheres exist in fact). Given that they can move them, however, it follows that there is no object so huge that evil spirits cannot move it from its place with a hefty shove-provided, of course, they do not thereby disturb the order of the universe, which means they will not have the power to move an entire element from its place-and it is with this agreed limitation in mind that one should understand that what Firmilianus wrote to St Cyprian about the earth, would not be true of local or partial motion. Nor will \[evil spirits\] be able to change the way the heavens move in their ordained orbit. But an evil spirit can move bodies very quickly by means of local motion and it is because of this motion that we see that the heavens are being set in motion very rapidly. So this is how \[a spirit\] can move something from one\'s sight and substitute something else so instantaneously that he deceives the minds and eyes of onlookers, and persuades them that the first thing has changed completely into the second.

Such, as St Augustine observed, were the kind of transformations pagans thought they should believe in-that of Diomedes\'s companions into birds, \[and\] that of Iphigenia into a deer. Astyrius disclosed a fraud, the perpetration of which Eusebius rescued from oblivion. \'At Caesarea Philippi, which the Phoenicians calls \"Paneas\", a sacrificial victim is brought to springs which burst out there from the lower slopes of Mount Paneion. (These springs are said to be the source of the \[River\] Jordan.) On one of the pagans\' feast-days \[the victim\] was slaughtered, thrown \[into the water\] and, through the force and power of an evil spirit, vanished from people\'s sight in an extraordinary way. Those who were present thought this a most remarkable miracle. Astyrius happened to be there on one occasion while this was taking place, and when he saw so many people astounded by it, he was very distressed by their mistaken belief. So, he looked up at the sky and humbly entreated God, the ruler of everything, through Christ to restrain the evil spirit who had shat⁷¹ on the people by leading them so badly astray, and to control him because of the fraud and deceits he is in the habit of practising against human beings. When he had prayed to God this way, the sacrificial victim suddenly floated on top of the springs, and so the \[spectacle\] it had been their custom to find so astonishing was completely ruined, so much so that in future no miracle would ever take place there again\'.

The second method applies active things to passive. By altering or changing things this way, an evil spirit often creates wonders whose causes are natural but unknown to us, although very well known to him because he sees the essences of every thing and is acquainted with the particular properties of each one of them. He knows the right times to apply \[the active things\], and there is scarcely any clever device or contrivance he does not know. After all, what has he not achieved by assiduous daily experience and observation? In particular, he knows everything good angels have managed to do since the beginning of the world, what human beings have thought, and very many things good angels have learned from God and which they reveal to Satan at God\'s command. Therefore, very often many things are done instantaneously, which Nature could never have done by herself alone unless evil spirits had assisted her by means of this ingenious application of theirs. Under those circumstances, they are using natural agents as their instruments. The nature of an instrument, however, is to produce an outcome, not simply one which is equal to the power of the instrument, but one which is more excellent by far, and corresponds to the active property of the person in charge of the instrument and sets it to work. An axe has, of itself, merely the ability to cut, but in as far as it is the instrument of a skilled carpenter, it has the ability to make a table or a statue. The innate heat of human semen, in as far as it is heat, has the ability to warm, to rarefy, to separate, and to condense; but in as far as it is an instrument of the soul, it has the ability to produce bones, nerves, and flesh. I shall use similar reasoning to look at the natural causes used by evil spirits to produce much better outcomes from the power belonging to those natural causes, because those causes could never have produced those outcomes the way they usually work had not an evil spirit applied them \[to this end\]; and under these circumstances, as instruments set in motion by evil spirits, they produce marvels in respect of \[a change in\] their substance, the way \[the change is made\], or the speed \[with which it is done\]. These operations, however, never escape the limitations \[put on them\] by Nature, because they do not exceed the natural power of their principal cause; and since these effects actually exist in the natural agents which have been applied in this fashion, the devil confers nothing on those agents when he applies them, apart from the \[particular\] circumstance without which the outcome would not be effected. This is because although the evil spirit uses the natural power of the things he has applied, he operates principally by means of artifice, just as when a cook prepares nice food and cooks it, using fire as the medium of his craft, even though fire works naturally, it would never have achieved the \[desired\] outcome had it not been applied for the purpose of the craft \[of cooking\]. But what does the cook confer upon the fire other than method and circumstance, without which \[the outcome\] would not take place? He exercises over the fire no power which deserves to be called a physical cause.
[Page 24]

One should note two things at this point: (i) that one must not consider only the activity of the natural object, according to what it wants to do, or only the evil spirit\'s application \[of that activity\] according to what it can do, but natural power as applied by the evil spirit; (ii) that the thing which has been applied is the instrumental cause in these operations, while the principal cause is the evil spirit himself who is applying it. Consequently, so that it can be said that an outcome cannot be produced naturally, it must be such that it surpasses the capability of both these causes together.

So much for \[what I have to say\] about effecting change. Now \[let me turn\] to the delusion \[of the senses\]. When neither local motion nor application of the type I have been discussing is sufficiently effective, magicians usually take refuge in travelling showmen\'s tricks and so play upon people\'s senses with their lying pretence that even the smallest thing magicians do may be considered done by deception. (I am expanding the word \'deception\' to include conjuring-trickery, deception, and error, even though these are normally kept distinct). The Devil has three ways in particular of deceiving people-by changing an object, by changing the medium \[via which the change is done\], or by changing the sense-organ itself, and because the Jesuit \[Luis\] Molina has explained this better than anyone else, let me follow what he says.

He tells us that as far as the object is concerned this \[change\] is possible (i) by rapid movement of the objects which have been shown \[to onlookers\], suddenly hiding them, putting them in a different place, or secretly separating them or joining them together, which is what mountebank conjurors do with various strips of cloth, little balls, small cups, and so forth; (ii) by a skilful use of optics, placing the object in a particular position in the path of the onlooker\'s eye-for example, when lines are drawn in an irregular order, it turns out that when someone sees them from a position directly opposite by looking through a small aperture or by means of a whirling board, they present him with the sight of a cleverly painted picture. Very important scholars think that if one shines lamp-light, burning with \[a mixture of\] wax and snake-skin which has been impregnated with sulphur, on rushes and straws strewn on the floor, it makes them appear to be dancing snakes. (I prefer to classify this example as a mixture of natural and instrumental magic). (iii) When they suddenly fashion an object out of the elements, especially air, and present it to the spectators-for example, when they themselves pick up \[such an object\], or put an object made from air round something else, or make an object which looks like something one has imagined; (iv) when they put \[next to something\] a compound object, such as a mineral, which, because of its natural power is responsible for making the object placed next to it appear to be something quite different. In this case one is usually not sure whether the natural power belonging to the object which has been placed next to \[the first one\] is what is doing this, whether an evil spirit is imprinting that condition on the object, or whether he is simply producing that condition in the medium itself, and because this condition is acting as a medium, the appearances delivered via the medium are modified in such a way as to explain why the object appears to be something else. This is why a piece of light blue cloth seen through the green lens of a dioptra appears to be green.

In the case of the medium\'s being air, they do the following: (i) they stop the outward shapes of things from being brought to the eye or one of the other senses-for example, when they hide from sight the entire object which is in front of people, or simply a part of it; (ii) they imbue the medium with a condition in which, while they are going through that medium the appearances of things are modified in such a way that the object looks different from what it actually is-for example, if salt is mixed with vinegar and a scrap of linen cloth soaks up this vinegar and, not long afterwards, the rag is lit by a candle\'s being placed underneath it, the visual shapes which will be diffused via the medium \[of the rag\] which has been lit up by this flame will make the faces of those who are present look terrible. Likewise, if one were to make a candle out of ass\'s semen and wax, and this candle was the only light during a dinner-party, all the diners, (if one can possibly believe the Bishop of Paris), would look as though they had donkeys\' heads.⁷⁵ (iii) If the medium round the object is made thicker, the object\'s appearance is altered, as when a coin thrown into a bowl filled with water looks bigger when it lies at the bottom and is viewed from the surface. The same thing can be said if the vapours of the air in between are condensed, which is why the moon seems to grow smaller and undergo change in various ways, so that we think a cockerel is dragging a wooden beam along when in fact it is dragging a straw. (iv) If there is movement in the air which is the medium, visual images are set in motion at the same time, and this is why people travelling by ship think that trees are moving in tandem with the motion of the vessel. (The Devil made this happen so that the ship which was bringing Mother Idaea would look as though it was following Claudia\'s belt).⁷⁶
[Page 25]

Sometimes it turns out that the organ \[of sight\] is deceived by a different arrangement or a multiplication of sensory images. This sometimes happens because of the shape of the instrument through which we see them-for example, when a single vantage point shows us more than one form of a single object. Sometimes, because of the way the instruments are arranged and placed, (for example, if we put a large number of mirrors in different places), it will be possible for so many images to multiply themselves \[by appearing to be\] in several places at once that those which are in one place do seem to be in another, and we automatically think that we are seeing the object which is actually in front of us. Sometimes it can happen through the mere transition of the sensory images which exist in the imagination to the external sense, or at least to the common sense, and these senses are affected by the images the same way they would be if the images were sent there from external objects. Certain people refer to an apparition, described by St Augustine, which appeared to a philosopher who was trying to resolve some problems.⁷⁷

Deception with respect to the organ \[of sight\] is no less varied. (i) It happens when the placement of the organ is changed, and this causes an object which is in one place to appear to be in another, or to appear as two when there is only one. For example, if you stick your finger between the eye \[and\] the space in which the eye is enclosed and push upwards or downwards on the eye with your knuckle, you will cause a single object to appear to be two. (ii) If the humours are agitated or disturbed, one sees things other than they are, which is what happens in the case of drunks or people who are completely out of control. They believe \'they see a double sun and two of Thebes.⁷⁸ In this category one must include the illusions of a Jew, Zedekiah, who lived at the time of Louis the Devout \[778-840\]. He would throw a man into the air, tear him to pieces, collect the pieces together, and reunite them again, and he would also swallow a cart laden with hay, together with its horses and driver, in the presence of the entire population. (Several of these \[tricks\] are a mixture of the first and second kind \[of delusion I have just mentioned\]). A third type usually involves interfering with sensory perception-obstructing the pathway of the \[vital\] spirits \[spiritus\] with some rather thick humour so that they cannot reach the senseorgan or blunting the ability to perceive sensorily in some other way. (This is, perhaps, how the angels struck the people of Sodom with blindness), \[Genesis 19.11\]. However, when an evil spirit convinces human beings they have been changed into animals, he does not merely change the organ but sometimes the medium as well, and he always changes the imagination at the same time, as in the case of Praestantius\'s father, described by St Augustine.79 The Devil, you see, can put together and arrange semblances of things in such a way that even while someone is awake, (which is what St Augustine says), he will dream as people who are asleep do, and think he is feeling things he does not feel, the way mad people do. This is how a Turkish incantation to get back runaways works. (It is described by Bartholomej Georgijević in his book on Turkish customs, folio 93.

Furthermore, one can detect the effects produced by conjuring-tricks by means of two rules. The first is that if the effect does not arise from local motion and goes beyond what natural causes can do when they are brought into play-resuscitating a dead person, for example, or curing genuine blindness--- deception and a conjuring-trick has taken place. The second \[rule\] is that when something one has seen vanishes in an instant and is no longer visible, this is a conjuring-trick. An example of the first is when magicians put a bow made from a particular wood in a room and shoot an arrow made from a different wood from a string of a particular thickness. When they do this, they make it seem as though a river as wide as the flight of the arrow has sprung up in that place. \[An example\] of the second is when magicians seem to carve up and eat a horse which, not long afterwards, is produced sound and whole. Likewise in the incident reported by Nicetas about a counterfeit snake.
[Page 26]

\'When, from a rather high place in the palace at Constantinople, Michael Sicydites saw a skiff which was carrying pots and dishes, he \[uttered\] a magical incantation and made a sailor get up and carry on breaking the pottery until he had reduced it to dust. But not long afterwards he seized hold of his beard and began to weep and, once his mental blindness had cleared, he started to wail that he had been driven \[to do it\] as though by the wrath of a divinity. Asked why he had treated his merchandise this way, he told a painful story. He was concentrating on his rowing, \[he said\], when he saw a dreadful snake stretched out on top of the pots. With unblinking eyes, it turned towards him as though it was going to devour him and did not stop coiling itself up until the earthenware had been shattered. Then suddenly it vanished from his eyes\'. William of Newburgh testifies to the conjuring-tricks of Eon, a heretic and a magician. \'He used to bewitch \[fascinasse\] people\'s eyes so that they thought there was a great crowd of men in front of them, and an abundance of delicious food fit for a king, and the most magnificent furniture\'. At length, however, he was sentenced at the Council of Rheims, and while he was being led to his death he quite frequently said to the earth, \'Earth, open up!\' a fruitless attempt we read about in Robert\'s Chronicle.

This is how the evil spirit amuses himself with conjuring-tricks and, not content with imitating miracles by means of conjuring-tricks, tries to represent those very conjuring-tricks to heretics \[as miracles\] by means of crafty deceits as well. I have read an example \[of this\] which was so delightful and amusing that, because it diverted me, I cannot deprive the readers of this book of it and so, for the sake of giving them respectable pleasure, I am now happy to undertake the trouble of transcribing it for them.

\'At the time of Sigismund I, King of Poland, Jakob Melstinki, governor of the town of Brzeziny \[Brezin\], in a particularly frivolous frame of mind, appropriated to himself the authority and name of Christ and, as the Twelve, chose the two Zatorskis from Krakow, while the remaining \[ten\] were thieves like himself. He gave each of these the name of one of the Apostles and called himself Jesus Christ. While they were walking through villages all over the place, they used to work wonders after the fashion of jesters and conjurors. They would secretly suborn other cheats and thieves to play dead, and then resurrect them; they would first put fish in muddy bogs where fish could never be born, and then in the name of Christ catch them with their hands; they would put loaves in ovens when there weren\'t any there beforehand, and then draw them out in the name of their \'Christ\', to the astonishment of the simple-minded common people.

On one occasion, \[Melstinki\] came with his apostles to the monastery of Czestochowa, famous for its icon of the Blessed Virgin. They stayed there for several days and secretly persuaded one of their number to act as though he were under the influence of an evil spirit, and by these means used to obtain food. He, you see, would walk through the guest-houses and snaffle pieces of meat from the kitchen, and throw them to his companions who would make the sign of the cross over them and eat them. When a large crowd of people had gathered in front of icon of the Blessed Virgin, \[Melstinki\'s\] tricksters brought their possessed companion to the altar, wearing two shirts so that he could put the things he needed into the space between. They actually put pebbles inside his undershirt and while they were bringing him to the altar, he broke free in a raging fury from the hands of those who were leading him, rushed up to the altar which was laden with offerings, snatched up some coins, and hid them in the space between his two shirts. At this interruption to the divine office, one monk fled from the altar. The other monks hurried to loosen \[the possessed man\'s\] belt. At that point, only the pebbles fell to the floor, while the money remained in his double shirt. The monks thought the money had been changed into pebbles by an artifice of the evil spirit and they tried, by means of exorcisms, to turn \[the money\] back into its former shape. 82 When the pebbles remained as they were, however, a monk full of indignation threw his holy book on the floor, saying, \"We have never had an evil spirit like this one. Away with him to all the devils!\"
[Page 27]

These tricksters fled to Silesia with the genuine money, and there they turned aside in a certain village to the house of a noble married woman. But because her husband was not there, she was unwilling to give them admittance and they merely asked for a napkin or a piece of cloth so that they could celebrate Mass. The woman offered them a little bundle of cloth and then they said, \'We shall take this with us, and Christ will bless you so that your flax grows more abundantly. Tell us if you have another one\'. Since they wanted to take this as well, the woman refused because she was afraid of her husband, but they secretly rolled up a scrap of kindling along with fire into the cloth and gave it back to the woman. Consequently, a chest was set on fire because of the cloth and the house was set alight because of the chest, and when the husband came back his wife said this had happened because she had treated Christ and His Apostles badly.

Her husband, however, incandescent with rage, said, \'This man was an absolutely good-for-nothing thief, not Christ!\' So, he and his neighbours went in pursuit of them and caught up with them in one of the villages. Then the pseudo-Christ said to the man who was called Peter, \'My suffering and the cup I am about to drink are approaching\'. Peter said, \'I see they are near at hand for me as well, Lord. But \[Melstinki\] said, \'Peter, I shall be obliged to escape through the window\', and Peter said, \'I shall not abandon you as long as I live. Wherever you run away to, I shall follow\'. So, he fled through the window and the rest of the \'apostles\' also escaped in whatever way they could. The country folk followed them, and felled them with their sticks and clubs, saying, Prophesy to us, Christ and your apostles, in which wood did these sticks grow?\' Having been punished by these blows, therefore, they later amended their life, saying, \'It is difficult for us to undergo Christ\'s Passion and the Apostles\' sufferings\'. This is what the chronicles of Poland say.

Reader, I think you realise that the same spirit of irreligiousness and blasphemy existed in these tricksters as once existed in Cyril, the Arian, and exists later in Calvin and his followers. All have undoubtedly been an instrument of the same Satan. The topic of the Arian Cyril is very well known, and that concerning the Albigensians is well-known from the writings of Lucas de Tuy who lived during that period. \[Juan de Mariana reports several similar things worth reading in Book 12, chapter 1 of his History of Spanish Affairs. Matthieu de Launoi, who kept a record of events, tells us about Calvinists in the Spanish Netherlands in his narrative of the reasons for his conversion from Calvinism to the Catholic religion. Read, if you will be so kind, Reader, his second Defence which he published with his colleague \[Henri\] Pennetier, also a convert. It was printed a few years ago in Paris, but as I do not have the book to hand at the moment, I shall conclude with Apuleius.

\'In front of the Painted Porch \[in Athens\] I saw with my own two eyes a juggler swallow a cavalry-sword which was sharp at one end and had a dangerous point, and not long afterwards I saw the same man plunge a hunting-spear, lethal end first, right down into his guts; and, do you know, from behind the blade of the spear, where the shaft of the inverted weapon went into the man\'s throat, there appeared a pretty, effeminate boy who climbed up and did a dance, writhing round \[the shaft\] in a series of complicated bends and stretches, as though he had not a tendon or a bone in his body, to the astonishment of every one of us who was there\'. There can be no doubt an evil spirit did this by sending images of a false object into \[people\'s\] eyes via the medium of the air which he saturated with them.
[Page 28]

Book 2, Question 9

How remarkable were the effects the ancients attributed to magicians?

Arnobius, Book 1, \[section 43\], dealing with remarkable things done by magicians, calls our attention to their acts of divination and harmful magic \[maleficia\] as the principal things they do. \'Who is not aware that these men either busy themselves trying to find out in advance what is going to happen, things which, whether they like it or not, take place because that is what they have been appointed to do: or to inflict a deadly, wasting disease on anyone they want: or to rupture affection in families: or, without keys, to open things which have been locked: or to render people unable to speak: or to disable, spur on, or hold back the horses during chariot races: or to let loose the flames and furtive desires of forbidden love on wives and licentious foreigners, male or female?\'

In Book 1, chapter 10 of Polycraticus, John of Salisbury says, \'With the Lord\'s permission, they throw the elements into confusion, deprive things of their outward appearance, very often make known things which are going to happen, disturb people\'s minds, send dreams, and kill people simply with the violence of a chanted spell \[carminis\]\', etc. It is not my intention to make a collection of every \[reference\] or to collect a reward for my trouble, but simply \[to bring together\] the references which occur to me now and seem to be particularly worthy of note. Let me start with the North from where, as the prophet testifies, every wicked thing is spread, \[Jeremiah 1.14\]. In his book dealing with the north, Saxo Grammaticus writes that giants and magicians performed an unheard-of kind of wonder by means of various conjuring-tricks and, with their supreme skill in deluding \[people\'s\] eyes, disguised their own faces and those of other people with a variety of images, and hid their true appearances with convincing masks. Olaus Magnus writes about Lapps, Finns, and Biarmi, \[saying\] that they take whatever shape they want and likewise that they spy on what their friends or enemies are doing in another part of the world, and also sell travellers by sea any wind they want for the price of an article of clothing made from linen.

Alessandro Guagnini tells us that beyond the kingdom of Kasan there were three small Tar tar provinces which were unusually addicted to incantations, and that by these means they frequently stirred up fogs and other bad weather in the sky, and in this way overthrew their enemies.⁸⁷

If we proceed from people in general to individuals, a good many \[examples\] present themselves. Zoroaster used to draw things like sparks from the stars. Dositheus performed wonders long ago, but his pupil, Simon Magus, surpassed him-I dealt with his marvels earlier-and Glykas writes briefly in Part 2 \[of his chronicle\] as follows. \'He made statues walk, and when he threw himself into a fire, he was not burned. He would fly into the air, make bread from stones, and flit from one shape to another. Sometimes he would have two faces. He used to change himself into a pillar. He would make closed doors open of their own accord and domestic crockery seem to move of its own accord to perform its duties for humans. \[He made\] ghosts walk in front of him and said they were the souls of dead people. Once, when the Emperor was asking after him, he was struck down by terror, abandoned his own shape, and fled to another one\'. In a later passage, Glykas writes, \'He had a dog chained at the threshold of his house, and this would devour anyone who tried to visit Simon without being invited. The dog told \[St\] Peter to come in and, in a human voice, announced that Peter had arrived\'. You notice the Devil\'s power yielded to the power of God which was in Peter, and that Cerberus fawned upon \[St Peter\] whom he was unable to harm.
[Page 29]

Several people have ascribed what the poets have made up about Orpheus and Amphion and the power of their music to the magic of both men. Pythagoras showed off his golden thigh, tamed an eagle with an incantation, and often used to have a conversation with it. Numa Pompilius put on show the trappings of a sumptuous banquet, even though he had no food at all in the house. Any time Bajan, King of Bulgaria, wanted to change himself into a wolf or some other animal, he was seen to change shape. Did he sometimes do this so that he would not be seen by anyone?⁸⁸ Ollerus used to sail over vast stretches of the sea on an enchanted bone as though it were a ship. Eric, King of the Goths, would draw a favourable wind from wherever he turned his hat, and Philostratus tells us wonderful things about Apollonius \[of Tyana\], and Suidas about Julian the Chaldaean. Both Apuuleius and Anastasius tell us that during the reign of Domitian this Julian surpassed Apollonius in this craft and when it came to checking a plague which had suddenly fallen on Rome. (There is a mention of Julian in Arnobius, Book 1, \[52\].) When two learned men have a rambling conversation while they are putting Vulcan back, Anastasius adds the same thing, and from him Cedrenus \[repeats the information\] that Manetho was so skilled in working magic that he was always openly contemptuous of Apollonius to his face.

Apuleius, himself a magician according to the evidence given by many people, writes about two women who were most deserving of astonishment. First of all he tells us about the landlady of an inn-a \'queen of wise women\', to let you know that even in those days some of them were honoured with that title. 89 \'A wise-woman\', he says, \'has the power of a divinity to bring down the sky and lift up the earth, turn springs into stone and cause mountains to melt away, to raise the spirits of the dead to the surface of the earth and bring the gods down under it, to quench the stars and light up the underworld itself. Because her lover had dishonoured her with another woman, with a single word she turned him into a wild beaver, because when that animal is afraid of being caught, it escapes from those who are chasing it by biting off its genitals, \[her idea being\] that, because he had had sex with another woman, something similar would happen to him.

Since a neighbouring landlord was in competition with her, she changed him into a frog, and now the old man swims around in a barrel of his own wine and speaks to his former customers in polite croaks while he grovels abjectly in the lees. She changed someone else, an advocate who had spoken against her in court, into ram and now that is how he pleads his cases. This same woman condemned her lover\'s wife to perpetual pregnancy, (because she had said something satirical at her expense), by closing up the woman\'s womb and retarding the foetus\'s growth, and according to everyone\'s calculations the poor woman has now been distended for eight years, as though she were going to give birth to an elephant.90 Later on, he says of Pamphile, \'She is believed to be a magician \[maga\] with a very great reputation, and to be mistress of every kind of graveyard incantation. She knows how to plunge the entire light of the starry universe into the depths of the underworld and turn it into its ancient chaos, \[simply\] by breathing on twigs or pebbles or worthless things of that kind. As soon as she catches sight of a good looking young man, she is captivated by his beauty and immediately has no eyes or thoughts for anyone else. She instantly turns those who are of low rank and not complaisant enough into rocks and cattle and any other kind of creature. Others, however, she destroys completely.⁹¹
[Page 30]

Pliny does not trouble himself at all to copy similar stories about Lucian\'s ass, but he does quote Asclepiades scornfully. \'Torrents and standing pools are dried up by throwing the herb aithiops into them, and at its touch anything which is closed is opened. If the herb achaemenis is thrown at an enemy\'s battle-line, the men become alarmed and confused and turn tail. The King of Persia used to give his envoys the herb latace so that they would have plenty of everything wherever they went\'. He says a great deal about the effects of parts of a hyaena, and something much more unbelievable about various stones. \'In Persia, storms are averted by the fumes from \[burning\] agates, and \[the flow of rivers is stopped if the stone resembles a lion\'s skin. \[An agate\] like a hyaena\'s skin averts discord in people\'s houses. The agate which has only one colour makes athletes invincible. Milo \[of Croton\] derived the same power from a cockerel-stone\', etc.⁹²

Now let me turn to poets. According to \[Diogenes\] Laertius, Empedocles boasted considerably in the following vein:

\'You will obtain the drugs with which to drive away illnesses and lighten old age,

And I am communicating them to you alone. By means of your rituals you will restrain the violence of winds Which suddenly spring up and devastate the fields with their raging blasts.

If you wish, you will soon rouse sluggish streams \[to run\] high And bring back fine weather from the midst of a storm. You will bring health-giving showers in the midst of summer, And the winds which blow over everything when the harvest is dry, And you will recall a dead person from the black underworld.

He also adds, from the histories of Timaeus, that whenever the etesian winds were blowing so strongly that they flattened the crops, Empedocles ordered that donkeys be flayed and after \[their skins\] had been turned into bags, he said they were to be moved to hills and the tops of mountains to collect the breezes.

Once the winds had stopped because of this, he was known as the xwhuoavéMas \[wind-stopper\]. This single author from the Greeks is enough. There is a greater abundance of Latin witnesses who have described these things in greater and more elegant detail.

Vergil: \'Magical chants can draw down the moon from the sky. With magical chants Circe changed Ulysses\'s comrades. By magical chanting the cold snake is burst open in the meadows\', etc. \'Moeris himself gave me these herbs and these poisons which he gathered in Pontus. They grow very plentifully in Pontus. I have seen Moeris often become a wolf and hide himself in these woods, And often summon souls from the depths of their graves, And draw away to someone else\'s \[land\] corn already sown,\' \[Eclogue 8.69-71, 95-99\].

In his Metamorphoses, after Ovid has told the story of Ulysses\'s companions being turned into pigs, he expatiates on Circe\'s love and hatred for King Picus, among other things, and about Camens, or Canens, Picus\'s wife.

\'She was remarkable for her beauty, but even more remarkable for the way she sang, Which is why she was called \"Canens\". She would make woods and rocks move, Soothe wild creatures, and interrupt the flow of long rivers With her voice and hold back roaming birds \[from flying away\]\'.

A little later, he tells us about Circe herself, who had caught sight of Picus and had fallen violently in love with him. She says,

\'But you will not escape, \[even if\] the wind is allowed to carry you away, If I know myself, if the power of my herbs has not vanished entirely, And my magical chants do not fail me\'. She finished speaking and fashioned the likeness of a non-existent boar without a body, And told it to run across in front of the King\'s eyes And seem to go into a grove where the trees were thick.
[Page 31]

While Picus came along with his horse in pursuit of the non-existent boar, she overtook him and, with words and a wicked spell, lured him into an act of passion.

\'She took to prayer and spoke words of supplication, And entreated unknown gods with an unknown incantation, One with which she was also accustomed to disfigure the face of the snowwhite moon And affix to its ancient head clouds which suck up moisture. Then, too, by chanting an incantation she made the sky grow dense, And the earth breathed forth mists\', etc.

But when the man chastely rejected the adulterous woman, she became mad with rage.

\'Then she turned twice to the west and twice to the east. She touched the young man three times with her wand, and three times spoke her incantations\'.

He was changed into a bird and saw feathers on his body. Finally, when his servants used threats to demand their king \[be released\] from the power of her harmful magic, She inflicted harm upon them by sprinkling them with poisonous slime and juices, And summoned Night and the gods of night from Erebus and Chaos, And prayed to Hecate with drawn-out wailing cries. The woods-marvellous to relate-leaped up from their place, The ground groaned, the neighbouring trees turned pale, She sprinkled the animals\' fodder and made it wet with drops of blood. It seemed as though the stones were producing hoarse bellowings, That dogs were barking, that the earth was squalid with black snakes, And the thin souls of the silent \[dead\] were flitting about. These evil portents filled the astonished crowd with panic. She touched the amazed faces of the panic-stricken with her magically poisonous wand, And at its touch the monstrous appearances of various wild beasts Fell upon the young men. No one retained his own shape\'.

Of Medea he says in his foolish way, \'She strives to draw down the unwilling moon from its course And to hide the horses of the sun in darkness. She curbs waters and makes winding streams stand still. She moves woods and living rocks from their place. She wanders among \[newly-dug\] graves, noticeable because of her loose hair, And collects particular bones from pyres which are still hot. She curses people who are not there, fashions images out of wax, And drives thin needles into their wretched liver\', \[Heroides 6.85-92\].

Tibullus: \'As a truthful wise-woman \[saga\] Promised me with the aid of her magic. I have seen her bring down stars from the sky, And turn the course of a fast-flowing river with a spell. She has split open the ground with an incantation, drawn the spirits of the dead from their graves, And called down bones form a pyre which was still hot. Now she rouses the companies of the underworld with her magical hissing, Now she sprinkles them with milk and sends them back again. When she pleases, she drives clouds from a gloomy sky. When she pleases, she calls up snows from the summer sky. She is said to be in possession of Medea\'s herbs And to be the only one who has tamed Hecate\'s dogs. She has composed for me incantations with which you could deceive \[people\]. Intone them three times and spit when you have finished chanting. \[Your husband\] will not be able to believe anyone who \[tells him\] about us, Not even himself if he were to see us \[together\] on a soft bed.

You must, however, have nothing to do with other men, because he will see everything else. It is only in my case he will see nothing at all. What shall I believe? This woman has certainly said She can destroy my loves with her incantations or herbs\', \[Elegies 1.2.4363\].

Elsewhere this same poet \[says\],

\'Surely some old woman has not cursed you in the silence of the night With her incantations and herbs that make people pale? A magical chant draws crops from your neighbours\' fields. A magical chant stops an angry snake in its tracks. A magical chant tries to draw down the moon from her chariot, And would do so if trumpets did not sound from far away\', \[Elegies 1.8.1722\].
[Page 32]

Propertius, Book 1, Elegy 12: \'Were we at risk of envy? Surely a god has not crushed me? Or is it a herb, picked on the heights of Causasus, which has separated \[us\]? I am not the man I was\', \[vv.9-n\].

Petronius Arbiter introduces a wise-woman \[saga\] who boasts in the following fashion:

\'Whatever you see in the world obeys me. The earth, which abounds with flowers, Becomes listless when I wish it, and withers when its juices have dried up. It pours forth its riches when I wish it, and a crag with its bristling rocks Hurl out their native waters. The sea submits motionless Waves to me, and the west winds place their silent blasts At my feet. Rivers obey me. So do Hyrcanean tigresses, and snakes I have commanded to stand still. Why am I telling you about quite trivial things? The image of the moon comes down, Drawn by my incantations, and Phoebus in alarm is compelled To turn round his frenzied horses, once I have rolled back the vault \[of the sky\]. So great is the power of words. The fiery passion of bulls dies down, xtinguished by a virgin\'s rites, and Circe, Phoebus\'s daughter, too, Has changed Ulysses\'s companions with her magical incantations,\' \[Satyricon 134\].

Nemesianus, a writer of eclogues, \[says\]:

\'She chanted the incantation which makes the moon swell up and a snake burst open, Crags to run, crops to leave their field, and a tree to be uprooted, \[Eclogue 4.70-71\].

The prince of the Latin lyric \[in his description\] of three very beautiful wisewomen \[sagae\] says this about Folia:

\'She snatches down the stars and the moon she has enchanted With her Thessalian voice\'.

In the same poem, Canidia chants:

\'Not because of the usual potions, Varus, (Believe me, you are going to be in floods of tears!) Will you come running back to me; and your right mind Will return, but not because it has been summoned by Marsian incantations. I shall prepare something stronger, since you disdain me. I shall pour you a stronger dose. And the sky will sink below the sea With the earth stretched out \[over both\] Before you fail to burn with love for me, Like pitch with its black fires\'.

And presently \[she says\]:

\'I can make waxen images move, As you know yourself, thanks to your curiosity; And with my incantations I can tear down the moon from the sky.

I can rouse the dead after they have been cremated, And make potions which excite desire.

More accurately than anyone else although, because of his overblown diction and lofty arrogance, somewhat obscurely, Marcus Lucan \[says\] of Erichtho:

\'By means of Thessalian women\'s incantation love, which has not been prevailed on by the Fates, Has flooded into hearts hardened \[against it\], And grace old men have burned with illicit flames. Nor is it only their noxious Potions, or the pledges of future love for their young, which they remove, Swollen with juice, from \[a foal\'s\] forehead, which have an effect.⁹⁷ Even when it has not been defiled by the bloody matter of the poison someone has drunk, Once the mind has been enchanted, it perishes. Those whom the harmony of married sex And the power of a seductive shape does not bind together, \[These women have influenced by means of the magic whirling of a twisted thread.⁹⁸ Nature\'s changes have ceased. Drawn out by a long night, Daylight sticks fast. The upper air has not obeyed the law. The headlong sky has become sluggish because it has listened to their incantation, And while he bears down on the heavens which are driven forward on their swift axles, Jupiter is amazed they are not loving. Now \[the women fill everything With rain, and draw clouds in front of hot Phoebus, And the sky thunders without Jupiter\'s knowledge. With these same incantations
[Page 33]

\[The women\], their hair flowing loose, have driven far away The damp mists and rainclouds. Since the winds are abating, The sea swells high. Forbidden once again to experience storms, It is silent while the south wind is throwing it into confusion, and the sails which carry the ship, Have swollen against the wind. A torrent has hung from a steep cliff, Stuck fast, and a deep-flowing stream has run In a quite different direction. Summer has not brought a rise in the \[waters of the Nile; The Meander sends its waters in a straight line, and the Arar Has dashed headlong upon the sluggish Rhône. Mountains have lowered their summit and loosened their ridges. Olympus Has looked up at the clouds; and when winter was making things stiff \[with ice\], Even though the sun had not been shining, the snows in Scythia have melted away.

The Thessalian women\'s incantation has driven back from the shore they were defending The sea-goddess Tethys who was urged forward by the moon. The earth, too, has violently shaken the axes of her steady weight And, because of the exertion as she bends towards the centre of her sphere, has tottered. The cargo of such a great mass, struck by the incantation, Has fallen back and given a sight of the sky gliding round it. Every living creature with the power to kill, and which was born to do harm, Also dreads the Thessalian women and provides their practices with \[the means of death. Greedy tigresses and the noble anger of lions Fawn upon these women. The snake unfolds his icy coils for them And stretches himself out upon the ploughed and frosty ground. Knotted vipers burst open their body and unite again, And a snake drops dead, breathed on by human poison,\' \[Pharsalia 6.452-491\].

A few verses later, \[he says\]: \'By those women the stars were first Brought down from the sky as it rushed headlong, and the cloudless moon, Besieged by the dreadful poisons of their incantations, Looked pale and burned with dark light from the earth, Just as if the earth were stopping her brother\'s reflection, And inserting its shadows in the way of the flames from heaven. Pressed down \[under the weight of the incantation, she suffers these great pains Until, having come nearer \[the earth\], she deposits foam on the herbs beneath her\', \[Pharsalia 6. 499-506\].

No less learnedly, Lucan\'s uncle, famous for comedy and tragedy in what remains of \[ancient\] drama in Latin, my favourite, Seneca.

\'Now, summoned by my rites, come, planet of the nights, Assume your most evil aspects, threatening in every one of your faces. For you, after the custom of my tribe, I have set free my hair from its bonds And have wandered your secret groves with naked feet. I have summoned water from clouds which have no rain, And driven the seas down to their depths; and Ocean Has sent his heavy waves further inland, since his tides have been overwhelmed. Because the law of the upper air has been thrown into confusion, The world has seen the sun and moon side by side and, 0 Bears, You have touched the sea which is forbidden to you. I have changed the sequence of the seasons. The summer earth has shuddered at my incantation And Ceres, under this compulsion, has seen harvest in winter. The Phasis has returned its violent streams to their source And the Ister, divided between so many mouths, has squeezed together Its savage waves and is sluggish on all its banks. The surge has resounded, the maddened sea thundered, Yet the wind is silent. The shelter of the ancient grove Has lost its shadows at the command of my incantation. Phoebus has abandoned the day and has come to rest in mid-sky; And the Hyades, moved by my incantations, are ready to fall\', \[Medea 750769\].
[Page 34]

According to Seneca, the nurse claims no less for herself:

I have commanded a grove to bloom in the midst of winter\'s cold, And a discharged thunderbolt to halt in its tracks. I have stirred up a strait, Even though there was no wind, and I have calmed the wild sea; And the dry earth has opened up with fresh streams of water. Rocks have begun to move. I have burst open the doors Of the underworld and \[scattered\] its ghosts, and at my prayer of command The spirits of the dead talk, and the dog of the underworld has spoken. The sea, the land, the sky, and the underworld are my servants. The middle of the night has seen the sun, and day \[has seen\] night, And at my incantations, nothing keeps its laws\', \[Hercules Oetaeus 452463\].

Finally, the labour and study of my youth, Claudian:

\'Because I have magical power, and there is in me An ardent desire to foresee the future. I know with which incantation A Thessalian woman tears away the radiance of the moon, what power The wise Egyptian\'s hieroglyphs have, and the technique whereby the Chaldean tribe Commands the gods it has summoned. The juices which flow From trees and the power of deadly \[herbs\] are not unknown to meWhatever flourishes on the Caucasus, rich with lethal plants, and blooms on the crags of Scythia\[Herbs\] which savage Medea and cunning Circe gathered. I have quite often propitiated the frightening spirits of the dead, and Hecate, With rituals done at night, and by means of my incantations Dragged corpses back to life after they have been buried. I have also destroyed many people with my chanting, Even though the Parcae were still spinning their threads. I have compelled oak-trees to wander about and a lightning-bolt to halt in its tracks.

I have bent rivers and turned them round to flow backwards And return to their sources. So that you may not think That perhaps I have told you lies, see how your own house has changed\'. She finished speaking. The snow-white pillars, to his amazement, began To change themselves into gold, and the roof-beams suddenly Started to gleam with precious metal, \[Against Rufinus 1.145-163\].

This vaingloriousness claims so many things for itself, and such great things, too. Some of them are untrue, some of them true; but which are false and which are true I now have to discuss because other people have failed to do so. By using a reliable method, I shall reduce the whole subject to its principal headings, always bearing in mind that magicians can do whatever an evil spirit can do because of the pact between them, and that magicians cannot do anything an evil spirit cannot do.

Book 2, Question 10

What power do magicians have over the natural order and the laws of the universe?

Magic, as you have read, boasts that Nature obeys her and that she is superior to all the laws of the universe.

\'The sea, the sky, the land, the underworld are my servants. At my incantations, nothing keeps its laws\', \[Seneca, Hercules Oetaeus, 461 and 463\].

This is absolutely untrue. It is something reserved to Almighty God alone. One cannot change the natural order, only Nature\'s creator \[can do that\]; and one cannot abrogate the laws of the universe, only the one who put them firmly in place \[can do that\]. It is a theological principle, well known and firmly established, that nothing which can go beyond Nature can be done by local motion, (which is the unmediated action of an evil spirit), or by the application of active and passive things through alteration, corruption, or generation, (which are his mediated actions), and likewise anything whereby that order or the law of the universe may be changed. This is what St Augustine and St Thomas \[Aquinas\] say, and is proved by Scholastics on the authority of St Paul: \'God has not made the world which is to come subject to angels\', \[Hebrews 2.5\], although in fact according to the not incorrect interpretation of St \[John\] Chrysostom, Theophylact, St Thomas \[Aquinas\], and \[Adam\] Sasbout, those words deal rather with the new world or the Church whose head is Christ, something St Paul \[speaks about\] more than once. They prove this by the somewhat better argument that the part cannot be more than the whole, and from this axiom one deduces many specific points.
[Page 35]

\(i\) Magicians cannot produce any substantial or accidental form directly with the assistance of an evil spirit, because the law of Nature is that a physical substance cannot be altered directly by something which is non-physical. The Devil is non-physical, but those forms have to be brought out from the potentiality belonging to a physical object. Hence it follows that the Devil cannot directly imprint any physical, permanent property-for example, heat, cold, whiteness, etc.

\(ii\) They cannot create anything properly speaking, as it were \'out of nothing\', because the action of a natural cause does not extend to something which does not exist. This is \[an ability\] which belongs to the infinite Power.

\(iii\) They cannot make anything from any natural object, either, because they cannot introduce a form directly. In order to introduce a form indirectly, they have to start with an appropriate ordering \[of their material\], but no object is capable of \[receiving\] every arrangement of material which every form requires. Hence it is that they cannot achieve any effect by means of an instrument or cause, the reason being that on the one hand they depend on the pact they have made, which prescribes specific ways of operating, and on the other, since they are not operating directly, the cause or instrument must be proportionate to the effect.

\(iv\) They cannot, as I said, produce substantial forms without arranging the material in advance and making alterations to it. They cannot do this instantaneously, (because created things cannot make things happen except in time), and they cannot, however much they want to, bring anything to completion in a small or uniform amount of time, because more time is required for one thing and less for another according to whether more arrangement \[of material\] is required for one thing than for another, or \[that arrangement\] is more complete in one than in the other.

\(v\) In accordance with a presupposition made by Aristotle and his followers, which is quite welcomed in the Peripatetic Lyceum and schools of theology, they cannot introduce a vacuum into Nature or, in fact, anything which has no limit to it. They cannot return from a state of deprivation to one of being in possession, nor can they change or overturn the order which exists between the forms of things in Nature and the ways in which their matter is arranged by introducing extremities and overlooking what lies between them, or by doing things which come later before those which come first. They cannot transfer one end of an object to the other without transmitting what lies in the middle, nor can they move an object a long way away from itself, because the mover and what is moved have to exist in a place simultaneously-otherwise it would follow that they could move an object through a vacuum or through any kind of intervening space-and an object cannot move or be transferred in an instant or at any speed one may want, because there are specific boundaries which place a limit on the power of objects to move.

Therefore, what Tertullian wrote, \'Evil spirits are everywhere in a single moment, and likewise, \'The whole world is a single place to them\', needs to be understood properly,¹⁰¹ because one is not right to say, for example, that \'the whole world is a single place to them\' means they are in the east in such a way that they are not, at that moment, in the west. They are either in a place because their substance is there, (which is more likely), or (as many people interpret it) by applying their power, \[they make\] the place they are occupying adequate and their own. In everyone\'s opinion, \[an evil spirit\] has a specific, limited range of movement proportionate to his power or his substance. To be everywhere and to fill everything belongs to God alone. \[An evil spirit\] has also acquired the ability to move so fast that human beings can scarcely detect his movement.
[Page 36]

\(vi\) Because it has been said it is not possible to remove the connection between and subordination of the \[various\] parts of the universe, it also follows it is not possible to remove their being preserved, which is the reason for and intention of that order and connectivity. Furthermore, there follow what I shall be saying in the next question about the movement of the heavens and the removal of the elements from one place to another. It also follows that it is not possible to impede them proximately and directly, (which is what God was doing when He preserved the martyrs unharmed in such varied and immense torments). It is not possible, I say, to impede the action of a natural agent this way, because \[the agent\] has everything it needs to act. In this connection, however, it is possible \[to act\] indirectly by interposing some device or medicine, as human beings do, namely, in the first place, when one provides a contrary cause which stops the power of the other cause from making an appearance-for example, if one were to throw into a flame a napkin made from the soft threads of the stones which people bring from Carystus, and which are woven into something like fabric, it would not only not be burned, but would come out more brilliant and more beautiful. Likewise, if you bring an adamas towards a lodestone, \[the lodestone\] will be prevented from attracting iron.

If by chance the Devil deceived pagans when he prevented a flame from burning the hair of Iulus, or Servius Tullius, or Lucius Marcius, or the tunic belonging to Anagnini\'s servant, it is still possible for other things to be offered by way of explanation for such a well-known prodigy. These fires, you see, were that kind of flame popularly known as \'will-o\'-the-wisp\'. It is explained by natural philosophers as follows. Vapours from cold and extremely condensed air are retained near us. Later, because certain vapours are violently compressed as they follow one another in turn, they heat up and burst into flame. They also take on different colours and shapes according to whether the exhalation is thicker or thinner, and in accordance with the different ways in which it has fashioned itself. The flame looks red or black when the material is thicker, whiter and brighter when it is thinner, and it displays the rest of its different appearances because of the way its light is reflected. Apparitions of this kind-the French call them des aduis, the Germans Stallichten also appear in between horses\' ears, or above the top of human beings\' heads when the humans are growing violent because of wine or anger, or for some other reason. \[They also appear\] next to grave-stones, cremations, or gibbets because sticky vapours come out of dead bodies, and that movement, it is said, makes those vapours burst into flame. They are thought to go before or come after a traveller because a human being moves the air because he is moving at quite a pace. Therefore, if you try to catch hold of them, they seem to run away from the air which you have thrust aside.

I have read a recent writer, a lawyer, who thinks that all these things are evil spirits\' tomfoolery, and that there is a wicked genie innate in these fires. \'Why otherwise\', he says, \'do they lure human beings into the whirlpools of marshes and rivers, and precipitous places?\" They do not lure them, my fine fellow. It simply happens that the silence of night and the novelty of the flame induce fear and consternation in those who do not know what is causing it, make people stray from their path, and so rush into some kind of danger, especially rivers and marshes; and just as the flames Etna belches forth are carried into the strait, these fires, by their very nature, are also borne towards water, as \[Pierre\] Le Loyer corrected observed in his book on ghosts, Book 1, chapter 5. So those who have been thrown into confusion, or frightened, or led by curiosity, follow them and often run into danger of being drowned. If by any chance at some time or another one finds out there has been an external compulsion \[on them\] to follow \[the fires\], that is certainly not natural, but the work of an evil spirit, because I do not deny that sometimes it can happen that an evil spirit joins himself to these flames. But since I believe these flames have a natural explanation, I do not think one should take refuge, as the common folk do, in unnatural explanations.
[Page 37]

But why are horses\' ears or people\'s heads not scorched by them? I should think the reason is that, if we follow the opinion of ancient natural philosophers, there are two types of, or rather parts to, fire: the flame and the live coal. The live coal has the natural property of burning but does not emit light. Flames, however, emit light and burn when the flame is one which is complete.
