When it is not complete, as is the case we are dealing with here, it is able to emit light, but does not have the power to burn; and the name \[ignis fatuus: \'silly fire\'\] is a good enough indication that this is not a real, complete fire, but one which is like fire, just as the wild fig and the dried fig is not a real fig, but something degenerate which looks like a fig. \[The ancient writers\] confirm this, because they tell us that a flame comes out of \[a rock\] at the waters of Scantia. \'It is weak when it comes out and cannot last long in a different material. Above this burning spring is an ash-tree which conceals it and is noticeably always green\'. This would not be the case if the flame were real and complete. Such, too, is that brilliant white fire which appears to sailors after a storm and, while resting confidently on top of the mast and tackle of ships, does not set fire to anything. (The pagans called this the white star of the Dioscuri or the Castors. To us it is St Elmo.)

See now the remarkable weather-observation by the idle prattler Luther, (Commentary on Genesis, chapter 6, folio 146), where he argues about the rainbow. Not content with mixing up a rainbow with a halo or circlet, he adds, \'I have no doubt that leaping nanny-goats, flying snakes, and suchlike are brought about by evil spirits \[malorum spirituum\] larking about like this in the air, either to frighten or deceive human beings. The heathen declared that the flames appearing on ships were Castor and Pollux. Sometimes, too, a moon appears above the ears of horses. It is certain that all these are the frolickings of evil spirits in the air\'. Thus he, with his \'It is certain\' and \'I have no doubt\' overturns natural philosophy and provides no reason for doing so with the same authority he used to overturn the Faith, appropriating authority to himself in the case of Nature, just as he did in the case of God. He calls \'the moon\' what I said are little flames, although if he actually did see a moon resting upon horses\' ears, he did so when he was moon-struck \[lunaticus\]. Yet he thinks this \'is a very well-established, very reliable demonstration\'-the words are his-\'that all those \"impressions\", as they are called, are the works of God or even of evil spirits\'. The rainbow and, indeed, a comet are, according to him, \[works\] of God: everything else \[is the work\] of evil spirits. Let those who like being silly take pleasure in this kind of learned man!

Now, I have read about roaming balls of flames which emit a human sound, and about a corpse\'s right arm which was amputated, along with the shoulder blade and ribs, for use as a light during the night. The ends of its fingers burned with a sulphurous, violet-coloured flame so that witches \[striges\] could pour all their wicked venom upon someone who was sleeping and complete what they had decided to do. When at length the flame was put out, the fingers were as whole and uninjured as if they had not been a source of light earlier. Rémy claims that female magicians \[magas\] do this as often as they want, and has quite rightly attributed the entire thing to the cunning tricks of an evil spirit. On the other hand, when Claudius Claudian says that the snows on the summit of Etna remain unaffected because the immense heat \'under the snows protects them\', and \'its harmless flame licks the frosts next to it\', this is not a myth, because historians and natural philosophers vouch for it.
[Page 38]

But it is one of Nature\'s hidden mysteries. Perhaps a flame ejected too forcibly has less effect when it touches so fleetingly the things which lie around it? If this is true, it relates to a method of preventative action which happens later on, because the other mode of preventative action takes place via a swift, violent movement of the air in between, whereby a natural action is prevented from penetrating an object, just as we are accustomed to move the air to drive away smoke so that it doesn\'t harm our eyes, and just as the Devil did, too, so that the water Tuscia the Vestal was carrying in her sieve would not spill out. This is also how the apparition of a malevolent evil spirit stopped the flame which had been thrown into the shrine of Jupiter at Apamea from destroying it until, by a miracle from God, a sprinkling of holy water drove the evil spirit away. Likewise, poets write that Medea\'s incantations made the breath of flame-vomiting bulls harmless, because the Devil always tries to imitate God\'s miracles. One knows that \'by faith\' saints \'have quenched the assault of fire\', as St Paul says, (Hebrews 11.34), as did the three Jewish children, (Daniel chapter 3), St Francesco di Paola, and others.

I shall be content with a single example in which the merit of religious or blind obedience, along with faith, also shines forth. \[Bernardino\] Scardeone writes as follows. \'It happened once, perhaps by chance, that an Augustinian prior there was invited to lunch and sat down with these people\', (the Jesuit followers of the Blessed \[Giovanni\] Colombini). \'After the food and drink had been removed and the spectators had withdrawn, and permission to speak had been given, he started to discourse, interminably and arrogantly, about the merits of the way Dominicans made their vows, tacitly reproving \[the Jesuates\] because, although they were counted as monks, they would not publicly and openly acknowledge their profession of the three vows along with all other monks in accordance with the usual form. To this the superior of the Jesuits, an uneducated individual, to be sure, but a clever man and one who was dear to God, craftily replied, \"Father\", (he said), \"since we happen to be having conversation along these lines, do you want one of our brothers, who has offered himself to God in a straightforward way, or that companion of yours who has made his three vows in public, to show us who is the more genuinely obedient to his superior?\" \[The prior\], not realising \[what he was doing\], willingly agreed. Then the Jesuit turned straight away to our Brother Marcus who happened to be serving those seated at table and said, \"Brother Marcus, these friends of ours are cold.

By virtue of holy obedience, go quickly to the kitchen and bring them burning coals in your bare hands, so that they can warm up straight away\". \[Marcus\] did not hesitate, (because he was quick to do as he was told), and believing there would be nothing he could not do if commanded under the rule of obedience, without any hesitation quickly carried from the kitchen to the table as many burning coals as he could in his cupped hands without any damage or harm to himself, and offered them to each man so that he could relieve his cold. Then, on the order of his superior, to everyone\'s astonishment he took them back again whence he had brought them. The Dominican \[i.e. Augustinian\] prior I mentioned, immeasurably taken aback by this, looked at his companion as if he were about to tell him to do the same at once, but he, realising the situation, came to the conclusion he should not hesitate and immediately burst out as follows. \"Reverend Father, for goodness\' sake don\'t order me to tempt God. If you want, I shall bring fire in a sherd as I usually do, but not in my hands\". But they, filled with astonishment on account of such a great miracle, were silent and stunned on the one hand, and on the other, pleased by their comrade\'s unexpected reply, looked at each other and smiled.
[Page 39]

Any enemy of the Jesuits may perhaps smile while he is reading this, but Scardeone was not a Jesuit and, frankly, his knowledge of the Jesuits, who had not long arrived in Padua at that time, was very scanty. He calls us \'priests of the name of Jesus\', (Book 22, class 5, folio 95). But in the story I have just quoted, (Book 2, class 6, folio 113), he calls people \'Jesuits\' when their correct name is \'Jesuates\'. The founder of the Order was Blessed Giovanni Colombini and almost every one of them was a layman. The founder of our Order was St Ignatius Loyola, and in each of our houses there are many priests. They were confirmed in 1369 by Pope Urban v; we were first confirmed later, in 1540, by Paul III. They are called Jesuates of St Jerome; we are called clerics of the Society of Jesus. (For information about them, read Paolo Morigia\'s book on the origin of religious Orders, chapters 33 and 35. For information about us, read the life of Fr Ignatius Loyola, accurately recorded by Fr Pedro de Ribadeneira and Fr Pietro Maffei).

Scardeone, therefore, like everyone else who mixes up the name \'Jesuits\' and \'Jesuates\', was in error and because of this mistake Bartolommeo Fumo, without sufficient thought in his book The Golden Bracelet: What Religion Says, no. 4, says that Jesuits are not, properly speaking, members of a religious Order, and he praises the author Abbas\'s rules in the case of Regulars, number 3, although Abbas simply writes that they are not properly called \'Regulars\'. This, too, is false because Jesuits are Regulars on both counts since their clerics profess a specific Rule and are members of a religious Order. For instance, they take the three solemn vows which Abbas says are necessary and, notwithstanding there are those who do not take them, the Supreme Pontiffs Gregory XII and Gregory XIII defined that, by taking simple \[vows\], they are members of a religious Order. So Abbas was factually mistaken and Fumo quoted Abbas\'s opinion in ignorance. Both of them, however, were talking about the foresaid Jesuates since in Abbas\'s time there was no thought of Jesuits yet.

The books of Abbas, Fumo, and Scardeone should be corrected, and I am surprised this has not been noticed by those who have been in charge of recent editions. Fumo is clearly worthy of forgiveness if he wrote before the Society of Jesus was established. \[John\] Bale remains unworthy of forgiveness. In his list of the writers of Britain, the Englishman adds little stories, (as those people usually do), and tells us that \[Jesuits\] are Theatines and were first established in Venice by Paolo Carafa who later became \[Pope\] Paul IV. One is not particularly surprised at this. Our name was not known to the English Calvinist, but one can be astonished at his obvious malice. Then there is the Parisian advocate, \[Jean\] Papire Masson, who had lived not a few years in our Society. His stupidity resulted in his becoming a pettifogging ranter in the law-court, and he left our Society because of his scurrilousness in railing in our name, as though he were taking part in a tournament. In poems on the life of Pope Paul IV, he writes that in Spain we are \'Ignatians\' and that in Nola in Campania we are called \'Jesuins\', which is what he calls us.

Why? In order to give the impression of knowing \[what he was talking about\] and to cover up his apostasy. He had a very poor knowledge of those from whom he defected so unwisely and with such a bad example. If he knew them so little, why and how did this examiner of hearts and minds thrust himself into the secret places of the heart \[in such a way\] that he is not in the least afraid to claim that \[Jesuits\] alone think they have a contract to belong to Jesus\'s fellowship, and that in this they are inordinately shrewd because they think they are going to command Heaven or, on occasion, \[Jesus\] Himself? How could someone who had explored such deeply buried secrets of their heart have been ignorant of what they are called? If he had not known what they are called, he could not have thrust himself into these deep hiding-places of the heart. I think you are a spiritual apostate. It is the irrational habit of brutish people to try to rip to pieces, with a mouth full of nonsense and a poisonous tongue, those they have abandoned because they would not put up with being corrected, so that it does not look as though they deserted them for no good reason.
[Page 40]

I now return to my subject from the digression into which I was dragged by Scardeone.

Book 2, Question 11

What power do magicians have over heavenly bodies, the stars, and the elements?

As I pointed out in the preceding question, one has to put up with Empedocles\', Sosiphanes\', Apollonius\', Vergil\'s, and other poets\' attributing far too much to magicians. One has to put up with it because they were pagans. But who can put up with a Christian, and one who is a Catholic, who likes to listen to someone in The Transgressor Bound reciting the following verses

\'Words, for example, can stop the wandering stars high in the sky, Words can guide the powerful moon at night which is heathenish, stupid, and completely incredible. (i) It is not possible to arrest the movement of the stars by magic, or to turn them back in their ordained courses, because this would be to throw the order of the universe into confusion. This ruins the ancient story about drawing down the moon. It no more agrees with reason than the more recent silly notion of a certain nation in our time, which thinks that the moon was swallowed by an ass because a herd of animals in Arcadia drank from a river in which, as does happen, the face of the moon was shining while \[the herd\] was drinking, and when \[the animals\] moved away the moon could not be seen because it was suddenly hidden by a cloud. Consequently, the people threw the unfortunate rider of Silenus into prison, fell on a colt, cut open its belly, and disembowelled it in a most savage fashion in order to restore the moon to the world. St Augustine confirms my conclusion that the origin of the story stems from not knowing that the moon suffers a loss of light in a natural way. Plutarch also tells us about it, as does an ancient poet, a fragment of whose \[work\] Adrien\] Turnèbe has passed on to us. This \[fragment\] is well worth \[reading\] and is the kind of thing one reads in several places.

\'But let me say why the curved orb, shining along the mass of the earth, Grows livid when the world is tired, And why the brilliant radiance of its snow-white face grows weak. It is not a hateful wailing woman who drags it down below to dusky hollows From its watch-tower which wanders on high. It has not been overcome by an incantation And she \[the moon\] is looking for the conquering clamour. Indeed, through the upper air Where we locate her path, in confusion she separates \[herself\] from the pure \[air\], And passes on, inviolate. But the earth, Which occupies mid-heaven at its lowest point with her vast bulk, Holds back the light \[of the moon\'s\] brother By means of turning-points which cast a shade. Then, When the star is without purpose, The smooth shadow of her chariot grows pale during the time it is swiftly crossing The axis of the heaped up mound \[of earth\]. Once set free, she restores her brother\'s flames To the rose-coloured watch-tower throughout the sky.

Later on, he adds, most eloquently, a great deal whereby he confirms this opinion. (It will be possible to read there who the text of the ancient authors is talking about).

\(ii\) \[Magicians\] cannot transfer an entire element from its natural place to somewhere else. The reason is the same as that \[I gave\] in the preceding conclusion. It is an opinion common to theologians and is what Firmilianus meant when he wrote to St Cyprian, \[Letter 74.10\]. Earthquakes, however, can make this happen-certainly after any violent exhalation has been discharged into hollow places in the earth, or air which is shut up in the bowels of the earth has been violently disturbed. It is not only poets who claim that magicians command the winds. So does Philostratus when he writes that when he was among the Brahmins \[of India\], Apollonius saw two large jars, and once these were opened, storms and winds used to burst out, but when they were closed, fine weather and calm air would return, \[Life of Apollonius 3.14\]. The natural philosopher Sopater, too, was put to death because he was accused of fettering the winds by means of a magical incantation so that the harvest could not be brought to Byzantium. I have already talked about Empedocles and the King whose surname was \'Wind-cap\'.
[Page 41]

\(iii\) (Something which is associated with this.) Magicians can calm storms, rouse flashes of lightning, claps of thunder, hail-storms, and rain-storms. They can also stir up similar kinds of weather and send them against fields they want \[to attack\]. In olden times the philosopher Seneca laughed at this when he wrote as follows:

\'It is incredible that at Cleonae there were people publicly appointed to be халаÇopuráxas \[hail-guards\] to watch out for an approaching hail-storm. When these people had given the signal that such a hail-storm was near at hand, what do you think happened then? That people ran off to get their raincoats or get under cover? Not a bit of it! Each person would offer a sacrifice for his own benefit-one a lamb, another a chicken, and straight away, after they had tasted some of the blood, those clouds would start to veer off in another direction. Does this make you smile? Listen to something which will make you smile even more. If someone did not have a lamb or a chicken, he would lay hands on himself-but not to the extent of doing himself serious hurt, and I should not like to make you think that clouds are greedy or cruel. He would prick his finger with a really sharp pen and make his sacrifice with this blood. The hail would turn away from this man\'s little piece of ground just as much as it would \[from the land\] of someone who had persuaded it to do so by sacrificing bigger victims.

Certain people look for a rational explanation of this practice. One group, as befits people with a very good knowledge of the world, denies that anyone can make a covenant with a hail-storm and buy off storms with paltry \[presents\], even though gifts do prevail even upon the gods. Others say they suppose there is in the blood itself a power which is strong enough to turn and drive away a cloud. But how can there be a power in such a small amount of blood great enough to penetrate the sky and make the clouds feel it? Isn\'t it easier to say that this is a tall story and a lie? But Cleonae used to prosecute those who had been charged with looking out for a storm should it happen that, because of their negligence, vineyards had been beaten down or crops flattened. Here, too, in Rome, the laws of the Twelve Tables forbid anyone from enchanting people\'s crops. An antiquity which was still uneducated used to believe that rain could be attracted and driven away by means of incantations. That this cannot happen is so evident that one need not join the school of any natural philosopher in search of an explanation for it.

How forcibly is he asserting something he did not know? He is sounding off and inventing the false arguments he is refuting himself. He would not have mentioned them at all had he known that these things happened because of a pact with an evil spirit. Those more recent \[writers\], Catholic as well as heretic, who deny-and offer very potent arguments-that a human being cannot create even a single drop of water, (as if I were actually saying that only a human being can accomplish this by his own power), have heard this and have quite often singled it out. But I ascribe it to an evil spirit who is operating in accordance with the pacts he has made. They add that I am irreligious in ascribing this to an evil spirit because in Holy Scripture the origin of snow, hail, rain, and the like is ascribed to God. I was not unaware of this. On the other hand, in various places in Holy Scripture very many things are attributed to God which it is agreed are not beyond human power, such as beating, killing, and curing. But there can be no doubt that God has done all these things as the principal, independent, universal efficient Cause, whereas created beings \[do them as particular, dependent, less than principal efficient causes.
[Page 42]

In his dialogue addressed to the Archduke of Austria, Ulrich Molitor avoids the error of others to some small extent and ingeniously pretends that the Devil cannot disturb the air, cause hailstorms, etc. when he wishes, but offers no proof to explain why he cannot do so. Instead, \[he says\] that when \[the Devil\] foresees that a storm is coming, he incites wise-women \[sagas\] to do what they usually do to cause a storm, and persuades them that the power to whip up a hurricane lies in the things \[they are using\] or in what they are doing. I do not deny that sometimes they are successful, but I maintain that, for the most part, it is quite a different story because wise-women \[sagae\] are unanimous in confessing that they can accomplish these things whenever they want, and experience confirms it every time they are forced \[to confess it\], against their will, by judges. The effect \[they want follows, so an evil spirit is making it happen at the same time as he is given the signal \[to do so in accordance with the pact \[he and the woman have made together. Molitor is also mistaken when he offers extremely jejune, feeble arguments here and in the whole book to prove that an evil spirit cannot do with without God\'s permission. What Catholic has ever said he can? We all say loudly that God\'s permission is necessary, but we maintain that when God gives His permission and natural causes do not get in the way, an evil spirit can do this whenever he wants to, notwithstanding that under those circumstances no storm which is about to happen will have a natural existence.

I know that Burchard, (Decretum Book 19, under the title \"The Practice of Magic\', or \'Have you ever believed\'), denied this and thought that magicians \[magos\] who believed it were superstitious. They are superstitious because they are magicians! Likewise, if they believe they can do these things even when God does not give His permission then, according to this chapter, they become heretics as well.¹²¹ But they are correct in their supposition if they think that, once God\'s permission is in place, they can do this by means of an evil spirit because of the power of the pact they have with him. The entire consequence of these remarks-and this is the common opinion of theologians and legal experts-is proved (i) from Holy Scripture, because there Satan makes fire fall from the sky and consume Job\'s slaves and flocks, and he also rouses a strong wind which overwhelms \[Job\'s\] sons and daughters while they are eating and drinking \[Job 1.16 & 19\]. The orthodox Fathers of the Church tell us that Holy Scripture says the Devil did this. (ii) Holy Scripture specifically says that the hailstorm with which the Egyptians were punished was sent by wicked angels, \[Exodus 9.18\].

\(iii\) Four angels standing at the corners of the earth offer to stop the winds from blowing and thus to inflict very great damage on the living, \[Apocalypse 7.1\]. Many of the saints thought these were demonic spirits \[genios alastores\]. Finally, why does St Paul so frequently call evil spirits \'princes of the air? Mainly because of their great power over the air. Not only does an ancient law of the Twelve Tables, which Pliny and St Augustine praise on that account, confirm this but so do Imperial and Papal decrees. All the Fathers of the Church whom \[Burchard\] cited-Clement, Jerome, Augustine, Andreas \[of Caesarea\], \[Walafrid Strabo\], St Thomas \[Aquinas\], and many others besides: for example, Origen in Against Celsus, Book 8; John of Salisbury, Book 1, chapter 10; and Henricus Teutonicus with whom Thomas of Cantimpré agrees, Book 2, chapter 57, part 29.

\(iv\) It is proved by histories and exemplary stories about the winds and the storm calmed by magicians in the time of Xerxes, as Herodotus bears witness. Olaus \[Magnus\] writes as follows about the Finns and the Lapps. \'Once upon a time they used to offer to sell winds to merchants. They would use magic to consecrate three knots. When \[the merchants\] undid the first knot, they would have calm winds; when they undid the second, the winds would be stronger; and when they undid the third, the winds would be very strong\', \[History of Peoples in the North 3.15\]. Aristaeus once said that the inhabitants of the Cyclades used to attract trade-winds during summer by means of certain rituals and sacrifices, and so, with their pleasant chill, keep the fields free from being squalid and barren. There are also plenty of records kept by the ancients, which deal with showers called down by magicians.
[Page 43]

The Indians in Cianita procure rain by means of a ridiculous ceremony which is described in letters dated 1590 and 1591 from my Jesuit brethren in Peru. Gaspare Spitilli gives the following extract. \'When they are in need of rain, one of their magicians cries out in a loud, impassioned voice, speaking faster and faster, \[saying\] that they must all go to the mountain, and that when they get there everyone must abstain from food according to custom. (This \"fast\" of theirs, however, refers merely to salt, pepper, and cooked food.) When they have done this, they call earnestly upon the stars at the tops of their voices and pray for water. Then they return to their lands and pagan stronghold, taking a certain potion, which they drink when a young girl of a noble family offers it \[to them\]. It causes them to lose their senses and their reason, with the result that they look as though they are dead.

After they have recovered, however, they mingle honey with water and maize and sprinkle the mixture in the direction of the clouds. Next day they place a well-born old man, or at any rate one of good reputation who is held in honour, on a couch and light a slow fire under it. When he starts to sweat, the magician catches the sweat in a jar, mixes it with goose-blood and the water from a crushed root, and again throws this liquid into the air as if to soak the clouds, asking, by the sweat, the blood, and the water, that the water they desire be bestowed upon them; and if by chance it rains on the day they carry out their superstitious ceremonies, they give thanks to the stars and shower the magician with many gifts\'. There can be no doubt that the Devil finds it quite easy to adhere to such an important pact when people demand rain or hail with the intention of ruining others, and he scarcely ever lets them down; but when these Indians demand this favour for everyone\'s use, he makes complete fools of them since their magicians, no less than our diviners \[sortiarii\] operate by means of the power of their pact \[with him\].

This marvel involving water should not surprise anyone, because Pausanias writes that in Hypaepa it is common for a very bright flame to be produced from pieces of wood by means of an incantation in a foreign language, even though no fire has been applied to them, \[Description of Greece 5.27.6\]. Modern writers have published more recent examples, and what Rémy records in The Worship of Evil Spirits, Book 1, chapter 25 of the ritual used by witches \[strigum\] in our day is worthy of astonishment. \'I certainly remember this, which was testified and maintained by the free and voluntary assertion of two hundred people, more or less, whom I, in my capacity as a local magistrate, sentenced to be burned to atone for the crime of fortune-telling \[sortilegii\]. \[They said\] that it was their habit to meet on specific days and assemble on the bank of a fen or brook, especially if there was one which would be well hidden from the eyes of passers-by.

There, with a wand they had been given by an evil spirit, they would keep on beating the water until they raised thick clouds of vapour and steam by which they were all borne aloft together. Then \[they used\] whatever they had stirred up in this fashion to clothe themselves in thick, dark clouds, and after they had enveloped themselves and their evil spirits in them, they would drive and thrust them wherever they wanted and at last shake out a lot of rain and hail on to the lands below. In this case I don\'t think there is any deception involved other than their thinking they are raising those vapours by thrashing \[the water\]. The evil spirit is doing it and carries the witches \[striges\] aloft through the air in those clouds and makes sure they do not suffocate. If those women are being carried along by the clouds, can it be denied that they could fall to earth from the clouds if the evil spirit abandons them?
[Page 44]

But more of this later. Rémy adds that before they beat the water like this, they would sometimes throw into it an earthenware jar in which the evil spirit had enclosed something secret, or even stones the same size they wanted the hail to be when it fell. Sometimes \[they had\] candles which they took down to the fen and held downwards while alight until drops \[of wax\] falling into it had sufficiently tainted the fen. In addition to this, they would sprinkle poisoned dust into the water. They would usually use black wands they had been given by an evil spirit to thrash the water and would beat the water with them without any kind of pause, at the same time repeating the words of dreadful imprecations. Certain women have also confessed that, with the help of an evil spirit, male and female witches drive big-mouthed jars through the clouds as far as a place they have in mind. \[The jars\] then hang upside down over it, are burst open with rocks and flames and, in a swift attack, destroy and pound to pieces anything in their path.

Rémy gives many examples in chapters 25 and 29 of the same Book. Let me add two, one pleasantly amusing, the other ghastly. In the jurisdiction of Trier there was a peasant who, along with his eight-year old daughter, was planting cabbages in his garden. He happened to praise the little girl because she was carrying out this task well and she, a chatterbox because of her sex and age, said she knew how to do other, much more remarkable things. Her father asked her what she meant and she said, \'Stand aside a little and I shall make it rain all of a sudden on any part of the garden you like\'. He was surprised and said, \'All right, I\'ll stand aside\'. So he did so and the little girl dug a trench, poured water into it from her \'feet\'-I\'m using the Hebrew euphemism-stirred the water with a stick, muttered something or other under her breath, and lo and behold, suddenly rain \[fell\] from the clouds on to the designated spot. Her father was astounded. \'Who taught you this?\' he asked. \'Mummy\', she replied, \'and she\'s very good at this and other things like it, too\'. After a few days the farmer, stirred by zeal, pretended that he, his wife, and his daughter had been invited to a wedding. Hastily they dressed up for the wedding and he put them into a wagon, drove them to the nearby town, and handed them over to a judge to pay the penalty for their crime of harmful magic \[maleficium\]. (I was told this story by men whom I find entirely worthy of being believed). If you want to know where the method of making a small trench and using a stick to beat down what you have thrown into it, you can find it in Propertius Book 4, elegy 5 which is about an aged wise-woman \[saga\].

\'In fact, she also brought hillside herbs to the ditch, And anything lying in it would be dissolved into running water\'.

The following exemplary story was recorded by \[Giovanni\] Gioviano Pontano. Ferdinand, King of Naples, was closely besieging the town of Marcos which lay near a fortress situated on Monte Dracone at the end of the Massican Hills, because it favoured the Angers party. A failure of its water-supply had almost forced it to surrender when a number of ungodly priests ventured to use magical practices to draw down rain. They were drawn from townspeople and the besieged who, under darkness of night, deceived the castle-guards and stealthily made their way along very uneven rocks to the shore, hauling with them an image of Christ nailed to the cross. First of all, they loaded it with abuse and frightful incantation, then they cursed it, plunged it into the sea, and invoked a storm from the sky upon the sea and the land. At the same time a number of priests, more villainous than anyone else, (seeing that they were busying themselves in carrying out unholy military manoeuvres to their satisfaction,) performed an abominable ritual whereby, it was said, they thought they could draw down the rain they needed. They stood an ass in front of the door of a church, chanted a deadly incantation, and then thrust a consecrated Host into the roof of \[the animal\'s\] mouth. They repeatedly addressed the ass in loud voices with deadly chants and then buried it alive on that spot in front of the church door. Scarcely had they completed the ritual than the air started to cloud over, winds began to stir up the sea, and darkness to pour over the brightness of midday. At one point the sky would flash with bolts of lightning, at another the light would be snatched away, and the sky and land would quake beneath the thunder. Trees ripped up by the winds were flying \[through the air\], and rocks split open by lightning were filling the air with their fragments. So much rain burst out of the clouds, and so heavy was the downpour, that not only were the cisterns incapable of collecting the water, but they poured out stones and rocks which had been burned and made thirsty by the sun, and torrents \[of water\] which spread out in every direction. Thus, the King, who had relied on taking the town by making it thirsty, was frustrated of his hope and returned to his former camp in Savo\'.
[Page 45]

I have included this the more willingly to warn you, my Reader, that because of misuse and the negligence of bishops, rituals which have crept into certain areas in Germany and Aquitaine involving dragging images of Christ crucified and of saints such as St Martin and St Urban and others in order to procure favourable weather, and against whose use in Cantabria Martin de Arles published his book on superstition, are thought to have originated in the superstition of magic and are quite rightly regarded as suspect, because \[they are based on\] a tacit pact. Vincent de Beauvais also tells us that the people of Constantinople were persuaded by a magician to immerse a statue of the Blessed Virgin in the sea and that it discharged a storm against the Saracens\' fleet. You notice, don\'t you, Reader, that images of the saints are very hateful to an evil spirit? From this perhaps you may understand the spirit \[genio\] which drives the iconoclasts of our day. A canon of the First Council of Braga does not contradict what I am saying at all. It says, \'The kind of person who would believe that any impure created things (e.g. the Devil) make thunder and lightning and storms and droughts on their own authority is anathema, along with Priscillian\'. The real point of the canon, you see, is to condemn those heretics who used to think that the Devil is the prime origin of these things and has the power to create them and can produce them even when God does not allow it. All this is heretical and worthy of the magician Priscillian.

What I am maintaining now is clearly conformable to the principles of divine and human wisdom, and entirely in accordance with our experience not only of rain, hail, and wind, but also of lightning, which has the same explanation. Andrea Cesalpino, an Aristotelian, writes as follows in chapter 11 of his Inquiry into Evil Spirits. \'We have come across men who would stir up not only hailstorms, but lightning, too, but used to say they could not harm anyone they wanted, only those whom God had abandoned\'-that is, in my opinion, who had fallen from God\'s grace through mortal sin. \'One of these men used to do this. He would invoke the prince of evil spirits in open fields and beg him to send one of his servants to strike with lightning someone he \[the magician\] had chosen. Later he would sacrifice a black chicken to him at a crossroad and throw it into the air, and once the evil spirit had received it, the magician would stir up a very violent hurricane, accompanied by lightning. This, however, rarely fell on the individual he had chosen\'. God surely allows an evil spirit \[to do\] more against fields than against human beings!

\(iv\) Just as \[magicians\] can raise storms, so they can summon darkness. I have read in Marco Polo that the Mongols have such power because of their evil spirits\' conjuring-tricks that they can spread darkness when and where they will, and that on one occasion \[Polo\] himself escaped with difficulty from robbers after they had surrounded him with this magic. Hayton also tells us that a Mongol fighting-line was prepared for battle by their flag-bearer, a magician, who chanted a spell and spread deepest darkness over the enemy, and that they were victorious once light had been restored.

\(v\) According to Origen, \[Against Celsus\] Book 8, \[magicians\] can corrupt the air and the water in certain places and render them corrosive and deadly, and this is proved by the argument that they can make them smell dreadful as, for example, \[Mt\] Avernus does in Italy or the Dead Sea in Judaea. Is it easier to believe this of water? When it comes to air, the proof is that although air flows continually, its power of resistance is weak, and it becomes cold or warm for the slightest reason. It is, for example, cold in the morning during autumn, warm at midday, and cool in the evening. So why would something so susceptible to heat be more resistant to a foul smell? Finally, while Maruthes, the Bishop of Mesopotamia, was doing his official duties at the court of Yazdegerd, King of Persia, and Yazdegerd was showing the bishop favour because of his religious devotion, the Magi, fearing for their religion in case the King was going to convert, raised an intolerable stench and made up a false accusation that his Christian friends were responsible for it. But the Magi were arrested and, after a more thorough investigation of the matter, put to death, Nicephorus, History of the Church Book 14, chapter 18.
[Page 46]

\(vi\) Magicians can impede the action of fire in such a way that someone can walk unhurt along burning coals and not be burned, (unless God prevents this, as He does particularly in the case of those sentenced to the fire). Thus, in olden times the priestesses of Diana at Castabala were not harmed by fire, (Strabo Book 5); among the Falisci, the Hirpi would walk through fire unharmed, as Strabo Book 12 and Pliny Book 7, chapter 2 testify. Likewise, they can make sure that no one is drowned in water, as we see happening every day in the ordeal by cold water. They can make rivers temporarily cease to flow, dry up the channels of springs but still allow water to continue \[bubbling up\], wash away rocks and stones with springs they produce from the earth, and interrupt streams and drive them back to their source with the result that they seem to be flowing backwards-something Pliny says happened in his day. Lastly, they can do things connected with the weather by using minerals, although even if these things seem to be extraordinary, they are in fact natural. For example, they collect together aluminous minerals from cavities in the hills, mix them with natron, and set them on fire. This causes a sudden vapour which rises to the mid region of the air where, at length, it resolves itself into rain.

Book 2, Question 12

What power do magicians have over external things, or the good things Fortune brings?

Because human beings are very greedy for these, and the allurements used by an evil spirit are quite remarkable, I shall deal with them individually, proceeding from those which are easier \[to explain\] to those which are more difficult.

\(i\) No one doubts \[magicians\] can kill flocks and cattle. They do this by sprinkling or rubbing poison on them, or by applying it some other way. Occasionally, too, it happens by local motion when an evil spirit enters the animals\' bodies and suffocates them, throws them headlong to the ground, or tears them in pieces; and sometimes, simply because someone has praised them, the evil spirit secretly brings destruction upon them. This is the only way one can explain something \[Aulus\] Gellius wrote. \'Written in those same books I also came across this, which I later read in Book 7 of Pliny the Elder\'s Natural History. In the land of Africa, there are certain groups of people who cast a spell \[effascinatium\] with their voice and what they say. If these people happen to bestow somewhat fulsome praise on beautiful trees, rather extensive cornfields, rather delightful children, very fine horses, or well-fed flocks in excellent condition, suddenly all these things die, punished for no reason other than this\', \[Attic Nights 9.4\].

\(ii\) They can steal away other people\'s harvests and fruits and transfer them elsewhere, as Servius in his commentary on Vergil\'s Eclogues, Apuleius in his Defence, and St Augustine in The City of God, tell us; and this is not surprising, since it is done by an evil spirit simply using local motion. It does not seem to be irrational to suspect that this was the discovery of the magician Dardanus, and certainly in Turnèbe\'s opinion Dardanians are called after Dardanus, \[their name\] meaning \'threshers of grain\' because they were either believed to remove heaps of other people\'s stuff into their own barns by the use of magic or were charged with doing so. A few years ago, while I was staying in Mainz, a very notorious wise-woman \[saga\] was put to death in Trier. She had driven a stopcock in the wall of her house and would draw off all the milk from other people\'s cows-that is to say, an evil spirit would milk the cows and remove the milk very quickly by this means.

\(iii\) They can quite easily destroy any produce and fruits of the earth and make fields barren. The magicians summon an evil spirit with a wicked incantation, and he quickly destroys the crops and the year\'s produce. This is what one should understand by Ovid\'s \[verses\]:
[Page 47]

\'The corn, damaged by an incantation, vanishes into barren grass; The waters of a spring damaged by an incantation fail; Acorns fall from their trees and the enchanted grape from its vines, And the fruit falls, off even though no one is shaking \[the branch\],\' \[Amores 3.7.31-34\].

According to Rémy, they have a habit of sprinkling a tiny bit of dust an evil spirit has given them, and this causes canker-worms, winged and wingless locusts, slugs, caterpillars, and this kind of noxious bane of fields and gardens, to make an immediate appearance. They lay waste to everything, pillage the standing corn, garden-vegetables, fruit, and sometimes the grass itself; and when they have done this, they sometimes raise a large number of mice which bury themselves in the earth straight away and eat the shoots and all the roots. Sometimes they attack draught-animals with a gadfly which, in the end, kills them cruelly with repeated stings. Sometimes they strike a particular herb against a tree and bring wolves out of what appears to be a hollow in the tree to the place where they want them to attack a sheepfold and its flock, and \[the wolves\] do not go away until they have caused great carnage. Rémy records the judicial confessions of all these people in Book 1, chapters 21 and 24, and thinks that the evil spirit does not produce these afflictions afresh for the occasion, but that he gathers things quickly from all over the place into a single spot. There is certainly no doubt at all that \[an evil spirit\] can do this.

It seems likely that, because they harm and lie in ambush for precisely the particular flock and sheepfold to which they have been directed and which the person directing them wants them \[to attack\], those wolves are evil spirits who do this kind of damage under the guise of wolves. We may think that nothing stops an evil spirit from producing locusts and every other insect from material which has been placed here and there, by applying active things to passive, since they can be born from rottenness, although he does this at the time he needs and needs only a very short time \[to do it\]. Fresh examples happen every day, and I have brought together a very large number from other \[writers\]. In Spain I also read the letters of inquisitors who were staying in Pamplona at the time, and these provided evidence that they were coming across this constantly. Some people also find they are convinced by the authority of Supreme Pontiffs whose Bulls on the subject are still in existence-Innocent VIII to the inquisitors of Germany, Julius II to the inquisitor at Cremona, and Hadrian VI to the inquisitors of Lombardy. (See \[Bartolomeo della\] Spina, Inquiry into Witches, chapter 15).

\(iv\) They can destroy houses with fire, as happened to the entire town of Sciltochium in Swabia which was reduced to ashes by a wise-woman \[saga\] in 1533.

\(v\) They can harm someone\'s reputation in many ways, primarily by the use of conjuring-tricks they have learned from evil spirits and by deluding the senses. An example is the well-known Triscalinus from Le Mans who pretended that a parish priest was carrying a pack of playing-cards under his arm instead of his breviary and even persuaded the priest himself \[that this was so\], and caused him, red with embarrassment, to throw the book away. Such was the evil spirit of Mainz, an extraordinary slanderer, about whom \[Conrad\] Lycosthenes writes in his Extraordinary Events under the year 858, and another evil spirit who acknowledged that a monk in Santarém in Portugal was a saint. (See further below, Book 6, chapter 2, question 3, section 3).

They could also, as \[Bernardo\] da Como maintains at the end of number 13 in his Inquisitors\' Lantern, pass themselves off as innocent people by assuming their appearance during their meetings, if God did not prevent them from doing so. \[Bernardo\'s\] saying that God has allowed this hitherto in the crime of magic, in which a pact with an evil spirit has taken place, is something I have not read or heard of before, and because of this pact and the conspiracy in which they are in league, it is usually possible to reveal wise-women \[sagas\] even when they are not in one\'s presence, as Pierre Le Loyer has said correctly, arguing against \[Andrea\] Alciato in Book 2, chapter 5 of his Spectres. In fact, all wise-women \[sagae\] confess that those who have no experience of this crime or inimical pact cannot make an appearance at their meetings, as the suffragan Bishop of Trier, Peter Binsfeld, tells us with \[the help of\] many instances and an extraordinary learning which deserves our belief no less than does his merit. It is also quite obvious that this is related to God\'s foresight, with the result that \[the witches\'\] treacherous lack of faith cannot do any such thing to innocent people. But if God does allow something like this for a short time, He soon rips apart the evil spirit\'s crafty tricks as though they were spiders\' webs and guards an innocence which is in good order and carefully hidden-something I read in connection with St Silvanus, Bishop of Nazareth, and St Kunegunda, (this I read in \[Albert\] Kranz), who was slandered by an evil spirit with behaving licentiously. There is also the well-known story about a large number of evil spirits who were celebrating a feast at night and, even though they were deceiving demons, lied that they had come from a neighbouring lodging-house. St Germanus disclosed the deception.
[Page 48]

God may be more ready to permit them to take on the appearances of magicians and of those who have sometimes been ensnared by this crime, but it should not be denied that an evil spirit can fully adopt the appearances of innocent people if God allows \[him to do so\], although when and if He does allow it, He soon either uncovers the deception, as I have said, or allows it either \[as a contribution\] towards the remission of other sins \[the innocents have committed\], or for their greater merit and glory in enduring \[the experience\]. (See Bartolomeo della Spina, Investigation into Night-Flying Witches, chapter 14 and \[Three\] Defences \[against Giovanni Ponzinibio\], Book 3, chapter 3). Finally, they can harm a reputation by putting the tools for working harmful magic in certain places where they innocents they want to malign may be thought to have concealed them, which means the innocents are taken to be workers of harmful magic. I find that Mammon, an evil spirit who laid siege to the virgin St Eustochia of Padua, did this. God allowed her to be possessed this way for the whole of her life so that she could gain greater grace and glory, and when she was dead the name \'Jesus\' was found written in indelible writing on her little body. (Read Bernardino Scardoneo, The Antiquities of Padua, Book 2, class 6, folio 121). \[Magicians\] can likewise harm a reputation by revealing hidden sins, as demoniacs usually do, or by making a false accusation, as someone did in Germany during the reign of Emperor Louis II, (a story told by Sigebert \[of Gembloux\] and Vincent de Beauvais); and another evil spirit made a false accusation against \[St\] Dorotheus in relation to the same crime, as \[Simeon\] Metaphrastes tells us in \[Laurent\] Sauer, \'January\'

\(vi\) They can cause banquets or imaginary things to appear as, I think, did the Egyptians Celsus talks about according to Origen, the Brahmins according to Philostratus, and Pasetes according to Suidas, and some years ago \[Girolamo\] Scoto from Parma used to cause \[banquets\] to appear, but those who dined there, while seeming to be fully satisfied in Scoto\'s eyes, were tortured not long afterwards by genuine hunger.¹⁴⁴ Then there is real food. When an evil spirit offers this to witches \[sagis\], for the most part it consists of carrion which tastes bad and smells terrible. Undoubtedly God in His wisdom rarely allows \[an evil spirit\] to put pleasant food in front of them so that this does not afford gluttons the opportunity to fill themselves up with it. For the most part there is no salt, and often no bread either-perhaps so that the evil spirit can organise revels which are the opposite of God\'s Eucharist? In the Old Testament, loaves of shew-bread were placed before God every day, and every sacrificial victim and oblation was sprinkled with salt. Now, too, salt is mingled with \[the water of\] baptism, and in the Eucharist we feed upon the living flesh of Christ the Lord veiled under the appearance or accidentals of bread. So, could this be the reason for \[evil spirits\'\] hatred of bread and salt? I have no doubt that if God allows and wishes, \[an evil spirit\] can put salt and bread and very well-seasoned food in front of \[witches\]. But he rarely does this, and not infrequently he orders those he has invited to wear a badge and to bring along anyone who is willing to eat with them. This is something witches \[sagae\] have often confessed to their judges.

\(vii\) When God permits, \[magicians\] can snatch prisoners away from their prisons and bonds-only local motion and breaking and entering are needed for this; and since human beings can do this, why not an evil spirit, too? \[Johannes\] Trithemius tells a story about Leopold, Duke of Austria, which is worth repeating. \'In 1533 Frederick, Duke of Austria, who had been elected Emperor instead of Ludwig of Bavaria, was beaten in a great battle between Ettingen and Moludorfum and handed over to Ludwig who sent him to a wellfortified tower to be kept under guard. Meanwhile, in Austria, a magician \[magus\] promised \[Frederick\'s\] brother Leopold that, if he were paid well, he would bring Frederick safely \[out of the tower\] within one hour by means of magic. The Duke made generous promises, and so an evil spirit was commissioned and flew to Bavaria. He assumed the appearance of a foreigner and came to the prison.
[Page 49]

\'If you want to be set free from captivity\', he said, \'mount this horse and I\'ll take you safe and sound to your brother Leopold in Austria\'.

\'Who are you?\' asked the Duke.

\'Don\'t ask me who I am\', replied the evil spirit, \'because that is beside the point. Just mount the horse I am showing you\'.

At this point the Duke, who was in other circumstances a very courageous man, was seized with dread and protected himself with the sign of the cross. The spirit \[spiritus\], along with his black horse, disappeared and returned emptyhanded to the \[magician\] who had sent him. The magician loudly rebuked him for not bringing back the prisoner, and the spirit gave him an account of what had happened.

Philostratus writes that Apollonius \[of Tyana\] had escaped from his imprisonment, and the prayers of the Romans\' Vestal Virgins were believed to halt runaway slaves who had not yet left the city.

\(viii\) I am in no doubt that, with the permission of God, who is the God of Sabaoth and grants victory to anyone He pleases, \[magicians\] are able \[to perform\] the art of magic through evil spirits, conjuring-tricks, and various activities whereby, (as though by their own authority), they exercise more power over the rest of humanity; and there is no lack of examples that they can free cities from siege, (as I proved earlier in question 11 with the example of Marcos), and be the cause of victory in battles; and nor is this at odds with reason, because it is not uncommon for praise to be awarded to people in accordance with what their victory deserves. Among the Swedes, Ullr, famous for his exploits in arms, escaped by magic to such an extent that \[public\] opinion credited him with being divine; and Saxo Grammaticus writes about Oddo, a Danish pirate, as follows. \'He roamed the sea without a boat and often drove away enemy vessels by raising storms with a \[magical\] incantation. He was a danger to traders, but merciful to peasants. Having started a fight with Normans, he dulled his enemies\' sight with the power of his incantations in such a way that they began to think that flashing rays afar off were the Danes\' drawn swords, and so when it came to hand to hand combat they could not even look at a sword drawn from its scabbard, the consequence being that their battle-line was defeated because it was incapable of bearing a flashing light which had been produced by a conjuring-trick. Olaus \[Magnus\] says similar things about the Biarmenses \[early Finns\], Saxo about Haakon of Norway, and \[Johannes\] Cuspinian about the Bulgars who defeated Anastasius.

\[Martin\] Cromer says this about a memorable victory of the Tartars over the Poles. \'In 1240AD the Poles were fighting with the Tartars and were beginning to drive them forcibly in the direction of Legnica and press hard on the fugitives. In the Tartars\' rear-guard there was, among other banners, a prominent flag with the letter X on its leading side. On the pointed end \[of its pole\], however, was the dreadful black figure of a bearded head. When the flag-bearer shook this flag with a degree of force, the head would breathe out smoke and a very dense, most disgusting vapour. This vapour would not only stop the Poles from seeing the barbarians but would also kill them with its utterly loathsome smell. The Tartars had made this happen by means of certain incantations which they use in their soothsayings and the rest of their divinations, (just as they do on other occasions, and especially in war), and they are also accustomed to guess the course of future events from human entrails. When the barbarians sensed that the Poles were in disarray, they encouraged each other, made an assault on \[the enemy\], threw their ranks into confusion, and caused great slaughter. The number of Christians killed in this battle was so great that the single ears the Tartars cut off. filled nine enormous sacks.\'
[Page 50]

In the third Book of his history of \[the Kingdom of\] Bohemia, \[Jan\] Dubravius writes that in a battle in which Siderius was the victor, a wise-woman \[saga\] \[made sure\] that all the Luczani could neither go further forward nor retreat, and she fixed each man in his place by casting on him a marvellous numbness so that he could not run away. The Chinese worship Noema, a virgin, for her sanctity. \[Juan\] González de Mendoza writes about her in Book 2, chapter 2 of his history of China. \[He says\] she had acquired the worship because of her practice of magic. When, by order of the King, a war-leader called Compus was leading his fleet towards an island, he could not pull up anchor, however hard he tried, so that he could sail away. At last, he noticed Noema sitting, quite unexpectedly, on his ship\'s figurehead. He approached her, full of reverence, and begged her for help and advice. She replied that unless she guided them, they would not be victorious over the Chinese, and so he set sail with her and hemmed in the enemy who were putting their trust in rather powerful conjuring-tricks. They poured oil on the sea and wrapped everything around in pretended flames, as though to destroy the fleet with fire. Noema put them out and dispelled similar stunts by the enemy with her more efficacious magic, the result being that when they saw that their attempts were unsuccessful, they acknowledged they were beaten and surrendered to Compus. (Mendoza adds several other things, too.)

Whenever wretched peoples\' sins demand them, God certainly permits \[the use of\] the very ancient military conjuring-tricks of magic, because we read in the proverb-writer Diogenianus, that the Ephesians had certain little marks and words of magical power which they used to obtain victory in every fight. Suidas also testifies that a man from Miletus was taking part in a wrestling match with a man from Ephesus during the Olympics and was unable to win his bout because the Ephesian had \'Ephesian marks\' \[attached\] to his ankle, and it was noticed that as soon as these were removed by the referees the Ephesian, who had already exhausted thirty \[competitors\], was defeated. Polyaenus, too, tells us that Chrysame, a woman who used poisonous magic \[venefica\] procured a victory for Cnopus over the Erythraeans by means of her magic and drove the enemy mad, not with an incantation, but with poisoned pieces of bull\'s flesh, \[Strategems 8.43\]. In Book 4, chapter 28 of his History \[of the Franks\], Gregory of Tours records that a Frankish army under King Sigebert was overwhelmed by the Huns because apparitions were cast in its path by an incantation.

For the most part, however, God does not allow a victory \[to depend\] on the power of an evil spirit, and in this case, too, the Old Fox often prefers to deceive his followers. Magentius, of course, thus filled with worthless hope, resigned himself to being conquered by the Emperor Constantius, and King Eric of Sweden, who cultivated this kind of vanity to the highest degree, could not escape Ostanes\'s hands, even after so many victories. In the same history of Sweden, we read that Hadding, who was taking his orders from an evil spirit called Odhinn, clashed with the Curetes in battle and was overwhelmed. \[Odhinn\] had given him strength with a \[magic\] potion, but Hadding could not be victorious as long as he was following the plan for an army which was going to be drawn up for battle by an old man. Evil spirits full of deception laid him open to very many different misfortunes in his wars, at one time proclaiming him victorious, at another depriving him of all help and so, overcome this way by the magnitude of his despair, he decided to hang himself in front of his people.
[Page 51]

Next \[example\]: among the other causes of the great massacre of Flemings by the French in 1302, \[Jacob de\] Meyer in Book 10 of his chronicles records that the commander of the Flemish army, Willem van Gulik, personally employed diviners and necromancers \[sortilegos et necromanticos\], and conjured and consulted evil spirits. (\[Robert\] Gaguin and other French historians ascribe the same reason for the defeat and captivity of Ferdinand of Portugal, the Count of Flanders through his wife-\[namely\] the witchcraft \[sortilegium\] of his mother. The Flemings are silent on the subject). In connection with the year 1304, Jacob de Meyer also says this of the Willem I mentioned. \'Gossip has it that he was deceived by a necromancer from whom he had learned a \[magical\] technique which he promised would render him invisible whenever he wanted during danger, however great \[the danger might be\]. But the spell did him no good at all when it came to saving his life\'. It was exceedingly useful \[in bringing him\] to the torment of a real, eternal death!

The people of Pergamum once tried to free themselves from a siege by using magic, but their attempt came to nothing and their effort was wasted, as Paul the Deacon tells us in Book 20 of his history of Rome; and during the reign of Leo the Isaurian, Masalmas \[Maslama ibn Abd al-Malik\], the leader of the Saracens, seized Pergamum without any difficulty even though the citizens, who were relying on a certain magician, had made an appalling sacrifice to the Devil by cutting open a pregnant woman, removing the child alive, and boiling it in a cooking-pot while all the soldiers polluted the right-hand sleeve \[of their tunics\] with this wicked sacrifice. (For which, see Theophanes.)

I remember another noteworthy example of a \[magical\] stunt in connection with this topic, too. Bretislav, Duke of the Luczani-this is what Aeneas Silvius says-\'the founder of Bratislava, had started a war with his own nephew, Gremozislaus, Duke of Bohemia. The story is that when her stepson was about to go to war, there was a woman there who predicted that Bretislav would fall in battle and that the greater part of the populace would die with him, but that the young man \[her stepson\] could escape if he did as she told him. The youth replied that he would carry out her instructions, and so she told him he was to kill the first man he met, cut off both his ears, and stow them away in his wallet. Then he was to draw a cross on the ground with his sword between his horse\'s front legs, kiss it, and make a speedy retreat. Battle was joined on a plain called Thuscus, and Bretislav was killed. The young man who had done as his stepmother had told him escaped safely home, but he found that his wife, whom he loved dearly, had been killed. Her ears were missing and her breast had been pierced through. Aghast and unhappy, he realised that the ears of the enemy, which he had cut off, were those of his wife\'.

The price he paid was worthy of the wise-woman \[sagae\] he had consulted about victory. Perhaps his wife was also a practitioner of poisonous magic \[venefica\] and engaged herself in the battle, something the women of Bohemia have a habit of doing, or in this case it was a kind of harmful magic \[maleficii\] done with the help of a fatal image which was displayed during the battle while an evil spirit damaged the unfortunate woman at home. (I shall have to discuss this topic in Book Four.)

In Book 7 of the Annals of Bavaria, we read that the Fridericus Stuphius who falsely called himself Fridericus II and was preparing a war with the help of money \[he had got\] by magic, was overwhelmed, taken prisoner, and burned by Rudolf of Habsburg. So, Pope Innocent acted no less judiciously than he did religiously when he took pains to expel from Rome magicians who had been invited there by pagans from Tuscany on the promise that they would free the city from a siege by Alaric, the same way they boasted they had rescued the town of Larina. Not unlike this was the good sense, (but more severe judicial punishment), in the case of the Count of the Allobroges. This man was a follower of the Duke of Anjou and had charge of the Castel dell\'Ovo, a heavily fortified \[castle\] in Naples, washed on all sides by the sea. The Duke sent for him because he wanted his advice. Should a certain person\'s offer be accepted or not? The \'certain person\' was a magician who was boasting that he had surrendered this very fortress into the hands of Charles the Peacemaker who had withdrawn into it at the time, and that he was prepared to hand it over to the Angevins by using the same wily trick. He would create a thick cloud, \[he said\], build a bridge by using conjuring-tricks, and immediately extend it to the top of the fortress\'s walls; and once the defenders had been forced by fear to surrender, the soldiers who were going to come over the bridge would be so much on their guard that they would not be able to make any sign of the cross with their hand, their arms, their legs, or their weapons, and at that moment the bridge would be dispersed like smoke, and those who were crossing over it would die. The Count took note of this and argued that the Duke should send for the magician so that he could give him \[the Count\] more information about the whole business. The wretched man came and repeated his promise to the Count. The Count retorted that he would never commission him to convince such hard-working soldiers and experienced war-leaders that Charles the Peacemaker could not be vanquished without \[recourse to\] forbidden practices and such disgraceful behaviour. He then ordered that the magician be handed over to the public executioner and be beheaded. (These events took place in 1383.)
[Page 52]

\(ix\) There are many would-be persuasive \[exemplary stories\] about the high offices and honours to which someone is promoted by the use of magic. First, people adduce Roman Pontiffs who are recorded by various historians as having ascended to the highest Apostolic office this way-Martin II, of course, Benedict IX, John XXII and John XXIII, Silvester II, and Gregory VII. I reply, however, that in the case of Martin II it is said he came to the Pontificate by means of wicked practices, but it is said by people who hated him. One does not have to understand \'wicked practices\' as referring to magic, either. Bribery is a wicked practice, so is slander, so is simony, and so forth. Silvester II was a first-rate geometrician and wrote a book on the subject, a manuscript \[copy\] of which, as everyone knows, is held in the Farnese library.

He was also an expert in natural philosophy during a time which was so ignorant that natural philosophers and geometricians were considered to be magicians, and in consequence of this commonly held delusion many a fairy-tale was told about him, spread without heed by Martin, a Cistercian, and Calfridus. There are also widespread stories about both Johns and Benedict and Gregory, and there can be no doubt at all that these were disseminated to please the schismatic Emperors of the time. Cardinal Benno, who was a sworn enemy of Gregory during his lifetime, crammed his book with so many and such shameless lies that he seems either to have wanted to paint the picture of a Pontiff who was very wicked because of his character, just as Xenophon \[painted a picture\] of a very good King in his book on Cyrus: or the book was written, with Benno\'s name attached to it, by some heretic or other within our fathers\' lifetime.

Certainly, writers who lived in Gregory\'s day openly spoke against him, as Onofrio \[Panvinio\] and \[Roberto\] Bellarmino tell us. Nor can I believe that God will ever allow the Apostolic See to be defiled by an occupant who practises magic. Secondly, they adduce certain other examples such as that of Theophilus, the man who recovered the high office of Vidame with the help of an evil spirit, and similar \[stories\]. But one should not be surprised that magic can do what bribery, simony, and other corrupt practices can do because when God gives His permission, magic is successful; and when He does give His permission, He usually elicits thereby either timely punishment of the offenders, or an increase in reverence for God, or some other good.

The Devil himself, however, for the most part deceives magicians and dispenses to those with the empty hope of high office or princely dominion, (who should be burned), a straw instead of a sceptre and a funeral pyre instead of a crown. (The Annals of Bavaria tell us about Stuphius; \[Enguerrand de\] Monstrelet about the Breton Baron \[Gilles\] de Rais in Book 2, chapter 248 of his Histories, and Meyer-at length-about the Duc d\' Orléans in Book 13.) Furthermore, the story told by the author of the trashy Arbatel is ridiculous. \[He says\] that a Jewish magician used magic to cause the last King of Italy to be thrown out of the kingdom of Naples and \[to make sure that no Italian can ever rule over it, and that there is no remedy \[for this\] unless that magician is evoked by another, more powerful \[magician\] and forced to undo what he did. (This is what that complete liar, the author writes in aphorism 35). Nothing can be more inane and stupid than that, and I have mentioned it only so that the worthlessness of magicians may be apparent to everyone.

\(x\) One comes to the same conclusion in regard to genuine riches and treasures as one does in regard to high office-that an evil spirit can \[grant them\] if God is willing to let him do so, although He does not usually give His permission except in very rare circumstances for a variety of reasons. When Psellus writes, \'The Devil provides his worshippers with absolutely none of the things he promises he can undertake to do, only things which are useless to look at\', if he is talking about what the Devil usually does, what he writes is true; but if he is talking about what the Devil can do, it is not true. The same goes for what has been recorded by a well-known man¹⁶¹-that an evil spirit cannot, with God\'s permission, (because this is taken for granted), produce any of the genuine, real material \[from which one makes\] money, or give it a real form.
[Page 53]

One has to make this distinction because, although the \'form\' of money is the inscription on the coin, and this inscription requires craftsmanship, one can be in no doubt at all that the Devil is able to stamp out any kind of gold and silver coins he wants. He can also produce material to make a coin-that is, an object consisting of a metal compound-by applying active things to passive by way of a natural and artificial production-after all, he can produce living insects and these are more perfect than any metal-but this production must not be called \'creation\'. The Devil cannot produce the material for coinage by genuinely creating it because genuine creation involves producing money out of nothing, and this is a work proper only to Almighty God.

Consequently, theologians are united against Platonists, Simonians, and Avicenna in saying that God could not create the world by using angels to be a kind of natural cause, achieving their end independently, the way the causes of things in Nature usually do, because it seems to me very much more likely that what God does by using instrumental effective causes among \[a number\] of symbolic causes which make something happen, (just as the dead will be raised at the sound of the angel\'s trumpet at the Last Judgement), He can do by using angels. Consequently, God could, with His untrammelled power, allow the Devil to do or say anything, and create gold from the start. But in fact, under those circumstances, the principal efficient cause would be God\'s permission and will, independently making this happen through the medium of a natural cause, and the Devil would merely be the symbolic instrument of this creation.

If you consider this carefully, I do not think it can be denied. If you object to the way I have said it, I do not want to engage in a war of words. The proof of what I have said is that an evil spirit knows the hiding-place of every treasure, where riches have been submersed in the sea, \[the location of\] gold and silver mines, and where pearls and gemstones are hidden. So, he could take what he wanted without anyone\'s being able or daring to resist him. He can also remove coins from someone\'s purse or pocket much more cunningly and secretively than any mortal, and so although petty thieves and cutpurses often do this, he will certainly have the ability \[to do it\] as well. Various \[writers\'\] exemplary stories confirm this. Damhouder, in fact, while discussing money in a disparaging way, gives four examples which you will be able to read in that discussion, and Stuphius, (whom I have mentioned), paid an army with money \[he obtained\] by magic. Nevertheless, I add that the Devil can indeed enrich his followers if God were to permit it and \[the Devil\] wanted to do so; and the same applies if God were to order \[him to do so\] and he did not want to do it, (because he would be forced unwillingly to obey God). But the evil spirit does not want to do this, except on rare occasions, and God never orders it, and so He very rarely gives His permission.

It is clear \[the Devil\] is unwilling, because we see that he frequently makes a fool of his followers, and although he sometimes bestows a little real money on a few of them, as he did on Dr Vlät in Triers, for the most part, however, he deceives them with worthless hope and the \[deceptive\] appearance \[of money\]. \[Nicholas\] Rémy confirms this in Book 1, chapter 4 of his Worship of Evil Spirits, with many examples from his personal experience \[as a judge\]. He says, \'In Dieuze on 30th September 1586, Sennel from Armentières received money-so she imagined-from an evil spirit and, in transports of delight, went home quickly to count it, but when she shook out her purse, she found nothing except bits of tile and pieces of charcoal. In Dieuze on 4th November 1586, Catharine from Metz found pig shit. Claudia Morèle in Serre, on 3rd December 1586: Benoît Drigie in Haraucourt, on 15th December 1585: Dominique Pétrone in Pagnysur-Moselle, on 20th October 1586, and very many others \[found\] tree leaves. In Masevaux, on 5th June, 1585, Jeanne le Ban found a gold coin wrapped in paper on the road, just as an evil spirit had predicted she would, and when she eagerly showed it to her husband she noticed, (not without embarrassment), that she had stumbled across a dark red pebble and not a gold coin after all; and what is more, at the very first touch it began to break up into little pieces. Catharina Ruffa from the Vallée de la Moselle, on 28th July 1586, was the only one to acknowledge she had once received three coins without being deceived by the \[evil spirit\]\'. All these examples were taken from judicial records by the judge himself, and I am happy to make use of them because they are vouched for by the public credibility of the notaries who were present at the trials.
[Page 54]

Rumour has it that when the magicians Faustus and Agrippa were on a journey, \[once they arrived at\] their lodging-houses they were in the habit of counting out coins which were real to look at, but after only one or two days those who had received them would find they were bits of horn or absolutely worthless scraps of rag. \[An evil spirit\] had given an unfortunate, but well-born, married woman from Moselle, who was handed over to the flames at the confluence of the waters a few years ago because of the crime of sorcery \[crimen sortiarium \] a basket filled with what looked like scudi. She put them in a money box, but when she wanted to make use of them, she found horse shit in place of the coins. Several other \[stories\] of this kind are told because \[the evil spirit\] takes pleasure in deception and in provoking human beings to anger. Lorenzo d\'Anania gives another explanation, (why is he unwilling to accept this one?)-that evil spirits \[malos genios\] have dedicated themselves to covetousness and to guarding \[hidden\] treasures and money for the Antichrist, the son of perdition, so that he has enough for his expenses, and that this is what an evil spirit \[daemonem\] said in answer to a certain soothsayer \[ariolo\], although one should not believe an evil spirit at all, since he is a liar. But what he says is somewhat inconsistent with the truth.

The more reliable explanation is that God never orders this, (because He does not co-operate with evil), and rarely permits it. Why otherwise should we see that there is nothing more base, more beggarly, more unprincipled, and more hateful than the kind of human being to whom an evil spirit makes such glittering promises of riches, high office, and the favour of princes? If they were rich before, he beggars them. If they are beggars, they never become rich. Lucian says that Apollonius used magic to look for treasure, but neither Lucian nor Philostratus testify he ever found anything from which \'he would bring back a more perfumed head. This is also relevant to God\'s providence and His righteous governance of the world and the Church militant. His \[providence\] is absolutely clear and for that reason one might infer a distinction: how many people could be drawn to their death by genuine riches when countless numbers are drawn by those which are spurious? If the Devil could make anyone he wanted rich at his own pleasure-and almost all mortals have a wicked hunger for gold-one would have to be very afraid that he would drive almost all humans to devil-worship, especially those who have strayed from the true Faith. For this reason, the Devil would supply irreligious people with money, the sinew of war, and in consequence he would easily crush the devout unless God came to their aid with a fresh miracle.

Quite rightly, therefore, God has reserved to Himself the power and decision to make human beings rich. God gives everything but does not have less as a result. The earth is the Lord\'s, and its fullness\'. \'He opens His hand and fills every living thing with His blessing\'. \'He gives to each and offers no reproach\'. \'In His left hand are riches and glory\'. He enriches wicked people so that they may acknowledge His goodness and be converted, or at least so that He may in some fashion reward their good deeds. (This is well-known in the case of Cyrus and Nebuchadnezzar). He also enriches the good so that they may use \[their wealth\] properly and spend it on works of mercy.

The Devil, for his part, does not have anything to give and, in fact, pursues evil-doers with a proverbial hatred, flattering them merely to deceive them and bring them to everlasting torments; and to achieve this the more quickly, he not infrequently betrays his comrades-in-arms and hands them over to a judge as if to an armed force because he takes pleasure in the miseries, ruin, and death of human beings (whom he hates because \[they have been made in\] the image of God). Hence it happens that we see that he dupes humans with the empty hope of finding treasures and persuades them to look \[for them\] even though human laws tell them not to do so. Then he either makes them faint from fear or cruelly suffocates them. André Thevet relates a story he heard from an eye-witness about a Greek chappie called Macrianus who was swallowed by the earth while he was looking for treasure on the island of Paros, \[George\] Kedrenos says something similar in \[his account of the Emperor\] Anastasius, and several other people \[tell\] other stories. I shall record one which is not unworthy of one\'s astonishment.
[Page 55]
