\'In about 1520 in Basel, a simple-minded tailor with a stammer entered, (exactly how is not clear), the tunnel by which one can get into Augusta Raurica and, having gone further than anyone else had managed, he brought back a report of a number of extraordinary things he had seen. He lit a consecrated candle and went down into the tunnel. First, (he used to say), he had gone through an iron gate. Then he came out of one vault into another, and then into gardens which were in full, very beautiful bloom. In the middle he observed a hall, very richly decorated, and a young woman, very beautiful down to her private parts. Her head was circled with a golden crown, her hair was loose, and her lower half ended in \[the form of a frightening snake. She took him by the hand and led him to an iron chest. Two black hounds were lying on it and when they approached, \[the dogs\] set up a dreadful barking, but the young woman, with a menacing air, restrained them. Then she removed a bunch of keys she was in the habit of wearing tied round her neck, opened the chest, and brought out all kinds of gold, silver, and bronze coins, and he used to tell people that, as a result of the young woman\'s generosity, he had brought back not a few of these \[coins\] out of the tunnel itself.

He added that the young woman told him more than once, with dreadful imprecations, that when she was born, she was already cursed by her royal descent, and had been changed into the kind of monster \[he saw before him\], and that she had no good reason to think she would recover her wholesomeness. But if she were kissed three times by a young man of undiminished propriety, her original form would be restored to her and the whole treasure which was lying hidden in that spot would be made over to her liberator as a dowry. He claimed he had indeed kissed the young woman twice, \[but\] that twice he observed her making such dreadful gestures in anticipation of her liberation that he was afraid she would tear him to pieces while he was still alive. Some of his grandchildren brought him to the theatre, but afterwards he could never find the entrance to the tunnel, let alone go into it\'.

Who does not detect \[that this was\] an illusion? The young man was not entirely compos mentis, to say the least. That evil spirit, which belonged to the genus \'vampire\' \[lamiarum\], was asking for a kiss. She would have devoured him after the third one, but God did not permit it. The two dogs were other evil spirits, either genuine or fictitious guardians of the treasure. Some of the coins may have been real and, with God\'s permission, were given \[to him\]. After a few years another citizen from Basel went into the same tunnel with the intention of relieving his family\'s poverty but found nothing except the bones of human corpses. Seized by a sudden dread, he made a rapid exit and was affected by fits of madness until he died a wretched death on the third day afterwards.

A few years ago, a prior from \[the abbey\] of Margoulline, along with two companions, went into a pit in the cave of King Sala near Puteoli in search of treasure, but died a wretched death and was not seen again, as \[Jacques de\] Villamont tells us in Book 1, chapter 23 of his Voyages;¹ and in his Inquiry into Evil Spirits, chapter 12, Andrea Cesalpino confirms these points when he writes as follows. \'I am certainly aware of many treasure hunters\', he says, \'because of their great skills, but I don\'t know any who have been successful in finding anything. In Pisa, during my lifetime, some of them tried to dig, but stopped because they were defeated by the difficulty of what they had undertaken. Finally, after a year, they started to dig in a spot where places built by Nero were said to have existed-the place is now occupied by a convent-and where there was an enormous pine tree. But they were so plagued by evil spirits that they were forced to give up the moment they began because they were afraid. (During this time, many of the sisters living there were obsessed by an evil spirit)\'. What is surprising about this if they had allowed an illicit search for treasure to take place on their own soil? God inflicted a punishment on them in this world to spare them one in another life. No one, in my opinion, has discussed this subject more intelligently or more accurately than Paolo Grillando in question 3, number 12 of his book on divinatory magicians. Consequently, I think it a good idea to transcribe his words for you.
[Page 56]

\'They boast that by using this kind of \[magical\] practice they can find treasure which has been hidden away in very remote places underground, and there comes a time when they bring people to such a point of insanity that they dig pits as large as they can manage in order to find this kind of treasure. It seems to me that this is one of the more shameful illusions an evil spirit can cause a human being because the truth of the matter is that I have never read, seen, or heard that any necromancer, worker of magic, or diviner \[sortilegis\] of this sort has ever found treasure, or gold or silver anywhere else. But how often have I seen that those who devote themselves to this task and make extraordinary exertions to put it to the test finally, after a great deal of hard work and worshipping of the Devil, have seen and found nothing except earth! The reason is that God neither permits nor wishes an evil spirit to be able to lay up treasure, because otherwise the result would be that these workers of magic, necromancers, and all the other professors of faith in the Devil would be richer and more distinguished than the Christian faithful; and one would find very many from among the faithful of Christ who would follow them with the intention of becoming rich, and would not care about turning their backs on the Christian faith itself in order to acquire a great abundance of treasure and be treated with respect by everyone. But actually these days we see the complete opposite, because these diviners, magicians \[magi\], necromancers, and the like are poorer, more abject, and more base than the rest of Christ\'s faithful and generally lead, (because God permits it), a miserable life in this world, finally coming to an unfortunate end and being tortured in the flames of eternal fire, (as it says in the chapter \'most strongly\', from \'Concerning heretics\', and the chapter \'it is not surprising\', 26, question 5.) They are also keen to deceive us with the same cunning and similar promises to the ones the evil spirit uses to deceive them. But they take prisoner only some people who have a shallow knowledge of things and lack understanding, or those who are too credulous and inquisitive and, because of a kind of vehement desire, shamelessly seek to know things it is not proper for them to investigate without any reason and for no good purpose. (See the chapter \'it is not surprising\', the paragraph headed \'divinations\' \[auguria\], in the direction of these words, \'of a powerful\'.)

Thus far, Grillando. I find that the Indians of Malindi are in the habit of boasting that they try to draw rewards from the bottom of the sea by means of incantations-with what result I do not know. Read \[Fernão Lopes de\] Castanheda\'s Indian History, Book 1, chapter 30. With God\'s permission, the Devil sometimes produces rewards in order to strengthen his followers in their superstition. Without God\'s permission, he cannot \[do this\] and throws the blame on some mistake made by those performing the incantations. One should also note in connection with this that certain people are said to have found treasures by the use of magic in which they are experts, and by clever guesswork-the Saracen, for example, in the time of Guiscard the Norman in Apulia, whose magical deed \[is described\] by \[Bartolomeo\] Platina and \[Pandolfo\] Collenuccio. But Petrarch ascribes it more to practical good sense, and as far as this is concerned one will refer to the maxim that everyone can weigh up the subject on a scale which is balanced true.

Let me close this chapter with the most powerful and most devout Emperor, Charles v, who made an excellent decision when he ordered Cornelius Agrippa and two other noblemen into exile from the court and his realms because they had offered people the hope of \[finding\] treasure with the help of magic. May you and your crown be fortunate, Charles. You already enjoy felicity. May your posterity live long and be victorious, as long as they protect the Catholic \[faith\] and shrink from doing anything irreligious and meddlesome. If they fall into doing that kind of thing, they will be unlike you in virtue, just as they are in good fortune.
[Page 57]

Book 2, Question 13

Do magicians have the power to cast a spell on brute creatures?

In the case of snakes, not only poets but also Christians have believed this. I commended poets earlier in Question 9, especially Vergil, Ovid, and Nemesianus. To these let me now add Lucilius, Satires 20:

\'As a Marsian bursts snakes apart with his chanting, After he has made their veins swell;

Horace, Epodes \[17.29\]:

\'My head is distracted by a Marsian incantation\';

Ovid, Medicines for a Woman\'s Face \[39\]:

\'Snakes are not split in two by Marsian incantations\';

In \[Aeneid\] Book 7, Vergil says they fell asleep because of an incantation, \[vv.757-758\], and likewise Publius Silius Italicus:

\'Athyr was skilled in disarming snakes of their deadly poison, And sending water-snakes to sleep at his touch\', \[Punica 1.411-412\];

and Seneca, who describes Medea at some length in Act 4, \[vv.680-690\]:

\'With her left hand she makes supplication at the gloomy shrine, And summons all the deadly diseases brought forth by burning Libya\'s sand, And all those the Taurus confines with its perpetual snow, numbing them with its northern cold, And every \[kind of monster. Dragged out by her magic incantations, A scaly throng leaves its hiding-places and makes an appearance. Here a fierce snake drags its endless body, Flicks out its three-forked tongue, and casts about for those to whom It can come, bringing death. At the sound of her chanting, it is mesmerised. Its body swells, gathers itself together in knotted folds, And forces itself into coils.\'

Manilius, \[Astronomica\] Book 5. \[vv.391-393\]:

\'\[Ophiuchus\] makes the bodily parts of snakes harmless to those born \[under him\]. They will receive \[snakes\] in the folds \[of their garments\] and in their flowing robe, And will safely plant kisses on the dreadful poisonous creatures.\'

One has to read \'sinibusque\' \[folds\] in these verses-it is written correctly in the Gembloux manuscript-just as the ancients wrote \'Vergil\' and \'Virgil\', so also they wrote \'senus\' and \'sinus\'. It\'s the same in Manilius, isn\'t it? \'\[A dolphin\] derives energy from the sinuous ways in which it moves \[sinibus\] and imitates the curling billow\', \[5.421\].

Critics will have remarked why these corruptions happen, so I return to my starting point. \[Manilius\] later confirms at greater length what I have said when he deals with those born under the sign of the Bear, and this confirms what I have read in the annual letters from my Jesuit brethren in Peru, dated 1590. In this collection, \[Gaspare\] Spitilli tells us about a harmless contact with a viper, \[something\] devised by an evil spirit during stunts put on by Indian magicians, perhaps to make light of St Paul\'s miracle.

\'They also celebrate another festival no less superstitious than the one I mentioned earlier. In a small room they shut up a boy who has a beautiful face and is considered by the whole stronghold to be very good-looking, and order him to fast until his hair has grown long. Then magical conjurors, along with the elders, fetch him out and bring him to the house of one of his friends who has, for example, a very close connection with him. In the house, elderly women weep plaintively over the boy, and the common folk who are assembled and come together while this is happening, gradually cut off the boy\'s hair, starting at the front. Each person is offered a cooked goose. On the next day the magician dispatches \[some\] of the younger men to hunt wild animals, deputes others to make ready a suitable feast, orders girls to boil up some good wine and older folk to assemble musical instruments. From that moment, they start to play them, and from then on, those whom the magician sent away on his errands are free to pillage whatever they find out with anyone\'s house. They invite the neighbouring tribes to come to the feast wearing a variety of weapons.
[Page 58]

The boy, who has been garlanded at the time of the feast, has an offensive weapon in one hand and a living viper hanging from the other. He sits in a chair which has been placed in splendidly decorated spot, and it is easy to deduce from what I have said that an evil spirit is at work, because the viper does not bite him. Several other dead vipers, too, are tied to the boy\'s body. These were caught by the young men when they went hunting and are placed on one side of the boy along with boiled and roasted geese. His other side is hemmed in by a large amount of wine. After this, soldiers invited to the feast come in according to their military rank and distinction, make a reverence to the viper, go up to the boy, drink the wine next to him, and take the food which has been prepared and placed next to the boy. Then they retrace their steps, make a reverence to the viper again, and ask for a second helping of food and drink. They spend three whole days in this fashion, after which they kill the viper. Then the women, (who have been shut up indoors for the whole period of the festival), come out shouting, \"Victory!\" During this kind of celebration an evil spirit frequently appears, urging \[people\] to drink, eat, and spend the festal days in cheerfulness and merriment. \'

Pedro Cieza, too, writes that all the huge snakes of the Andes mountains have laid aside all their ferocity and power to harm because they were once disarmed by the incantations of an elderly woman, and are now harmless and terrify only because of their appearance. Alvise Cadamosto also confirms the same thing in his history of sea-voyages to the Indies, because \[he says\] that this power is attributed to the Negro peoples of Africa. However it started, Christian Fathers-St Jerome, St Basil, St Augustine, Arnobius, Theodoret, Alcimus, Bede, and Euthymius-think it is indicated in passages of Holy Scripture, since they take these at face value and acknowledge what the ancients recorded about the Marsi as likely to be true. I do not think St Hilarius disagrees because he understands \[passages\] about the evil spirit allegorically \[while\] the other Fathers take them in the literal sense. He thinks \[what the ancients say\] about the Marsi is a fairy-story in as far as the common people used to think that these things happened because of the power of the incantation.

The rest \[of the Fathers\] understand them in the sense Hilarius denies and I retain-that is, that an evil spirit \[malum genium\] flies towards the \[sound of the\] incantation as though it were a signal, and either disarms the snakes, or puts them to sleep, or kills them. The well-known example of the magician from Salzburg makes this point. When he was trying to force all the snakes-and this means one thousand of them!-into a furnace, he was finally killed by one huge old \[snake\] which struck out at him. An evil spirit, of course, had driven the rest of them into the furnace, but was unwilling to compel this one, preferring to rouse it against the magician. After all, if the rest of them had been driven into the furnace by the power of an incantation, that same power would have extended to this snake as well. This is clear from the ridiculous and tasteless form of words magicians use, and if other people had not spread it all over the place by writing it down, they would have done better to reflect on \[the story\] and ask some intelligent questions.

There are two passages of Scripture. In David we read, \'\[Their madness is\] like that of a viper which is deaf and stops up its ears: which will not listen to the voice of those who are chanting and of the worker of poisonous magic \[venefici\] who is chanting discreetly,\' \[Psalm 57.5&6, Vulgate\]. Natural instinct, therefore, tells it that power can be brought to bear against it by means of an incantation. (Euthymius records that this kind of snake is called a \'Palamnis\'). In Jeremiah \[we read\], \'I shall send venomous snakes for which no incantation exists\', \[8.17\], that is, which cannot be enchanted. All the rest, therefore, can \[be enchanted\] although-let me not pretend otherwise-the sense can be that snakes will be so deadly that their bites cannot be cured. The Septuagint confirms this interpretation when it translates \[this as\], \'death-bringing snakes, for which no incantation exists: and they will bite you in a way for which there is no cure\'.
[Page 59]

The passage from David, then, is a batter one to prove the point and let us understand that magicians have this power over snakes because of the force of their \[demonic\] pact. I have no doubt they can exercise it over every other kind of beast-hence Silius \[Italicus\], \'Kartalo who was not afraid to stroke pregnant lionesses\', \[Punica 1.405\]-and in Book 3, chapter 12 of his Voyages, \[Jacques de\] Villamont tells us that fishermen are accustomed \[to use incantations\] against crocodiles. But I acknowledge St Augustine\'s notion that because of the deception practised on our first parents by the serpent, and because there is a greater intimacy between snakes and evil spirits, (not by Nature but by divine permission), God more frequently permits the enchantment of snakes than of other wild creatures. \[Paolo\] Grillando quite rightly says at the end of Question 8, \'On another occasion, during the time of Hadrian VI, before he arrived in the city as Pontiff, I saw a Greek magician who had a remarkable reputation because with words alone he had restrained the violence of a very ferocious bull which was part of a herd living in a wooded place. He rooted \[the bull\] to the spot like this, as I said, and, having humbled it, got hold of it by the horns and, with a quite fragile piece of rope, (which, however, had been made by magic), tethered it and led it where he wanted, in the middle of the night, for about four or five miles. This was seen by two hundred and more men\'.¹⁷⁵ But Grillando is mistaken when he thinks all this could have been done by the actual power of words, because in Book 1 I have refuted the claim made by the magician he mentions. Arnobius Book 1. \[43, Against the Heathen\], Jerome in his Life of Hilarion \[20\], and Cassiodorus, \[Variae Epistolae\] Book 3, letter 51, tell us that in races in the Circus, people were accustomed to use an incantation to speed up their own horses and slow down those of their opponents.

Suppose someone asks whether evil spirits really can change animals\' colours the way they can change their feelings. I can have no doubts \[about this\] because they know the waters with which this is accomplished naturally. In Book 3, chapter 12 of his Inquiry into Living Things, Aristotle testifies this as follows. \'There exist rivers in many places, and when sheep drink from them and step into them soon afterwards, they give birth to black lambs. In the Assyritis district of the Chalcidice in Thrace, for example, there is a river known as the \"Psychrus\" because of its excessive cold, which does this; and in Antandria there are two rivers, one of which makes flocks white, and the other black. The River Scamander, too, is believed to make sheep yellow which, they say, is why Homer called it the \"Yellow River\". Varro, quoted by Solinus, also recorded that on the coast of the Red Sea there is a spring, and if sheep drink from it the condition of their fleece changes from being white before they drank to a swarthy black \[afterwards\]. Magicians relate more miracles in connection with rivers, and if these really did happen, \[evil spirits\] could \[be doing\] all these things to living creatures by \[using this kind of water. I shall add one or two \[more examples\]. Publius Naso \[Ovid\], Metamorphoses Book 15.

\'In the middle of the day, horned Ammon, Your stream is cold. At dawn and sunset, it is warm. There is a story that the Athamanes set fire to wood which has been soaked by its waters When the moon has withdrawn into her smallest orbits. The Cicones have a river which, if drunk, turn one\'s internal organs to stone And turn the objects it has touched into marble. On your shores, the Crathis and its neighbour the Sybaris Make hair like look like amber and gold; and what is more extraordinary, there are waters Which have the power to change not only bodies, but minds as well. Who has not heard of the ill-omened waters of the Salmacis \[fountain\] And the lakes of Aethiopia? If anyone drinks from their sources, He either becomes mad or sinks into a stupor remarkable for its heaviness. Whoever slakes his thirst from the spring in Clitor Avoids wine and is happy to remain sober with pure water The river in Lyncestis has the opposite effect. Whoever drinks even a moderate amount of this Staggers about, just as though he had been drinking undiluted wine\'.
[Page 60]

Book 2, Question 14

Can \[magicians\] use their craft to produce or bring into existence anything which is of more than one kind, and in particular, things which are capable of sensory perception?

Here is the first proposition. They can quickly produce incomplete living creatures-flies, worms, frogs, and insects of this kind-and other things which are born from putrefaction, not by creating them, but by applying active things to passive. Thus St Augustine, followed by the school of theologians. But magicians usually produce these things because of their pact with an evil spirit which applies active things to passive. For the most part, you see, the magicians themselves do not know it is the pact which is giving rise to these things and are ignorant of what is causing them: for example, when the Devil gives wise-women \[sagis\] a very small amount of fine powder and they throw it into the air, and various kinds of winged and unwinged locusts, mice, caterpillars, and different destructive sorts of similar little creatures come into existence at once. Sometimes \[the Devil\] orders them to tear up the first sprouting shoot they come across in the field they want to devastate, or to knock off a plant from a tree they intend to destroy, and to employ different kinds of wonderful magical whining \[naenias\] which have no natural power. As matters stand, magicians could even produce the kind of somewhat defective geese which are usually born from the fallen fruits of trees, which are rotting in the sea or, of course, from the timbers of decayed ships, which the Scots and the inhabitants of the Hebrides acknowledge does happen. (They call them klakis and bernicles.)

But are the living things to which they give these names things which are fully complete? I think it more likely that they cannot be \[if they happen as a result of\] applying active things to passive, because it is more likely that these things cannot be born from putrefaction. \[That can happen\] only by means of the generative seed of parents, either from the father or from an evil spirit \[acting as\] an incubus who pours it into the mother, as will be discussed later. An evil spirit could also enclose things of this kind, which he had produced already, in the woman\'s womb and then skilfully bring them out by means of some kind of secret route belonging to a hidden passage he knows about. He could also cherish them, feed them, and make them breathe in the remote places where they seem to be born, seeing that flies, mice, and defective things such as that \[are born\] among rocks on which things which are quite often defective are born. I absolutely believe this is what happened in the case of the dogs William of Newburgh spoke about as follows. \'While \[workmen\] were splitting open a huge rock in a particular quarry with their iron tools, two dogs appeared. They filled to capacity the space available to them in that rock which had no hole at all through which they could breathe\', (meaning \"perceptible hole\").

\'They seemed to belong to the breed of dogs known as \"greyhounds\", but they looked ferocious, smelled bad, and had no hair. It is said one of them died not long afterwards, but they say that Henry, Bishop of Winchester, played with the other one, which had a quite extraordinary appetite, for a good many days\', (History of the English, Book 1, chapter 28).

He follows this by saying that a toad was found in another stone with a small gold chain round its neck. I think an evil spirit added the chain and that the toad itself could have been born there, just as enormous flints have been found in cola-mines in Liège, inside which live toads have been formed and were lying hidden as though in their birth-canal.
[Page 61]

Evil spirits can also produce a number of strange monsters, such as the one from Brazil which was seventeen palms\' tall, covered in lizard skin, with swollen breasts, lions\' limbs, and staring, flame-coloured, glittering eyes, and a similarly coloured tongue, (something like the monsters with half-human faces which were captured in the forests of Saxony in 1240). It may be possible that these were born from an abominable coitus between human beings and wild animals, which is undoubtedly the origin of most monsters. This is why Alcippe gave birth to an elephant at the time of the Marsic War \[91-88 This is why a Swiss woman bore a lion in 1278. This is why a woman produced a cat in Pavia in 1470, and another woman a dog in Brescia. This is why, in 1531, a woman in Augsburg in one and the same birth first produced a human head wrapped in its membranes, then a snake with two feet, and thirdly an entire pig. (One can read other things of this kind in more recent authors, too.)

One \[story\] which caps the rest, extraordinary though they may be, is the one reported by Castanheda in his Annals of Portugal. Because of a crime \[she had committed\], a woman was taken away by ship to an uninhabited island, and when she had been put ashore there a troop of monkeys, in which the place abounded, stood round her, growling. Then one who was rather big, to whom the rest of them gave way, arrived on the scene, took her gently by the hand, and led her away into an enormous cave. There he and the rest of the troop set before her a large number of apples, nuts, and various roots and, with a nod, invited her to eat. When she had finished, the wild creature forced her to have sex with him, an outrage he continued without interruption for many days, the result being that she gave birth to two children. The unhappy woman how much more preferable would death have been!-lived like this for several years until God in His mercy sent a ship there from Portugal. The soldiers came ashore to fetch water from a spring which was next to the cave, and by good fortune the monkey was not there. The woman came running up to the first human beings she had seen for a long time, threw herself at their feet, and begged them to take her away from \[the monkey\'s\] villainy and \[her\] appalling slavery. They took pity on her misfortune and agreed to take her on board with them. But-and I\'d like you to notice this point-the monkey bore down on them and, with uncouth gestures and deep growls, called back his \'wife\' (nonwife). When he saw that the sails had been spread, he ran forward and held up one of the children to its mother, threatening that if she did not come back, he would hurl it into the sea; and he was not slow in carrying out his threat. Then he dashed back to the cave and just as quickly returned to the shore, holding up the other child, made his threat, and drowned it, after which he pursued the ship closely, raging all the time, and swimming after it as long as he could until the waves proved too strong for him. The whole of Portugal can bear witness to this very famous incident. The King condemned the woman to be burned in Lisbon, but because of the prayers of certain people who begged for her life, he commuted the death sentence to perpetual imprisonment.

Are you astonished, Reader? Look for yourself at Saxo Grammaticus and \[Johannes\] Magnus and \[Olaus\] Magnus, Bishops of Uppsala. They tell a story about Gothic kings who are descended from \[the union of\] a bear and a noble virgin, and João de Barros \[who tells us\] about the Pegusian and Sianitan tribes of India who are descended from a dog which mated with a woman.
[Page 62]

Actually, I do not put much faith in these tales because I am sure that a real human being cannot be born from \[intercourse between\] a human and an animal. An animal\'s seed lacks that completeness which is required to house such a noble soul, so if anything is born from intercourse of this kind, it will be a monster, not a human because a foetus of this kind conforms to the condition of the inferior seed and in consequence at best it will produce something which merely looks human. I cannot believe that a brute beast which has one or other parent of this kind can be considered fully complete. (The children born to the Portuguese woman, for example, were neither monkeys nor humans). It is, rather, a monster of a mixed type which is neither one thing nor the other, such as a mule, which comes from a horse and an ass, or a jackal, which comes from a hyena and a wolf. A story told by the judicious historian Pedro Cieza \[de León\] confirms this opinion. He tells us that the men of the Andes are accustomed to have sex with huge monkeys, and that the children born from this union have a human head and private parts, while the rest of them resembles a monkey. They are wild and ugly, they do not speak but shriek in a terrifying manner, and the Indians themselves consider them to be monsters. Lastly, in the province of Aulaga, an Indian woman bore three monsters to a dog. (See his History of Peru, Part 1, chapter 95.)

Moreover, in some of the exemplary stories I have cited, an image-making energy, corruption of the seed, and an abundance of superfluous humours in the womb can each lay claim to its own role, and in addition to this, it can be the case that some women belonging to this group are to be counted as witches \[strigum\] and an evil spirit, acting as an incubus, has poured the hot seed of these creatures into them. Why can someone who misuses human \[seed\] this way not also be able to misuse that of a wild animal? I certainly think it can be said of the exemplary stories about the Indian peoples and the Gothic kings that an evil spirit forced himself on the women in the likeness of this kind of creature, and the following Question will inform you that he does have this ability. To these examples I add a story which is entirely true. Right here in the Spanish Netherlands there was an abominable individual who had sex with a cow. People later saw that the cow had become pregnant, and after a few months she gave birth to a male offspring-not a calf, but a boy. More than one person was present to see him falling from the womb of his mother, the cow.

They picked him up from the ground and handed him over to a wet nurse. The boy grew up, was baptised and instructed in the precepts of the Christian religion, dedicated himself to the practice of religion and, on his father\'s behalf, earnestly devoted himself to works of penitence. To be sure, he was a complete human being, but one whose mind had bovine leanings which made him want to graze in the fields and chew the cud. What is one to make of this? Wasn\'t he a human being? I believe he clearly was, but I deny that the cow was his mother. Why do I do so? The Devil knew about his father\'s sin-indeed, he instigated it-and shortly afterwards made it looks as though the cow was pregnant. Then, at a time of his choosing, he produced an infant he had stolen from someone else and placed it under the cow, (which was \[actually\] \'pregnant\' with wind), while she was \'in labour\', with the result that \[the child\] appeared to be produced by the cow.
[Page 63]

In consequence, a piece of fantasy was born, and the boy was persuaded he was the son of a cow. His imagination got to work and so he thought he had imbibed certain propensities from his supposed mother. To think that he was a real human being born from material provided by bovine seed, and that the fully formed organic arrangement of a human body was achieved in the womb of a cow by means of the heat and power of a bovine soul is something I think all natural philosophers will agree with me should be reckoned as one of the áδúvata \[impossibilities\].

But when Manilius ascribes the production of monsters to influxes from the stars and the destiny Stoics talk about, that is the madness of someone who belongs to an \[heretical\] sect.

\'\[There is a power which\] often joins the bodies of wild animals To those of humans. The offspring will not come from their seed. What do we have in common with wild animals, Or what adulterer has committed the crime of giving birth to a monster? The stars fashion strange shapes, and the sky has sown hybrid creatures\', \[Astronomica 4.102-105\].

Yet Andrea Cesalpino\'s paradox \[says\] that fully complete beasts-horses, oxen, elephants, and indeed a human being, as far as his body is concerned can be born simply as a result of the heat of the sun and putrefaction, and he tries to prove this with many \[arguments\] in his Peripatetic Questions, Book 5, question 1, according to which it would follow that all these things can be produced magically by the application of active things to passive. I have not yet been able to be convinced or believe that this is true. I am not unaware that the preparation of matter for the forms of living things which are called \'fully formed\' depends on a generative and formative power which has the job of fashioning the fully formed human being, but not on the mixture of primary properties and the measured constitution it receives from an influx out of the heavens and the heat of the sun.

It demands in addition the co-operation of a fully finished living being, as all theologians and natural philosophers acknowledge. My Jesuit brethren from Coimbra cite these, too, in their learned rebuttal of this falsehood-see their commentary on \[Aristotle\'s\] De Caelo, Book 2, chapter 3, question 6, articles 3 and 4-and I urge you, inquisitive Reader, to read them. Consider this point, too. To say that a human being is begotten this way is either self-contradictory, or merely says that a monster can be begotten-indeed, not a monster, because it is \[actually\] something without a soul. My question, you see, is, by what \[power\] will the lump of a human being be animated with a soul? Human? But this is Paracelsus\'s madness and heretical, as I have pointed out elsewhere. Animal? But the soul of animals cannot inform a human body because that requires a quite different balance of its constituent parts, entirely unlike that \[required\] by the soul of an OX and a dog. It is generally agreed that a dog\'s body cannot be informed by the soul of an OX, so what kind of a thing is this monster, or what will it be?

It is certain that a monster is sometimes born as a punishment from God. The punishment meted out to the Frieslanders of Dokkum because they murdered the saintly bishop, Boniface, and his companions tells us as much. \'God,\' says Kempius, \'has avenged the shameful death of these saints upon the children \[of those people\'s\] children, with the result that to this day random members of the families whose ancestors carried out such a wicked crime have thick whitish hair on the back of their head, rather like a brute animal\'s tail.
[Page 64]

So, you might justly say, \"Their descendants still feel \[the result of what \[our original\] parents did\". Thus, Cornelius Kempius from Dokkum in Book 2, chapter 21 of his Origin of the Frisians. Let me imitate Samson and link tails together. In Book 13 of his English History, Polydore Vergil writes to this effect. King Henry II was beginning to reveal himself as an enemy of the holy man, St Thomas of Canterbury. \'\[St Thomas\] started to be so slighted, despised, and hated by the common people that on one occasion when he came to Strode, a village on the bank of the River Medway, a river which flows near Rochester, the inhabitants of that area, eager to inflict some dishonour on the good Father they so despised, did not hesitate to cut off the tail of the horse he was riding, thus bringing down a perpetual disgrace on themselves: because afterwards, in accordance with God\'s will, it turned out that all the members of the family which had committed that crime were born with tails, as though they were brute beasts. But that mark of infamy, together with the family of those individuals, has long since disappeared.\'

This is what Polydore says. God knows whether he attached the last clause to please \[his readers or because it was true. Certainly the whole race joined in the abusive insult and it \[still\] persists among shameless individuals who, with their rather freakish language, have nothing of importance \[to say\] and babble, whether \[what they say\] is true or false. If any remnant of this disgrace were still in existence, William Tooker would have attributed to his queen the wonderful power of being able to remove this appendage. A further passage: I find in Book 2 Thomas of Cantimpré\'s work entitled Bees that a certain nobleman profaned feast days by going hunting, and his wife bore him a child with the distended jaws of a dog and the long floppy ears of \'hounds\', as they call them.

186 Polydore Vergil, (c.1470-1555), Italian historian who lived for many years in England. His Historia Anglica was printed in 1534, although it had been written several years before that. This quotation comes from Book 13, section 7.

Book 2, Question 15

Do evil spirits \[known as\] incubi and succubae ever exist, and can offspring be born from this kind of \[sexual\] intercourse?¹⁸⁸

Axiom 1: Male workers of harmful magic and \[female\] witches \[malefici et lamiae\] are accustomed to carry out the sexual act with evil spirits, the former with succubi, the latter with incubi. Several heretics, (indeed the majority of them), falsely deny this. So do some Catholics, but not very many-Philippe Broïde from the Spanish Netherlands and \[Girolamo\] Cardano, \[Gianfrancesco\] Ponzinibio, and \[Giam battista della Porta from Italy-but the influence of these men is not great if you compare it with those who maintain the opposite. What this axiom says has been so convincing to many people that one should be afraid of being obstinate and rash if one disagrees with them. This is also an opinion common to the Fathers \[of the Church\], theologians, and learned natural philosophers, and one verified by the experience of all ages and nations. So that I do not have to list everybody, \[let me mention that Plato in his Cratylus, Philo, Josephus, ancient Judaism, St Cyprian, St Justin Martyr, Clement of Alexandria, and Tertullian were in agreement \[about this\], as well as others who have quite rightly felt that, in as much as the more ancient Fathers thought that evil spirits can do the deed with women, they are justly rebuked by more recent \[scholars\], because they interpreted the words of Moses about the sons of God and the daughters of Cain as referring to this kind of copulation.

Consequently, I think one can select more substantial approval of the opinion \[expressed in Axiom 1\] from chapter 6 of St Jerome\'s commentary on St Paul\'s letter to the Ephesians, and from St Augustine who, along with St Isidore, is followed by the general school of theologians. The Bull of Pope Innocent VIII against workers of harmful magic \[maleficos\] does battle on behalf of that same \[opinion\]. These points are proved and explained at the same time by reason. Evil spirits, (as I shall discuss later), can assume the bodies of dead people, or fashion afresh and shape for themselves out of air and other elements \[bodies\] which feel like flesh to the touch. These they can move about as they wish and give them warmth. By artifice they can make a show of sexuality they do not possess naturally, and abuse men by appearing to them as a woman, and women by appearing to them as a man. They counterfeit women and lie underneath the men. They can also bring along real semen taken from someone else and imitate a man\'s natural emission. Therefore, they are able to do everything the first axiom says they can, and since they can do so, (and experience tells us that they do), there is no reason for us to doubt it.
[Page 65]

Axiom 2: Offspring can be born from this kind of copulation with an evil spirit acting as an incubus. There is greater difficulty attached to this axiom, but it is removed when the whole topic is explained properly and clearly. One needs to be aware, then, that the Devil can transfer semen he has taken from someone else-during the nocturnal emission which happens while a man is asleep, for example; and because he is nimble and has practical experience of Nature, he can preserve procreative heat in the seed, no matter how delicate, airy, and easily dispersed that \[heat\] may be; and finally, at the moment the woman is in the best state to conceive-and he is well aware of when that moment happenshe pours it into her womb via intercourse, or produces it to be drawn there by its natural force, and so mingle with the woman\'s seed. (The authors I shall be citing later have held to this conclusion.)

Axiom 3: Evil spirits, however, cannot, as living creatures do, generate life by their own power and from their own substance. The proof of this is that evil spirits do not multiply among themselves either individually or as a species. Evil spirits do not have a body made of air or any other kind \[of body\] at all as, contrary to the Platonists, Marco Antonio Natta has told us in his treatise on God, Book 7, folio 75; and this is what Francesco Giorgi and \[Tommaso\] Cajetan think, too.

Consequently, they cannot take part in sex in their own body, (as \[Benito\] Pereira explains very well in Book 8, dispute 2); nor do they have any semen, still less any, however weak, which is procreative. (This was the mistake made by Marcus of Ephesus, Josephus, and perhaps Athenagoras, Tertullian, and the more ancient Fathers). How can they have their own semen when semen is part of a living physical substance and, according to the opinion of the better writers, what is left over from very well digested food? But evil spirits are incorporeal substances and therefore cannot furnish physical semen from their own \[body\]. They also lack a soul, and the function of a quickening soul is to use its natural heat to digest the food \[the body\] has received. Therefore this axiom, which the authors cited in the preceding axiom acknowledge, is fully proven-and certain writers of great repute, such as St \[John\] Chrysostom and others, whose opinion I reported and explained some time ago in my commentary on Seneca\'s Hercules Furens, verse 447, have at least been willing to maintain this conclusion and have not denied the conclusion which precedes it.

Opponents sometimes object that these are exemplary stories about silly deluded women. I neither rebut these \[stories\] nor reject them, especially those marvels brought forward as witnesses by \[Nicolas Jacquier and Martin of Arles. But I do maintain that \[objectors\] draw improper inferences from them and are sometimes deluded, and therefore \[what they infer is never accurate \[because\] from a positive particular they deduce a universal which is also positive, and in a topic which is unsuited to it this is inept.

So, I say that a child can sometimes be born as the result of sexual intercourse between an incubus and a woman, but in these circumstances the real father of the child will not be the evil spirit but the human male whose semen the evil spirit has misused. Plutarch in \[his life of\] Numa, the heretic Paracelsus, Ulrich Molitor in the final chapter of Women Who Foretell the Future, and Nicholas Rémy in Book 1, chapter 6 of his Worship of Evil Spirits, have denied this, but the arguments with which they prop themselves up are insignificant. My fellow-Jesuit, Benito Pereira, does not deny it, although he does have his doubts, (Book 8, disputation 3 of his commentary on Genesis.) But in times past the Egyptians acknowledged it, as Plutarch bears witness, and so generally did the Schoolmen, all of whom were very good natural philosophers. It is proved by clear reason from what I said during my explanation of the second axiom.
[Page 66]

Very many exemplary stories told by those \[authors\] and others are in agreement and if they are true, they should undoubtedly be explained in accordance with these conclusions. Antiquity swallows its demi-gods, Hercules, Sarpedon, Aeneas, the Servii Tulli: England its Merlin: and Pannonia the Huns born from the Gothic witches \[strigibus\] of Arlon and fairies. In chapter 27, part 2 of his history of Peru, Cieza writes that in Hispaniola an evil spirit called Corcoton was in the habit of having sex with women, and the children born as a result of this had two horns. Hélinand reported the same in reference to the Dukes of Cleves, (he is quoted in Book 4 of \[the Speculum Historiale\] by Vincent de Beauvais); the Japanese say their Xaca is the same kind of person; and there is no lack of people who say that Luther falls into this category. Six years ago, in the leading town of Brabant, a woman was punished because she had borne a child to an evil spirit, and Luis Molina, a theologian of the Society of Jesus, has published that this has also happened during the present day. Several other writers from different nations, too, have confirmed it and reported examples of it.

You see now how flimsy are the arguments with which others oppose this opinion, especially Nicholas Rémy. His first argument is: (i) an evil spirit and a human being belong to different species and therefore offspring cannot follow from their copulation. My reply is that that consequence does not follow, partly because mules, jackals, leopards, panthers, and other hybrids are born from a stallion and a she-ass and other creatures which are different in kind, and partly because in this case generation is not attributable to the evil spirit, but to the man whose semen it is, as St Thomas \[Aquinas\] correctly says in Quodlibet 6, article 8, answer to argument 6, and therefore in this case the human being is born from the man and the woman.

\(ii\) He argues that an evil spirit knows nothing about how life and death begin, and therefore he cannot be the originator and source of a life-giving act. My answer is that the life-giving power does not belong to the evil spirit but to the semen itself, just as the power to warm does not belong to the cup with which one pledges someone\'s health, or to the spout which pours out \[the wine, but to the wine itself. (See St Thomas, the work cited above, answer to 5, and \[Jakob\] Sprenger, Malleus \[Maleficarum\], Part 1, question 4, answer to argument 2.)

\(iii\) He argues that witches \[sagae\] say that the semen which the evil spirit pours into them is cold and makes them shudder rather than feel pleasure, and that therefore it will not be possible for generation to follow. This was the argument of Marcus of Ephesus as quoted by Psellus. Rémy confirms it from the confession made by witches \[sagarum\] who said that all sense of pleasure is absent from such a copulation and that they find it extremely painful.

My reply is that the worthlessness of this argument forces me to spend more time than I might wish on refuting something which is both inane and disgusting. So it seems certain that when an evil spirit wishes to deceive by taking on the appearance of a particular man and does not want anyone to know he is an evil spirit, he can give a most exact imitation of whatever is required during copulation between a real man and a woman; and therefore, if he actually does want generation to follow-something which very rarely happens, since he himself never intends generation to take place because of him, because he cannot procreate anything like himself, although sometimes, at least, he does attempt generation by means of another male\'s semen which is collected by a tart \[succuba\] who does this for him as a favour-on that occasion he necessarily takes care to employ what is necessary for the purpose.
[Page 67]

Therefore, he seeks out procreative semen, and when he has found it he preserves it and brings it back so quickly that its live-giving essence \[vitalis spiritus\] does not evaporate. Then when it is needed, he pours out however much is required. But when he does intend that generation \[should follow\], he pours out something which is like semen, but hot so that his deception is not discovered. Likewise, he also tempers the body he has assumed so that the woman\] will not be struck by fear or dread or disdain at its touch. But when he does the deed with women who are aware he is an evil spirit, he most often produces semen which is unreal and cold, and I grant that offspring cannot be born from that. Secondly, it is well-known he often asks tarts \[succubas\], \'Do you really want to be impregnated?\' If these women say yes, he obtains real semen which has fallen during the carnal act somewhere else, as I have said and as \[Jakob\] Sprenger tells us. But the question arises not so much in relation to what \[the evil spirit\] usually does as to what he is able to do, and the answer in confirmation of this argument is obvious.

When a sense of pleasure is not present, it is not present either because God providentially prevents it, or because the evil spirit wishes it not to be present so that the sin\'s filthiness and wickedness may be greater. This sense, however, can be present if God does not prevent it and the evil spirit wants the tart to feel it. Indeed, it is well known that everything requisite for pleasure has been provided by an evil spirit acting as incubus. \[Jakob\] Sprenger, \[Peter\] Binsfeld, and \[Lorenzo\] d\'Anania give a very large number of examples, and there is one in particular about a wise-woman \[saga\] from Sardis, and a noble Spanish virgin who was deceived. (You can find this in \[Antonio de\] Torquemada, dialogue 3. See also \[Paolo\] Grillando, question 7, nos. 8 & 9. Alfonso a Castro makes the same point by means of a confession \[he records\] in The Just Punishment of Heretics, Book 1, chapter 16.)

Lastly, they add that one cannot believe that God agrees with this kind of act or invests the body of someone born as a result of this kind of copulation with a soul and \[so\] adds a final flourish to the work begun by an evil spirit. My reply is that in this case, as far as the working of Nature is concerned, the evil spirit is simply an instrument and merely directs the principal agent, which is the human semen: and that therefore God agrees to the final balance of the constituent parts of an organic body which has been born from the human semen, but that something which is a casual incident is also simply a moral matter, a sin in the case of the witch \[sagae\] and malice in the case of the evil spirit. God, as Nature\'s creator, is not pleased to prop up Nature by means of a real sin which He did not create. If arguments of this kind were worth anything, it would follow that offspring could not be produced from fornication, or adultery, or incest. They answer that the reasoning is different, since in these cases Nature\'s order is preserved, but not in the evil spirit\'s nuptial arrangement. This answer is frivolous because in the case I have given, Nature\'s order is preserved with respect to the main physical principles of generation, since Nature demands merely that offspring be born from the procreative seed of a man suitably mixed with that of a woman. Ulrich Molitor argues that, according to the Conciliator \[by Pietro d\'Abano\], procreation begins with the heart and therefore an evil spirit cannot make use of that seed for generation because the heart lacks the necessary power to temper its heat. My reply is that whether the power to procreate arises from the heart or the head, a generative vapour \[spiritus\] is implanted in the seed itself in the very separation of the seed from the body \[during procreation\], and the evil spirit merely preserves this vapour along with its heat.
[Page 68]

So why, someone may say, will it be possible for children to be born from a man acting as an incubus and an evil spirit acting as his tart? Long ago the Egyptians did not agree that this can happen, and I do not believe it can, either, because for this to take place mothers have to supply very much more than fathers for the procreation and birth of offspring, and the efforts of each party are by no means equal. After \[the coital act\], everything concerns the mother alone. A period of time is required for the seed to be changed into an organic body and for the building of the human body to be finished and brought to a complete conclusion. The uninterrupted operation of the quickening soul is also required for the nourishment of the foetus, and other, almost innumerable secrets of Nature, all of which-no matter how many they may be-I very much doubt, (in fact I rather think), are impossible as long as an evil spirit is able to be in control of the body he has assumed. If he can \[remain in control\], even then the tart will be able to conceive. Meanwhile, someone may demonstrate that those whom we read have been born to an evil spirit acting as tart and mother have either been substituted for the genuine children of another woman, or are offspring fabricated by another evil spirit, as not infrequently happens, too, in the case of the first example of a substituted child.

I am perfectly willing to note, along with Johann Reynardus, that celebrated histories of the French refer to the origin of the Counts of Poitou from Melusine \[a fairy\]. Polydore Vergil says the same about the genealogy of the Angevin family; Vincent de Beauvais, relying on Geoffrey \[of Monmouth\], tells the same story about someone else; and Sabinus relates a story he was told by the Dukes of Bavaria and Saxony.¹⁹⁸ What others say about a Bavarian nobleman is not dissimilar. He was bearing the death of his wife without much fortitude, \[but\] one night the woman came back to him and said she had returned to life. She convinced her husband \[of this\] and conceived children by him. At length, however-and this is something she had predicted-she vanished, leaving behind her woman\'s dress with her husband who did not refrain from uttering loud reproaches and blasphemies. An evil spirit thus cheated the nobleman and foisted upon him children it had stolen from someone else. To the second category of fraud, I refer little children who are not born from incubi but through fraud, as though they came from evil spirits acting as tarts. The common folk call these \'exchanges\', and say they are so unsatisfied by milk that, even though they had drained four wet-nurses dry, they would continue to be thin. They are, however, very heavy and finally vanish after a number of years. (The Italians call them \'squallers\' because of their continual crying. The Germans, to indicate they are substitutes, call them \'changelings\' or \'exchange-children\'.

On these, see \[Jakob\] Sprenger, The Hammer \[of Witches\], Part 2, question 2, chapter 7 \[8\]; Guillaume d\'Auvergne, The Universe, last part, chapter 25; \[Lorenzo\] D\'Anania, The Character of Evil Spirits, Book 4; and \[Ulrich\] Molitor, Women Who Foretell the Future, chapter 6.) Fifteen years ago, a beggar was carrying a child very painfully on his shoulders through Galicia and the Asturias in Spain, and I think \[the child was one of these changelings. One of my Jesuit brethren found him en route near a river and, moved by pity, took a back seat on his mule so that he could take them across. The strong beast was scarcely able to land them on the other bank. A little later, he caught hold of the beggar who told him that the child was not a child but a devil who had promised him he would inveigle everyone into giving him alms, as long as he would carry \[the devil\] round in the guise of an ailing child swathed in bandages.
[Page 69]

That evil spirits can bring it about that a woman, while remaining in mind and body a virgin, can conceive, although not in the absence of a man\'s seed, is proved because it is possible to pour real, fertile semen take from someone else into a virgin while she is asleep and knows nothing about it, without carnal congress and leaving her hymen and the other barriers belonging to her virginity unimpaired. But an evil spirit is unable to \[make sure\] her virginity is preserved during childbirth, because genuine penetration of bodies is required for this, and virginity \[under those circumstances\] is reserved to the power of God. It cannot be accomplished without a specific miracle. Moreover, since Calvinists deny that this barrier remained closed during the virgin birth of Christ, our Lord, they certainly remove the outstanding special claim that this was a genuine miracle and a real virginity. The exemplary story about a girl from Bamberg, told by \[Johannes\] Nider, should be read with caution and understood simply as one about the virginity of the mind, since she had lost her physical innocence by means of the kind of copulation one has been talking about and had conceived thereby.

A woman cannot be known by a man without the loss of her virginity, and she cannot become pregnant without being known. I am aware of what many writers say about various women who have conceived merely because semen had been spilled earlier in the bath-tub in which they were washing themselves, but I don\'t believe it. I notice André du Laurens denies it, too, in his Anatomical Account, Book 8, question 11, because the power of the womb to attract \[semen\] does not have the ability to extend so far away from itself. Nor does water have the expertise an evil spirit has to stop the evaporation of the semen\'s essential capacities \[spirituum\]; and had the semen been fertile, it would have sunk to the bottom of the water and not floated on top of it. The Jews pretend that Ben Sira was born this way from a daughter of the prophet Jeremiad, but they are lying, because Jeremiah was always a virgin. Averroes makes the same kind of claim, and in chapter 1 of The Formation of a Human, Albertus Magnus talks about another \[such\] woman. Less unbelievable is what St Thomas \[Aquinas\] writes-that perhaps a woman can conceive without a miracle and with her virginity in every way intact, \'as we are told happened\', (he says), \'to a young girl who was just reaching the age of puberty. In order to guard her modesty, her father used to keep her in his bed. \[But\] when he defiled himself while he was dreaming, his semen descended into his daughter\'s womb and therefore the girl became pregnant. This was the result, and in his commentary on Genesis 19, \[Alonso Tostado of Ávila claims that an evil spirit can make the same thing happen.

I am likewise aware one should ask whether evil spirits sometimes practise the same perverted lust with witches \[sagis\] (Judges who try brigands ought to know this.) Some people deny it altogether-for example, a large number who follow the authority of theologians, Antoninus, Compendium Part 1, under the chapter heading, \'On Lust\', \[Gianfrancesco\] Ponzinibio, Vampire Witches, no. 64, and Benedictus, Compendium of Sins, Book 2, chapter 8. It is not found in the confessions of the witches \[sagarum\] from Spain, Germany, or France, but when it comes to the vampire witches \[lamiis\] of Italy, Silvestro \[Mazzolini\] da Prierio claims this in his Hag-Magicians as though it were very well known. So does D\'Anania in his Character of Evil Spirits, Book 4, folio 148, and even Ponzinibio, in the passage cited above, says they confirm it. Indisputably nothing that xxxoupyós \[evil-doer\] thinks of is so despicable. In order to entangle human souls in a more certain death, he offers each person the means whereby he hopes \[that person\] can be captured. There is no crime the dregs of humanity will shun, so judges can look into this as well. Nor is there any convincing argument to the contrary. Furthermore, a confessor should be aware that under those circumstances the mortal sin seems to be twofold-(i) it is contrary to the natural order of things, (ii) it takes place outwith the natural vessel-and therefore it is a more serious sin than if they were to have sex with an evil spirit in human, natural fashion.
[Page 70]

A final uncertainty arises in \[the form of\] this question: can it happen that giants, or pygmies, may be born as a result of the power of magic? In the case of giants, Francisco Vallés maintains in chapter 8 that it is possible if the spirits do not select just any kind of semen, but that which is abundant, very thick, very hot, overflowing with vital essences \[spiritibus\], and free from serum. \[He says\] it is easy for them to procure this by choosing men who are hot, robust, and have plenty of semen for those with whom they have sex, and women who are of the same kind of nature as those with whom they have sex. \'The pleasure brought to both of them, men and women, by the discharge of semen, is greater the more abundantly\', (he says), \'it is emitted. Moreover, they can increase the pleasure by titillating their imagination a lot and letting it picture most of what they are doing in that activity, because most of the impulses to make love flow from the imagination. Consequently, men devoted to the monastic life and so dedicated to God that they curb even their imagination, do not feel this kind of impulse. Evil spirits, therefore, were able to bring about the birth of robust, fully-grown men\'. That learned man, at once natural philosopher and philosopher, tells us very clearly what could have happened, but he is mistaken to the extent that he thinks the giants of Holy Scripture were produced from evil spirits in the forms of incubi and succubi. I have already said that this has been disproved by theologians, and for this reason I maintain merely that this does not run contrary to what an evil spirit can do and intend to do, since one cannot deny that giants were the result of shameless wantonness. Holy Scripture bears witness to it in Genesis, chapter 6.

The chronicles of all nations offer many similar \[instances\] Diodorus, Books 5 and 9; Plutarch, Sertorius; Philostratus, Heroicus; Pliny; Solinus; Marianus Scotus; and others.²⁰³ I recall I noted several \[examples\] in my Commentaries on Seneca\'s Tragedies, and I am astonished that Jan Gerartsen van Gorp decided to make such a fuss about the stories told about our giant in Antwerp. He could not cope with how large its tooth was. But how big was it in comparison with the larger molars of the Sicilian Cyclopes which the celebrated historian, Tommaso Fazello, saw and had in his possession? I think my fellow-citizens will not find it unpleasant to read what he has to say, so that they do not allow this treasure of their ancient history to be taken away from them so easily by someone from Kempen.

The subject is clearly an extensive one. I shall write down brief extracts \[from Fazello\]. In 1342, a corpse was found in a cave on Mt Eryx. It was of a quite extraordinary size and part of the back of its skull had the capacity of several Sicilian pecks. (Boccaccio mentions it, too, in Book 4, chapter 28 of his Genealogy of the Gods). In 1516 in the territory of Mazzarino, masons dug up the corpse of someone about twenty cubits tall. Its head was the size of a large round cask, and each of its molars weighed five ounces. \[Fazello\] says he saw them, and that he had in his possession not a few other teeth belonging to giants which were dug up in Melilli, \[each\] weighing four ounces, and that he also possessed the almost preternaturally wide shoulder-blade of another giant from Hyccara \[Carini\]. In 1547, Simeone Pigliono gave him two other molars, each weighing four ounces, from a corpse eighteen cubits tall, \[which had been found\] near Palermo.

In the following year, another corpse was discovered there by Giorgio Adorno, twenty cubits tall; and in 1550 a corpse was found at Calatrasi about twenty-two cubits tall, whose head measured about ten feet round. \[Fazello\] adds many similar \[examples\], but there is no reason for me to spend time enumerating each one of them. He notes the fact that although the rest of the bones fell to dust at a touch, the teeth remained intact. They were like ours, but bigger, whitish in colour, and free from decay. One used to see this long ago in the tooth from Antwerp as well. The British writer \[William\] Camden mentions an ancient epigram by Antonius Pomponius Letus about Etruscan giants, and others \[mention epigrams\] about others. St Augustine himself writes that he saw a molar on the shore of Utica so big it could match a hundred ordinary molars, (The City of God, Book 15, chapter 9). I pass over what Francesco Patrizi said through the mouth of Hammun, about the Ephemim and the Gigim, because it is a fairy-story.
[Page 71]

Now I must talk about pygmies. \[It is said\] that there can be no doubt that an evil spirit is able to fashion them once he had employed methods which stop them from growing and result in their not reaching the proper height of the human body. Thus, we see that human ingenuity contrives those tiny pups which are the delight of young women by making them drink brandy and confining them in little pots; and parents who are greedy for cash use certain drugs, (in a fashion undoubtedly contrary to religion), so that their children will be born as dwarfs. But I am entirely convinced that in this situation there is also a minimum size of the human body, even if we do not know what that is, below which the rational soul cannot use its powers and inform the body effectively.

Consequently, I agree with those writers who, although they acknowledge there are people a cubit tall or manikins three spans high, whom they call \'pygmies\', the enemies of cranes, and which Philostratus in Hercules Asleep, Pliny Book 7, chapter 2, Claudian, Juvenal, Solinus, and others have described, following Aristotle, Inquiry into Living Things Book 2, chapter 12, correctly think that these are not real human beings, but animals or monsters, and should be classed as monkeys. (Please see \[Aulus\] Gellius, Book 9, chapter 4; Caelius Rhodiginus, Ancient Readings Book 4, chapter 3; \[Girolamo\] Cardano, The Variety of Nature Book 8; \[Juan\] Luis Vives, \[Commentary on St Augustine\'s\] City of God, chapter 8; and Bonifacio Simonetta Letters Book 4, chapter 10. Fr. Ruiz writes in his letters, which I read in 1601, that a province of dwarfs was found in Peru in 1600.)

It cannot be denied that pygmies have existed, and that their height was very short, too. I gather both \[meanings\] from Ezekiel 27.11, Pygmies were in your towers\'. I know this is translated in different ways, and St Jerome gives more than one version of this passage. But mine has quite properly followed Aquila who translates it as \'pygmies.\' Symmachus translates it as \'Medes\', and Theodoret agrees with him. The Aramaic paraphraser renders it \"Cappadocians\'. But the root of the word is the \[Hebrew\] G, and therefore Theodotion is not altogether sure what the Hebrew would mean. I have retained the Hebrew \[word\] GMDYM, \'gamadim\'. St Jerome is aware that GDM means \'an arm\'s length measurement\' or \'cubit\', Judges 3.16. Hence GMD (\'cubit\') translates as \'pygmies\', this being a reference to how tall they are. Likewise, St Jerome, considering Aquila\'s Greek word, did not accept \'pygmies\' as a proper noun, but as an appellative, because he translates it as \'warriors in full readiness\', as if it derived from πuruñs, one translation of which is \'fight\' or \'boxer\', the other, \'a cubit\'s measurement.

That is the one followed by St Augustine in The City of God Book 16, chapter 7. This is why differences of translation arose, and when it came to those people properly called \'pygmies\', people thought it a good idea to accept \[both\]. Certain \[writers\] pretend \[that pygmies\] were very warlike, had very accurate eyesight, and were very skilled archers, and that this is why they were entrusted with the guardianship of the towers. (This is what Dionysius the Carthusian says).

But what is their proof of this? It is scarcely credible that \[pygmies\] could have penetrated a breastplate with their darts. So, Nicholas of Lyra devised another, quite ingenious \[interpretation\], saying that the stronghold and fortification of Tyre was being described, and that this was of such a kind that its towers could be defended against the most powerful enemies even by those dwarfish creatures. This \[interpretation\], too, is distorted and does not pay sufficient attention to, and is not consistent with, the context. It is my firm opinion that the text mentions \'guards\' or \'lusty men\', as the Septuagint translates it, and that these are called \'pygmies\', not because of their height, but because the towers were so tall that even the bravest and largest men appeared scarcely a cubit tall to those at the bottom looking upwards. Consequently, I think this is how one should reconcile the Vulgate with the Septuagint and with the Hebrew original. (I am prepared to follow someone who tells us something which is more convincing.)
[Page 72]

In his Library, folio 3, Photius refers to what Nonnosus says about these dwarfs. \'While Nonnosus was sailing from Pharsa, he came to the furthest of the islands where a remarkable, unheard of thing happened. He came across some \[creatures\] which had the shape and appearance of human beings but were very short in stature and black in colour. Their whole body was covered in thick hair. Their women came along with them, and their children who were even shorter than the men. They were all naked, except that the older men and women concealed their shameful parts with a small piece of skin. They did not seem to be fierce or wild. They did have a human voice, but their language was unknown to everyone who lived round about them\'.

Book 2, Question 16

The nocturnal meetings of witches \[sagarum\]. Are these women really transported from one place to another?

The principal opinion is that those women are present at this kind of equitation and meeting simply because that is what they think they are doing, and because they are taking part in an illusion \[created by\] the Devil. Many sectaries hold this opinion, as did Luther and Melanchthon. But certain Catholics do, as well: among the Spanish, the Franciscan, Fr. Samuel in a whole book, the author of The Fortress of the Faith, and the canon lawyer, Martin of Arles; among the Italians, \[Gianfrancesco\] Ponzinibio in almost a whole book, \[Giambattista\] della Porta in Book 2 of his Natural Magic, and \[Andrea\] Alciato, Supplement Book 8, chapter 22. (At his time, the topic had not yet been investigated satisfactorily.) Among the French are \[François\] Douaren, \[Pierre\] Ayrault, and Michel Montaigne; among the English in earlier times, John of Salisbury, Polycraticus Book 2, the end of chapter 17; among the Germans, Philipp Camerarius has the same opinion at the end of chapter 72 of his Works of Occasional Hours, and Ulrich Molitor, Women Who Foretell the Future, chapter 8. (Leonardo Vairo also hints that this is what he thinks in The Evil Eye Book 2, chapter 13.

They rely, however, upon arguments which are not especially telling. After all, why is this claim made only by women, as it turns out in Alciato? For what reason do so many learned and famous men, sensible according to the age \[we live in\], say the same thing every day and are punished for it? Yes, there may often exist people who are deluded by an evil spirit which has overwhelmed their internal and external organs of sensory perception and lulled them to sleep, and perhaps even damaged their imagination and thrown it into confusion. Yes, after he has damaged someone\'s physical powers and mental faculties, the Devil may be able to make more sights convincing to him than those which people overpowered by a lot of wine or sick with melancholia think they are seeing, as St Augustine tells us. There may, of course, be three kinds of mental image, as \[St Augustine\] knew perfectly well. So, what follows from that? What can you infer from these points other than that witches \[sagas\] can be deceived? But it does not follow from this that the women are always mistaken, nor does it offer stronger proof of what he said when he wrote to Evodius about the extraordinary things people see.

I acknowledge that at that time souls did not leave their bodies, and I detest the fact that \[Jean\] Bodin was rash enough to say they did. I acknowledge that the physical senses are often clearly in a state of unconsciousness, and that \[mental\] images can make such a powerful impression that after people wake up, they believe they have heard, seen, and done things which have never happened. Nor am I unaware of many examples of this \[sensory\] deception: the defendant, for example, who kept on saying he had been in Pluto\'s kingdom; Gennadius who used to think he had taken part in the choirs of the blessed; the Platonist philosopher \[who said\] that another \[philosopher\] used to appear to him in dreams and give him instruction; and the father who, in spite of seeing his daughter in broad daylight, thought he was looking at a cow. Nor does it weigh with me that their bodies have frequently been found lying in the same spot and have not moved from there, as Olaus \[Magnus\], \[Alonso\] Tostado, \[Paolo\] Grillando, and others have told us about various individuals. Pertinent to this is an incident in the life of St Germanus about women \[mulierculis\] who thought they were at a party when, in fact, they were found to be asleep at home-and so on and so forth. Nothing more is proved by these \[examples\] than that these women \[mulierculas\] are sometimes deceived. But there is no proof that this is always the case, and indeed there could be some force to the argument in Alciato\'s question, why can we not say that an evil spirit was at the meeting in place of the woman rather than that she was in bed with her husband?
[Page 73]

If we used to depend on presumption alone \[in the past\], we are not moved by it now, but by the unanimity of all ages and nations, and by the confession of defendants of both sexes, even those who hold office in the Church and those of noble rank. Against this, no presumption has force. Michal deceived her father\'s attendants by substituting a statue in place of David \[in his bed\] This is how an evil spirit can and usually does deceive the husband, by assuming \[his wife\'s\] body and placing it in the bed. You see, once the night-flying witch \[strige\] has set out for her meeting, \[the evil spirit\] usually plunges the husband into a deep sleep so that he does not realise she has gone away, or he puts something to take the women\'s \[sic\] place so that if the husband wakes up, he can think it is his wife. We learn this from court records.

Certain of them say: \'At Forbach, on 1st September 1587, a female hairdresser made known that she had often done this, and that in order to put her husband into this kind of sleep, with her right hand she smeared his ear with the ointment she was in the habit of using on herself when she wanted to go to this kind of meeting, and then pinched it. Eller, the wife of the dean \[of a guild\] in Oettingen, in the same place and on the same day said she had put a child\'s bolster \[in her place\], and Seichen May from Speirchen \[said she had substituted\] some thin branches. Both said they had first pronounced the name of their evil spirit, and that they had often deceived their husbands this way. In Homburg, on 5th June 1590, Maria, the wife of a tailor in Metzer Esch, \[said she substituted\] a bundle of straws she had sullied with something like ointment, and that this would vanish at once when she came back home. Catharine Ruffa declared that her evil spirit himself had sometimes taken her place in the bed. These \[examples come from\] Nicholas Rémy, \[The Worship of Evil Spirits\] Book 1, chapter 12.

In addition to this, objectors say that in St Augustine\'s Spirit and Soul, chapter 28, where you have almost the same words as the canon Episcopi, it is a good idea that those who put their trust in the incantations of silly old women of this sort²¹⁶ be excommunicated. This canon is the Achilles of one\'s opponents: they press it \[on one\], they hurl it \[at one\]. My reply is that that book is not by St Augustine, or even by St Gregory to whom the Carmelite Jan van Beets attributed it in Book 1, exposition 4, chapter 4 of his \[Commentary on the Ten\] Commandments, but by Hugh de Saint Victor or Hugo Etherianus. Whoever wrote, its opinion is the same as that of the foresaid canon. Certain people make light of the authority of the canon on the grounds that it is \[that of\] a merely provincial Council which could have erred. But I have no intention of taking refuge in that. Others deny it \[came from\] the Council of Ancyra because today it does not appear in the Greek or Latin transcripts of this Council.

This solution, however, does not please me, either, because the canon is found in a number of ancient collections of Councils\' \[decrees\], in the Lives of the Pontiffs by Damasus, (if he was the author of them), in Burchard\'s Decrees Book 10, chapter 1, and those of Ivo, part 11, chapter 30, and it was correctly preserved in Gratian\'s Decrees by order of Pope Gregory XIII. I prefer, along with \[Juan de\] Torquemada \[on Gratian\], \[Hugh de\] St-Victor number 32, \[Bernardus\] Basinus, Alfonso a Castro, and others, to reply that certain things are related in that canon which cannot, in the nature of things, be done by an evil spirit, such as speaking to and personally seeing Hercules and Achilles who are in Hell. Likewise, riding on real animals, which cannot cover such great distances so quickly or \[go\] through the air. Likewise, riding with Diana or Herodias, since there no such goddess as Diana survives, and the little dancing-girl Herodias mentioned \[in the canon\] does not ride anywhere on earth because she has been put on the rack in Hell. Likewise, acknowledging any divine nature other than God, because making this kind of statement would actually be heretical.
[Page 74]

Other things, however, which are not incompatible with the nature of things and do not surpass Satan\'s power are mentioned in this same canon-for example, the actual subject one is dealing with at the moment, and the canon does not deny that such things can happen.
