Its intention was simply to point out that one should not believe they always happen aútorpooonws \[personally\], but that sometimes they should be attributed to a fault in one\'s imagination. Therefore, what the canon makes clear is correct: those who believe the claims of witches \[sagis\] that everything which happens later and everything which happens earlier does happen to them, (which is the story those women used to tell), are condemned as heretics. It is enough, you see, for the condemnation of any opinion that any part of it be contrary to the Faith, because something is true when it is entire, \[whereas\] any defect renders it untrue. This is how I interpret what Dr Navarrius has said\[a man\] who misleads not a few people because of his rather confused way of putting things.

Consequently, it appears that neither the canon I have been talking about, nor the passage from Hugh \[de Saint-Victor\] are contrary to the common opinion of theologians, or the received practice of inquisitors and judges. (I shall have to deal with this canon at much greater length in Book 5.)

One last point. Alciato argues that sometimes all the people present at this meeting have vanished when the name of Jesus is pronounced. They were therefore imaginary people, not physical, because a physical object cannot vanish in this way. My reply is that the witches \[sagas\] who had been present at the meeting did not vanish, but that the eyes of the onlookers were blinded and \[the participants\] were taken away very rapidly by their evil spirits. This is how they are said to have \'vanished\'-in a broad sense and in the proper meaning of the word, \'they ceased to be seen\'. One must not hide \[the fact\] that Alciato genuinely thinks an evil spirit cannot move bodies from their place, because the result of that is his claim that Christ the Lord was not carried off by the Devil to the pinnacle of the Temple and the mountain-top. This he \'proves\' on the authority of Origen\'s thirty-first homily on Luke and St Jerome on Matthew. Philipp Camerarius was rash enough in chapter 72 of his Works of Occasional Hours to believe what Alciato said. Later on, however, I shall point out that very many more Fathers \[of the Church\] have held the opposite point of view. Certainly, St Jerome offers Alciato no support, and Origen is clearly opposed to him, because in putting his objection, Alciato crops the words he is citing and omits Origen\'s reply.

Experience raises an objection-that after the women have anointed themselves, sometimes they are not carried off but are found lying in the same spot in when they anointed themselves. \[Bartolomeo della\] Spina testifies to this in the case of one woman from Avila, and three or four \[others\], and other \[writers have done the same\] in relation to other cases. My reply, along with Spina in \[Inquiry into Night-Flying Witches\], chapters 30 and 31, is that there are various reasons for this, partly to do with God, partly to do with the evil spirit. In the case of the latter, when it is advantageous to him, he deceives the witches \[sagas\] by not moving them from the spot, so that in this way he can convince judges and princes that what people say about such transvections is not true, and thus hinder the execution of justice. He therefore renders the pact ineffective, because then he hopes the human race will suffer a greater calamity as a result. He also keeps the pact, (when God allows him to do so), in the hope of trapping more people this way. In God\'s case, if people\'s sins require it, He very often allows the Devil to keep the pacts he has made with magicians. Sometimes He prevents this-for example, when judges are moved by curiosity to make this kind of investigation-and the result is that the judges are confused about the punishment of the wrong-doing and, so to speak, blinded. Both happen perfectly properly, and for these same reasons the evil spirit sometimes wishes, (and God allows him), to move the women from where they are and to leave a likeness of them in their place, so that people may think they have stayed there and have not been taken away.
[Page 75]

A second opinion, then-and how very true I think it is!-is that \'an evil spirit sometimes transfers witches \[sagas\] from one place to another riding on a goat or some other creature\', (for the most part in their imagination, that is, the evil spirit fashions a body out of air, or even \[one\] in the shape of a human being, and puts it on, and sometimes he transports three or four women at the same time), \'or on a straw or even a broomstick, (although the evil spirit does the work and holds them up), and physically attend their wicked meeting\'.

This opinion is much more widely held among theologians and practising lawyers in Italy, Spain, and Germany, and there also are very many Catholic writers who support it: Albertus Magnus, (quoted by Thomas of Cantimpré, Book 2, chapter 57, part 19); \[Pedro\] Ciruelo, Superstition Part 2, chapter 1; William of Newburgh, whom I shall cite in a moment; \[Juan de\] Torquemada, whom I cited earlier; \[Paolo\] Grillando, Divinatory Magicians Book 2, question 7, no. 8, in which he says he has changed his opinion; \[Bernardus\] Basinus, Magical Practices, proposition 9; \[Nicholas\] Rémy, Worship of Evil Spirits Book 1, chapters 14, 24, and 29; the authors of The Hammer \[of Witches\] in more than one passage; \[Francisco\] Peña in \[Nicholas Eymeric\], A Directive for Inquisitors Part 2, comment 2, and others; Pietro Damiani letter 4, chapter 17. There are also more recent physicians such as \[Baudouin van\] Ronss, whom I shall cite in a moment; \[Giovanni Battista Codronchi\], Illnesses Caused by Poisonous Magic Book 2, chapter 8 and Book 3, chapter 8; \[Andrea\] Cesalpino, Investigation into Evil Spirits, chapter 20. A large number of theologians maintain this and discuss the subject very carefully: Johannes Dodus in his defence against Francisco Samuel; Guillaume d\'Auvergne, last part of The World, chapter 23; Silvestro \[da Prierio\] on the language used by heresy, no. 3; \[Alonso Tostado\], \[Commentary\] on Matthew, chapter 4, question 47; \[Jan de\] Beets, op.cit supra, chapter 4; \[Hugh de\] Saint-Victor, op.cit.supra; \[Tommaso\] Cajetan 2.2, question 95, article 3; Sixtus of Siena, Library Book 5, annotation 73; \[Pierre\] Crespet, The Hatred of Satan, discourse 15; Sébastien Michaëlis, Discourse of Spirits Book 1; \[José\] Anglés in \[Flowers of Theological Questions\], second single question on the practice of magic, article 4, difficulty 5; Peter Binsfeld at the end of the work cited earlier, conclusion 11;

\[Bartolomeo della\] Spina against Ponzinibio; and \[Lorenzo\] D\'Anania, The Character of Evil Spirits Book 4.

Didn\'t St Hippolytus the martyr also realise that such meetings would take place at the time of Antichrist, (The End of the World, not far from the start)? The Salic Laws were aware of them, as is clear from law 17, and according to Crespet who quotes him, the Calvinist Lambert Daneau writes that a regular meeting took place in Geneva near the cathedral. But listen to what Hippolytus says. \'At that time, that iniquitous individual, insolent in his arrogance, will gather together his evil spirits in human guise\'-don\'t they dance this way during their meetings, and have sex with the witches \[strigibus\]?-\'and he will despise those who have invited him to rule over them. He will also corrupt many souls and indeed will appoint princes for them from among his evil spirits\'.

His superior, the Devil, does this every day when he assigns his evil spirit as a little schoolmaster to each of the witches \[strigum\]. In the chapter about dreams in his book on the soul, \[chapter 46\], Tertullian declares that \'people\'s homes also lie open to evil spirits, and people are surrounded by mental images of them, not only in their chapels but also in their bedrooms\'. Observe now by what pact this secret rite of iniquity was already operating in times past, and I think one should refer to something William of Newburgh tells us. I quote, \'In Yorkshire, not far from where I was born, something extraordinary happened which I knew about from the time I was a child. Several miles away from the sea in the east there is a village, and next to it are the well-known waters commonly known as the \"Vipse\".
[Page 76]

A peasant from this village, who had gone to see a friend in the next village, was returning, somewhat less than sober, late at night and (listen to this!) he heard the sounds of people singing and having a good time \[which were coming\] from a nearby hillock. (I have seen this hillock quite often. It is two or three furlongs away from the village.) Astonished that anyone would be rupturing the silence in the dead of night with cheerful celebrations, he wanted to investigate more closely and noticed an opening in the side of the hillock. He went up to it, looked inside, and saw a big house, brightly lit, full of men and women who were sitting down to what looked like a formal dinner. One of the waiters noticed him standing in the doorway and offered him a cup.

He took it, but deliberately refrained from drinking, poured the contents away, but kept the cup, and left quickly. The removal of the cup caused an uproar at the party and the guests chased after him, but he escaped because of the speed of the mule he was riding and retreated to his village along with his remarkable plunder. In the end, this vessel, which \[was made\] of an unknown material and had a strange colour and unusual shape, was offered as a gift to the King of England, Henry the Elder, and then, after he had refused it, to the Queen\'s brother David, (namely, the King of Scots) and was kept for very many years as one of the treasures of Scotland. A few years ago, I am reliably informed, William, King of Scots, surrendered it to Henry II who wanted to see it\', \[History\] of English Affairs Book 1, chapter 38.

The common people usually call such secret spots in mountains \'the mount of Venus\', and they say there is also one of these in Italy, near Lake Nursia. (This is well-known from letter 46 by Enea Silvio.)²²⁴ Robert Gaguin, General of the Trinitarian Order, writes as follows in Book 10 of his history of the French. \'At that time, Guillaume Edelin, a learned theologian and Prior of St Germain-enLaye, was sentenced to perpetual imprisonment in Évreux for the crime of breaking his monastic vows. This distinguished man was in love with a woman and was unable to enjoy her companionship. So he invited an evil spirit to be his patron, and worshipped him under the guise of a ram. Later \[the spirit\] taught him how to pick up a besom and put it between his thighs, as though it were a horse. It would then take him instantaneously where he wanted \[to go\]\'.

Among the Avignon witches \[striges\], (as \[Sébastien\] Michaëlis tells us in his Discourse of Spirits, annotation 6), they arrested a boy who told the judges that his father has taken him to the \'synagogue\', (which is what they call their meeting), and that while he was there he had seen them carry out many wicked, dreadful things. In consequence, \[he said\], he was terrified and crossed himself and said Jesus, what is the meaning of this?\' As soon as he had said it, the entire mob disappeared, and he found he was alone in that place. Next day he came back to his home which was about three miles away from the \'synagogue\', and denounced his father to the judges, and this is why the boy\'s neighbours gave him the name \'masquillon\', that is, \'little sorcerer\' \[sortiarius\]. This boy was still a prisoner in Avignon when Michaëlis was writing his account in 1582.

Listen to another eye-witness \[account\] taken from \[Paolo\] Grillando whose words I am quite happy to write down. \'When this\', (that is, of course, homage), \'has been done, he,\' (the principal evil spirit), \'appoints an evil spirit to take custody of the woman, whom he must never leave, but serve her every wish; and whenever she happens to come to the theatricals, he will tell the woman this, bring her there, and give her instruction. Actually, this evil spirit keeps her company the way a husband does his wife. They often go to those assemblies, where a very large number of women get together, and they do not see the things I have mentioned mentally or intellectually or apparently.
[Page 77]

They come to the foresaid places in their real, natural person in the following manner and ordernamely, first of all, one or two days before the assembly takes place, \[the woman\] is informed by the evil spirit who has been given custody of the woman how to prepare herself at such and such a time to come to the show. If the woman were to have a good reason which prevented \[her attendance\], he takes her and, provided her reason is genuine, excusable, and one she can legitimately offer as an excuse, she is given a hearing. Otherwise, if she were to pretend to have a reason which was not a reason for non-attendance, she would not be taken there against her will, but would stay at home. Nevertheless, as punishment for her lie, the evil spirit would torture her so vigorously and distress her so powerfully in mind and body with very great, continual pains and sickness internal and external, that she would have no peace inwardly day or night but would always be suffering affliction.

Everything she might do would have no effect and would come to nothing as she did it, to the point where, to make all her sufferings cease, she would confess her fault and promise, even to the extent of swearing an oath, that she would not refuse any more to go \[to the meetings\]. Now, when she promised to go, the moment the night and hour arrived, a voice like that of a human would summon her. \[It came\] from the evil spirit himself, although they do not call him an evil spirit, but \'little teacher\'. (Some women \[call\] their teacher \"Martinet\" or \"Little Martin\".) she had been summoned this way, she would pick up a small box of ointment and smear certain parts and bits of her body with it. After doing this, she would leave the house and would always find her \"little teacher\" waiting for her at the door, in the form of a goat. The woman said she would mount on his back and cling hard with her hands on to his hair. The goat would immediately rise through the air and, in a very short time, carry her off to an almond-tree in Benevento where he would put her down gently\', etc. \[Question 7, no. 28\]. It is worth noting that the rest \[of the passage\] agrees with what the other scholars I cited have written usually takes place in a meeting. The theologians I have cited adduce various exemplary stories, and to do defendants\' confessions which all agree that the act of transporting, the way it is carried out, the ceremonies at the meeting, and other circumstances, are done physically.

At this point I shall pursue the most important of these \[points\] briefly and add some more which I heard from Pierre Dheure who is as dear to me as a brother and well-known for his erudition and integrity. He was present in his capacity as inquisitor and judge at the trial of wise women \[sagarum\] and vampire witches \[lamiarum\] in Stavelot in 1597 and 1598. As for the broomstick, Sébastien Michaëlis notes, (op. cit. annotation 7), that Abraham, son of Ezra interprets Leviticus chapter 19 as a prohibition of harmful magic \[maleficii\], as follows. \'You will not carry out experiments with drawings, sticks, things you have made, movements, days, and hours.\'

With these words he covered most of the ceremonies of witches \[strigum\]. By \'drawings\' he means the circle, (I have already dealt with this); by \'stick\' he means the wands they use for harmful magic, and others which \[they use\] for paßδopávtia \[rhabdomancy\], and the reed or the shaft of the besom on which they ride to go from one place to another. The Devil delights in the wand because he wants to imitate Moses\'s staff which effected miracles, or the other staff belonging to Aaron, which burst into flower. \[The women are accustomed to smear this stick with an ointment made from various kinds of most extraordinary things, the fat of slaughtered infants in particular, although sometimes they do not anoint the stick, but their legs or other parts of their body, as is clear from what \[Paolo\] Grillando says, and from the verdict of the court in Avignon, which I shall discuss in full in Book 5. It would be possible \[for them to go to a meeting\] without \[using\] the ointment, but in actual fact they apply it liberally, with the result that they kill as many infants as they can in the belief that without this fat they cannot be transported to their meeting. This is evidence that the first time they go, it is enough for them to borrow the ointment from others, but that on subsequent occasions the women have to prepare the ointment by killing a child.
[Page 78]

So, once they have anointed themselves this way, they are usually carried away sitting on a stick, a pitch-fork, or a distaff, or standing on one leg in a basket, or sitting on a besom, a reed, a bull, a pig, a male goat, or a dog. (Rémy furnishes examples of all these in Book 10, chapter 14). So this, I am saying, is how they are normally carried away to the meeting-\'the sport of good fellowship\', as they call it in Italy. There, on most occasions, they light a foul, dreadful fire and an evil spirit sits on a throne as president of the assembly. His appearance is terrifying, most often that of a male goat or a dog. \[The participants\] come forward to worship him, but not in the same way. Sometimes they supplicate him on bended knee; sometimes they stand with their back to him; sometimes they even throw their legs in the air and hold their head, not pointing forwards, but tilted right back so that their chin is pointing up at the sky.

They offer him candles made from pitch or a child\'s umbilical cord and kiss him on his backside as a sign of homage. (What do you think about that?) Sometimes, too, they imitate the sacrifice of the Mass, (the greatest of all their crimes), as well as purification with water and similar Catholic ceremonies. (I shall demonstrate this from Book 3 of the narrative of a learned man, the most distinguished senator Raymund). In addition to this, they offer their children to the Devil during the meeting, as the mother of Cuno della Rovere did some years ago, according to \[Peter\] Binsfeld, and, according to \[Nicolas\] Jacquier, chapter 7, another woman offered her three children in 1458.

But, according to the way they both interpret it, when they offer their own children, or those of others, to the evil spirit, and kill them, \[the women are performing, in a wicked and cruel fashion, an utterly foul sacrifice to the idol, Moloch; or they dedicate a great quantity of their very seed to him, as that extraordinarily wicked magician did when he mingled his seed with holy oil while having sex with a woman in church, a story told by Jacquier on folio 58 of his Scourge; or they receive the sacred Host during communion, keep it in their mouth, and then take it out and offer it to the evil spirit and trample it underfoot in front of him, as many witches \[striges\] confessed in answer to Jacquier\'s questions, (chapter 8).

Once they have committed these and similar atrocious and execrable abominations, they sit down at table and start to enjoy the food supplied by the evil spirit or which they have brought themselves. Sometimes they perform a vigorous dance before the meal, sometimes they do it afterwards. There is usually more than one table-three or four, in fact-loaded with food which is sometimes very dainty and sometimes tasteless and insipid. Each person has his or her place allotted according to station or wealth. Sometimes each person\'s evil spirit sits beside him or her; sometimes the workers of harmful magic \[malefici\] sit on one side of the table, and each person\'s evil spirit on the other. They do not fail to say a grace worthy of such a gathering, using words which are always openly blasphemous and in which Beelzebub himself is declared to be the creator, giver, and preserver of all things. (This information comes from a list of graces which they stick under the tables placed at their disposal.

I have read a copy of these formulae, written in the hand of a very famous worker of harmful magic \[malefici\]). Sometimes they take part in this feast without hiding their face. Sometimes they cover it with a mask, a linen cloth, or some other veil or mask. Usually they wear masks which look like human faces \[personati\]. After dinner, each evil spirit takes the pupil of whom he has charge by the hand and, in order to do everything with the most absurd kind of ritual, each person turns his or her back, joins hands in a circle, and tosses his or her head like a lunatic. Then they begin to dance. Sometimes they hold lighted candles with which they worship the evil spirit, exchanging kisses in his presence. They sing very obscene songs in his honour or jump up and down in time to a drum or pipe which is played by someone sitting in the fork of a tree. They behave ridiculously in every way, and in every way contrary to accepted custom. Then their demon-lovers copulate with them in a most repulsive fashion.
[Page 79]

When they sacrifice, they usually start with an act of adoration, but they often make sacrifices outwith the meeting. Thereupon, (they add), each person gives an account of the wicked deeds he or she has done since the last meeting. The more serious and detestable these are, the more fulsomely they are praised. But if they have done nothing, or if what they have done is not dreadful enough, an evil spirit or some older \[male\] worker of harmful magic \[maleficus\] gives them an appalling beating. Finally, they receive powders-which some writers say are the ashes of the he-goat whose shape had been taken by the evil spirit they worship, and which has suddenly been consumed by fire in front of their eyes-or they receive other poisonous substances, often with the highly esteemed decree to do harm to every individual, and the command of the evil spirit, their false god, hanging over their heads-Avenge yourselves, otherwise you will die, so that you may know there is a law which is the opposite of love\'. Each person then goes home. If they live nearby, they go on foot, but if they live further away, they retire the way they were brought. For the most part, the meetings begin either in the silence of midnight when the power of darkness is strong, or at midday which reminds us of the well-known verse of the Psalmist about the evil spirit of noon, \[Psalm 90.6\]. Abraham ben Ezra writes that magicians observe certain days and hours. Our witches \[striges\] seem to have different appointed days in different places. In Italy they gather on Friday night, (as \[Bernardo da\] Como says in his \[Inquisitors\'\] Lantern), at about midnight, (as Sébastien Michaëlis says in annotation 7.) In Lotharingia, on the other hand, they meet on Thursday and Saturday night, as Rémy says in Book 1, chapter 14. (I have read in other writers \[that they meet\] on Monday night.)

Their evil spirit could transport the women without using an ointment, as I have said, and sometimes he does, but for various reasons he prefers to use it. This is because sometimes the witches are too timid to make the venture or because they are too weak to bear the ghastly contact with Satan via the body he has assumed. Sometimes he stupefies their senses with the ointment and persuades these wretched people that its power is very great. On other occasions he mimics the holy sacraments instituted by God, and by these quasi-rituals imports a degree of reverence and veneration into his orgies. The ointment\'s power, however, adds nothing to the transportation and therefore it is obvious that even if those who wish, out of curiosity, to be present at a meeting at the appointed time anoint themselves and are actually transported through the air-something which, as has been proved on many occasions, God permits in order to punish their rash, self-indulgent curiosity-nevertheless, if someone is firm in the Faith and armed with charity were to anoint him or herself with this ointment with the intention of disproving and routing the evil spirit\'s deceptions, and confirming the true Faith, there can be no doubt, (as Binsfeld correctly says), that no transportation would follow.

The reason is that this individual renders every pact with an evil spirit null and void, and God would not allow him or her to make one. Moreover, when witches \[sagae\] anoint themselves out with the appointed time of a meeting, they do not fly away and are not carried off, because this is not part of the pact. They know this and therefore do not anoint themselves unless they hear the signal for a meeting. According to \[Bartolomeo della\] Spina, chapter 30, sometimes they are given notice of a meeting by their \'little Martin\', sometimes by the voices of the witches\' \[sagarum\] queen and her entourage as they fly past, sometimes in another way. But there are certain workers of harmful magic \[malefici\] who are carried elsewhere whenever they want at a particular signal specified in their pact-the ointment, the way they are wearing their hat or cloak, or something else. What I said earlier about this, however, is their more usual practice.
[Page 80]

There is proof that this transportation is done physically, primarily because nothing can be said which makes it impossible. What is missing? Not a moving body. The Devil assumes the body at once. There is no resistance from the body which is moved, and its weight does not stand in the way because the power of the evil spirit which is moving it is far greater. He could move mountains from their place. The speed of movement to a far-off place in a short space of time does not stand in the way, either, because the speed and power of an angel\'s nature can make this happen, as St Augustine and St Thomas \[Aquinas\] say. Nor does God not permit it, because there are almost countless examples of His having given permission, as is perfectly clear from Holy Scripture.

The first passage some people \[adduce\] says that Christ our Lord allowed Himself to be carried off by an evil spirit on two occasions, and that this was done by a wicked spirit \[malo spiritu\] is accepted by Origen, Ambrose, Chrysostom, Augustine, Gregory, Strabo, and others. I am not making use of this example because, although many Fathers \[of the Church\] declare that Christ was seized and carried off by an evil spirit and flew through the air, a batter interpretation-as Origen and Euthymius point out-is that Christ was simply guided. The Devil went past and Christ followed, and this is how He came to the pinnacle \[of the Temple\] and to the top of the mountain. St Luke clearly intimates this, and St Matthew does not present an obstacle, because not infrequently means \'to take up\' simply by taking the lead.

Learned men also interpret it this way in this passage, so this example does not have a great deal of force. It is common for a second passage to be adduced: that evil spirits entered a herd of pigs and drove them headlong into the sea. There is a still weightier passage which they adduce as a third example-I mean Philip the Deacon who was brought by the Spirit from the desert to Ashdod, and \[there is also\] Habakkuk who was fetched by his hair to Daniel in Babylon. Ulrich Molitor\'s reply to these passages is that we are offering a faulty proof in going from good angels to evils spirits, because the power and strength of angels in this matter is far greater than that of evil spirits. This answer is full of faults because there is no reason for us to say that a good angel can do more than a bad one by local motion, and the general consensus of theologians concedes that devils have retained the properties with which they were endowed and have lost those with which they were graced. So even though it was a good angel who carried Habakkuk, with God\'s permission a devil will be able to transport a human being. Why is it astonishing that evil spirits receive power from God over human bodies when they have received the far greater \[power\] of deceiving the soul?

One\'s experience of so many examples also provides proof of it, with the result that no one, without being wantonly impudent, can disagree. The most venerable and most holy Fathers \[of the Church\] say that Simon the magician was lifted up into the air, ferried on the wings of evil spirits, and appeared to flit about in front of Peter\'s eyes. One reads similar things about Abaris the Scythian.

The History of the English tells us that Bladud, King of Britain, was a match for anyone in his skill in flying, but that he also had a more unfortunate death than anyone else. Johannes Magnus, historiographer of the Goths and Swedes, relates of Eric, King of the Swedes, that he would immediately be transported in the direction in which he turned his hat. In his Chronicle, Guillaume de Nangis recorded that Berengar, a heretic and a magician, was in Rome and sang the lesson \[of the day\] in a church in Tours on the same night and, speaking about portents, he tells us that in England, in 1045, a female worker of poisonous magic \[veneficam\] was publicly put on a black horse by an evil spirit and carried through the air. (In the next Book, I shall give an example similar to this about the Comte de Mâcon, by the venerable Peter, Abbot of Cluny.)²³⁶ At the end of Book 11 of \[A Discussion of Miracles, Caesarius \[of Heisterbach tells us about an elderly woman leaping out of a cauldron, and in his Chronicle of Flanders, \[Jacob de\] Meyer deals with works of harmful magic \[maleficiis\] carried out in Artois in 1459. Vincent \[de Beauvais\], quoting Peter Damian, tells us about the five-year old child of a man from a very noble family. This boy became a monk and after being carried off one night was found next morning in a locked-up mill. In answer to questions, he said that certain people had taken him to a great feast, urged him to eat, and afterwards thrown him into the mill from a height.
[Page 81]

In Book 2, question 7 of his Divinatory Magicians, Paolo Grillando relates that in 1524 a woman called Lucrezia was brought before him in his capacity as inquisitor. While she was being taken home from a witches\' meeting \[congregatione ludorum\] immediately after dawn, she heard the bell being rung to call the people to morning prayer, and all of a sudden, the evil spirit who was carrying her abandoned her in a field full of thorns. By chance a young man she knew was passing through \[the field\] and the wretched woman call to him by his name. The young man, seeing she was completely naked, was afraid to approach unless she covered her private parts with her thighs and unbound hair. She kept on coaxing him gently and finally he came near her and asked how she was feeling and why she was there. At first, she hid all the other details and told him a lot of lies until the young man did not believe her and said he would not help her unless she told him the truth. She made him promise to keep it secret and \[then\] confessed what had happened to her and why it had happened. The young man took her home and she showered him with presents, but in the end, forgetting he had given his word \[to keep quiet\], he told the story to one person and then to another and so, little by little, the story leaked out. The woman was arrested, and the young man forced to provide evidence of the truth. (All this is what Grillando tells us.)

He adds another \[exemplary story\] which is as follows. A woman from the diocese of Sabina used to profess this devilish craft, and because her husband had his suspicions about it, he questioned her on several occasions, but she always denied \[any knowledge of it\]. Her husband, however, continued to be suspicious and anxiously kept on pressing her for the truth. He acted in such a cunning way that one night he saw her smearing herself with an ointment. Once she had done this, he saw her leaving very quickly, like a bird, swooping down from the upper storey of the house to the lower. Her husband followed her in order to see where she was going, \[but then\] he saw her no longer, and when he came to the front door of the house, he found it closed. This caused him great astonishment. The next day, however, the husband questioned his wife again about what he was anxious to know, and she, (as one might expect), kept on saying she had no idea \[what he was talking about\]. But so that the woman would not be able to deny things any more, her husband told her plainly everything he had seen her do the previous night and then gave her a good beating with his cudgel, threatening to beat her harder if she did not tell the truth. But he did promise to forgive her if she told him everything.

So, the woman understood she could not keep her secret any longer, told her husband the truth frankly, and asked his forgiveness, which her husband granted on condition she take him to gatherings of this kind. In order to make sure of his forgiveness, she readily promised what he asked, and fulfilled her promise with Satan\'s permission. So, she took her husband to the place where the entertainments \[ludi\] were taking place. He had a good look at the sport, the dances, and everything else, and finally sat down at table with everyone else to eat. He thought the food was tasteless and asked for salt. There was none on the table and although he asked for it again and again, he was never given any. Finally, after he persisted in asking and had waited for a very long time, he was given salt, at which he said, \'Now praise be to God the salt has arrived!\' When he said these words, the evil spirits, who run away when they hear God being praised, made an immediate retreat. Everyone else disappeared, the lamps were extinguished, and he stayed there, alone and naked, until dawn broke and he saw some shepherds.
[Page 82]

He asked them the name of the district in which he was, and they replied, \'the countryside of Benevento in the Kingdom of Naples\'. This was a hundred miles away from where the man lived and in consequence, even though he was rich, he was forced to beg along the road so that he could get back to his own home. The moment he arrived he accused his wife of being a vampire-witch \[lamiae\], and related everything that had happened, (the way it has been told here), to the judges and they, upon investigating the whole business thoroughly, as was proper, found that everything I have been saying was true, and the woman\'s confession confirmed it.

The foregoing comes from Grillando. The Franciscan Alfonso a Castro, in his chapter 16, adds that he can give an account of many other pieces of evidence which he had come to know, from entirely trustworthy witnesses, \[referred to events which\] had taken place in Spain. Bartolomeo della Spina, Master of the Sacred Apostolic Palace, gives us examples which are no less reliable in chapter 17 ff. of his \[Inquiry into\] Night-Flying Witches, and I shall write down one of them. \'A young woman\', he says, \'who was living with her mother in Bergamo, was found one night in Venice in the bedroom of a kinsman. She was discovered naked in the morning and was recognised because she was his cousin. When they asked her how she had come there, or what she was doing there and why, she started to wail, and after they got her dressed, she suddenly told them the following story. \"Last night, I was lying awake in bed and saw my mother, who thought I was asleep, get out of bed, pull of her underwear, and smear her body with an ointment she took out of a little pot she removed from underneath some bricks.

Then she took a stick she had ready there, climbed on it the way one mounts a horse, and was carried away out of the window, and after that I did not see her at all. Then I got out of bed, too, and anointed my body the way I had seen my mother do and was carried away out of the window. I was immediately brought here where I saw my mother waiting in ambush for a boy who was lying in the bed. I was frightened when I saw that my mother was also startled by my arrival and was threatening to attack me, so I invoked the name of the Lord Jesus and the Blessed Virgin, and after that I did not see my mother and was left there, naked and alone\". Once the girl had told her story, her cousin wrote down everything she had said for the Father Inquisitor in Bergamo.

\[The Inquisitor\] arrested the woman and when she was put to the torture, she confessed everything and added that she had been carried off by her evil spirit more than fifty times so that she could kill the foresaid cousin\'s child, but that she never been successful because his parents always protected him well with the sign of the cross and holy prayers\'. \[Bartolomeo\] also adds another example, and I shall write this one down, too. \'One Antonio Leo, a coal-miner from Ferrara \[a Montagna\] in Valtellina, told me this year that he had found out the things written below from someone in his home district, to whom they had actually happened. Because of what many people told him, this man became suspicious that his wife was going on a \"voyage\", (that is, to a meeting of witches \[sagarum\]), \'while he was asleep at night. One night he pretended to be fast asleep and observed his wife get out of bed, anoint herself with ointment from a small pot she had kept hidden, and straight away she was nowhere to be seen. Her husband was stunned and, drawn by curiosity, got out of bed and did the same as his wife. Immediately, (so it seemed to him), he was carried off up the chimney-which was how, (he thought), his wife had also risen upwards-and was brought to the wine-cellar of a noble Count.There he found his wife along with several other people. When she saw him, his wife immediately made a sign and she and the others left, leaving her husband in that place. He was found by the house-servants next morning and, amid uproar, arrested as a thief and brought before the Count. He was given time to speak and gave a vigorous account of what had happened, as a result of which his wife was arraigned before the inquisitor and at length confessed and paid the appropriate penalty for her crimes.\'
[Page 83]

This is what Bartolomeo della Spina tells us. Still better witnesses are three examples of witch-transportation Nicholas Rémy adds to these three Italian ones in his Worship of Evil Spirits Book 1, chapter 14, of which I cannot deprive the reader. This is what he writes. \'In May 1589, villagers were celebrating a rural festival in Luz, which is a village not without a certain reputation in the foothills of the Vosges. Claude Choteau was coming back from this village at nightfall to a neighbouring village called Wisembach and had already climbed up a good part of the hill which rises between the two villages when he was battered by a sudden whirlwind. Astonished, he stood still and looked around \[to see if there was any reason anywhere for such an uncommon occurrence, since the air was perfectly calm and still everywhere else. He noticed six masked women in a retired spot-the locals call it the Morèle cleft. They were dancing round a table set with a lot of gold and silver, throwing their heads all over the place as they did so and \[acting like\] people who are driven hither and thither by fits of madness.

There was also a man on a black bull, standing near them. He was looking at them as though he were someone in the street with nothing else to do. In the meantime, \[Claude\] stood where he was while he recovered himself and looked at the whole scene more closely. While he was doing this, the entire mob disappeared in an instant, as if it withdrew from his sight. So, his fear left him, and he resumed his journey. He had already got over the summit of the hill when would you believe it!-those women started following him closely behind, throwing their heads about as before and preserving a deep or agreed silence. A man with a coal-black face was walking ahead of them. His hands were curved, like grappling-hooks, and he was preparing to let them loose on \[Claude\'s\] face, had not \[Claude\] not drawn his sword and thwarted him. But because he did so, \[the man\] stopped taunting him and, as though in fear of his life, vanished.

Nevertheless, the women made another appearance and the man who, as I said, was seated on a bull and was watching their dances, was with them. Choteau, who had now become more confident, came closer to him and said, \"Oh, there you are, Didier Gaxet!\"-that being the man\'s name-Please, protect me if you can. Honestly, I give you my solemn word I\'ll not say anything at all about what I have seen\". Hardly had he finished speaking when a whirlwind or cloud swept round him again, and when at last he spun himself round and freed himself from it, he found he was alone on the ground in a place a long way away from the path. But, by retracing his steps to where he had last been on it, he stumbled on to it and took himself back home as fast as he could.

Three days after giving this testimony, he was summoned before the investigating judge again and added the following to the very large number of details he had given earlier. He remembered, (he said), that when he had gone closer to the table to see what kind of a banquet has been set out on it, an evil spirit had immediately flown at his face with his nails, and while he was engaged in defending himself with his sword, he had been lifted up once again by a rather violent wind and carried off to the Combrimons Falls, a spot not less than two hundred paces away.

Lest anyone think this was perhaps the raving of a drunk frightened by the loneliness of the night and the place, in Sandeodat, on 22nd May, 1589, one of those women, Barbelline Caxet, had also said the same thing to the investigating \[judge\] a month earlier in almost the same words, adding that Desiré Gaxet and his wife had given Choteau two measures of wheat and two cows\' milk cheeses to keep his mouth shut about what he had seen. Consequently, when the two of them were brought face to face, they agreed in every particular except one which was that Barbelline said the evil spirit had not tried to lay hands on Choteau because he had gone near the table-this was a lie he had told during his statement-but because he had been on the point of stealing a gold cup from it\'.
[Page 84]

Rémy gives another example of the same thing. \'Johann von Hembach had scarcely ceased being a youngster when his mother, a worker of poisonous magic \[venefica\], took him with her to nocturnal meetings of evil spirits and, on the grounds that he knew how to play the pipes, told him to play them and to climb a tree close by so that he could be heard more clearly. He did so and, being free to have a good look at their jumping and dancing, was astonished at their noveltyeverything there was so preposterous and ridiculous-and casually said, \"Good God, where has this silly, stupid mob come from?\" Scarcely had he said this than he fell to the ground and hurt one of his shoulders. He begged those who were standing near him to help him but found himself alone.

He talked about this quite openly, and people had different opinions about it-some maintained it was a vision, others that it had actually taken place. But them something happened which removed people\'s doubts, because one of the women who had taken part in the dancing, Catherine Prevost, was arrested not long afterwards in Freissen on 2nd September 1589 on suspicion of casting lots for divination \[sortilegii\]. She gave an account of the whole business as written above without being interrogated first, unaware of the version Von Hembach had already propagated. Otilla Kervers and Agnes Eisarbz, who were sentenced for \[practising\] poisonous magic \[veneficii\] the following year-Kervers in Werderst on 6th and 7th August 1590 and Eisarbz in Dieuze on 3rd November 1590-each in turn made the same declaration and in addition made it evidentially stronger by giving the name, (Mayebuch), of the place where it happened\'.

The third \[example\] is not only a clear piece of testimony, but also makes as clear as daylight what had happened. It goes as follows. \'Nicole Lagbernhard was coming back from the mill in Guermange to Assenoncourt on 25th July 1590 and was making her way, at full noon, next to a hedge which was part of a wood, when she noticed men and women dancing in a circle and jumping about in the neighbouring ploughed field. But because they were doing so with their backs turned to each other, contrary to the usual way other people did it, she took a closer look and saw that, in addition to this, there were, mixed with those who were dancing and jumping in the circle, some who were deformed because they had the feet of goats and oxen. Almost dead with fear, she began to invoke the saving name of Jesus, (as people do when something unlucky and inauspicious threatens them), and to ask Him humbly that it might be possible for her to return home safe and sound. At that, all the dancers seemed to vanish, except for one called Petter Gross-Petter who was borne quickly into the air. But a small brush, such as bakers usually use to clean their oven before they put their bread in it, was seen to escape \[his grasp\] and fall; and while this was going on, Nichole, too, was swept up by a rather strong wind, started panting and puffing, and could scarcely draw breath, and when she got home she lay in bed, ill, for three whole days.

She and her neighbours talked about this, and the news spread through the whole district. At the first opportunity, the Petter I mentioned rather hot-headedly brought an action before a judge, so that he might not seem to be acknowledging and confessing to such a great crime by keeping quiet about it. In the end, however, he made up his mind to abandon \[his action\] because he was afraid that, since he was having no success with his case, he would be creating greater danger for himself. So, after due reflection, he abandoned it. This increased the suspicion against him even more, and many people considered it was because he had a bad conscience that he was \[now\] taking no notice of a wrong of which he had at first been so acutely aware.
[Page 85]

So, the judge investigated his life and behaviour the more carefully, and when he discovered, by means of reliable tokens of proof, that the case \[against Petter\] was not without grounds, he arrested him and prevailed on him to co-operate and confess his crime and, in the end, to expose and convict the others who had taken part in the crime with him. Among them were Barbelline, wife of Jean Latome \[who was tried\] at Dieuze on 27th January 1591 and Mayette, wife of Laurent Grosmaire, likewise \[tried\] at Dieuze on 16th February 1591. They acknowledged, separately but in the same words, that the information their accomplice Petter had disclosed about the Sabbat, when they danced back-toback along with cloven-footed individuals in their midst, was true.

On 1st March, 1591, a third person, Jean Michel, a herdsman from Dieuze, agreed with \[what\] these women \[had said in their\] confession, and added that while all this was taking place he was doing the job of pipe-player by putting to his mouth a sheep-hook someone happened to offer him, and letting his fingers flutter over it, as though he were playing notes on real pipes. When Nicole, (as has been said), full of fear, called upon Jesus and made the sign of the cross, he fell headlong from the rather high branch of the oak-tree on which he was sitting. Then he was caught up in a whirlwind and carried off to a meadow called \"Weiller\" where, not long before, he had left his flock grazing.

But in the end, the weightiest and most powerful piece of evidence relating to this incident was that the day after Nichole had told \[people about it\], the place where they had been dancing in a circle appeared to have been trodden down in a ring-the kind horse-trainers have to exercise their horses in a circuit. It was also chequered with recent traces of hooves belong to goats and oxen, and these remained visible until the field was brought under the plough and furrowed the following winter. Verbal evidence was given by Nicel Klein, Didier Vernex, and Gaspar Schneider, and they were all brought quickly to court and questioned about it by the judge.\'

Isn\'t this clearly sufficient proof of dances, meetings, and transportation from one place to another? At this point, will our sleepy-heads who deny everything say that these dreams have connected such different people in such an apposite fashion, like rings \[joined together\] into a single chain? Was Petter\'s brush also dreaming it had fallen down? Was Nicole dreaming and stuck in her bed for three days by the soporific anxiety of a dream? Did all those witnesses dream the ceremonies, and were the foot-marks so indelibly imprinted on the field by the power of unadulterated imagination? Had Apollo brought the flocks there from the \[River\] Amphrysus, or Hercules the Erythraean oxen from Cadiz, and did the cattle dance with practised skill on the soil of Lotharingia? Will over-nice people, (that is, the bothered-and-bewildered) go on being afraid that \'innocents\' of this kind claim they have committed offences which they never have done, but think they have because they\'ve got the wrong idea? Why this is so, and how stubborn is the desire of the human race to protect its enemies \[I shall discuss\] at much greater length in Book 5.

No less confirmatory is another \[example\] from Baudouin van Ronss\'s Letters on Medicine, \[chapter\] 50 which tells us something which had happened in Holland in his own day.²⁴² \'In the village of Oostbroek, not far from Utrecht, there was a widow who had taken in a male servant because her situation demanded she get help with her household expenses. Quite often, in the dead of night, as soon as the house-servants had gone to bed, he would look through the lattice \[over his window\] in the usual nosy way servants have, and see his mistress come into the stable, make her way to a specific, pre-arranged spot, and then stretch out her hands and grasp hold of the hay trough next to the horse\'s stall. Wondering what on earth she meant by this behaviour, he decided to try doing the same thing at the same time, without his mistress\'s knowledge.
[Page 86]

So, on the spur of the moment when his mistress came and did what she usually did and seemed to have gone away, he followed quickly, took a good look at the spot and then, imitating his mistress\'s example, threw his arms round the hay trough. Immediately he was snatched up into the air and carried off to the town of Wyck, to a cavern hidden underground, where he found a company of female workers of harmful magic \[maleficarum\] talking to each other about the acts of harmful magic \[maleficiis\] they had done. His mistress and her companions were astonished at her servant\'s unexpected appearance, and she asked him what trick he had used to get there in an instant, and why he had done so.

He told her, step by step, how he had done it and she immediately began to turn incandescent with rage, afraid that the result would be that those clandestine meetings at night would be brought to an end. So, she decided to talk to her partners in crime and \[ask them\] what they thought should be done in this awkward situation. Finally, they agreed among themselves to be friendly and make an exception of him, but that they would make him promise and swear an oath to remain silent and not communicate or reveal to anyone the secrets which he-beyond his merit or comprehension-might happen to see. He, for his part, made no such promise, but spoke to them in soothing tones and, with a view to getting them to behave with greater mildness, pretended that all his desires were fixed on one thing-that they would be gracious enough at some future date to let him attend their \"witches\' guild\" \[sortilegiarum collegio\].

In the meantime, while they were discussing this, the minutes glided by, and it was time to go. Once again, his mistress stirred up uncertainty about whether he should be sent home, which would be dangerous for the whole assembly, or killed for the general good. In the end they all agreed to choose the more merciful of the two and decided to send him back once they had bound him by an oath \[of silence\]. His mistress undertook this task and promised that, once he had given his word, she would take him home on her shoulders. They got themselves ready and were \[then\] carried swiftly through the air by a wind from the east. But now, listen to this! They had covered a good part of their journey when there came into view a lake covered with a jungle of reeds. This provided the maleficent old woman with an opportunity and, fearful lest the youth repent of his (pretended) initiation into their ghastly festivals, she saw a way of getting rid of him. She shot forward suddenly and threw him off her back, hoping, one must suspect, that the violence of the fall would kill him and that he would be swallowed up on the depths of the lake. But how very merciful is God, who does not seek the death of a sinner, but rather that he be converted and live, because God put a stop to the madwoman\'s

intention and did not allow the innocent youth to be drowned. Indeed, He gave the young man the considerably fairer boon of living right up to the present day. You see, as chance and good luck would have it, he did not receive a fatal blow when he fell into the lake, and the violent impetus of his descent was somehow broken by the reeds. It was starting to become light. The poor wretch\'s body was tortured by a thousand agonising pains, and the only part of it which worked was his tongue. So, he kept on groaning and gasping for a long time until some huntsmen who were passing gathered round him, extremely surprised by the unexpected sound of wailing, and found a man who had dislocated both his hips. They asked where he had come from, and what kind of misfortune had happened to him, and he told them everything, just as it had happened. He was brought back in a carriage to Utrecht where the noble Johan van Culemborg, the city magistrate, struck with consternation and full of astonishment because of the strangeness of what had happened to the man, subjected the details \[of his story\] to close examination and then ordered his men to hunt down the lady with the evil eye \[fascinatrix era\], arrest her, and put her in chains; and the moment she came into the magistrate\'s hands, she confessed everything\', etc.
[Page 87]

That is what \[Van Ronss\] says. What can the shameless mouth of Weyer, or Gödelmann, along with their oracles Luther and Melanchthon say to that? Will they say that because the silly woman was suffering from an excess of black bile, she believed it and was deceived? What \[can one say\]? How was the young man himself mangled this way and how did he get his dislocated hip? Did black bile suddenly dislocate it? Perhaps he made it up. Well, if that is so, how did he get to the reed-bed if not from the cavern mentioned in the story, and how did he get to the cavern if not from the house? If he didn\'t move from the house, how did he get from Oostbroek to the reed-bed? If he went of his own accord, where did such a serious and complete dislocation of limbs happen to him? Did people suffering from an excess of black bile find the young man there and bring him to the magistrate in Utrecht?

What shameless obstinacy! Reflect rather, Reader, on what this narrative tells us: (i) that there is a novel way of signalling \[to the evil spirit\], which is to seize hold of a hay trough instead of anointing oneself; (ii) that evil spirits usually choose particular places for themselves to hold their meetings and maleficent little assemblies. (Last year, for example, Jean del Vaulx, who was beheaded in Stavelot, was saying that one of the principal places in which they held their general congregations was within the jurisdiction of Utrecht, and that he had been taken there more than once from Stavelot;) (iii) that-take note of this!-not only are these female workers of harmful magic \[maleficas\] carried through the air, but with the assistance of an evil spirit they can carry others on their shoulders; and (iv) that it is the height of stupidity to commit oneself to be merciful to their very cruel, venomous women. How much safer it would have been for this young man to protect himself with the saving name of Jesus, or to have crossed himself and made that whole assemblage take to its heels as though he were blowing it away! To be sure, he would have remained alone in the cavern and would have had to endure the discomfort of a rather long journey on foot. (I could add many examples of this kind, because there are many of them in \[Giovanni\] D\'Anania, op.cit. Book 4; the author of The Madness of Evil Spirits Book 2, chapter 4; \[Antonio de\] Torquemada, dialogue 3; \[Peter\] Binsfeld, op.cit. Part 1, conclusion 12; Andreas Libavius in \'witches\' transportation\'; \[Bernardo da\] Como, The Inquisitors\' Lantern; and (older than these), Johannes Caesarius \[of Heisterbach\], A Discussion of Miracles Book 10, chapter 1; and others.)

One derives very strong proof from these examples. Since those who anointed themselves out of mere curiosity really were carried from one place to another which was so remote, it must be clear that this does not happen merely in the unadorned imagination and madness of silly women. Indeed sometimes, after a meeting has suddenly been dissolved, people have found tables and silverware and, later on, things their owners do not recognise; and their wives have confessed that they brought them with them to the meeting. Secondly, casters of lots \[sortiarii\] make careful note of all the places and all the things they eat and every place in the neighbourhood; they discern trees, hedges, rivers, fields, and houses; they recognise their fellow-guests, provided they are not wearing masks \[non larvati\], and they also often greet and have a conversation with those they meet on the way. Not infrequently, Catholic men and others of sound mind notice them going to or coming back from a meeting. Sometimes they are seen to be naked and arrested without their clothes on, either when they fall out of a whirlwind or a cloud, or when they have been wounded or maimed somewhere on their body. (See further Book 5).
[Page 88]

Likewise, they are often so tired out by their transvection that when they come back home, they take to their bed for three whole days before they can stand on their feet again, as Rémy tells us, op.cit. Book 1, chapter 24. They all say the same thing in their confessions and do not differ in the slightest detail. Others who do not share their crime and error confirm this. The condemned confess it at the stake itself, when pretence is of no avail and tortures have already been brought to an end. But when it comes to things which are worthless and delusive, there cannot be a conclusive proof which is so constantly one and the same, and unanimously agreed by everyone, and such great unanimity among people who are very different (and so numerous) in respect of the places \[they come from\], their circumstances, their ages, and their zeal. This is because the way the mind works is peculiar to the individual, and one person\'s imagination operates differently from that of someone else. The way the brain or the imagination is arranged-which would be necessary for an evil spirit \[to concoct\] this kind of image-is not the same, and there could not be such a great conformity in the telling of lies, and in my opinion this argument is sufficient to convince someone who is not obstinate.

This argument is confirmed because in op.cit. supra, no. 3 \[Bernardo da\] Como says, (and he says it well), that such dreams and fantasies are not in someone\'s power to the extent that he can make them happen or not happen as he pleases. But once witches \[strigibus\] have been converted in earnest and do not want to be present at a meeting any more, nothing more of the kind happens any further, simply because they do not want it \[to happen\]. This is clear proof that these things do not happen in dreams. Moreover, those who maintain that these things are dreams and wanton delusions undoubtedly sin against the reverence they owe to Mother Church, because the Catholic Church does not punish crimes unless they are proven and obvious. Nor does she consider people heretics unless they have clearly been arrested for heresy, (canon \'Consuluisti\', question 5.) For a good many years, however, she has regarded witches \[striges\] as heretics and, via inquisitors, order that they be punished and handed over to the secular arm, as is clear from books by \[Jakob\] Sprenger, \[Johannes\] Nider, \[Nicolas\] Jacquier, and \[Sébastien\] Michaëlis, and as experience tells us. Therefore, either the Church is in error, or the sceptics are, and anyone who says the Church is in error in a matter pertaining to the Faith must be entirely excommunicate.

\[Bernard da\] Como tells us the same exemplary story when he makes clear the truth of a denunciation. Unbelievers may put it to the test. \'Almost fifty years ago now, in Mendrisio\', he says, \'a town which was right next to Como, an inquisitor by the name of Master Bartolommeo de Homate, an official called Signior Lorenzo de Concorezio, and a notary, Giovanni de Fossato, were proceeding against these witches \[striges\]. One day the official, drawn by curiosity and wanting to test whether witches \[strigae\] would really go to their \"play\" physically, arranged a meeting \[with one of them\] and on a Wednesday came, with his notary and someone else, to a certain spot outwith the town, just as he had told the witch \[strigae\] in advance.

When the three men were almost there, they saw several people gathered together in the presence of someone-the Devil in the form of a goat-who was sitting in the manner of a great lord and, do you know, suddenly, at the Devil\'s command-and God permitted this because of the men\'s inquisitiveness-all the people in that gathering struck the official and his companions with sticks so hard that within fifteen days the official, the notary, and the third man died from the beating they had received. This is what he says. So should I have failed to persuade stubborn people to test what I have said, I shall not croak a warning against something which is very well known to everyone. You don\'t believe? Come and see.
[Page 89]

When they keep on saying that an evil spirit has no reason to do these things with individuals any more than he has to arrange the attendance of large numbers at his meetings, this is not true. He sees many advantages \[in doing it for the individual\]. (i) Through this association and the participation of more than one person, they are encouraged and more powerfully inured to evil-doing. (ii) The sins of individuals are made worse by this pact and people\'s participation in crime. (iii) By means of such enticements and the various initiations which take place there, they entangle more people more easily. (iv) A large crowd makes people more audacious when it comes to evil. It removes the fear of God from them and when they imitate one another, they are inflamed \[to commit\] greater offences, just as coals usually \[catch fire more easily\] when they have had oil poured over them. (v) The more people come to know criminal pleasure, the greater \[the pleasure\] seems to be. (vi) It is more difficult to convert people after they have delivered themselves to an evil spirit in front of several witnesses, and when the frequency of their actions has made them, so to speak, callous by habituation. (This is \[Bartolommeo della\] Spina\'s considered opinion in his Inquiry into Witches, chapter 20.)

In brief, therefore, I conclude that these transvections we are investigating can take place in four ways: (i) by mere thought, which Holy Scripture calls \'being carried in the spirit. Such was Ezechiel\'s vision. \'The likeness of a hand stretched out and took hold of me by a lock of hair on my head. The spirit \[spiritus\] lifted me up between the earth and the sky and brought me to Jerusalem in the sight of God\', \[8.3\]. The final phrase tells us how to interpret the words which precede it, lest we think this transvection is like that which happened to Habakkuk, about which \[the Book of\] Daniel tells us. (ii) Sometimes they make their way to meetings on foot, as \[Nicholas\] Rémy and \[Peter\] Binsfeld tell us. (iii) They are actually physically transported by an evil spirit by local motion, as I have said. (iv) Sometimes it can happen that they do not know whether they have been transported physically or merely thought \[that that is what happened\], such as in St Paul\'s case when he was snatched away one day, \[2 Corinthians 12.2\].

I find it recorded in certain of Fr. Juan de Maldonado\'s writings on evil spirits (a) that when \[witches\] want to be transported physically, they anoint themselves with an ointment made from baby\'s body-fat; (b) when they want to be present at meetings simply in dreams, they lie on their left side; (c) but when they prefer to stay awake and see whatever is happening at a meeting, as though it were being done in front of them, with the assistance of an evil spirit they spew a thick vapour out of their mouth, and in this they can look at images of what is being done, as though \[they were looking\] in a mirror. Perhaps the notorious Jean del Vaulx I mentioned meant to intimate this to his investigators when he said he knew how to tell whether he was taking part in a meeting physically or in imagination.

There is one thing left to be resolved which I know certain people have sometimes distorted. A rather learned natural philosopher and theologian asks how it can happen that, since an angel is incorporeal, he can move a physical object from its place. My reply is that he does not need God to assign him new power for this purpose, (which was Guillaume d\'Auvergne\'s idea): he does not need a distinct motive energy or power, (as \[Pierre\] Auriol thought): and an angel cannot, by will and intellect alone, without limits and without bounds move from its place any object he may wish, whenever he may wish, to wherever he may wish, and however fast he may wish. (One ought to be aware that an article of Paris condemned this and quite rightly so, because this implies unbounded power).
[Page 90]

But I reply that an angel can usually move and transfer a mass whose limits are fixed and known to him, and which is not too large for his power, with that speed and traction the angel\'s nimbleness and the natural constitution of the moved object permits, supposing he is present substantially in the object he has picked up-I prefer to follow those who have this idea-and, being present this way, pushes the object forward by means of his will alone and, by means of that same will, and using the object as his medium, moves another object next to it, or carries it in more or less the way a strong wind drives a feather through the air and finally sets it down in a particular place and thus it comes to a halt. This is how the angel carried the prophet Habakkuk simply by touching his hair. The hair did not support the body. This was done by the angel\'s power which was applied to the whole body through the authority of the angel\'s will.

Consequently, if an angel wants to moderate something he is doing, such as the speed of the transvection, he does not overwhelm the strength of what he is carrying. He makes sure the transvection of the human being is done without tiring \[the human\], and if he is unwilling to do this, the human must undoubtedly be extraordinarily tired out by the speed of his carriage through the air. Hence we understand that the diversity \[of experience\] which Rémy mentioned is not pre-determined, because transvections done by good angels are gentle and human beings are not distressed by them, whereas those done by bad \[angels\] are full of weariness, dread, and distress; and even though I believe this is what actually happens in the majority of cases, it can also happen that, if the evil spirit wishes, he can also convey \[human beings\] without fatiguing and vexing them. This is clear from the examples given by \[Antonio de\] Torquemada in his third dialogue, and Jean del Vaulx from Stavelot whom I mentioned said as much in his testimony about his own transvections. What is undoubtedly true is that these transvections by evil spirits are always full of danger and always cause fear.

Book 2, Question 17

Can an evil spirit change the size of bodies in such a way that he will be able to penetrate their various parts: and can he locate one body in two different places, or two bodies in one and the same place by penetrating them?

Briefly, I think the answer to all these points is no, because they are all contrary to the principles of natural philosophy and the way in which Nature is ordered. To deal with the second part first: the more accurate and more widely held opinion is that an evil spirit cannot simultaneously fill up places which are at a distance from one another and leave the space in between them unoccupied. Still less, therefore, will he be able to do this with a single object. This is something reserved to God alone. Nor do examples which tell us that many people have appeared to others in a different place aútorpoownws \[in their own person\] contradict this, in as much as the individuals really did appear at that time, not only to people who were asleep, but also to people who were awake. If saintly people have made appearances in a miraculous manner, as we read St Nicholas, St Agatha, and St Benedict did, these stemmed from God\'s unlimited power. If, on the other hand, they were made by evil angels, they should be considered simply as visual appearances produced by conjuring-tricks. I am referring to a case recorded by Origen in Against Celsus Book 6, \[where he says\] that Pythagoras was seen in two cities at the same time. There is another \[example\] in the life of St Germain about women who were guests at the same feast, and one in Zonaras about the son of the Emperor, who was shown to his father by the monk Theodore. (See Peter Thyraeus Book 2, chapter 8 and the following chapters.)
[Page 91]

In Book 4 of his Character of Evil Spirits, D\'Anania mentions, among other things, that an evil spirit cannot occupy the body of two human beings simultaneously, but when he is substantially in the body of one of them, he can use his power to tempt another person, or someone at a great distance, to \[commit\] some offence. I confess I do not understand this, because there is nothing to prevent two people from being so close to each other that they are the equivalent, so to speak, of a single space occupied substantially at one and the same time by the evil spirit. \[So\] I say that there is nothing to stop their being possessed simultaneously by an evil spirit. But if one person is a long way away from the person who has been possessed, so that the \[intervening\] space is greater than the radius of the evil spirit\'s activity, I do not believe the evil spirit which is possessing him can make any kind of assault upon the other person. You see, just as the Devil cannot try to move any object any distance at all from its place, so he cannot try to move the minds of just anyone a long way from their bodies-only those of people who are so close together that they may be considered to be, as it were, the equivalent of a single place \[occupied by\] the evil spirit. (Guillaume d\'Auvergne has several pertinent things to say on this subject in the last part of his Universe, chapter 13.)

The first part of my conclusions is proved because the more sensible natural philosophers, in company with Aristotle and St Thomas \[Aquinas\], do not grant that the physical aspects and dimensions of Nature can be penetrated. Therefore, an evil spirit cannot change the size of a body so that a human being can creep in the likeness of a cat or a weasel into a bedroom through a narrow chink, much less through closed doors. (The Carmelite Jan de Beets has dealt with this well at the end of chapter 4 of his exposition of the First Commandment). Consequently, the story that silly person from Madaura tells about a vampire-witch \[lamia\] in Milesia who turned into a weasel must be considered fictitious.

Those witches \[striges\] from Lotharingia, too, lied when they persuaded Nicholas Rémy that an evil spirit is accustomed to give power to experienced childkilling witches \[lamiis\] so that they can penetrate houses and, without any problem, create a way for themselves through any narrow places by contracting and shrinking themselves into \[the form of\] mice, cats, locusts, and other tiny living things of this kind and then, having gained entry, of restoring themselves to their former shape again. Scavius, in Book 5 of \[Johannes\] Nider\'s Ant-Hill, told similar lies, because these things are all craziness and deceptive illusion. It is merely that an evil spirit goes ahead, secretly opens and closes doors or windows wide enough to allow the women\'s bodies to come through them, and the women think they have clothed themselves in the shape of this kind of living creature, which is what more experienced Italian wise women \[sagae\] confessed to \[Paolo\] Grillando. But anyone who remembers what was done by the angel when he brought St Peter out of prison-when there is no need at all to say that this opening of doors and the guards\' being asleep were miracleswill have no doubt that an evil spirit can accomplish this opening and closing of doors in such a subtle manner.

Suppose someone objects that St \[John\] Chrysostom attributes this power to angels when he writes that Habakkuk was brought out and led away from an enclosed pit by an angel, while the seals \[on the entrance\] continued to be unbroken. This is what the oration says. It is about the disbelief of St Thomas, and is among the orations of the \[Church\] Fathers published separately in Rome: \'How the angel could take Ambakoum \[Habakkuk\] to the lions\' den and bring him out again, leaving the closed seals in good condition\'. A method witches \[striges\] sometimes mention is that the Devil opens up the walls themselves by making a kind of cleft or break, and so provides them with a way in and out, and afterwards makes the separate parts \[of the wall\] close up again. I know this will be seen as credible by those who admit that parts \[of things\] can be penetrated, and that reference will be made to what happened to the notorious Magdalena de la Cruz in Córdoba. (The wall suddenly gaped open, and the young woman was seen at her prayers by passers-by). But I think the whole thing was either a conjuring-trick, or an evil spirit, with immense speed, removed the requisite number of stones, retained others needed to keep the wall standing, and afterwards, with the same speed and dexterity, put them back in place again.
[Page 92]

I also think that since human eyesight cannot catch the speed of the stone-cutter from Hell, it happens to come to an erroneous opinion about a wall which has been opened by having parts of it squeezed together. Undoubtedly Weyer, along with his actual teacher, Calvin, is irreligious when he talks drivel about the way Christ our Lord came in to His disciples after the resurrection when the doors had been closed, because quite rightly Catholic writers, along with the most important and most holy Fathers \[of the Church\], recognise the argument for \[there being\] a clear miracle in this penetration of a solid object.

Likewise, \[Theodore\] Beza and those like him blaspheme on the subject of Christ our Lord\'s birth from an intact virgin\'s womb, and His resurrection when His sepulchre remained close shut. These bestial human beings maintain that these things opened up as long as He was passing through and closed again afterwards, as though a created thing could not obey its Creator in this, as in everything else. But these people do everything they can to disparage Christ our Lord\'s divinity and omnipotence in order to build a road for Turkishness and paganism.

Therefore, because an evil spirit cannot change the size \[of something\] this way, and so compress it that its parts penetrate one another, he cannot, (however large it may be and whatever its colour), make an object invisible to those in whose presence it exists, either, provided their eyes are sound and have not been subjected to conjuring-tricks. It can happen, however, that by chance they do not see anything else, because if the object goes somewhere else quickly, it is no longer in their sight. If, on the other hand, \[an evil spirit\] stopes the bystanders\' eyes from seeing things because he puts some object into the space in between, or because the movement of the air is much too great, or because the object changes shape for some reason he finds plausible-I find these are the methods he usually employs-under those circumstances the object will not actually be seen. In fact, it will remain invisible.

This is how, according to Philostratus, Apollonius removed himself from Domitian\'s sight, using a deception by evil spirits. This is how, according to Cicero, Gyges lay hidden, concealed by the fictitious ring. This is the story the silly Greeks tell about Perseus\'s shield, and this is how one should interpret what John of Salisbury writes about one individual: \'He was the most skilled of magicians, and the result was that he could make anything he wanted invisible, although at other times he was seen to transform them into other kinds \[of things\]\', (Courtiers\' Trifles Book chapter 9). Likewise, what the necromancer I mentioned had persuaded Willem van Gulik in \[Jacob de\] Meyer, Book 10; what Celsus was saying about Cleomedes of Astypalaea, who went into a large box and, when inside, pulled down the lid, and when they found he was not inside the box, they believed he had flown away. (Read Origen, Against Celsus Book 3). Then there\'s the story recorded by several people that he who wears the gemstone \'heliotrope\' becomes invisible. I don\'t believe it because, as Guillaume d\'Auvergne convincingly puts it, the stone would have to be able to remove every bit of colour from the object or, obviously, introduce others into it in such a way that \[the wearer\] could not be recognised. But what they say about the presence of such great power in the stone has not been shown \[to be true\] by reliable evidence from any authority.

Finally, from these \[points\] one can uncover evil spirits\' illusions and give them a good drubbing when \[it is claimed\] they make the parts of anyone\'s body appear to be huge, or non-existent, with the result that they seem to have been removed. (See the tales told in the Hammer of Women who Work Harmful Magic, about the men who thought their penises had been taken away, Sprenger Part 2, chapter 7, or the nonsense on reads in Book 2, folio 429 of The Belief one should have in Evil Spirits).
