Nor do I think an evil spirit has the power to stretch a small person\'s bones in such a way that he makes them gigantic in size, and so Pedro Cieza, in Part 2, chapter 42, is absolutely justified in classifying the Cichorani Indians\' boast that they could do this by means of certain herbs as a fairy-story.
[Page 93]

Book 2, Question 18

Can magicians transform the bodies of one species into those of another?

I am not talking about the fabulous metamorphoses in Ovid, Parthenius, and other \[writers\], nor about the natural changes which take place in living creatures which have not yet finished growing, as discussed by Aristotle and Pliny. I am talking about transformations brought about by magic which, as the result of the power of evil spirits, \[change\] one type of thing which is fully complete into the different shape of something which is fully complete, although Ovid and Propertius had this idea more than anyone else-for example, Propertius,

\'She was bold enough to impose laws on the moon she had enchanted, And to disguise her back during her nocturnal sport\', \[Elegies 4.5.13-14\];

And Ovid \[says\],

\'I suspect she flits, living, through the night shadows, And her aged body is concealed by feathers\'-\[Amores 8.13-14\].

The people of Catania used to think similar things about the Sicilian Liodorus, as I pointed out in Book 1. \[Similar\] stories are told about Simon Magus in the Voyages of St Peter;25⁶ likewise \[John of\] Salisbury about a particular individual in the book I mentioned earlier; likewise the annals of the English about King Bladud and, from the same source, a story that Uther \[Pendragon\] was transformed to look like Gorlois, Duke of Cornwall, by the incantations of Ambrose Merlin, so that he could sleep with Igerna as his wife, and from this irreligious copulation were born Arthur and Anna. (Read Geoffrey of Monmouth and Ludovico Pontico Virunio.)²⁵⁷ Moreover, this idea thrust itself into the New World, because the Nicaraguans used to believe that their witches \[striges\] could change themselves into cats, monkeys, and pigs, and the Guatemalans used to think that a notorious worker of harmful magic, Augustina, went about in the shape of a cow when Beatriz de la Cuva died in a huge flood and earthquake. (\[Pedro\] Cieza tells the story in his history of Peru Part 2, chapters 205 and 210.)

Now, although in the past, in company with the general school of theologians I have publicly professed an opinion about lycanthropes and things of this kind-namely, that they are all illusory and deceptive and are at odds with Nature-once again at this point, when the exigencies of the debate demand it, my judgement is that one must affirm that the degradation of a human being\'s becoming an animal is also illusory and deceptive. I add that question 5 of the canon Episcopi threatens the penalty of excommunication against anyone who maintains the opposite, a judgement which is supported by the authority of St Augustine.

Reason tells us the same thing. The human soul cannot inform an animal\'s body, just as the soul of a lion \[cannot inform\] the body of a horse, or a horse\'s soul a human body. The reason is that for any substantial form to endow its own \[body\] with the qualities essential to it, it requires arrangements peculiar to and harmonious with itself, and a special provision of physical senseorgans. Hence the action of a body with sense-organs is defined by the soul. Consequently, it appears that an animal soul cannot inform a human body, nor a human \[soul the body\] of an animal, as I said. So, the soul of a human being will not be able to migrate to the body of an animal in order to inform it. Still less will the soul be able to undergo change along with the body, as it appears Horace thought \[possible\] in his Epodes when he describes Circe\'s restoration of Ulysses\'s companions \[to their human shape\].

\'Ulysses\'s long-suffering oarsmen Stripped the rough skins full of bristles From their bodies, with Circe\'s consent. Then their reason and voice Glided back, and their former dignity \[returned\] to their faces\', \[17.15-18\].
[Page 94]

But they had not actually lost their human reason, that is, the most important part of the human soul. The soul is immortal and cannot be damaged or wounded by an evil spirit. Therefore, those who think they have been transformed into something else are always labouring under an illusion, just as those who think they are seeing something when they are not seeing it at all are often deluded, too. An intelligent person who does not wish to be deceived must distinguish the transformation itself from the effects which accompany it, and if

\[Jean de\] Sponde had done this, he would not have babbled so many juvenile inanities in his commentaries on Homer.

Let us conclude that this transformation is a delusion, although many of its accompanying effects are entirely real. Carnage, for example, is wreaked upon human beings or their herds (a) by an evil spirit in a body made of air, or in the body of a wolf, or (b) by human beings disguised as wolves, (I shall clarify this later), (c) or by human beings not disguised like this, who go about in human form but behave in a bestial fashion. It can happen, you see, that because of a defect in the humours and an excess of black bile, certain wolfish tendencies take over their mind, along with a hatred of flocks and humans, and the desire to attack them, tear them to pieces, and devour them. Hence physicians classify Aunáwva or huxavopwπia \[lycanthropy\] as illnesses. Arab \[physicians\] call this \'chatrab\' \[qutrub\], and Latin speakers, \'melancholia\' or \'lupine madness\'. Because of a similar defect, some people think they are dogs or lions or other brute beasts.

Those who suffer only from this kind of illness are not magicians, and although they think they are wolves and imitate the savage behaviour of wolves, to other people they look like human beings. According to St Augustine, Praestantius\'s father used to think he was a wolf, although no one else did; so did another individual, (see Thomas of Brabant or Cantimpré), the Patavian farmer in \[Job\] Fincel\'s Wonders, Book 2, the Prussian in \[Georg\] Schuler\'s commentary on Book 7 of \[Ovid\'s\] Metamorphoses, and the man described by \[Pietro\] Pomponazzi who is quoted by \[Johann von\] Ewich in his book on the character of wise women \[sagarum\]. But when other people, too, see them as wolves, dogs, cats, or other brute beasts-such as the woman in Palladius\'s Life of Macarius of Alexandria who appeared to be a mare, or the Neuri in Herodotus and Mela, or in our own day, according to popular opinion \[what happens\] to certain Irish people every year, (for which see the second part of \[William\] Camden\'s Ireland)-this is caused by magic and cannot proceed from illness alone.

The Devil usually deceives in a number of different ways. Sometimes he substitutes one body for another, and while the original person is elsewhere and has been put to sleep in some secret place, he puts on the body of a wolf, or makes one out of air, wraps himself in it, and does the things which people believe have been done by the poor wretch who is actually asleep somewhere else. (The story of this stratagem, which is revealed to everyone by a holy man, is told by Guillaume d\'Auvergne in the last part of his work I cited earlier, chapter 13). But sometimes he wraps the humans themselves as appropriately as he can in the real skins of wild animals. He does this when he gives them a wolf\'s pelt which they are supposed to keep hidden in some hollow treetrunk. This is well-established from certain people\'s confessions. Sometimes, in accordance with the pact \[he has made with them\], he surrounds the magicians with the likeness of an animal, which he has made from the air, and builds the copy round each part of their body, fitting head to head, mouth to mouth, belly to belly, foot to foot, and arm to arm.
[Page 95]

This usually happens when \[the magicians\] apply certain ointments to themselves for this purpose, as in the well-known case of the lycanthrope Dolanus, which has been published in court records, or when they merely use the words they have learned \[from the Devil\], such as \[the magician\] torn to pieces by dogs in front of the Duke of Russia, and one Bajanus in Bulgaria about whom Sigebert \[of Gembloux\] writes in his chronicle. (In this and the preceding case, a wolf\'s tracks left imprints on the ground). There is nothing remarkable in the last case if they are found afterwards with real wounds in those parts of their human body which had received a blow when they were \[wearing\] the body of the wild animal, because the air which was cast round them could not be relied on, gave way, and the wound inhered in their real body. When the real body has re-appeared, the Devil wounds that part of the body of those he has taken elsewhere, which he knows has been wounded in the animal body.

Bartolomeo della Spina tells an anecdote from his own time in his Inquiry into Witches, chapter 16. \'Another witness of this apparent transformation of night-flying witches \[striges\] into cats, a cobbler from Ferrara by the name of Filippo, made the following declaration to me under oath in court earlier this year. Three months previously, he said, a certain witch \[strix\] had undertaken to cure his child of a bewitchment for which, perhaps, she herself may have been responsible, on condition he would not stop her from coming near his child if he were to see any female cats fawning or behaving in a playful manner round him. Well now, that same day, an hour after she had left the house, Filippo and his wife notice a large female cat they had never seen before, coming towards the child at a fast pace. This frightened his parents, and they made several attempts to drive it away but were distressed to see that it kept coming back.

Finally, they lost both their patience and their temper with it. So, they shut the door and, while the cat raced round looking all over the place for a way to escape, Filippo took his time and then threw a spear at it and at last landed it a very heavy blow which forced it to hurl itself out of a window high up in the wall. As a result, the whole of the cat\'s body looked as though it been squashed flat. Now, from that moment onwards the elderly witch \[strix\] was confined to her bed for a good long time, since every bone in her body had been broken, a fact which strengthened \[the parents\'\] suspicion that the woman was indeed a witch \[striga\] and had subjected their child to the enchantment which was causing him very serious distress. The marks and blows the cat had received, you see, looking like the marks one finds on the bodies of the sick, were found on the elderly woman\'.

Not quite five years ago, in a village in the west of Flanders, not far from Diksmuide, very near the confluence of the Iperlee and the IJzer, while a plague caused by the evil eye \[pestis fascinaria\] was spreading in a remarkable fashion, I happened to hear the following story from a monk, a trustworthy individual, who was in the neighbourhood at the time. A peasant and his son were drinking in an ale-house, and out of the corner of his eye the man noticed that the serving-trollop was chalking up double the amount of ale when, as is their custom, he drank a toast to her. He pretended not to notice, but when he and his son had had enough, he called the landlady over and asked her for his bill. He was charged the sum which had been marked up in chalk, but refused to pay, and after the two of them had traded many words, he flung down on the table the amount he knew was correct and left. Boiling with indignation the woman called out, \'You won\'t be able to get back home today, or I\'m not the woman I am!\' But he paid her threats no heed and walked off. When he reached the stream where he had left his boat, however, neither he nor his son, (who was pretty strong), could shift it from the bank, even though they leaned on their poles with all their strength. You would think it had been nailed down in the mud.
[Page 96]

Now, it so happened that two or three soldiers were marching past, so the peasant hailed them.

\'Hey, mates! Come and help us get this boat off the bank. I\'ll pay you for it, and you can have a good drink on me\'.

They answered his call, came along, and pushed as hard as they could, but they pushed for a long time to no purpose. Then one of them, sweating and gasping for breath, said, \'Why don\'t be unload all this stuff from the boat? Perhaps we\'ll find it easier to get it down to the water if it\'s empty\'. So that\'s what they did and then-pay attention to this!-they saw a huge sallow toad in the bottom of the boat, looking at them with glittering eyes. One of the soldiers did not pick it up but drilled a hole in its swollen throat with the point of his sword and threw it into the water. There, as if dead, it rolled over on to its back, belly upwards. The rest of the soldiers inflicted further wounds on it while it was afloat, and suddenly the boat came free. The peasant was pleased and brought his helpers back to the same alehouse he had left earlier. He asked for a drink, and the waitress brought him one. He asked after the landlady and was told she had become very ill and was in bed, dying.

\'Stupid woman!\' he said. \'Do you think I\'m drunk? I left her standing over there scarcely half an hour ago, and she was in good health and a nasty frame of mind, just like you. I\'m going to go and see what\'s going on\'.

He went into the bedroom and found that the woman had died of wounds which had dug into and slashed open her neck and stomach. \'How did she get these wounds?\' he exclaimed. The waitress insisted she did not know and was sure the woman had not set foot outwith the house. The case went before a magistrate who came to the conclusion that the wounds had been caused by both slashing and stabbing blows, and that they had appeared in the very same places in which the soldiers had struck the toad. The toad itself was nowhere to be found. (There is another story, not unlike this one, which I shall tell in Book 5, section 3, towards \[the words\] \'they will be sufficient for torture\', concerning indication 8 talking about naming names.)

It is about ten years ago that one Peter Stumpf was put to death in Bedburg, a small town in the territory of Köln, because for more than twenty years he had consorted with an evil spirit in the form of a succuba. She had given him a wide belt, and when he put it on, he and others thought he was turned into a wolf. In this form he had ripped out the throats of fifteen children and eaten their brains. He had tried to devour his two daughters-in-law, and he had treated his own daughter and his godmother as his wives. All this is well-known from court records and from engravings which are openly on sale.

Anyone who wants further conspicuous examples of these transformations should read \[Jean de\] Sponde on Book 10 of Homer\'s Odyssey; \[Peter\] Binsfeld, Confessions of Workers of Harmful Magic, conclusion 3; \[Nicolas\] Rémy, Worship of Evil Spirits Book 2, chapter 5; Giovanni \[Lorenzo\] D\'Anania, The Character of Evil Spirits, Book 4, folio 180; \[Battista\] Codronchi, op.cit. chapter 7; and Petrus Thyraeus who gave careful consideration to more \[of these and in better order, than anyone else in his theses, The Apparitions of Spirits \[Spirituum\] Book 2 and Book 15 as far as chapter 24; and Brother Claude Prieur who used him to put together his dialogue in his book on lycanthropy.
[Page 97]

Those who want to argue for real metamorphoses, (such as \[Jean de\] Sponde, \[Jean\] Bodin, \[Kaspar\] Peucer, Philippus Camerarius, and a few others), produce a single piece of evidence from Holy Scripture, which it is worth our while to explain-Nebuchadnezzar\'s \[change\] into an ox, and that of Lot\'s wife into a statue of salt. But since both these happened as the result of divine power, they can have no importance for changes brought about by evil spirits. In the case of Nebuchadnezzar, too, most Fathers \[of the Church\] deny that he assumed the form of an OX or the likeness of an ox\'s body. They interpret \[the incident\] simply \[by saying\] that his reason was so estranged that he believed he was an OX. Not to drag out the argument, it seems to me that an explanation more consonant with what Holy Scripture says, the explanation of St Gregory in his Moralia Book 5, chapter 8, and reason, is that of interpreters \[who say\] that the condition of the King\'s body changed into the constitution of an ox, that his human shape had degenerated to some extent into that of a wild animal, and that he had received internal senses which were like those of brute beasts. So as a result, he started to walk on all fours, as cattle do, to eat hay, avoid human beings, and spend his time in the open air.

Finally, objection can be made to what Herodotus wrote-that sometimes a plane-tree has turned into an olive-tree-and some of the Alpine peoples \[have said\] that an evil spirit has changed human bodies into statues of salt. My reply is that if they were walking about a change which happens almost immediately and without \[the help of\] an intermediary, that was an illusion and a conjuring-trick. But if they were talking about a change \[helped by\] an intermediary, or \[effected\] by the various changes caused by decay and the production of a second piece of matter, there is no reason why both cannot happen naturally, after the appropriate arrangements have been introduced into the matter to \[cause\] these final forms. We read among the wonders performed by St Spiridon \[of Trimythous\] that he changed a snake into gold in order to deceive a greedy, pitiless rich man, and that afterwards he changed it back from gold to a snake again. This was a real change, because it was miraculous and effected by God who has the power to do it. Perhaps this change would not be impossible for an evil spirit, but \[he would have to do it\] in successive stages because a snake is something which has been divided into segments, and the nearest matter which is the same as that of gold and a snake is undoubtedly earth.

Book 2, Question 19

Can magicians make brute beasts talk, and can they understand what the brute beasts are saying?

Alfonso de Mendoza has examined this question in his Scholastic Topics for Discussion, question 5, following St Thomas \[Aquinas\], The Power \[of God\], question 6, article 5 and \[Francisco de\] Vitoria, Magic, number 9, but it is incumbent on me to speak more clearly and with greater accuracy. There can be no doubt that sometimes it can happen, by divine power, that those who possess no power of speech by Nature can, nevertheless, speak. Holy Scripture attests this of Balaam\'s ass, (Numbers 22). God Himself, who is the creator of all speech, knows in what way He makes this happen. Since He formed the organs of articulate speech we know about, He can also choose other instruments for this purpose, if He so chooses. But in general, I think He uses the services of angels who speak via inanimate objects or brute beasts. However, I make this distinction. \[When they speak\] through those things which have organs suitable for the purpose and are living instruments, such as magpies, crows, parrots, and so forth, language has been fashioned in their very bodies, in those circumstances, speech is an action connected with the creature\'s life, not one connected with its understanding.
[Page 98]

It is, however, connected with the understanding when angels do it. But in the case of those things which are indeed alive, but do not possess organs suitable for speech-asses and oxen, for example-I should think \[speech\] happens because the angel himself is speaking in them, or rather next to them, and has fashioned what he is saying in the air. The same can be said of things which have no organs of sensory perception, such as fire, air, water, earth, dead bodies, severed heads, statues, and trees. In those circumstances, when a voice is simply fashioned next to those objects, the speaking should not be attributed in any way to anything other than the angel, as the one who is giving form to the air and producing it as his medium and instrument, and not as a living action. If the air is being given form inside a body which is capable of sensation, but inadequate \[for the task of speaking\], or even inside one which is incapable of sensation, \[the ability to speak\] can be bestowed on it \[with the body acting\] as its instrument.

Now that these points have been made, it will be easy to come to a judgement about the following miraculous examples of speaking \[we find\] in sacred history, such as Balaam\'s ass, (on which you should consult \[Cardinal\] Cajetan\'s commentary): the angel speaking to Moses and to Esdras from a bramble-bush; Spiridion\'s dead daughter, Irene, indicating the \[hiding\] place of something which had been left with her: someone who had been killed, indicating that he had been murdered on Macarius\'s orders: the other Macarius\'s conversation at the execution-ground about the burial-places of the condemned, and the venerable confessors of Africa who still used to speak clearly even though their tongues had been torn out by the root during the Vandal persecution. (This has been confirmed on the Emperor Justinian\'s authority as something very well attested by the histories of his own time. Polydore Vergil tells a similar story about an English priest in Book 13 of his history of the English). In these \[examples\] one miracle happened after another as God, in the role of efficient cause, filled the place of the parts which had been cut away.

Let me add one \[example\] from Antonio Bonfini. \'About three years after the Emperor Sigismund was disastrously defeated by the Turks at Nicopolis, many people were going down into the plain and heard a voice coming repeatedly from among the bones, crying out the names of Jesus Christ, our Saviour, and the Virgin Mary. They found a speaking head among the corpses, and when it realised there were people in its vicinity, it said, \"Why are you standing there so amazed, gentlemen?

I am a Christian. I fell, unconfessed and unshriven, in this war. Mother Mary is not allowing me to be condemned to eternal torture. She has kept me still in control of my tongue so that I can confess my wrong-doings and purify my mind with Apostolic rites. So please, send for a priest to hear my confession and give me absolution\". Asked why he had deserved such a great favour from the Virgin, he replied that during life he had had a particular devotion to her, celebrated her seven feast-days every year with very great reverence, and honoured her on religious fast-days \[by taking\] only bread and water. So, they sent for a priest from the nearest village, and he duly heard \[the man\'s\] confession, who received remission of all his sins and, after receiving this final cleansing, suddenly fell silent and remained quiet ever after\'. This is what he says. Those who want to make changes should read it and come to their senses again in the face of such an evident miracle which commends devotion to the Mother of God, fasting, observance of feast-days, and the sacrament of confession. (You will find a story not unlike this in Thomas of Cantimpré, The Universal Good Book 2, chapter 29, sub-section The Devil apes God. He has attempted many things similar to what I have been talking about.
[Page 99]

His, however, are pseudo-miracles. Sometimes God allows him to do this so that divine vengeance and justice can be shown clearly, such as when a severed head indicated that Cercydes was a murderer, (my source is Aristotle), and when the head of Orpheus predicted to Cyrus, King of Persia, that he too would be killed by a woman. Sometimes he makes up any old oracle, such as the one Phlegon of Tralles tells us about. He took it from Heron, and its tenor is as follows. Polycritus married Locrides and slept with her for three nights. On the third day he died, leaving her pregnant, and she gave birth to a monster of both sexes. It was brought into the midst of a meeting of the people so that they could be consulted about what this presaged, or what should be done about it. Polycritus appeared as a ghost in the middle of the people and predicted that a disaster would come to the Aetolians and Locrians, and that the people would have no power to resist it. He removed his monstrous son\'s head, swallowed \[the rest of\] him, and vanished, and not long afterwards the head spoke of the same disasters. Phlegon, quoting Antisthenes, a Peripatetic philosopher, adds that when the consul Acilius Glabrio defeated Antiochus,

King of Asia, the Romans had been terrified by oracles \[which said they should not invade Asia any more, and the head of Publius, the Roman commander, which had been left behind by a wolf after it had devoured the body, as he himself had predicted when moved by a divine frenzy, (so it is thought), chanted in a long speech that disaster would befall the Romans.

Certain \'speakings\' among the pagans were clearly conjuring-tricks and counterfeits carried out with the help of an evil spirit for the monetary gain of the pagans\' priests, who not infrequently were magicians and fortune-tellers. The two speaking doves at Dodona are an example, as is the oak at Dodona, Argus\'s boat, Achilles\'s horse, the \[Indian\] ascetics\' elm-tree which greeted Apollonius, and the Rover Causus which said, \'Hallo, Pythagoras!\' (In his Miscellanies, Clement \[of Alexandria\] says that Pythagoras was the owner of books belonging to Zoroaster the magician). Simon Magus\'s dog spoke to St Peter and others, (see Clement \[of Alexandria\], Journey and Glycas, Annals). \[Paolo\] Grillando, whom I have often praised, ascribes the same thing to a wise-woman \[sagae\], Francesca of Siena, in question 7. Theoctenus, a magician in Antioch during the reign of Maximinus, used the power of magic to make a statue of Jupiter pour forth prophecies which roused a hatred of Christians among the citizenry and whetted the Emperor\'s persecution. (Read St Cyril of Alexandria, who reviews several of the things I have said earlier, in his Against Julian Book 3, and add, from Valerius Maximus Book 1, the statue of Fortune in Rome, which spoke on the Via Latina).

No angel, however, can give animals the power to know or understand what they are saying. An evil spirit, though, could make animals say certain things in a way particularly apt to their place and time, things adapted to individuals and situations which were relevant at that moment. But although the evil spirit could do this on purpose, the animals could do it only by chance and by accident. To the ignorant and uneducated this might furnish \[evidence of\] a certain quickness of mind and intellectual understanding. \[But\] magicians, too, sometimes cause animals to make remarkable mimicking gestures and movements-examples are Valerius\'s story about the snake in Epidaurus, Philostratus\'s anecdote about Apollonius\'s lion, and \[Pierre\] Belon\'s tale about a donkey in Cairo²⁷¹-and ignorant individuals think they could not do this unless they were able to reason and make judgements. With the help of an evil spirit, a magician can pretty easily corrupt the organs of the body and cause a human being to be heard to bark, howl, croak, or bray while he is trying to speak.
[Page 100]

After all, we see that several people are quite happy to do this by artifice, and we read that in 1546 Margarita from Esslingen became so swollen because of pains in her belly that its bulk began to hide her face and seemed to be more than ten palms\' breadth all the way round. Those who were standing round her bed heard cocks crowing, hens clucking, geese honking, dogs barking, sheep bleating, pigs grunting, cattle lowing, and horses whinnying. An extraordinarily large worm came out of her side, as did about a hundred and fifty snakes-a marvel which would be convincing were it not for that fact that these were nothing more than evil spirits\' conjuring-tricks, invented by the girl\'s mother in order to make money. Sometimes infants\' cries are heard in the wombs of pregnant women, but this is a natural occurrence and Andreas Libavius has provided many examples, and carefully proved it with the addition of arguments from natural philosophy throughout the whole of Volume 2 of his work on remarkable things; and Margarita\'s story has no place in it.

But do magicians understand what brute beasts are saying? Porphyry writes that in his day it was commonly said of Apollonius of Tyana that when he heard a swallow crying out loudly with many \[others\], he explained to the companions he had with him that she was revealing to the rest of the \[swallows\] that an ass\'s cold laden with grain had fallen down in front of the city and the grain had spilled all over the ground.²⁷³ By doing this Apollonius wanted to pretend he understood the birds\' chattering, because the ancients had said this about Tiresias and Melampus. But these \[stories\] are unbelievable. Since brute beasts lack reason, they cannot employ verbal intercourse and verbal intercourse, which points out the meaning of things or actions, is necessary to enable \[people\] to carry on a deliberate conversation. I admit they are able to indicate their physical states and wants with their voices, and that human beings can gain knowledge of these by daily observation, and an evil spirit, which has a most thorough understanding of these things, could teach them to magicians. Whether he wishes to do so, or has ever done so, I do not know. It is not unbelievable that he has.

But the question is, do brute beasts, of the same species, at any rate, understand each other? As far as animals\' physical states and the ways \[they show\] what they want are concerned, daily experience tells us they do. As far as everything else is concerned, Porphyry thought, and tried to prove, that birds understand the speech and songs of their own type and can point out to other birds what is suitable for them. Plato thought the same, and so did Oppian when dealing with what elephants think, because if an elephant falls down into the mire and cries out, when his companion sees this, it goes away and comes back bringing the herd with it so that they can make a combined effort to protect the one which is stuck.

There is also the example of the Libyan scorpion. When it has not been able to strike a human being all by itself, it comes back with several other scorpions, and they link together to form a ring and attack the human simultaneously. Lastly, there is the example of a fish which escapes through a break in a trap and warns other fish of the danger, the result being that no fish of that species are seen that day. (See Pierre Grégoire, Republic Book 15, chapter 5 where he mentions these very points and learnedly tells us that animals lack genuine understanding and ability to speak). I add that an evil spirit cannot endow animals with either of these because he cannot bestow a ration soul on them, creation of this being reserved to God alone. Moreover, \[speech\] requires physical organs appropriate to that purpose, which is a topic I shall investigate in the following question.
[Page 101]

Book 2, Question 20

Can an evil spirit give animals the ability to speak?

This investigation depends on whether brute beasts have been endowed with any kind of reason. If they have been, an evil spirit will easily make them able to converse and have a full understanding \[of what is being said\], once he has removed anything standing in the way of their being able to do so. If they have not been \[so endowed\], he certainly cannot make them able \[to talk and understand\]. Now, there have been not a few people who attribute reason to most animals. Empedocles, according to Sextus Empiricus who cites him, said that all animals and plants have understanding and foresight, and so did Democritus, according to Stobaeus in his Eclogues on Natural Phenomena. It appears Lactantius was of the same opinion in his \[Divine\] Institutes Book 3, chapter 10. Porphyrius \[says so\] quite openly in Sacrifices Book 3, as does Plutarch in his essay \'Brute beasts make use of reason\' and \'Do creatures which live on land have more reason than those which live in water?\' and in his essay exhorting people to study the arts, Galen writes that there is an ÉVδIábetov Xóyov in all living creatures. Erasmus translated this, (not very well), as \'reason capable of feelings.\' \[Andrés\] Laguna of Segovia \[translated\] it better as \'internal reason\' or \'reason which shelters inside.

The result is that Galen does indeed attribute to brute beasts the faculty of reason which also exists in human beings but makes the simple distinction that human beings are able to put their reasoning into words, whereas brute beasts may have reason but cannot express their mental concepts in words. This was also Quintilian\'s opinion in his Education of an Orator Book 1, and among more recent authors with the same opinion are Antonio Cittadini in his commentary on Aristotle\'s Posterior Analytics Book 1, chapter 3: Lorenzo Valla in chapter 9 of his Dialectic: and Cardinal Hosjusz, indeed, had the idea that we are distinguished from brute beasts not by reason, speech, or intelligence, but by religion, and worship and contemplation of God. I should think these words of his should be explained religiously, since he spoke the way Holy Scripture does, denying that he was saying these things are of less importance, not that he was judging them to be of no importance at all, so that the sense of what he thought is that a human being differs from brute beasts more in religion than he does in those other things-speech, intelligence, and reason.

One\'s conclusion, therefore, is that to attribute reason, rational discourse, intelligence, foresight (in the proper sense of the word), and discernment to brute beasts runs counter to correct natural philosophy and the Faith, but that there is in brute beasts some kind of ingenuity which imitates reason, intelligence, and rational discourse. This is the opinion of Albertus Magnus in Book 3 of his work on the soul, and of Philoponus in text 157 \[of his commentary on Aristotle\'s De Anima\] where Aristotle \[says\] this quite openly, as does St Thomas \[Aquinas\] in 1.2, question 13, article 3 and \[Pierre\] Grégoire, op.cit. Book 3, question 1. In Book 7, chapter 1 of his Inquiry into Animals, Aristotle attributes traces of the human mind and a likeness of intelligence to them.

This is where Arnobius gets his opinion from in Against the Heathen Book 2. \[17\] when he writes that many \'likenesses\' of rationality and intelligence are seen in brute beasts. In his essay on the usefulness of parts of the body, Book 1, chapter 3 Galen, says that Hippocrates was of the same opinion, and Averroes in his \[commentary on Aristotle\'s\] Physics Book 7, Girolamo Maggi in The Fiery Destruction of the World Book 2, chapter 5, Gabriele Zerbi in \[his commentary on Aristotle\'s Metaphysics Book 1, question 7, (which it is well worth your while to read), and Cardinal \[Francisco de\] Toledo in his \[commentary on Aristotle\] The Soul Book 3, question 7, have said the same.
[Page 102]

They say that brute beasts do many things skilfully and are taught the things they seem to do as a result of rational discourse \[with their trainer\]. Many people say as much, and in his Century 1, letter 50, \[Justus\] Lipsius has collected what a very large number of sources have said about elephants. Aelian \[has done the same\] for the rest of the animals-for example, lions, (The Character of Animals Books 5 and 7): bulls (Book baboons \[cynocephali\] (Book 6): other monkeys (Book 5): stags (Book 11) where, however, кало́́vтш σuvíel should not be translated as \'he understood them when they were talking\', but \'he understood them when they were calling\', that is, using the meaning of the Greek word given to it by the rearer and tamer of wild animals, just as in Book 8, chapter 4, the same author says of fish калобрегос Éπanoúelv, \'when summoned, they can hear\'. The protonotary Luis Pérez de Portillo \[has recorded\] wonderful things about dogs in his single volume on dogs, folio 16 sq. and about horses in his single volume on the horse, folio 83 sq.-a few years ago he dedicated both books to the sagacious Philip II, King of Spain-and Joachim Camerarius has brought together exemplary stories about many other living creatures in the tenth decuria of his books on problems.

Whether animals are guided by reason can be tested in a few words. It would be perfectly rational to say that if they are guided by reason, they would operate out of choice, and if they were operating out of choice, every creature of the same kind would not always operate in the same way everywhere, as they do. If they were endowed with \[the ability to hold\] rational discourse, they would know certain universal principals without which no one can discourse positively or negatively. It is generally agreed, however, that brute beasts do not know universal principles. Therefore, if they have been endowed with reason and \[the capacity for\] rational discourse, \[they have also been endowed\] with knowledge, foresight, and discernment. Therefore, they know the reasons for things and how to distinguish things which are more good \[from\] those which are less good. If they have been endowed with reason, they have therefore \[been endowed\] with free will, too, and draw rational conclusions in relation to the things about which they discourse rationally. Therefore, they can sin and have merit. Therefore, one cannot deny them the powers of will-justice, temperance, fortitude, liberality, and obedience.

Will any natural philosopher talk such foolishness? No one, certainly, other than Mahomet who opens Heaven to the lower animals. Therefore, to avoid these absurdities, we have to say that eminent writers sometimes say that brute beasts have been endowed with reason, not because they actually do possess reason, but because they do many things as cleverly as human beings, who have been endowed with reason, usually do them. It is also said they have foresight, because they behave as though they did have it and act as if they were taking foresight as their guide in what they do. Now, foresight is a quality which puts us in a state of readiness so that we can choose the means which are suited to some purpose. Brute beasts, however, do not pursue a purpose and the means which have that purpose in view. They are borne along to it by chance.

This is difficult to understand, but Girolamo Fracastoro has offered a lucid explanation as follows that certain actions of animals go back entirely to natural impulses, but that first one has to explain whether these impulses are linked with cognition or not. Some people say that \[animals\] are directed by the intellect which knows their purposes and cannot make a mistake. This, however, does not remove the difficulty because if they mean that brute beasts are directed by God in such a way that God makes brute beasts do what they do without an intermediary, they are obviously making a mistake. But if they mean that God does this with an intermediary \[acting\] through the animals, the question recurs whether animals do this by a simple impulse of Nature or by means of their cognition which acts as intermediary.
[Page 103]

\[Fracastoro\] therefore cleverly and learnedly replies that when God created the universe, once He had provided it with something like an inborn heat, with the same moral perfection and power with which He made everything He also made their individual intrinsic characters capable of attaining the purposes due and appropriate to the whole. This is why it turns out that individual things have another purpose in mind and do many things of their own accord, while Nature and the universal order have a different end in view. The result is that that generative heat can effect such wonderful things, and warm, attract, and repel, and give shapes to parts \[of the body\], and produce paths, receptacles, locations., orderly arrangements, connections, and indeed the soul, just as if it understood the purposes of each individual. It also seems that even trees and plants make, guard, and defend their leaves, buds, flowers, fruits, and seeds with wonderful ingenuity, and it is certain that these things are done by things which have no recognition \[of why they are doing them.\]

But it has to be said that these things are done by things the things I mentioned, partly per accidens and partly per se-\'per accidens\' to the extent that each one has a particular individual character, but \'per se\' to the extent that each of them is a part of a universal Nature which God has ordained for a purpose He knows. Thus, (says Fracastoro), brute beasts, too, do certain things, not because they know why \[they do them\] and are doing them, so to speak, \'per accidens\' because they are particular individuals, but because they are parts of a universal Nature set in order by God: and they do things \'per se\' and are intent on purposes which are known, not to themselves, but to God.

For example, when a bird\'s eggs are fully grown, she is stimulated by their weight and her discomfort, and wants to lay them. This is the aim the bird knows. Another is the aim which the universe has in mind, that is, preservation of the species. This aim is not known to the bird, but is known to God and follows, so to speak, from the other one. The bird, therefore, lays her egg \'per se\' because of the stimulus, but does so \'per accidens\' in respect of her offspring. She lays her offspring in respect of herself because she is this \[particular\] bird, but she lays it in respect of the universe because she is a part of it. This is why the bird has a strong desire to lay her egg in a safe place-\'per accidens\', to preserve the species, but \'per se\' because she loves the egg as a part of herself. Hence other instances of \'taking note\' \[subnotiones\], too, happen to animals according to the perceptible forms they receive of things they need for this purpose

Therefore, (says Fracastoro), the great wisdom and foresight of Almighty God can be seen, who, when He was about to give order to the whole universe, gave every individual character a particular mode of behaviour. They do not know the reasons \[for the way they behave\], but they do everything in the way which results in \[the fulfilment of\] the aims which He alone knows, and in accordance with this mode of behaviour, they do the other things we think are so wonderful. From this, it must seem that the foresight which appears in brute beasts does not actually exist, but is merely a kind of impulse of Nature and the order of the universe, established by God. (Anyone who is going to find this unsatisfactory should read Andreas Libavius\'s disputation, \'A consideration of the understanding of beasts\' in Part 2 of his Remarkable Things which I acquired when it was first published.)

Book 2, Question 21

Can evil spirits deprive a human being of feeling, or make him sleep for a very long time, or go without food?
[Page 104]

As far as the first \[point\] is concerned, my question is not whether an evil spirit can cause a body to have no power of feeling, but whether he can actually cause something which is sensitive by nature not to feel, for example, a body subjected to torture not to feel pain. In his book on the Egyptian mysteries, just before the middle, Iamblichus, speaking about pagan diviners, testifies that many of these soothsayers were not burned when fire was applied to them or, if they were burned, they did not feel the burning or any of the other tortures; and he adds that this happened because there was a god blowing from within \[them\], driving away the fire and quenching everything else. Who was this god of the magician Iamblichus other than an evil spirit, some kind of Eurycles?

What were these diviners other than workers of harmful magic \[malefici\]? Did pagans think the edge of a sword is blunted by a magical incantation in such a way that it is unable to graze the skin lightly when it touches it? There is no lack of people who, to do away with this superstition, note that God often suspended the power of a flame, of water, of wild animals, and similar quite dreadful torments by means of a miracle, and rendered them all harmless to the martyrs, but that when they had been delivered to the axe or the sword He did not restrain the power of the sword, so that the nations would understand that these things must be ascribed to a miracle and not to magic.

I am very much inclined to have my doubts whether this is true or not. This is not because of the passage from St Paul on which certain people rely-Hebrews 11.34, \'They stopped up the mouths of lions, they have quenched the assault of fire, they have put to flight the edge of the sword\', where what it says about the fire, the lions, and the edge of the sword is ascribed to a miracle. This, however, does not alter \[my opinion\], because the accurate reading is \'they escaped \[the edge of the sword\]\', as is clear from the Greek text and the Syriac translation.

The Apostle also speaks about holy people in the Old Testament who, we read, were not snatched away by a miracle because the edge of the sword was blunted, but because they miraculously escaped the swords of the enemy-some of them, such as Elias, in time of peace, but others, such as Samson, Caleb, Joshua, David, and the Maccabees, etc.-during a battle. No, other things change my opinion, because I also remember \[the passage\] about the woman struck seven times with a sword, but to no effect. The pagans also used to refer everything to an incantation, albeit falsely. I have read two other better explanations of this \[phenomenon\]: (i) the cutting edge of the sword was impeded, which could be ascribed to the negligence of the executioner; (ii) the sword is the foremost and entirely legal and regular instrument of avenging justice.

In our own day, certain irreligious soldiers think they will be invulnerable if they enchant their weapons by means of a particular ritual or, with sacrilegious daring, they stab, split, or break up the image on a crucifix, (see \[Battista\] Codronchi, Illnesses Caused by Poisons Book 3, chapter 18), or if they wear the linen garment they call \'Hell\'s vest\' under their clothes which has ghastly pictures drawn on it, or if they wear certain amulets \[periapta\] inscribed with various magical characters, or \[if they wear\] prayers which very blasphemously and untruthfully they attribute to St Leo or Charlemagne, marked with \'the Great Names of God, (which is how magicians refer to them).

I knew a lawstudent called Quirinus, a distinguished Bachelor \[of Laws\], who, putting his trust in one of these parchments, joined in quarrels and fights, often received blows without ever being wounded, and in the end was killed by a very slight wound at a party in Rome in 1572 or 1573. In this connection I should refer to what the author of The Ant Hill writes about Scafius. \'The principal author of acts of harmful magic \[maleficiorum\] in the territory of Bern was someone called Scafius. He was bold enough to boast about this in public, \[saying\] that he could make himself look like a mouse in the eyes of his rivals whenever he wanted and slip away out of the hands of his deadly enemies, and this, it is said, is how he escaped from the hands of his mortal enemies. But divine justice wanted to put an end to his roguery, and at length his enemies discovered him sitting gingerly on a stove near a window; and since he had no fear of them while they were laying siege to him, they ran him through with their swords and spears without warning via the window, and he died a miserable death in that place because of his crimes\'. Thus \[Johannes\] Nider, \[Formicarius 5.4.\]
[Page 105]

This is how the evil spirit deludes his followers. Every day, workers of harmful magic \[malefici\] escape the fierceness of their tortures upon the rack because they overcome all their torments either with a smile, or sleep, or silence. An astonishing story belonging to this genre is told by \[Claude\] Caron. A fifty-year old women whom he knew made light of it, in the way \[I have described\], when melted lard was poured all over her body and every part of her body was pulled apart. When she was taken off the rack she felt no pain, and she was unhurt and uninjured except that her big toe had been pulled off during the torture and had not been restored, although the damage did not hurt or hinder her at all. But even as she made sport of all her torments and obstinately denied everything, Satan, who had accused of her harmful magic via the mouth of a possessed woman, killed her in prison by tearing out her throat, (Antichrist Revealed, Fr. Bernard Olivier, who was Provincial of the Society of Jesus for the Spanish Netherlands at the time, told me that in 1599 a little witch-girl \[puellulam strigatricem\] was arrested near Amiens.

She used not to feel it, he said, when her feet were very savagely scorched and she was severely whipped until, on the advice of a particular priest, they placed a wax image of the Blessed Lamb against her neck. Then her evil spirit\'s conjuring-tricks were forcibly removed by the power of the sacred medicament, and she began to be fully aware of the force of the pain. Consequently, it is clear that this freedom from pain, which \[Alonso\] Tostado also acknowledges in question 354 \[of his commentary\] on Genesis chapter 13, does not arise from any physical cause, but is introduced \[into someone\] by the agency of an evil spirit; and Bartolomeo della Spina tells us in Night-Flying Witches, chapter 31, that there can be no doubt that the well-known witches\' \[sagarum\] ointment which they use for their transvections does not even have a natural power to send them to sleep, and in their ignorance of the \[actual\] causes, they usually attribute this \[inability to feel pain\] to a miracle or \[their own blamelessness.

It may happen, however, because of some remarkable strength of body, or the steadfastness of mind which both pagans and Christians have not infrequently exhibited. Let me copy out from Silius Italicus \[a passage\] about the steadfastness of one man, Marcus Attilius Regulus.

\'There was no end to the fires, the glowing hot steel, the whips Which cut his body to shreds everywhere with innumerable lashes, The murderous hands, the deadliness poured deep into his marrow, The flames visible in the midst of his wound. It was a dreadful thing to see and is a dreadful thing to relate. His limbs were stretched by \[the torturers\'\] savage craft, As far as the engines of torture would permit; and when all his blood had gushed out, His bones smoked and burned after his bones had melted. But the man\'s spirit remained unbroken. He rose above his pains And laughed, as though he were a spectator of them. He loudly rebuked the torturers for being tired, And loudly demanded to be crucified like his master\', \[Punica 1.171-181\].

Some of them use ointments and natural drugs made from poppy juice, nightshade, and similar things which throw the humours into confusion, procure a deep sleep, stop up the ends of the nerves, stupefy the senses, and hinder sensation. (Dioscorides attributes this power to the Memphite stone from Egypt). But most of them rely on a pact they make with an evil spirit, and amulets \[periaptis\] made of little Bacchic faces, or from the ashes or the nails of slaughtered unbaptised children; or they swallow a king bee; or they tie to their body small pieces of parchment \[inscribed\] with a certain type of character. They also whisper a wicked incantation, and examples of all these are provided by those who have written about the sentences \[handed down\] by criminal courts. One can see the similar operation of an evil spirit in the identifying sign of marks which the Devil imprints on witches \[strigibus\]. This \[mark\] is so devoid of sensation that driving a very long needle into it causes not the slightest pain and draws no blood. Others have written a great deal about this, \[but\] I shall copy out what \[Nicholas\] Rémy says.
[Page 106]

\'Isabelle Pardée was arrested in Épinal on 7th May 1588 on a charge of fortunetelling \[sortilegii\]. She revealed to the mayor of the city the part of her body which had been marked in this fashion by her evil spirit, and he had the idea of carrying out a test \[to see\] whether what people used to say about its insensitivity was true. So, he ordered a small pin to be thrust into it and rammed down as far as it would go. This was done in front of quite a large crowd of people. No blood at all appeared from the wound and the wise-woman \[saga\] gave those who were watching not the slightest sign that she was feeling pain. On 30th October 1590, Claude Bogart in Porrentruy was about to be put to the torture again.

Her skin had been shaved in accordance with the usual practice, and because of this a scar on top of her bare forehead became visible. The investigating judge therefore suspected the truth-namely, that this was an imprint of her evil spirit\'s fingernail, which used to be hidden by her hair before \[she was shaved\]. He ordered a small pin to be thrust very deeply into it, and when this had been done it was seen that she had no sense of being hurt, and that no blood, or very little, flowed from the wound. But she persisted in denying the truth and kept on saying that the numbness stemmed from an old blow from a stone. Not long afterwards, however, when she had been put to the torture, she not only admitted that \[the mark\] had been inflicted on her by an evil spirit, but she also gave a detailed account of the very many other things she had suffered most undeservedly at his hands. Very recently, on 14th June 1591, at Essey, which is a village a mile away from the city, the local landowner was present and ordered the recorder to put Mugette, who was charged with divination \[sortilegii\] to the test. So, this man stripped off all her clothes in order to see if he could find the identifying mark anywhere. This finally made its appearance on her left thigh, looking very much like a wart. He probed it with a knife as deeply as possible, \[but\] could not draw or force a word from Mugette, or even a drop of blood from the wound, although when he lightly stabbed the place next to the blemish, she emitted a great cry of pain and blood flowed out profusely\'. Thus Rémy, Book 1, chapter 5.

In the book by Claude Caron I have already recommended, he writes that the mark of the notorious wise-woman \[sagae\], Bayaronne, which was well hidden on her left thigh, very indistinctly outlined, the size of a penny-piece with, as it were, fine threads running out from its centre to the periphery, was entirely and completely insensitive. I also suspect that the sign of a double piece of coal on the feet of a woman from Narbonne was \[such a\] mark. (I recount this story, taken from \[Jean\] Gerson, Book 3 or 5, later.) It is common for this mark to take various shapes-a hare\'s footprint, a toad\'s hand, a small black chain. Just such a mark was seen on the back of Jean Del Vaulx, and Pierre Dheure, the investigating judge in the affair, noticed something remarkable. If you ran a needle or a pen-nib quite deeply into it, the accused did not feel any pain and did not ask what was going on. But if you threatened his chain-mark from behind at a distance with a finger held out stiff, in such a way that he could not see you, the accused man would suddenly be convulsed as though he were being tortured severely. I find no physical cause for this. I suspect a moral one-that \[the man\'s\] utterly arrogant evil spirit was smarting at being despised, and that this made him angry and furious at the torture of his slave.
[Page 107]

Very long-lasting sleep can also be produced by narcotic drugs: for example, the sleep of the peasant in Germany, who passed a whole autumn and winter asleep; the people who got drunk at the feast of Venus Apaturia and, according to Eudemus, slept for many days; and \[the sleep\] of Epimenides of Crete. While looking for a sheep, he went into a cave during the midday heat, was overcome by deep sleep, and slept, according to Pausanias, for 77 years, (67, according to Eudemus, and slightly fewer, according to others). Later on he woke up, thought he had had forty winks, and began to look for his little sheep. Perhaps there is a natural explanation for why these people slept, although in the case of Epimenides I should rather attribute it to an evil spirit. The sleep of the demi-gods of Sardis was a fairy-story, because these people were actually dead. They seemed to be sleeping and to be woken up because of a conjuring-trick by evil spirits. One kind of long sleep \[such as this has sometimes happened because of a miracle and has been sent by God, examples being that of the seven holy sleepers, (see Laurent Sauer), and that of five others, (see Paul the Deacon).

The final point I mentioned \[in my initial question\] was about going without food. There can be no doubt the Devil is able to cause someone to go without food for many months because this is something which can happen naturally, and Indians say that they drive away hunger with the help of the herb Coca which they keep in their mouth, (Pedro Cieza, History of Peru Part 1, chapter 96 and Agustin de Zárate, History of Peru Book 1, chapter 8). In his Epitome, the heir to Dioscorides, \[Pietro\] Mattioli, also writes as follows. \'The Scythians have found a Scythian herb growing round Boeotia. It is very sweet to the taste and they are full of praise for it because when they have it in their mouth, they feel neither hunger nor thirst. Something they call \"hippice\" is excellent and produces the same effect in horses, too. With these herbs, it is said, Scythians can go for twelve days without food and without feeling thirsty\'. \[Cornelius\] Gemma acknowledges that abstinence from food can last naturally over a period of many years.

In his book on flesh, Hippocrates denies that hunger can be prolonged naturally beyond the seventh day. He should be understood \[as saying\] that the stomach and hungry intestine are devoid of chylous humour into which natural heat can go, and the usual result is that the intestine increases in size and so people die. But if, either from the malady I have just mentioned or from another cause, a raw, sluggish, viscous phlegmatic humour is left behind in the body, the \[body\'s\] natural heat uses it as nourishment until every bit of the humour is consumed. The same thing will happen if, out of the moisture at the top of the brain, a kind of thin material full of phlegm descends from the head into the body and during that time serves the body as nourishment, because under those circumstances anything which would be exhaled or would flow from the body would be restored by this humour which would be continually changed into nourishment, and so the person would not need food until this humour was entirely consumed.

I should think this was the reason for the abstinence from food and \'nonimbibing, (as Plautus puts it), of Roger Bacon\'s English girl who, he said, ate nothing for twenty years. \[It also explains\] the French priest who abstained from all food and drink for two years in the time of Pope Nicholas v, and the girl from Speyer who, on the orders of Emperor Ferdinand, was closely watched and observed by many physicians in 1540 to see if it was true that, after a serious illness, she had already persisted in abstaining from food for more than four years, but was still physically strong and healthy and had not lost weight, and her body was merely covered in phlegmatic scurf. (She would, however, frequently burst into tears). She had the usual dirt in her nostrils and ears, and she used to sleep and stay quiet for quite a long time. But she did not discharge faeces or urine either from her anus or from her female parts. This gave rise to a great deal of argument-among the Italians, by Simone Porzio, and among the Germans by an eye-witness, one of the physicians who was observing the girl, Gerhard Buchholtz. If these things can happen naturally, why can\'t that jackof-all-trades, the Devil, carry them out with the same industriousness \[he uses\] to engraft other perverse states of mind on the body? (I shall point this out in connection with a girl from Arabia, from \[a work\] by St Prosper \[of Aquitaine\], in Book 6, chapter 2, section 3.
[Page 108]

One has to be very careful, however, not to put the fasts of holy people, which pass the bounds of Nature, into this category: for example, \[those\] of St Mary of Egypt, St Catherine of Siena, St Lutgarde \[of Aywières\], St Marie of Oignies, St Catherine the Wonderful, (see \[Laurent\] Sauer), and of St Simon Stock, the Carmelite, whom the Mother of God fed with heavenly manna for six years, (see Menaud des Rosiers and Roland Bouchier on his life). God, you see, who created them, sustained them at that time without physical food by His power. (See the remarks on these points in \[Gianfrancesco\] Pico della Mirandola, Foreknowledge of Things Book 3, chapter 5). Roger Bacon, however, irreligiously ascribes all this to influxes from the stars. If he is right, why do the stars which send their influx down to everyone in general bestow this ability to do without food simply on one person and not on someone else? Is it because the body of this person alone has been disposed in this particular way? But what is this disposition which is the only one to give passage to the influx? What great force and inflexibility in others \[is designed\] to keep it out? Still, I have already spent enough time elsewhere on the nonsense in Bacon\'s work which needs to be rebutted. (On this drawn-out abstinence from food, see Giglio \[Gregorio\] Giraldi, Dialogue 17. You can find this in \[Jan\] Gruter\'s Critical Thesaurus.

Book 2, Question 22

Can magical practices, with the help of evil spirits, change one sex into another?

I am not talking about removal of parts of the body-I have done this alreadybut about the actual change of male sex into female, or female into male. In his Cosmocratic Art, Book 1, \[Cornelius\] Gemma, based on what contemporary physicians say, acknowledges that such a change can happen naturally: \'We read that many men have been made out of women\'. In Book 6, Part 8 of his Epidemics, Hippocrates writes that in Abdera, Pitheus\'s wife, Phaetusa, was very near giving birth when her husband was sent into exile. For several months her menstrual flow ceased, she endured great pains in every part of her body, and her physical appearance changed into that of a man. Her body became manlike, (\'owµa ávδpwIn\'), she turned hairy all over, grew a beard, and her voice became quite rough. The same thing happened in Thrace to Gorgippus\'s wife, Anamisia, and in Book 42 Livy says the same thing happened to a woman from Spoleto during the second Punic war.

Pliny (Book 7, chapter 4) says, \'Transformation of females into males is not a fairy-story. I have discovered in the annals that during the consulship of Publius Licinius Crassus and Gaius Cassius Longinus \[171BC\], a girl was changed into a boy under \[the eyes\] of her parents and, at the augurs\' order, was taken away to an uninhabited island. Licinius Mucianus has recorded that he himself saw a man called Arescon in Argos, who used to be called Arecusa. She had actually married a man, but then grew a beard, produced a man\'s organs of generation, and married a woman. \[Mucianus\] also says he saw a boy in Smyrna who had suffered the same fate. I myself saw a person in Africa who had turned into a man on the day of her marriage-Lucius Cossitius, a citizen of Thysdrus.

He was still alive while I was writing this\'. That is what Pliny writes. In his \[Attic\] Nights Book 9, chapter 4, Aulus Gellius says, \'In this collection of marvels, however, it is a good idea to note down something which Pliny the Elder, a man who had a great reputation in his own day because of his talent and position, recorded in the seventh Book of his Investigations into Nature-not something he had heard or read, but something he knew was true and something he himself had seen. The words I have quoted below are his own, taken from that Book, and they certainly make one hesitate to reject or ridicule the ancient poets\' very well-known song about Caenis and Caeneus\'. This is what Gellius says, substituting the words used earlier by Pliny; and when he talks about \'this wonder\' \[miraculosum\], he has used the word in its broad sense to refer to what is unusual and wonderful. (One should read Ovid\'s Metamorphoses on Caenus and Tiresias.)
[Page 109]

But what need is there for fairy-stories when histories are sufficient? In the last chapter \[31\] of Book 3 of The City of God, St Augustine, using pagan writings, mentions that women have been changed into men and hens into the male sex. (Later on, from Ausonius, I shall confirm the same of birds). Still, let me continue to talk about human beings. I come to a period closer to our own. In his History of Naples, Gioviano Pontano tells the story of a woman in Gaeta who was married to a fisherman for fourteen years and changed into a man: and another woman, called Emilia, who had been married to Antonio Spensa, a citizen of Eboli, changed into a man after twelve years of marriage, dissolved their union, married a woman, and procreated children. \[Pontano\] adds that another woman, having borne one son to her husband, suddenly grew male organs of generation and, not being a woman any longer, left her husband, married another woman, and got children by her.

Coccio Sabellico tells several similar stories about a number of people. I shall omit these and record two which have happened in Spain in my own time. They have been very faithfully recorded in Spanish by my countryman, Antonio de Torquemada. Amato Lusitano is the source \[of the following\]. In the town of Esgueira in Portugal, nine leagues from Coimbra, there lived a man of good family who had a daughter called Maria Pacecho. When she was of the age at which she would begin to menstruate, a male member burst from her places instead of a fluid humour-it had either lain hidden inside her or came out of her in some other fashion-and so a post-pubertal young man suddenly appeared out of the girl. When she became a man, she also took the name Manoel Pacecho. He went on board ship, set out for India, performed brave deeds, acquired the reputation of being an active soldier, as well as great wealth, and on his return to his own country, took as his wife a woman of good family. Amato makes no mention of children, but says he remained beardless, and that his face had a feminine look to it-signs that his manliness was not altogether complete.

Secondly, Torquemada tells us he had heard from a friend, a man with a considerable reputation and who was entirely trustworthy, that not far from the city of Beneventana in Spain, the wife of a peasant who was moderately well-off was treated in less than kindly fashion by her husband because she was barren. Sick of being treated badly, she ran away one night, dressed as a male house-servant and, thus accoutred, she passed herself off as a man in various places and earned her living by working as a servant. After some time, either because the natural heat \[she had\] inside her would have this power, or because her imagination, produced and strengthened by her continually wearing men\'s clothes and doing men\'s work, acquired this power, the fact is she discovered she had turned into a man. So, having been a wife for a long time, she decided to act as a husband and joined herself in matrimony to a woman. Her secret remained hidden for a long time because \'he\' did not dare to tell it to anyone, until somebody who had known her very well before noticed that her face was very similar to that of the peasant\'s wife who had run away. He asked whether, by chance, she was the woman\'s brother, and she told him the whole story, just as it had happened, and this is how it came to light.

\[Battista\] Fregoso writes the following. \'During the reign of Ferdinand I of Naples, Ludovico Guarna, a citizen of Salerno, brought up five daughters. The two oldest were called Francesca and Carola, and when they reached the age of fifteen, both girls\' genitalia burst out into what one expects of men. They changed the way they dressed and were considered to be men and took the names Francesco and Carolo. Likewise, in Eboli, during the same king\'s reign, there was a girl who had been betrothed to a man for four years already. On the first night of her marriage, when she went to bed with her husband and, either because of the vigorous friction or for some other unknown reason, the membranes which made her look like a woman were broken and men\'s parts came out. She went home, sued for a return of her dowry, and thereafter was considered to be a man\'.
[Page 110]

I have set these \[anecdotes\] in front of you so that Phlegon\'s narrative may seem less incredible. He says, \'A young woman called Philotis, who was born in Smyrna, was given in marriage by her parents to a man once she had reached \[the appropriate\] age, but produced a male member and turned into a man. In Laodicea, which is in Syria, too, there was a woman called Aeteta, and while she was actually having sex with her husband she turned into a man and called herself Aetetus. \[This happened\] when Macrinus was Governor of Athens and Lucius Lamia and Aelianus the Elder were consuls in Rome\'. (Phlegon, one of Hadrian\'s freedmen, adds that he has seen this person). In Book 1, chapter 21 of his Essays, Michel de Montaigne testifies that in his own time there was a girl in Vitry le François called Marie. She was dancing rather energetically when, all of a sudden, male organs burst out of her, and she was changed into a man. During the sacrament of confirmation, the Bishop of Soissons gave her the name \'Germain\', and this is what people started to call her. Later on, a beard grew on her chin. She lived a long time-into very old age-but never married. \[Montaigne\] attributes the change to her vivid imagination, but I add that her physical constitution was tending in that direction.

The author of Antimaeologium writes that \'in Auch in Gascony he had seen a man of sixty years and more, grey-headed, strong, with a full head of hair, who had been a girl up to the age of fifteen. But in the end, it so happened that when her thin ligaments burst and her pudenda emerged, she changed her sex, since she had never menstruated before\'. (See André du Laurens in his Anatomical Account, Book 7, question 8). Physicians-Galen in The Customary Usage of Parts \[of the Body\], and his book on the ways in which the anatomy is configured, Aegineta, Avicenna, al-Razi, and the rest-also point out that the reason Nature causes this to happen is not obscure. She is always aiming at what is more perfect.

A woman is like a monster, a kind of incomplete man. When her periods are held back for too long, her voice becomes rather strong, her skin turns hairy, and the rest of her physical condition gains strength; and all this transformation lacks is the outward appearance of the seminal vessels which are inside her. When it has grown and the fatty covering in which it has been wrapped has been broken, the thing which is called \'myrtle\', \'bride\', xantwpis xai inódepuis \[clitoris and prepuce\], and abathara or al-bandar in Arabic, is pushed out into a penis, which it closely resembles, and, having been wrapped up before, is now thrust out by an impulse from \[the body\'s\] pressing natural heat.

That is what those people say, but I have the very strong suspicion that these women turned into men not so much because they were lacking female attributes as because their male gender was close to their female gender, the result being that they were formed as hermaphrodites, with the male gender predominant, and this is why they were taken to be men. After I had written this many years ago, this year, 1606, I came across André du Laurens\'s painstaking work, Anatomical Account, and I was extremely pleased because it seemed to me that in \[Book 7\], question 8 this very learned physician carefully shows that the common opinion of physicians about a sex-change lying hidden in a woman is untrue, and that the genitalia of both genders are quite different not only in where they are located, but also in number, shape, and structure. In connection with this change he adds, \'In my opinion this is unnatural and scarcely credible, and if it does happen sometimes, it is very likely that the genitalia of both sexes exist in them.
[Page 111]

During the early years they lie hidden because \[the person\'s natural\] heat is weak, but in the end they burst out through the Èxháuxes \[\'shiners\'\] which have become prominent and shine forth; or one\'s thought has to be that there are some women who are very hot from the moment they are born and Nature encourages them in such a way that she reproduces the shape of their clitoris as a penis, and in consequence it sticks out and is distended like a penis, \[then\] becomes flaccid, and \[so\] counterfeits a man\'s generative organ and deceives those who are rather ignorant. Midwives frequently make a mistake \[about a child\'s sex\] because of the faulty formation of its genitalia-namely, when the penis is rather short and quite deeply concealed, as though in a cleft, and the testes are hidden away-and in consequence they can scarcely distinguish \[the child\'s\] sex. \[Séverin\] Pineau writes that in Paris in 1577 in the Rue Saint-Denis a woman gave birth at night to a son who was hastily baptised because he was weak, and was called Jeanne, as though he were a girl. After a few days, first his mother, then \[other people\] realised, not without great astonishment, that she was a son and gave him the name Jean. It is therefore easy to deceive the common people, who are rather ignorant.

That\'s what Du Laurens says. In Book 8, question 1 he tells us that Nature intends to produce women no less than she does men, and that it is unworthy of a natural philosopher to say that a female is a mistake and a lapse of Nature. Every perfection in the natural world has to be gauged from its goal, and the female is formed the way she is because otherwise the human race could not be preserved. Certainly, people say that a female is something unnatural, \[but\] I have always thought they are not being particularly respectful about the creation of the first woman. So, one ought to say that Nature is always reaching for what is more complete, not because she always intends to produce a man, but because when she turns her attention to a man, she intends to produce something which is entirely complete, and when \[she turns her attention to a female, she is also trying to make her entirely complete. Nevertheless, if Nature could do what so many authors claim, I should think that, with God\'s permission, that very cunning individual, \[the Devil\], relying on natural causes, can do it as well. Certainly, I should be happy to attribute to him those fictitious miracles which pagans attribute to their false divinities. Phlegon tells the same story about Caerneus, the Lapith, as Ovid does; Antoninus Liberalis, quoting Book 2 of Nicander\'s Metamorphoses, writes about more of them: Hypermnestra was sold for her value as a woman and later the gods, as a gift, changed her into a man; Siproeta, a Cretan, became a man when she saw Minerva washing; Galatea wrongly called her daughter Leucippus when she should have been called Leucippa, but when she changed sex, by the kindness of Phaetian Latona, she deserved the name her mother had given her.

Second conclusion: I have to believe that it is impossible for Nature and for an evil spirit to make a woman out of a man. This attempt was made in the case of Sporus by Nero who was utterly devoted to magic, but without any success. It was done by extreme withdrawal of his completed parts into female places, and I do not understand how Nature would degrade \[someone\] this way. I do know that Phlegon told his story about Tiresias in line with fairy-stories, and that Antoninus Liberalis had his doubts when he talked about Siproeta, with the result that you scarcely know which sex she changed from and which sex she changed into. If you also read carefully the whole of Ausonius\'s epigram from Book 1, you should pay attention to what he said about the man who degenerated into the female sex.
[Page 112]

\'A male bird turns into the female of his kind. And a pea-hen who was a peacock stands before your eyes Nor is \[the story\] particularly ancient which \[says that\] in Campanian Benevento One of their young men suddenly became a young woman\', \[69.3-4, 13-14\].

He says a girl was made from a boy and a pea-hen from a peacock, but I don\'t believe it-or at least I find it very difficult to believe. I should have said, rather, that they had both sexes in them, one hidden, the other obvious. In this connection, I also refer to the hare, and to the hyaena which Ovid talks about.

\'We may be surprised that \[the sexes switch, and that a \[female\] hyaena which recently Allowed a male \[hyaena\] to mate with her is now a male\'.

Unless I am mistaken, Pliny recorded that she was endowed with both sexes, Book 8.

Book 2, Question 23

Can an evil spirit give an old man back his youth?

There is no force or power which can bring it about that an old man can cease to be old and be young, or not have reached the age he has reached. Who can give back something which has been done, \[as though\] it had not been done and which has slipped away completely in a succession of things which have happened? But am I bold enough to decide that if God allows him \[to do so\], an evil spirit can cause someone who is already bent, enfeebled, and decrepit with age to regain his strength, and the appearance and sheen of youth: his grey hairs to be changed into a better colour: his cold to a gentle warmth and his torpor into activeness, so that his body can get back everything else of which old age had deprived him? Plainly I do not think what poets relate at all.

Aeschylus wrote about a ram changed into a lamb, and Bacchus\'s nurses renewing their youth. Pherecydes, Simonides, and Lycophron wrote about Jason who was rejuvenated by Medea, and the Greek author of Returns and the Latin poet \[Ovidius\] Naso in his Metamorphoses wrote about Jason\'s father, Aeson. What they say, however, is entirely untrue and repugnant to Nature, since it took place after all his blood had been drawn off through a wound and fresh \[blood\] substituted. It follows, therefore, that the body of a living man first died and was then resuscitated, and this is something reserved to the omnipotence of God. The rest of what \[Ovid\] was talking about could perhaps have happened because of the health-giving potion he drank.

\'His beard and hair Laid aside their greyness and seized hold of the colour black.
