His thinness was routed and fled; his pallor and mustiness went away; His hollow wrinkles were filled with increased flesh; His body became wanton. Aeson was astonished and remembered That he was like this forty years ago\', \[Metamorphoses 7.288-293\].

Everyone says-and it cannot in good faith be denied-that the eagle renews its youthfulness. The divine writer of hymns testified \[to it\]: \'Your youth will be renewed, like that of the eagle\'. Everyone says, \'it is renewed\', although the way it happens does not agree with our \[writers\] and \[those of\] the Jews. A verse in Isaiah is relevant to the same thing. \'Those who hope in the Lord will alter their strength and receive wings, as eagles do\'. The same thing benefits snakes which, (as everyone says), are base and mean in character, with the result that they strip off old age along with that thin \'slough\' of theirs and clothe themselves in youth. Why does our step-mother Hebe \[goddess of youth\] cut off all hope of this kindness from humankind right at the start, such as all the hope which was removed because of the ignorance of the carrier-ass which allowed a snake to drink such a health-giving drug sent to human beings by Jupiter?
[Page 113]

Why can\'t we find natural remedies which are as effective as this? Many, of course, have not yet been discovered and are unknown to humans, although they are known to evil spirits. Restoration of fundamental moisture, moderation of the excessive dryness of old age, substitution of a better sap for the one which has been spoiled, making good everything else which has been ruined and everything necessary for this transformation-none of this is contrary to the law of Nature. Changing the colour of hair is not impossible, either. Many people have recorded seeing white crows.

Finally, there is no shortage of witnesses who confirm the metamorphosis I am now defending. You can read two examples in Torquemada. In Tarento in 1531, there was a centenarian ready for the grave, whose hair was falling out, whose skin was peeling off, and whose nails were dropping out in the wasting away and foulness of old age. But everything changed for the better and from being an old man, he became young \[again\] and lived a further fifty years. In the Castilian part of Rioja, too, a similar thing happened to someone and was very well known because everyone was talking about it, and it was carefully investigated by the chief magistrates of Castile. Monvendro is a town, (once called Sagunto), in the Kingdom of Valencia. Valesco de Tarenta writes that during his lifetime there was an abbess of a convent here who, already a dry-bones, suddenly began to menstruate again. Her teeth grew back, her hair turned dark, the wrinkles and folds in her skin filled out, her pendulous, shrivelled breasts swelled up like those of a young girl, and in the end her features, face, and entire bodily carriage made her look like a young woman. As a result, shocked by the novelty of her situation, out of modesty she began to withdraw from everyone\'s gaze.

What more \[can I say\]? More recent, very trustworthy, writers on Portuguese history give us a long, well-established narrative about an Indian of good family who lived for three hundred and forty years during which the flower of his youth withered three times and blossomed again three times; and there is no lack of people who testify that in the New World there is an island called Bonica on which there flows a spring whose water, more precious than wine, restores youth to old people when they drink it. Pedro Cieza, however, not without good reason, includes a narrative, which originated in a healthier location in the region, about a similar spring on Lucaya: History of Peru Part 2, chapter 41. But whether such a spring flows on Bonica or Lucaya or both, I can easily see that some people find these \[stories\] incredible. But they should reflect. To withdraw one\'s trust in stories of this kind, which the rationality of a guided natural philosophy does not abhor, simply because you yourself have not seen any such thing, is a piece of remarkable impudence and shamelessness. Indeed, those who decide, simply on the basis of their own opinion or approbation, that something has or has not happened somewhere are more or less insane.

Suppose someone comes at this from the opposite direction-that God fixed a particular period for human life in advance and it cannot be prolonged naturally, at least not beyond a hundred and twenty years, as is proved by God\'s words, \'The days of humankind will be a hundred and twenty years\', \[Genesis 6.3\] and likewise by Moses who said, \'I am a hundred and twenty years old today, and I cannot go forward or back\', \[Deuteronomy 31.2\], and that this is confirmed by arithmetical calculation because this number is made up of three times forty, that is, four tens multiplied by three, and so when everything is added together it makes a complete whole-3x4x10.
[Page 114]

This argument is easily dealt with because God did not appoint one and the same period of life for all human beings, but one peculiar to each individual, and the fact is that once a term of life has been extended to them, their life cannot be prolonged naturally by this kind of transformation except with God\'s permission. If He does allow it, many people are agreed that it can surpass even a hundred and twenty years. Those words of God were not said about people of every generation, but only about those who were living at the time who were going to be given this limit as a penance before the Flood. Nor in what he said did Moses claim that he could not live for longer, only that he was unable and unfitted in his dotage to cope with the management of such a large number of people. The argument from numbers I mentioned earlier is one which Francisco Vallés found surprisingly satisfying, to such an extent that he thought he should pursue it at very great length. In fact, however, it is worthless and of no importance because numbers exercise no influence at all upon the human constitution, and our life does not depend on numbers, but on primary and secondary natural causes.

Another argument of this kind is advanced quite forcefully. Human life is preserved by natural moisture, and this keeps on being used up over one\'s lifetime. The power of food to nourish is impaired because it suffers in its turn from our activity and, in the opinion of experienced physicians, the nourishing \[power\] of food cannot restore as much \[energy\] as an old person loses. It is therefore necessary, because of the state of \[old\] age, that that moisture which is starting to be used up entirely, be refreshed quickly, and that the individual\'s proper measure of it, which has decayed and started to age, be restored. I am happy concede this much to those \[who put forward this argument\]. But, they say, physicians think this cannot be done by \[medical\] skill or by Nature. My reply to them is that although the underlying knowledge of how to restore fundamental moisture has perhaps remained unknown to physicians so far, it does not follow from that that it is unknown to an evil spirit, or is something he cannot \[know\], because if he does know it, why can he not, with God\'s permission, put it into practice and make use of his knowledge?

Perhaps you will urge that he could therefore keep a human being alive for ever and thus make him, as it were, immortal. That Egyptian windbag against whom Galen wrote kept on suggesting this, but I am arguing against it and deny the chain of reasoning. It is possible to extend life for many years. Therefore it is possible to extend it for ever because, although a long-lived human being remains subject to death-just as he was also going to be before \[original\] sin, and just as, by the mere will of God, (according to certain people\'s opinion), he then escaped death-so too according to the hypothesis of this argument, if God were to permit an evil spirit to keep on restoring his fundamental moisture, the human being would not then die because under those circumstances God, by means of such a dispensation, would deliver him from the necessity of dying. Secondly, if you take away this particular dispensation, I say it does not look as though God will ever permit this. At length, after several hundred years, the natural force in a human being would perish and be exhausted, and the result would be that no natural remedies an evil spirit could use would be enough to end up restoring the very tottering physical bulk of a constitution which had been, as it were, overthrown. But this would not happen except after a very long time. These points, therefore, do not contradict at all what I said about life\'s being prolonged, not perpetuated. So that you can see the fault in \[these people\'s\] chain of reasoning, consider the \'tree of life\' or, as the Jews call it, \'the timber of lives\'. This tree would produce life in the flower of youth, after \[someone\] had eaten \[its fruit\], and this would last for a period equivalent to many long lives these days. Very eminent writers have justly said that the \[tree\] had this power naturally and that it retained it after humankind\'s \[original\] sin. This is clear from Genesis 3, verse 22 where God builds a wall round Paradise so that Adam cannot enter it again and take \[fruit\] from the tree of life so that he could live for ever. Nevertheless, these same learned men have told us that this tree could not, on that account, have preserved humankind from death for ever.
[Page 115]

I have said this in order to point out the fault, as far as this subject is concerned, in the chain of reasoning \[which says\] that it is possible to prolong life for a long time and that therefore it would be possible \[to prolong it\] for ever.

Those who grant that the tree of life could have kept prolonging life, you see, do not grant that it could have done so in perpetuity, nor has anyone brought a charge against them on this account. Otherwise, the very learned men of this age disagree about what power the tree had and whether the \'for ever\' of Genesis 3, verse 22 should be interpreted specifically as referring to eternal life or to a very long life. I follow those who have interpreted it as referring to eternal life, excluding death completely. (Read \[Benito\] Pereira\'s commentary on Genesis, Book 3, question 3, and Cardinal Bellarmino\'s Controversies Volume 3, Book 1, chapter 19.

Book 2, Question 24.

What power does magic, or an evil spirit, have over a soul which is joined to a body and is animating it?

There are three powers of the soul. I have already said what \[an evil spirit\] can do with respect to the vegetative and sensitive \[powers\] as far as the exterior senses are concerned.

\(i\) He can certainly deceive the sight, the hearing, and the rest of the senses, as is proved by numberless exemplary stories by other people.

\(ii\) An evil spirit uses the internal sense as an instrument to assail or strive against the powers of the soul because he can deceive the fourfold working of the internal sense by throwing the \[passive\] imagination \[phantasia\] into confusion. The general opinion of theologians is that angels cannot change the \[active\] or the \[passive\] imagination by imprinting a new mental impression \[species\] on it, and therefore they cannot cause someone who has been blind from birth to have a correct mental image of the colours of things. But angels can perfectly well fashion some passively imaginary object \[phantasticum\], cast it in front of the eyes, and so thrust that semblance \[of reality\] upon them or, with the help of the \[active\] imagination, mix and jumble together the images of things a person has once seen, and manufacture images of things he has never seen-a chimaera, for example, which is a conflation of a she-goat, a lion, and a snake, or a mountain of silver, \[which is a mixture\] of a mountain and silver; or, once they have stirred up movements and feelings in the body so that the \[passive\] imagination is affected, they have even willed happy things, or sad, which the human has not seen and which have never existed; or, just as one sees in mad or frenzied individuals, they observe the different images of their \[passive\] imagination, caused by the disorder of their humours.

This is how, by adopting vapours suitable for what he has in mind and applying them to an uneven mixture of those same humours, and agitating them, an evil spirit can cause a human being to draw from them any mental images he \[the spirit\] wants and decide he is seeing and hearing things which, in fact, he has never taken in with his eyes or ears. It is from this cause that so many false apparitions, revelations, and ecstasies flow, as we are told by St Augustine, The Care One Should Have for the Dead, chapter 12; \[Francisco de Vitoria, Lectures \[on Theology\], section 35, \'On Magic\', (this makes a reply to the second point); José Anglés, question 3, doubt 5; Fr \[Luis de\] Molina, Commentaries on St Thomas \[Aquinas\], article 3. There is usually power in herbs, if what the botanist \[Pietro\] Mattioli says is true, because in his \'Dedicatory Letter\' he writes as follows.

Democritus mentions achaemenis, an amber-coloured plant without leaves, which grows among the Tradictyli in India. Its root is divided into little lozenges, and when criminals drink it in wine they confess all \[their crimes\] because of the tortures \[they suffer\] at night on account of their delusive imaginings \[that they are seeing\] divine spirits. Adamantis comes from Cappadocia and Armenia. When it is given to lions, they immediately lie on their backs and start breathing heavily. It is said that the origin of the name lies in the fact that, like steel \[adamas\], the plant cannot be crushed. Ophiusa is said to grow in Elephantine in Aethiopia. It has a leaden hue and looks dreadful, and when people drink it, they are terrified by the sight of snakes threatening them, the result of which is that, out of fear, they decide to kill themselves. This is why those guilty of sacrilege are forced to drink it. The antidote to it is palm wine. The story is that potamantis is found on the banks of the River Indus, and if people drink it they are driven mad because of the extraordinary things they see.
[Page 116]

(According to Dioscorides, if you drink an eighth of an ounce of the root of manic nightshade in wine, it makes you see various sights and images which are not pleasant). Theangelis grows on Mt. Lebanon in Syria, and if people eat it, they can foretell the future. Gelotophyllis grows in Bactria and along the \[River\] Borysthenes. If this is drunk along with wine and myrrh, they say one sees various things and cannot stop laughing until one drinks palm wine to which pine-nuts, pepper, and honey have been added. (Crateuas says that if oenothera is tied to their throat or hung from their collar, it tames the wild behaviour of all living things.) Appian of Alexandria writes that when the Parthians, who had been put to flight by Antony, were hard-pressed by hunger, and came across a certain herb, those who ate it would not remember or understand anything else at all, but would keep on digging up stones as though they were about to undertake some great task and then, raving and vomiting up bile, they would die\'. All this, it is clear, happened because their imagination had been damaged.

\(iii\) The Devil can and does, every day, unsettle the \[physical\] spirits \[spiritus and humours of the body\'s organs, and its internal sense, and throws into confusion the organs of the faculty of lust and anger, while at the same time disturbing the physical parts of \[a person\'s\] body. \[He does this in such a way that thoughts of various sins arise from this \[confusion\]. He does not do this by sending a new image to the \[active\] imagination, you see, or even the thought itself by forming it together with the feeling \[it provokes\]. He simply makes the potentiality \[of committing sin\] happen at the same time as the sight of the object, and thus prompts images which stimulate a memory of pleasures, as well as exciting and stirring up the members of generation and their seed, as the magician Marcus tells us in Psellus.

Since this sensory appetite lacks freedom and is drawn forcibly towards its object by a particular kind of compulsion, it is inevitable that the Old Fox can push it into doing something wicked, so much so that the sensory appetite is unable to gain that object. A movement of this kind, however, is entirely devoid of sin and when our will strongly resists it, we are given an opportunity of \[achieving\] greater merit and reward. This should be noted carefully so that \[Johann\] Godelmann, \[Lambert\] Daneau, and other heretics may not deceive anyone when they ascribe these movements of lust to sin and also, (which is quite ridiculous), include someone who does not want \[to sin\] and is actively resisting it. But these are the results of denying free will and misunderstanding the character of original sin.

\(iv\) An evil spirit can assist the memory by adapting its organ to receive images more easily and retain them for longer, either using local motion to drive away things which are harmful \[to his purpose\], or by applying things which are helpful to it. He can impair it a great deal by methods which cause conflict \[within it\], as Theodas did in the Life of Barlaam and Josaphat by \[John of\] Damascus. I think that Aethalides had this kind of memory, (\[Diogenes\] Laertius, Life of Pythagoras = 8.\[4\]), as did Apollonius of Tyre, (Suidas praises it), and a German cleric had an inability to remember \[stemming\] from this kind \[of demonic interference\], (\[Battista\] Fregoso, Exemplary Stories Book 1, chapter 6). Another lengthy period of forgetfulness happened to the Englishman, Simon Thurway. \[His memory\] drained away because of God\'s just punishment.

He was a very clever logician and had a great reputation as Professor of the Liberal Arts in Paris, \[but\] he was filled more with knowledge which puffs a man up than with charity which edifies and was not afraid to boast in the Schools that he knew the whole of Christ\'s law and could demolish it by overruling it. But he was struck so suddenly by forgetfulness that he could not remember his basic letters, could not recite the Lord\'s Prayer, and did not know how to read. While Nicholas of Durham was still a young man, he saw him being taught his letters by his own son, and \'\[Nicholas\]\', says Matthew Paris in Book 7 of his History of the English, \'told me about it while I was writing this\'. It happened in about 1201. (Trithemius says that Simon came from Tournai, but he is wrong because \[Simon\] was an Englishman from Cornwall. He is also mentioned in Book 15 of Polydore \[Vergil\'s\] History of England).
[Page 117]

Perhaps God induced this forgetfulness in this instance by sending it into him at the hands of a wicked angel. The Wicked One could also, (as I said), help the memory with natural remedies, as a physician does, \[or\] by giving advice, as a choir-master does, with professional skill and instructions, \[and\] just as, in our own times, there exist tracts by many people who have professed, without superstition, some technique \[to help\] the memory. Among these uneducated works is Thamus, by Alexander Dickson Arelius. It is obscure, but not without wit and shrewdness, and has been defended, (but not with the same cleverness), against biters from Cambridge by Heius Scepsius. (It was published in Leiden).

\(v\) He can improve the understanding and make it more acute as far as the workings of one\'s natural ability and judgement are concerned by making the ways in which the organ is disposed more serviceable, namely, by using to local motion to dispel the denser humours, or by applying natural agents as remedies, or by purging the sensual parts of the \[physical\] spirit \[spiritus\] and drawing them to the organs by the second of the ways I mentioned, or by multiplying those same spirits \[spiritibus\] by purifying the blood. The more, the better, the more purgative the spirits \[spiritus\] are, and the better and more free from impediments the organs of the internal sense are, inevitably the more unimpeded and more productive the understanding becomes. These, however, are, so to speak, the special gifts of good angels. Evil spirits \[mali daemones\] on the other hand, love to pour darkness into human minds by making the humours dense and the \[physical\] spirits \[spiritus\] few, weak, and impure.

They give their followers mischievous drugs by means of which they rob people of their right mind, just as not uncommonly they do in the case of those whom they have obsessed. They lead people astray with wicked suggestions, falsely persuading them of various things: at one time saying that something is illicit, and at another that in fact it is allowed; or that something is a grave sin when actually it is venial, (the intention here being to torture people with scruples); or inculcating the idea that things which are contrary to divine and human laws are actually lawful; or saying that things are contemptible and trivial when in fact they are lethal and ought to make one very afraid, (this being done to make people\'s consciences lax and wanton).

Perhaps you will ask whether an evil spirit can teach the arts and sciences. There should be no doubt at all that he can if he wants \[to do so\] and if God gives him permission. \[He can\] certainly do so by speaking and giving visible expression to what he has in mind, not only by appearing in visible form and speaking to someone, (which St Thomas \[Aquinas\] does not deny he can do), but also by speaking and making suggestions \[to someone\] internally after \'illuminating\' his understanding. I do not see why this \'illumination\', which has an evil goal in view, should not be attributed to an evil spirit, since this \[kind of\] realisation has nothing to do with God and is not done with any genuine cleansing of the mind. Consequently, whether it should be called an illumination or an illusion, or whether it should be called something else such as, (for example), an unadorned manifestation of the truth, is undoubtedly going to be a question of nomenclature. In \[Laurent\] Sauer\'s Life of St Norbert, \[Volume 3\], chapter 27, under \'6th June\', \[an evil spirit\] made one uneducated person very skilled in Holy Scripture. Today we know that many Anabaptists, by consuming a great deal of Scripture rather than food, and learning it by heart, think that what they receive is also a suitable way to confirm their errors. Who, other than the Devil, tells them this?
[Page 118]

Johannes Nider has left us a passage about the initiation of vampire-witches \[lamiarum\], and in this \[passage\] there is a confession by one of them. \'We lay siege to little children who have not yet been baptised, or even to those who have been baptised, especially if they are not protected by the sign of the cross and prayers. We kill them with our rituals while there are in their cradles or lying beside their parents, and afterwards it is thought they have been smothered or have died in some other way. We take them secretly from their graves, boil them down in a cauldron until the flesh has almost entirely come away from the bones and one can swallow it and drink it. From the more solid part of this matter, we make an ointment which is suitable for what we want to do-our \[magical\] practices and our shape-changing. We fill a flask or a bottle with the runnier humour, and once anyone has drunk any of this, to the accompaniment of a few rituals, he immediately becomes a participant in and a master of our sect\', \[The Ant-Hill 5.3\] This is the confession of a woman. It is followed by another which seems to be that of a man. It adds the other ceremonies used in their abominable initiation and advancement to the hellish office of \'Master\'. This is the order in which I was led astray. First, on Sunday, before the holy water is blessed, the person who is about to become an apprentice enters the church with the Masters \[of the craft\] and there, in front of them, he denies Christ, his faith, his baptism, and the Catholic Church. Next, he pays homage to the \"Magisterulus\", that is, \"the Little Master\", which is the only title they use for the evil spirit, and finally he drinks from the bottle I mentioned earlier. Once he has done this, he immediately feels himself receiving and retaining a mental image of our craft and of the principal rituals of this sect. This is how I was led astray, as was my wife\', etc.

In these words, you see that what they are taught comes from what they drink. But the ignorant fellow was deceived when he thought a new image was being formed independently in his mind in which everything pertaining to the craft would be shown to him, divided into its separate categories. The evil spirit could not do this, nor was the magician\'s intellect capable of this kind of cognitive effort. These things were suggested by means of some kind of internal, very rapid suggested, and since the magician did not notice the speed of the suggestion, he thought he had learned simultaneously, and in an instant, things he had received one after the other in a very short space of time. \[An evil spirit\] can also form images in the air very rapidly and display them like this so that they can be seen by our \[passive\] imagination \[phantasiae\], with the result that our mind seems to be catching sight of them all in a single act of seeing, as in a mirror, and thus the inner person thinks he is being illuminated.

This was undoubtedly the stratagem whereby the ancient serpent tried unsuccessfully to deceive Ignatius, the founder of my Society. Let me write down the whole thing in the words of \[Pedro de\] Ribadaneira. \'But visions, which he saw in the midst of his studies\'-he is talking about grammatical studies\'began to disturb him very frequently and in large numbers. He wanted to commit to memory the inflections of nouns and verbs, but such a great abundance of divine thoughts started to pour into his mind that he was unable to learn anything new at all, and if anything had stuck in his memory before this, it started to flow away utterly and completely. Nor was he able either to prevent thoughts of this kind from coming, or to drive them away when they started, even though he zealously made every effort \[to do so\]. Shocked by the strangeness of the situation, he asked, why does this happen when I am praying, when I am making my confession and receiving the bread which transcends its substance, when I am awake, and when I scourge myself? When I survey all the spiritual gifts I receive from God, such frequent, such great, and such powerful \[moments of enlightenment do not present themselves to me. So, when I am engaged in doing unimportant things and am not thinking about God when I am close to God, why do such beautiful images and heavenly illuminations come to my mind? I recognise, I do recognise how clever this most troublesome Enemy is. I recognise the Old Fox\'s tricks and cunning\', etc. The \[Enemy\] got hold of Ignatius\'s mind, which had already long been actively engaged in receiving intimations of heavenly things, because he knew in advance that if Ignatius\'s teaching were to be consonant with his religious devotion it would threaten him \[the Enemy\] with a greater harm. So he tried, with this display of better doctrine, to make \[Ignatius\'s\] task of the learning the rudiments of letters ineffectual, \'at this time especially\', (as \[Giovanni\] Mattei says), \'offering his understanding frequent illuminations, and throwing light on the abstruse mysteries of Holy Scripture\'. If he could do this in the case of things which are very holy-a man\'s faith in and manifest love of God-how much more easily can one believe he has brought knowledge of things which are profane and untrue to the minds of those without faith?
[Page 119]

Chrysanthius of Sardis, who was a magician and High Priest \[of Lydia\] at the time of Julian the Apostate, had a son and although \[his son\] had no skill in the art of versification or any part of \[the art\] of grammar, with a single glance at the sun he would pour out prophecies against the Christians. Breathed on in the same way by the divinity who lights up the night, various shepherds suddenly became poets-Evages of Bithynia, for example, and others. Amphiaraus also became a poet this way, and the old woman who was taught Pindar\'s hymn, and evil spirits in the guise of old men clad in skins, who lived in her house and fulfilled the role of incubi, told Sosipatra everything which was done elsewhere behind her back, and taught her everything which had been written by poets, philosophers, and orators.

This is how the little creature who apes divinity tries to make \[people taught by God\] himself. For the most part, however, one discerns the knowledges he pours out because it does not last and a human being does not make use of it when and how he pleases, but only via inspiration or an ecstatic trance in certain places at certain times and because of specific causes. \[The knowledge\] which God pours out, on the other hand, is always and everywhere manifest, as is clear in the case of Solomon, (3 Kings \[3.9-12\]), the Apostles, (Acts \[2.1-4\]), Bezaliel, (Exodus 35. \[30.31\]), the man in St Jerome\'s letter to Domnio, (Book 2, letter 4), and Abbot Joachim who, from being an illiterate, uneducated door-keeper, drank a certain wine which had been brought to him by an angel, and suddenly emerged not only a prophet, but also in an instant a subtle debater and learned theologian. (Laonicus Chalcondyles tells us about him in Book 6 of his Turkish Affairs). People who are aútoðíðaxtoi \[self-taught\] should be distinguished from \[taught by God\] because they acquire their learning by their own effort, and not by having it poured into them \[from above\]-\[being learned in\] logic, for example, (St Augustine, Confessions Book 4), and theology (Athanasius, for whom see Nicephorus, History \[of the Church\] Book 5, chapter 44).

These examples, however, are superior to teaching received from the Devil. In March in the year, 1600, in accordance with the sentence of the Inquisitors of the Faith, the bones of one Román Ramírez, together with a statue, were burned to ashes in Toledo. Ramírez had died in prison while awaiting trial. He was accused, among other things, of having received skill in medicine from an evil spirit, and since this criminal united in his person many aspects of magic, I shall not regard it as a chore to translate the \[procurator\] fiscal\'s accusation faithfully from Spanish into Latin.

\(a\) He said \[Ramírez\] had already been accused of the Muslim error and had abjured it, but added that he had relapsed-which is what that kind of muck usually does-and returned to his vomit. For many years he had kept the great fast they call \'Ramadan\', and observed the rites and ceremonies of Muhammad by first saying \'guadoc\' and \'zála, washing his whole body, putting on a clean outer garment, reciting the \'azoras\', (i.e. prayers), of that sect with his face turned to \'al-quibla\', (i.e. the rising sun), raising and lowering his head and bowing himself to the ground, extending his palms upwards to the sky while he was saying \'Alaqueur\', (i.e. God is great), and rising before daybreak to celebrate the fast \'zahor. Thus absolved \[of his sins\], he solemnly celebrated his Easter, \[called\] \'Ramadan\', for three days. This he did by abstaining from all work and manual labour, especially on the first day, by putting on a clean outer garment, and by making the declaration \'guadoc\' and \'zála\', along with many other Muslim rites and ceremonies. Then, adding crime to crime and sin to sin, he revived the overt pact and treaty he had made with an evil spirit many years before with the intention of doing something wicked. He worshipped this evil spirit, did him homage, and dedicated his soul to him on condition he assist him with his favour and advice as often as he might be needed or asked in connection with anything at all. So Ramírez asked and obtained from the evil spirit knowledge of and expertise in how to cure many secret concealed illnesses with herbs, fumigations, and superstitious incantations: and, as a matter of fact, he cured not a few people this way.
[Page 120]

\(b\) He persuaded people he had a wide and detailed knowledge of literature, both sacred and profane, and so they would give him the titles of stories and he would-quite openly for money-recite them, more or less word for word, as though he were reading them from a book open in front of him, something he did with the help of his evil spirit. In this way he amassed not a little power because of the money he was given by his audiences, and when someone asked him how he had acquired such a reliable memory, he replied that someone very close to him, a person very knowledgeable about the properties of herbs, who had an evil spirit as a close friend, had given him some kind of drink. He also said that on one occasion, when this same person wanted to go to Saragossa, which was fifteen leagues away, he made an invocation-de bon varón, as they say-and a horse suddenly appeared. They both mounted it and very soon afterwards arrived at the city gates where they left the horse after removing its bridle. They then did the business which was the reason for their journey, returned to the gate, threw the bridle on the horse again, and were brought back home almost in an instant.

\(c\) There was an incident which took place in the town of Deza. A woman went to bed angry, (as it happened), and put herself in the hands of an evil spirit. Suddenly someone-she had no idea who-removed her from that place and because she had disappeared completely, her husband came to Ramírez and asked him for his help. Ramírez replied that the man should not be downcast, and that he would restore his wife to him. He gave him a letter and told him to go to a particular place among the vineyards, make a circle there, and stand in the middle of it, and when he heard a crowd of people passing by, even if he saw no one he was to ask, \'Where is the King going to be?\' If the answer was that the King was crossing by on that side, he should throw the letter to the ground in front of him \[self\]. The man did as he was told and got his wife back, although he did not see who had brought her back or how he had done it.

\(d\) The procurator fiscal added that \[Ramírez\] said a good many very obscure things, and things which had happened in places very far away, about which he could not have known anything at the time he was revealing them, except through his pact with an evil spirit. On one occasion, (said the fiscal), while Ramírez was leaving the house he said a traveller was about to come and ask and entreat him to come and free his daughter from an evil spirit which was obsessing her, and so they should summon him at once. The other man came and Ramírez was summoned. While he was curing the illness, Ramírez behaved in such a way that it seemed perfectly clear that an evil spirit had been dispatched against the sick woman beforehand through an act of harmful magic \[maleficio\] by Ramírez himself. \[People thought this\] because, first of all, on the day of her marriage the unfortunate girl had suffered a flux of blood together with a mental blackout and pressure round her heart; and not long afterwards she had frequent, long-lasting fainting fits, and began to hate her parents and her husband, and could not endure the sight of him or sleeping with him.

They came to the conclusion she was obsessed. A priest was sent for and for eighteen days he tried to drive away her wicked guest with exorcisms and the usual remedies of the Catholic Church. Finally, one evil spirit spoke up and said that a hundred and one evil spirits had been dispatched against the woman\'s body, but that now he was the only one because all the others had gone away. So when the priest began to repeat the usual exorcisms from the Church\'s servicebook and to urge him to say where the rest had gone, he replied they had left for the town of Deza to visit the foresaid Román Ramírez and ask him what he wanted them to do, and that they would not leave until he had given them permission to do so. At this point, the unfortunate girl was cruelly beaten to the floor and stayed there the whole day in a swoon. When the priest began to order him to stop harassing the woman and to let her live with her husband as a wife should, and to give her back a precious jewel he had stolen, \[the evil spirit\] replied that he was under Román\'s control and that he \[himself\] had the jewel but could not give it back. It was after this that the parents, affected by what the evil spirit said, had sent for Román Ramírez to cure their daughter.
[Page 121]

The moment he entered the house, the woman began to say and do extraordinary things, crying out that she knew he had come, but did not know why he had done so. Then, as he entered her room and she saw him, she fainted, her mouth became contorted, and she remained like this for three days, unable to eat or drink, having no power to swallow anything at all. So the magician drenched her with a strong suffumigation composed of \[smoke from\] burning shoe-leather, juniper oil, and other things, summoned her back from her fainting fit and then, addressing the spirit which was obsessing her, said, \'Leave her alone. Leave her alone and go away. Flee, unless you want me to repeat the suffumigation\'.

The evil spirit, however, was unwilling to reply, so Ramírez told everyone to leave the room and, thinking no one could hear him, started to question the spirit and demand, \'Why don\'t you answer me? The spirit which attended my grandfather had more humility! What have I done that you do not deign to speak to me? Isn\'t it enough that I have devoted myself to you entirely for such a long time? So now make this woman sleep so that she doesn\'t hear anything we say\'.

Meanwhile, another evil spirit arrived, and the accused said to him, \'You\'ve come at the right moment. What did you do while you were in Deza? How are my wife and children?\'

\'Your son\', said \[the evil spirit\], \'was not well, but he\'s now out of danger. Your wife was worse. She has now recovered. Your grandson is still sick, and there is a danger he may die.

Then Ramírez replied that since they knew he had served them faithfully every day, and how much he was bound to them, they were not unaware he was being pressed for a debt of two hundred reals as payment for a garden he had rented. It was fair, \[he said\], that they do him a favour and leave the obsessed woman\'s body, at least for the space of fifteen days, and leave her healthy and sound in the meantime, so that he could receive payment of a large fee and increase his future reputation and good character, which would otherwise receive a notable blow. After that, they could attack her again, and take possession of her as far as he was concerned. The evil spirit then promised he would do this and obey him. But when Ramírez noticed his conversation had been overheard by other people, and that he had been found out as someone who had power, so to speak, over an evil spirit, he became angry and threatened he would throw \[the evil spirit\] into chains, and the memory of this would make sure he would not dare do anything like that again.

The evil spirit was expelled, but because the woman\'s parents had not paid a large enough fee for what he had done, Ramírez threatened that they would soon need his help again. Immediately, he transferred the condition \[caused by\] his harmful magic \[maleficii\] into the husband of the woman who was now free and told his evil spirits to go into him and molest him. They did so and afflicted him very cruelly for the entire day, inspiring him with a thousand frightening mental images and temptations to despair. So, they sent for \[Ramírez\] again and when he came to the man he said, \'You have been struggling valiantly with an evil spirit, my friend\', then, employing suffumigations, he drove the evil spirits out of him. He left the town, discontented with the tiny fee \[he had received\]. After he had gone, the parents discovered that not only had their daughter not been cured, but that she was growing worse day by day. So, they wrote to the magician and sent no small a sum of money along with the letter, promising a larger sum if he would cure the woman completely. He deigned to come, gave the courier the material for another fumigation, and wrote down the ritual he should use, adding that when he got back to the house, he would find her somewhat better and dancing with female friends: and that is how it turned out. But since the wife was still not willing to sleep with her husband, he suffumigated her and added the words he had been told \[to use\], namely, \'Evil spirits, leave this woman, because Román tells you \[to do so\]. Otherwise, I shall send for him, and he will come and drive you out by force, because this is what he has promised me\'. At this invocation, the woman gave a loud cry, was cured, and remained free and lived with her husband the way married couples usually do. (This comes from the court records I mentioned).
[Page 122]

\(vi\) The power of magic and an evil spirit over the will is much more restricted. They are able to affect the will only by putting in front of it some external object, which may be one it hates as well as one it likes; or by stimulating sights and thoughts in the imagination; or by attracting and bending the will by inflaming strong emotions in a sensory part \[of the body\]. The stronger these \[emotions\] are, the more fiercely they bend \[the will\], sometimes as though they are removing it altogether. But they never impose irresistible compulsion on the will. Those people who argue for any necessity or compulsion when it comes to sinning, even in the case of people ensnared by mortal sins, are telling us to banish the will\'s freedom and of course, had they lost their free will, they would have ceased to be human beings. There are indeed people who have been robbed of the grace which makes someone pleasing \[to God\], but they have not been deprived of the natural freedom of their will, nor of God\'s help which is common to everyone. So whence comes necessity in the case of sin? One does not commit a sin without freely consenting to it. Free consent, however, presupposes free will, and this is why, unless God stops him, the Devil can push \[someone\] into some action of emotion and this movement by its very nature would be a sin if free consent were involved. But he can never make it compulsory upon the will to sin, because he cannot force a contented person \[to sin\] if that person is unwilling to do so. God does not allow an evil spirit to make use of his entire strength to tempt someone, either. He makes sure that the tempter does not tempt to the extent that he could, but only to the extent that human weakness can bear. He never allows this \[weakness\] to lack sufficient help, not even when it comes to the gravest of sinners, who still have in common with the righteous what St Paul wrote was a great consolation of the soul, not simply to the good, but to the whole Church of Corinth in which there were many wicked people as well as many righteous: \'God can be trusted and will not allow you to be tempted beyond what you can \[bear\] but, along with the temptation will also\', (in accordance with the disposition and capability of anyone who has been tempted), \'create a means of escape so that you can bear it,\' Corinthians 10.13\].

This trustworthiness presupposes and includes preservation of the strength of the free will and of a divine help which is common \[to all\] and enough \[to help them\]-on this point I have taken these words from Theodoret-and because \[God\] faithfully delivers the preservation He has promised, the result is that not only does human frailty achieve a way out and an escape-this is how Augustine and Jerome interpret \'πoinoel TNV Èxβáoiv\' He will make the way out\]-from an evil spirit\'s temptation, \[such a way out being a happy outcome for a \[a person\'s\] virtuous will and effort, but also that once people have gone past the danger, they also become stronger when it comes to bearing \[temptation\] in future. As for losing \[the battle\], which is what would make people afraid, let us profit from temptation, as Photius put it very well. My translation also puts it very well, whatever the yelping from Moderns, because Exβaívelv means \'to go beyond. (See what Bede argued in relation to this verse from various passage in St Augustine). Since, therefore, sinners who rely on this support can avoid individual mortal sins, there can be no doubt that if they do succumb to temptation and have had the power not to give in to it, they will be succumbing voluntarily, not because they have been forced or compelled by a necessity which is not found at all in this case.
[Page 123]

But can the Devil at least hinder witches \[sagae\] from repenting? Ignorance of theology in regard to this question made Nicholas Rémy look like someone who was vacillating and undecided. It has to be said that just as \[the Devil\] could lead \[someone\] into sin, he is also able to encourage obstinacy in sin and feed \[that obstinacy\] with suggestions, seductive pieces of advice, and other temptations. But he can never entirely prevent other sinners, however grave they may be, or witches \[sagae\] themselves, from repenting and renouncing the pact they made with him. Therefore, by favour of the special help I mentioned, they will be able to repent whenever they earnestly want to do so, however much the Devil holds them back. Without that special help from God, they would not be able to do it. If it were otherwise, the Devil would have more power to hold them back than God does to rescue them, and that is obviously blasphemous. (Andreas de Vega proves this opinion, which Catholic theologians have in common, in his Justification, question 13.)

But in order to make clear by means of a particular example how far an evil spirit is sometimes accustomed to go in trying to constrain the will-not with complete success, but close to it, and not in actuality, but in appearance-for the consolation of those who have been tempted I shall record the words of Blessed Angela da Foligno. In chapter 19 of her Life, she describes one of her experiences.

\'I suffer more or less continually at the hands of evil spirits torments and sufferings of the soul and, I am telling you, there are more of them, and they are and incomparably more grievous than those suffered by the body. I don\'t know how to tell you what they are like, other than to say, think of somebody suspended by a rope upon a beam, with his hands tied behind his back and his eyes blindfolded, left on the gibbet still alive, without any help, any remedy, or any support. I am saying I am tortured in a greater state of hopelessness and more cruelly by evil spirits, because I see that the evil spirits are hanging my soul in such a way that, just as the hanged man has nothing to support him, so my soul does not appear to have any support left, and all my soul\'s courage is overthrown while my soul knows \[what is happening\] and looks on. When my soul sees that all its courage is being overthrown and is leaving, and that it cannot set oppose this, there is such great pain to my soul that sometimes I can scarcely weep because of my hopeless grief and anger. Sometimes, on the other hand, I can\'t stop weeping, and sometimes such great anger comes over me that I can scarcely hold myself back from ripping myself to pieces. Sometimes I can\'t stop myself from hitting myself dreadfully, and when I do that, I have sometimes made my head and other parts of my body swell up. When my soul sees all my courage vanishing and leaving, my soul starts to lament and I cry out to my God.

I also suffer another torment-that all my defects have come alive in me again and are now beginning to gather strength, \[and I suffer\], not because during my life-time they bring my reason under their control, but because they cause and bring me great punishment. Even vices which never existed in my body enter into me and are set on fire and bring me great punishment. But they do not last, and when they die again, they bring me great consolation because I see that I have been handed over to many evil spirits who cause the vices I abhor, and which were dead, to come alive again; and \[it is\] they who add the vices which never existed. When I remember that God was afflicted and despised here \[on earth\], I should like, (in as much as it is proper), all the evil things happening to be to be doubled. Whenever I am in the utterly dreadful darkness caused by the evil spirits, when it seems that all hope of good is entirely absent, the darkness is dreadful and the vices in my body, (which I realise inwardly are dead in my soul), are being revived. Outwith my soul, however, the evil spirits are reviving them and even those vices which did not exist are being revived. I also suffer physically in at least three places, because in my shameful places there is such a great fire that it was my habit to put physical fire to them to put out the fire of lust, until my confessor told me not to.
[Page 124]

When I am in that darkness, I believe I should more readily choose to be roasted than to suffer the things I have just been talking about. At that point, indeed, I cry aloud and call for death, \[begging\] God to allow it to come to me in any way at all. On those occasions I say to God, \'O Lord, if you must send me to Hell, please do it quickly and don\'t put it off and, since you have abandoned me, finish the job and plunge me into the deep\'. Then I realise it is the work of evil spirits and that those vices are not alive in my soul, because my soul never consents to them. My body, however, suffers violence, and there is such great pain and disgust that if it were to last, my body could not bear it. But my soul also sees that all power has been taken away from it, even though it does not consent to the vices. In spite of the fact that it does not have the power to resist the vices entirely, it also sees that it is opposed to God, and so falls and is tortured in them. There is also a particular sin which God has allowed to come into me, which never existed in me \[before\]. I recognise clearly this sin which God has allowed to come into me, and this sin is so great that it surpasses all my other sins. There is also a particular strength which God has clearly given me to counter that sin, and by this God sets me free by His special supernatural power. If I did not have a sure faith in God, the responsibility would lie with me and not with anyone else; and in that case a sure, untroubled hope in which I could have no doubt would be my responsibility. But the strength always prevails and the sin \[always\] fails, and the strength is in control of me and does not allow me to fall into sin.

The strength is so great that not only is it in control of me, it actually gives me so much of its strength that I really do recognise God in this \[situation\] and am illumined and strengthened to such a degree that I could not be moved to the smallest sin by anyone in the world, or any of the demons in Hell, or anything else; and my faith in God rests in me, along with this power. The sin, however, is so great that I am ashamed to mention it and \[to say\] that whenever the foresaid courage has been taken away from me, I also have the impression that what has abandoned me is not something which could protect me, either because of my feelings of shame or \[because it is\] a torment \[intended\] to stop me from rushing into sin straight away. But then the courage I mentioned comes over me and sets me free with its special supernatural power in such a way that I could not sin for all the good or evil in the world. These hardships I have borne for two years and more\'.

Thus far St Angela\'s translator, with insufficient Latin. In his words one can perceive three grades of \[St Angela\'s\] very oppressive and very troublesome temptation by the Devil, as well as the efficacy of God\'s grace which does not abandon the individual, and also that the will is never explicitly compelled by the Devil. However much its power to resist may be weakened and it may seem to succumb, it does not succumb because, with God\'s grace, a person has the power not to give his consent. Finally, you notice the \[spiritual\] progress which St Paul promised accompanies temptation. This section \[you have been reading\] is a kind of practical commentary on his words.
[Page 125]

Book 2, Question 25

What power does the Devil have over a soul which is going to be separated from the body, and over the separation itself? Ecstasy and extraordinary things connected with dead bodies.

The Devil can cause ecstasy or \'rapture\' in a human being by binding or loosing the external senses. He does this in two ways: (i) when he obstructs the pathways by which the sensory spirits \[spiritus\] go from the brain to the external senses, such as happens when people are dreaming; (ii) when he attracts the sensory spirits from the external senses to the organs of the internal sense and keeps them there so that they cannot descend to the organs of the exterior senses. This happens because the internal sensory pressure is too strong during a \'rapture\', and because the sensory spirits flow together to the organs of the internal sense and the external senses\' functions are so obstructed that the living body gives every appearance of being the immobile corpse of someone who is dead. This is the real, natural cause of a \'rapture\'. It does not exceed the Devil\'s power, and the ecstasies of witches \[sagarum\] and magicians belong to this kind of thing; and when they remain stupefied in one place, they are often deceived into thinking they have been in various places and have done various things.

This is the kind of thing Olaus Magnus tells us about Lapp magicians. If any traveller wants to find out exactly what is going on back home, they undertake that within twenty-four hours he will find out what is happening there, even if home is three hundred miles away. This is how they do it. A shaman \[incantator\] ceremonially summons his gods and then suddenly collapses and becomes unconscious as though his soul had suddenly left his body, because either he has no breath left in him, nor does any sensation, movement, or life seem to remain with him. But to make sure they are \[actually\] still there, there must always be someone with him to guard the body while it is abandoned and lying unconscious, because if this is not done evil spirits make off with it. Once twenty-four hours have elapsed, his breath comes back and the unconscious body wakes up with a groan, as from a deep sleep, as though the man who had fallen down were being called back to life from death. After he has been restored this way, he replies to questions and, to create trust in his questioner, tells him something his questioner recognises and knows for certain has taken place in his own home or in those of his relatives.

More people than Olaus testify to the truth of this, but one has to beware of the many errors in connection with this \[account of\] magical rapture, and similar examples of it which Olaus\'s ambiguous expressions favour not a little. Those who boast they have gone into a trance \[raptus\] by their own natural power and of their own free will, as \[Girolamo\] Cardano did whenever he wanted, are especially in error.³⁴² There can be no doubt at all that an evil spirit does this because it is part of his contract, and there is perhaps no surer indication of magic than this kind of voluntary trance. In the case of the virgin from Zaragoza-this is what I call her because her deception was finally uncovered in that city by the Archbishop in 1585-who pretended she had the stigmata of Christ, her \'raptures\'-and every one of them stemmed from a pact she had made with an evil spirit while she was feeding her sheep-were as frequent as those of Magdalena de la Cruz from Cordoba who was a notorious wise-woman \[saga\].

Bodin, too, is in error. It is a serious one and the censors should not let it pass and leave it in his book. Yes, I say he is in error when he thinks that the soul really does leave and forsake the body during this kind of ecstasy and, once separated from it, busies itself in other places while it is in that state.This was an error of the Kabbalists, and I suspect the Platonists were not averse from it, because of a well-known story told by Plato himself. Er, the son of Armenius, a Pamphylian by birth, had lain dead for ten days among the corpses of those slain in battle. He was brought home and afterwards, on the second day, when he should have been put on his funeral pyre, he came back to life and gave an account of some of the extraordinary things he had seen while he was among the dead.
[Page 126]

There was a similar story about someone from Clazomenae in Book 3 of Origen\'s Against Celsus, and one about Aristeas of Proconnesus which Pliny tells in \[Inquiry into Nature\] Book 7, chapter 52, and in his Life of Romulus, Plutarch, relying on Herodotus, tells us that the dead man came back to life many times and could recover his soul wherever he wanted, and that those who were standing nearby could see his soul in the shape of a crow, flying out of his mouth. This man, however, was not dead, but in a magical trance, and it was not his soul which was flying out of his mouth, but a devilish conjuring-trick. The same has to be said about the white weasel which emerged from the sleeping soldier\'s mouth and went back in again, and then went back and forth across a small brook over a sword which someone had laid down. This story, told by Hélinand \[de Froidmont\], was a joke by an evil spirit who did it in order to impress on \[people\'s\] minds this error about the soul\'s migration, and to convince old women\'s fantasies and children still wet behind the ears, rather than natural philosophers.

Death is nothing other than a separation of the soul from the body, and since this separation happens at the same time as its leaving the body, the only result can be that when it does leave, the human being dies. If he does die, who brings his soul back into his body again? It can come back only through the power of God. The Psalmist tells us that the souls of the dead have no knowledge of where they are: that is, they are not aware of their body any more. Therefore once the soul has been separated, it cannot enter another body and cannot enter and inform its own \[body\] again, unless God allows it to do so and guides it. So, when people raise objections about the Lapps and Finns, and Aristeas, and Hermotimus of Clazomenae, I am not unaware of these \[objections\] and am not affected by them. A Devil-inspired ecstasy did happen, but their soul did not leave their body. \'One should pursue anything\', says Tertullian dealing with this subject, \'rather than this license of the soul to flee \[the body\] without death supervening\', and elsewhere he recorded quite rightly as axiomatic that \'the soul cannot exist anywhere without the flesh as long as it is in the flesh\'; and the author of the short questions attached to the works of St Athanasius overtly denies that the soul travels outwith the body during an ecstasy.

Nor is there anything in St Augustine which is favourable to Bodin. Indeed, in one place and another he overtly says the opposite. Opponents also misuse the Scriptures because when they cite the verse, \'the death of His saints is precious in the sight of the Lord\' \[Psalm 116.15\], they do not interpret the words about the ecstasy of the prophets correctly. These must be referring to a real death, or to the separation of the soul from the body, and however much you might want to understand them as referring to rapture, the word \'death\' means what it says, because it brings before one\'s eyes the picture of a dead body; and even though these words are thought to be about the Apostle\'s rapture, they cannot prove he was outwith his body. God could have done this, but whether He did so, I prefer to doubt, (since Paul himself was uncertain about it), rather than maintain, as St Hildegard does, that his body and soul really were separated at the time. I remember that all the Fathers \[of the Church\] as well as all the Scholastics have their doubts.

Bodin, however, shows that he has little acquaintance with \[natural\] philosophy when he mixes up aphaeresia with ecstasy. In the latter the soul appears to be absent from the body because the body has been stripped of the functions of the sensory soul which makes it move and sometimes makes it understand. In aphaeresia, however, the body lacks not only the functions of the soul, but the actual soul itself. In the latter case, one should say that the body is actually without any sensory perception and movement. In the former, (speaking with reference to its natural constitution), it is unconscious both potentially and actually. What kind of an argument is that? If the soul is immortal, it must be able to operate in some fashion without the body and, since it is immortal, it can therefore operate during an ecstasy when it has been separated from the body. My dear fellow, I advise you to go to schools run by logicians and learn that when something has more than one function, because you have not proved something about all of them, it does not logically follow that that restricts you to \[dealing with\] just one of them. Good God, how many functions does a soul have while it exists in the body over which the body claims no part and no right for itself? Consider the actions of the will and the understanding and their reflections on it, and the other things one can look for in the books of those who deal with the immortality of the soul. But as far as those who are acquainted with Christian philosophy and not unacquainted with natural philosophy are concerned, these points are too well established to need a more protracted proof, and I have neither the time nor the inclination to light a lamp merely for the sake of making them clear.
[Page 127]

Only God could separate the soul through rapture in this fashion and preserve suitable conditions in the body while it was happening, so that the soul could be taken back into it again, and St Paul acknowledges he is not sure whether this did happen to him. I think this is how one must understand what is said about St Giles \[of Assisi\] in The Deeds of Franciscans. When he was asked whether during this life the soul sometimes left its body during an ecstasy or during contemplation, he replied that it did and that he knew a man to whom it happened; and he said it \[happened\] either because once every bodily function was removed, \[the soul\] forgot it completely, or because divine power was removed from the body and was then brought back into it. (Juan Salvador Trados tells the story in volume 1 of The Deeds and Sayings of the Illustrious Order of St Francis, p. 205).

Thirdly, those who do not separate these false and demonic raptures from a real, supernatural rapture are in error, and I am fully aware that the two I am dealing with at the moment are distinguished from one another in a more subtle fashion elsewhere. In the latter, a human being is raised via a divine afflatus to contemplate or reflect upon supernatural things, and his mind is removed from things he apprehends through his senses. St Thomas \[Aquinas\], as usual, discussed very clearly how far removed were the ecstasies of the prophets, the Apostles, and the saints from those induced by magic, such as the ones St Irenaeus spoke about when he was dealing with the magician Marcus, \[Against Heresies 1.13\]. Marco Antonio Mocenigo, in a work more verbose than compact, has reviewed the opinions of a very large number of pagan philosophers and Arab astrologers on the character of a genuine rapture and, with the exception of the ninth \[one\], has refuted the rest of them quite satisfactorily. Listen for a moment to what I think is worth keeping from what he says, and what I think should be rejected. I think he is wrong when he argues that the subject of a rapture is some substance midway between mortal and immortal, and when he says that this is our soul which, he says, is mortal with respect to its sensory part and immortal with respect to its rational part.

He is mistaken. If there is one part of our soul which is immortal and another which is mortal, my question is, does this mean the latter be separated from the former? In that case our soul will be divisible and therefore not immortal. Far be that from our minds! We believe the whole of a person\'s soul, in so far as it is his substance and essence, is immortal. It is not the soul which is intermediate, but the person himself with respect to the entire way in which he is constructedmortal in body, immortal in soul. The subject of the rapture is not the human being taken as a whole. The subject-complete, non-physical, indivisible and immortal,-is the human soul; and the soul is the subject of the rapture, not because the intellect is active but because it is passive since, as Mocenigo has put it very well, the formal cause of the rapture is the actual action whereby someone is raised to and united, so to speak, with those heavenly things. The final cause is the conjunction of the mind with God: in the words of Anicius Severinus \[Boethius\],

\'You are a serene repose for the devout. To discern you is their goalBeginning, bearer, guide, path, the end they have in common\', \[Consolation of Philosophy 3.27-28\].

The active or efficient cause can sometimes exist in two ways. The active intellect will be the instrumental \[cause\], but God will be the principal \[cause\]. I said it can \'sometimes\' be this because, although God is, of Himself alone, the sufficient cause, He usually provides secondary \[causes\] as instruments. Therefore, it will be possible for the active intellect to be an instrumental cause because, although its ordinary office is to detach images from illusions, (and in this case there are no illusions), when God winds Himself into our mind by means of a rapture, He usually shows us the things He wants us to see by showing them to us in some divine way.
[Page 128]

But when our intellect has perceived these spiritual things then, with the aid of the intellect in its active role, a certain kind of likeness and representation of the things one has seen can be created. (St Thomas \[Aquinas\] does not refuse to accept this way \[in which it can operate\]). I said that God is the principal cause because it is actually God who draws a person to Himself, as Mocenigo says and has not been able to deny. You see, just as the sun is not seen in sunlight, just as the eye does not see light in the absence of light, and just as the ear does not hear that the air has been struck unless it is full of air, so God cannot be recognised in the absence of God. But when the mind is filled with God, in so far as, illumined by divine light, it recognises Him, it is lifted up to God; and in so far as, set on fire by divine heat, it is borne towards God, it starts to boil and leaps upward to higher things; and no one, according to the correct opinion of the Platonists, is raised up to that which is higher and infinite unless he is being drawn and lifted up by the power of something higher and infinite.

Secondly, the active intellect alone cannot be enough to do this because, although Mocenigo says that this transition is voluntary as far as the human being is concerned, in actual fact it is not. We know that rapture often happens to those who do not want it to happen, as St Thomas \[Aquinas\] thinks and experience tells us. I know, and a thousand people still living know as well, that there was a virgin dedicated to God who was still alive in Burgos in Castile, in 1585-one of those they call \'beatas\' there. The moment she caught sight of the Eucharist and, scarcely had she received it in her mouth, than she was lifted into a rapture. This happened every day and to such an extent that she could not feel small pins applied to and driven into her feet, and because she was someone who was averse from human glory, she was in the habit of begging God, very ardently, (since she preferred to be holy rather than talked about or seen), not to let anything of this kind happen to her in front of other people; and when she came back to herself, she would hurry home as fast as possible, full of embarrassment and shamefacedness, and lock the door.

So being estranged from our mind this way is not something which is under our control and therefore not something we can do voluntarily. Nor will it be something our intellect can do, however much and however ardently we may wish it. The \[rapture\] is not so much something the intellect does as something which is done to it, since in this case the soul is behaving, (strictly speaking), primarily in the manner of something which is passive, and secondarily, (and not so strictly speaking), in the manner of something which is active. Mocenigo himself acknowledges that this \'transition\', (which is what he calls a rapture), of a human being to God is very much more passive in character than active.

I say, however, that God is the efficient cause of a rapture not only because of a conjunction \[of humankind with God\] which is universal, but also because of one which is marked out for and peculiar to this very end. Here \[Mocenigo\] runs into sand-banks and allows himself to be swept off them by the gales of pagans and heathen philosophers because, in putting forward his opinion, he claims that one should ascribe the efficacious power of a rapture only to the active intellect. He means that only by means of the universal conjunction with God can this supportive \[power\] present an image or picture of God to the passive intellect, and that thus the active intellect, by its own power, causes what was merely potentially apprehensible now to be actually apprehensible, as if the active intellect can make any image which shows us God-something theologians, as well as St Dionysius the Areopagite, quite rightly deny.
[Page 129]

But see how flimsy are the arguments on which objectors rely. Their first objection is that God is not the same as our soul and therefore He cannot act upon it. What does \'talking nonsense\' mean if this is the way they argue? As if nothing can act upon something else which is not the same as itself, and the analogy and likeness of the direct, instrumental cause were not enough! As if not everything is obedient to God! As if God, who is one and to an eminent degree everything, cannot act upon everything as He wishes! Begone from me these heathenisms! Their second objection is that God is understanding. Therefore, He can do nothing unless He recognises it. But God recognises nothing outwith Himself. Therefore, He does not recognise our mind. Therefore, He cannot act upon our mind at all. My reply is that God is understanding if we interpret \'understanding\' as an improper expression for spiritual substance in general. But He is not understanding if you take \'understanding\' to mean intellectual potentiality, and in this way the result of the argument says that He is understood by Himself. But this would not be \[the result\], nor would the antecedent be consistent with the inference if one interpreted it in a different way, because it is certain that spiritual substances can be active in ways other than recognising, such as moving \[something\] from its place, and things of that kind. If \[Mocenigo\] simply meant to say that nothing can act upon a subject unless the subject recognises it, that is certainly true because God recognises everything. But a spiritual substance such as an angel could act upon any subject it had not recognised, and in consequence it follows that the minor premise that God recognises nothing outwith Himself is absolutely untrue. Does this mean that something other than God recognises nothing apart from itself, and that \[God\] does not recognise trees, or horses, or sinners, or evil spirits, and that \[while He is\] in this condition He preserves and busies Himself with the world\'s created things and punishes sinners? We have to acknowledge, unless we are followers of Epicurus and atheists, that things exist outwith God and are recognised by God.

God is indeed pre-eminently everything, but not in respect of form. If, \[Mocenigo\], you are speaking about things which exist to a high degree outwith God, you are deceived, because nothing which exists does not exist to a high degree in God. In this sense, too, our mind does not exist outwith God. Consequently, it is clear that you are talking about things which exist outwith God in respect of form. But God recognises everything, whatever it is, even if it does not exist in God in respect of form, or even if God is not everything in respect of form. Therefore, God recognises our mind. He recognises it very well and will punish it if He sees it is unfaithful and is going over to the irreligious dogmas of pagans.

I am not unaware of the maxim that God understands nothing outwith Himself, which is attributed to Aristotle, but Aristotle deserves forbearance because his eye, like that of a night-owl, was not illuminated by any ray of the true Faith and was blind to that infinite light. One should not, however, grant forbearance to Christians if they stubbornly defend such nonsense and proceed, not to submit the pagans\' maxims to the rules of the Faith, but the facts of the Faith to their maxims-blind guides, in fact, choosing the blind with whom to fall into the pit of error. Rather than one of Aristotle\'s maxims, this is one of Averroes\'s inventions, and he is the detestable summit of the falsehood which comes out of Arabia.

From this starting-point he has misled his adherents to the point where they think the following flows from it: that if God were to join a human being to Himself through rapture, He would be joining something newly perfect to Himself; or, if God were to understand that other things exist outwith Himself, He would need sense-perception and a passive imagination \[phantasiam\]-all of which even two-bit theologians can blow away at once as things dripping with worthlessness and having no coherence at all. God sees everything at one and the same time in Himself, as in a mirror. In recognising other things, He bestows perfection on them. Because of our weakness and need, perfection comes to us by union with or recognition of \[Him\]. God possesses everything. He is not changed but changes the things He joins to Himself.
[Page 130]

Finally, Mocenigo thinks that every rapture is a series of successive events, and this needs proper explanation so that it may be true. A rapture can happen in an instant, because God is the efficient cause of activity and infinite potentiality. But the active or passive experience of a rapture subsequently lasts over a period of time, and if a human being sometimes does things beforehand which lead to a rapture, such as contemplating, those things happen as a series of successive events. Nowadays this is not enough for a rapture. I do not deny that a person is prepared and disposed for raptures by a certain ardour during his contemplation and desire, as Rodrigo Dosma Delgado correctly tells us in Book 1, chapter 12 of his book on the authority of Holy Scripture. But I maintain that, for the most part, this happens to those who suffer the false revelations of evil spirits \[malorum spirituum\] if they make an effort and try to fall into ecstasies, as happened in the past to the Montanists and as happens today to magicians and Anabaptists; and not even under those circumstances is the rapture produced physically by an efficient cause from the will, but only figuratively and, shall we say, by agreement. So, none of this supports Mocenigo or contradicts me.

The other absolutely certain way in which the soul really is separated from the body is through death, and it cannot be denied that the Devil can effect this, with God\'s permission. After all, how many thousands of Assyrians did a single angel kill in a single night? \[Isaiah 37.36\]. Didn\'t Asmodeus kill Sarah\'s seven husbands, \[Tobit 3.8\], and didn\'t the Devil kill all Job\'s children? \[Job 1.18-19\]. Doesn\'t an evil spirit kill very large numbers of people every day with acts of harmful magic \[maleficiis\]? (I shall discuss this at greater length later, in Book 3). After death has separated the soul from the body, the Devil\'s power has not yet been put to sleep entirely. He can do several things to the soul: he can do several things to the body. He can inflict tortures on the separated soul in accordance with how much God has ordered him to punish it. Whether he can send it back into the body, or cause it to appear, I shall discuss in a moment. As for the bodies of the dead, he sometimes clothes himself and appears in them, as will be said later, and he also has power over them, especially over those which people seek out in unconsecrated places.

Whatever power he has over other physical things, he exercises power over \[bodies\] in a particular way, and is accustomed to perform and display certain wonderful things in them-marvels to those who are ignorant of natural causes-as though they were being done by living bodies and the soul which is animating them: for example:

\(i\) making blood flow from a dead person\'s wound in the presence of his murderer. I have talked about this elsewhere;

\(ii\) preserving bodies incorrupt for a long time, so that they do not rot, or decay, or dissolve, or turn to dust. This, after all, can happen naturally, either as the result of unguents, or the character of a location and, indeed, the constitution of the body, or even as the result of different kinds of death. Plutarch writes, \'It is well-known to everyone that bodies struck by lightning do not decay\'; and so Euripides, not very aptly, brought Clymene on to the stage, saying of Phaethon, \'His dead body is decaying here in a ravine\'. On the way a body is constituted, \[Lodovico\] Ricchieri quotes Ammianus Marcellinus to the effect that the bodies of the slaughtered Romans fell apart at once and dissolved completely, whereas those of the Persians became dry, the way logs do, and so their body-parts did not liquefy or turn soggy after they had been covered in blood.
[Page 131]

Ammianus thinks \[this was because\] their life was more frugal and their native land was dried up by the sun. \[Girolamo\] Cardano believes and claims the same thing for the body of his father, Fazio. I do not think this explanation should be brushed aside lightly because scarcity of food prevents the humours from being abundant, and Xenophon notes that, because they are abstemious in regard to food, Persians do not cough or blow their noses.
