When it comes to the location itself, it can either have this power of preservation naturally because of the soil or the sun, or even because of its situation. In his Olympics, Aristarchus said that the corpse of an armed man who had died of his wounds some time ago was found whole and unchanged between the floorboards and the shingles in the roof of Juno\'s temple in Elis. Pausanias explains this by saying that because it was hidden away in these concealed recesses, it could not have been damaged by the summer heat or the winter cold. In the case of unguents, this is a well known method of preservation \[practised\] by the Egyptians, as Herodotus and Diodorus tell us.

It is also a miracle of Nature that the hearts of those who have died from heart disease or poison cannot be burned. I do not think an evil spirit is unaware of these properties, which have been imprinted on the heart, or that he cannot introduce a similar arrangement into a corpse. Therefore, he could bring it about-and let this be point (iii)-that a corpse could not be burned no matter how long people tried. He has sometimes done this in the case of the living. Why can he not in the case of the Dead?

\(iv\) He will be able to bring it about that a corpse\'s hair or nails grow, because a dying person\'s physical constitution can do this naturally when some of the animating soul\'s organising power extends into the body towards a bit of it which is not complete. This frequently happen in those who have hanged themselves when their breath is suddenly cut off. \'Those whom Jupiter washes\', (as the saying goes), \'Apollo anoints and dries off\'.

Often, however, as in the case of relics of the saints, these things happen as the result of a miracle from God. So that I can pass over everything else, \[let me tell you this\]. In Damme, a town near Bruges, I once saw a very large wooden image of the crucified Saviour which had grown hair and a beard. These were shaved off every year but would grow back again. The devout considered this to be a miracle because of the frequent cures God used to confer upon the sick there, and other records of God\'s munificence were noted on a large number of tablets there until Calvinists, in their irreligious, obstinate folly, destroyed the image with fire and, as is their custom, razed the church to the ground.

I can also quite happily class the following \[anecdote\] as a portent from God. \'While Charles of Austria was holding courts of justice in Linz\', says Pietro Bizzarri, \'a child was born to a peasant. There was nothing healthy about him at all. He lived for fourteen days, sweated for three days before his death, and sweated until he died. Immediately the stigmata began to appear on the dead child\'s hands and feet and in his side, just the way we see Christ\'s wounds depicted in paintings. A crown like a crown of thorns also made its appearance on his head, on the skull itself. When the Emperor heard this, he sent some of his people. They looked very carefully at the portent, and then gave him a detailed account of the whole thing, which made him realise that the situation was exactly as people had said it was.\' (See Bizzarri\'s Epitome of Remarkable Things in Europe, p. 302). What bit of this can be ascribed to Nature? I really do not see any of it which is enough \[to explain it like that\].
[Page 132]

Book 2, Question 26.

Is it possible, with the help of evil spirits, for the souls or spirits \[spiritus\] of the dead to appear to the living?

This is a very important question and includes another which should receive an airing first. Can men\'s souls ever appear at all? I discussed this in detail almost nine years ago in Douai during the \'disputations\' as they call them, but because that lecture has not been published, I shall share with the reader here what I said on that occasion, but without the rhetorical flourishes.

Book 2, Question 26, Section 1

The first conclusion must be the foundation, as it were, of the rest: the souls of the dead, with God\'s grace and aid, can sometimes appear, and do often appear, to the living. This accords with the teachings of the Catholic faith and the principles of true, genuine natural philosophy. (i) Many people reject this conclusion, but some of them are atheists who deny \[the existence of an immortal soul and think it dies along with the body. It is easy to refute these people because their heresy was taken apart and invalidated a long time ago by Plato, Aristotle, and other able philosophers, and the Church sent those who defended it to the stake. (I pointed this out at great length in an extended argument in my commentary on the chorus in Seneca\'s Trojan Women).

\(ii\) Others are philosophers too devoted to the senses. The claim that the soul outlasts the body and can be seen by the living, but deny that it comes to the living, simply because they do not understand how or why this can happen. They need to be taught why and how.

\(iii\) There are the prattlers, (they do not deserve to be called theologians), who are born of the same mother as we are and suckled from the same breasts-false brethren, the offspring of vipers who attack their blood brothers and look to stab their mother with the point of an irreligious sword. (Obviously I am talking about heretics). Some of these conclude that those who maintain they have seen spirits \[spiritus\] are raving mad and have heads full of fancies and should be sent away to doctors \[to be prescribed\] hellebore. Others declare that all accounts of this kind are superstitious, and the worthless dreams of mad old women. Others, such as facetious pantomime artists and those who are less bloodthirsty, invent many disgusting and ridiculous tales, in imitation of the well-known Dutch Lucian \[Erasmus\], in order to render every kind of apparition hateful. The subject is a very troubling one to all these people because they realise that this apparition of the dead very much strengthens faith in Purgatory.

Consequently, I am astonished that Girolamo Maggi, a Catholic, (and a man of wider reading than good judgement), has been rash enough to write that phantoms \[simulacra\] which are seen in the neighbourhood of tombs or cemeteries are never the souls of the dead, but always deceivers of human beings-evil spirits or wicked sprites \[genios\]. I am also amazed that he supports this by citing Porphyry and Iamblichus, sworn enemies of the Christian faith, as his authority, because in a moment I shall demonstrate that he groundlessly \[criticises\] what Theophylact and Chrysostom have to say on the subject.

At the same time, too, I shall demonstrate that the doubts of the philosophers I mentioned are worthless. The witticism of the heretics, however, are worthy of them: that is to say, they are irreligious, shameless, lying, and scarcely worthy a reply. Excessively rash, too, was the very learned man, otherwise known as a \'patron of lawsuits\', while in full flow in the Parlement of Paris called this opinion a \'superstition\' and was of the opinion that those who think souls return should be punished. (Please read the last Book of his Religious Administration, and you will see his audacity accommodating itself to and demonstrating the less equitable case. But this was when many Moderns had inflicted themselves on the French courts. \[André\] Tiraqueau, however, was of the same opinion. \[His notes on\] Alessandro Alessandri\'s \[Festival Days\] Book 2, chapter 9 prove nothing, but baldly assert that he considered all these things to be mere dreams, and he repeats this in Book 5, chapter 23. But what he thinks is never going to make this \[subject\] more true or more untrue).
[Page 133]

To go on from these rather feeble individuals-my opinion is proved in the first place by common pagan opinion. Why did they have a tradition of evoking the dead, which they used to carry out with such solemn chanting and ritual, if they did not believe that any souls of the dead appear to the living? Why, (unless it was because what the common people believed was also the opinion of those in the know as well), would the Phoebus of poets say of Anchises\'s son, \'The unhappy figure and shade of Creusa herself Appeared in front of my eyes, an image larger than the one I knew, \[Aeneid 2.722-723\].

Why, too, would the Latin Callimachus introduce Cynthia recounting such things as this after her death?

\'The spirits of the dead do have a kind of existence. Death is not the end of everything, And the wan shade overcomes and escapes its pyre At night we are borne away and wander. Night sets free the shades which have been imprisoned, And Cerberus himself casts aside his padlock and goes wandering. Laws bid us return to Lethe\'s fens at dawn. We are borne along, and the ferryman surveys the freight he is carrying\', \[Propertius, Elegies, 4.7.1-2, 89-92\].

Why would they do this if, (in my opinion), the crime of divination by the dead had not started with them, \[a criminal activity\] which Homer\'s Circe in the Odyssey, Ovid\'s Medea in his Metamorphoses, Lucan\'s Erichtho in Pharsalia, Seneca\'s Tiresias in Oedipus, Horace\'s Canidia in his Epodes, Papinius \[Statius\'s\] Manto in the Thebaid, and Silius \[Italicus\'s\] Autonoe in The Punic War, practised with such pains? Why was \[the ancients\'\] custom of calling back to their native land the souls of those who had died in foreign countries known only to relatively few people, when Didymus, Eustathius, and Pindar\'s scholiasts mentioned it? From where in the world did the common opinion arise that souls are in the habit of appearing to those who slaughtered them and expelled them violently from their body? That they are present everywhere, and that they harass people and menace them? Why, too-and this is something the poet who wrote about festivals \[Ovid\] observed-did the founder of the city of Rome institute the Remuria, (otherwise known as the Lemuria)? Why were the Feralia, Silicernia, Culinae, and Parentationes solemnly observed under the Roman republic unless it was \[to honour the returning spirits \[spirituum Plato himself was favourably disposed \[to the idea\] in his Phaedo and his Letter to Dionysius, as were the Platonists Porphyry, Athenaeus, Maximus, Philostratus, and Eunapius.

A person from Madaura \[Apuleius\] taught and explained this opinion which comes from the Platonist school, that is, humans\' souls are called \'souls\' \[animas\] while they are still in the body, and \'personal spirits\' \[genios\] and \'daemons\' \[daemones\] when they are separated from the body. If they have been good, they deserve to be looked after by their descendants, to occupy the home in the role of a peaceful, tranquil divinity, and called \'household spirits\' \[lares familiares\]. But if they have lived a wicked life, because of the injurious things they have done to harm life, they have no resting-place with good \[spirits\], but are punished by having to wander the earth, or by some kind of exile, and are known as \'spectres\' \[larvas\], a terror to good humans and a source of hurt to bad.

Apuleius also says, in the first part of his Defence, Aemilianus, may the god Mercury who goes between the gods of the upper world and those of the lower grant you the ill favour of the gods of both, and always keep on casting in your way apparitions \[species\] of the dead, any shades \[umbrarum\] at any time, any spectres \[lemurum\], any spirits of the dead \[Manium\], and any goblin creatures \[larvarum\], and may he thrust upon your eyes all the terrors of the night, all the nightmares of the sepulchre, and all the horrors of the grave\'.
[Page 134]

Arnobius (Book 2) testifies that \[the ancients\] were accustomed to beg certain divinities that souls wandering near their bodies be not held back here on earth. \[Cassius\] Dio testifies to visions of souls in \[the sections on\] Caracalla and Titus \[in his Roman History\], and so does Plutarch in his \[Lives of\] Cimon, Dion, Brutus, and Alcibiades. St Augustine has followed the Platonists and adds that these wicked \[spirits\] are called lemures. Servius Honoratus writes that they were called Manes as long as they had not yet transmigrated into other bodies.

This same Servius and \[Pomponius\] Sabinus separate \'shade\' \[umbram\] and \'likeness\' \[simulacrum\] from \'soul\' \[anima\], because they think (incorrectly) that a human being consists of a shade, a body, and a soul, that the soul seeks Heaven, the body comes to an end on earth, and the shade or likeness goes down to the underworld. They mean that the shade is not a real body, but a kind of physical semblance which, like the wind, cannot be touched. But they do agree with the poets that, whatever \[the shade is, something goes up to Heaven or down to the underworld, and that sometimes it appears to the living, (as I have said elsewhere). They also thought that magicians evoke it by means of an evil incantation. So alone among pagans, \'hogs from Epicurus\'s herd\', (as one of them calls himself), \'with a well-cared for hide\', but a neglected mind, they denied that souls survive or appear.

All the others proved that \[they do\] by giving innumerable examples of every kind of wicked human being-Plato on \[the subject of Castor and Pollux, Valerius on Miltiades, Pausanias on someone from Lipara, Aristotle on Achilles, Philostratus on Romulus, the author of Famous Men on Caligula, Suetonius on the Athenian philosopher, Pliny the Younger on Pausanias\'s troublesome girl, Plutarch on Emperor Severus, Xiphilinus on the others, Apuleius \[Metamorphoses\] Book 8 and his Defence, Suetonius\'s life of Nero, Quintilian\'s twelfth declamation, Horace Epodes 5, Valerius Flaccus \[The Argonauts\] Book 1, and others too many to mention. My contention is that these can refer to the illusions and conjuring-tricks of evil spirits, but they do clearly demonstrate what I am going to add, which is based on Christian authors-that it must be said that, with God\'s permission, real souls have been able to exist not merely as an empty áyadua \[portrait\] or, as glossators call it, a \'lernum\' \[likeness\], (unless this should be read as \'larva\'.)

Book 2, Question 26, Section 2

My conclusion is proved in the second place in opposition to those philosophers who are too attached to the flesh-as St Augustine said very aptly, \'In a carnal human being, the whole rule of understanding lies in the habit of discernment\', \[Sermon 242.1\]. These people believe nothing if they do not see it and therefore, in fact, believe nothing. Against these, I say, my opinion is proved by a single reason-that since God is omnipotent, anything He can do does not involve contradiction. What I am saying, however, involves no such contradiction, since it is conformable with Nature. Therefore God, who created the souls, will be able to cause them to appear as things separate and distinct. Indeed, if God does not stand in the way, it would be possible for the soul \[to do\] this by its own innate power, because opponents have not been able so far to suggest anything which would let them prove this runs contrary to the natural condition of souls.

So why is this? Why does the natural condition of a soul not permit this? Is it because souls do not, properly speaking, exist in any \[particular\] place? I know this is what a number of people have thought. They have had the notion that no \[particular\] place is appropriate for a separate soul because of \[the soul\'s\] natural condition. But what they have said about the space which is taken up by the outer surface of the body which contains \[the soul\] and is appropriate for physical things is not relevant to this \[argument\]. Since we are talking about things which are spiritual and indivisible, the place which is to be taken up is entirely to make room for physical things along with non-physical things which cannot find a place elsewhere. Therefore, it cannot be denied as a matter of fact, that separated souls do exist in a place of this kind. Faith tells us this and tells us that the souls of the good enjoy God and are not on earth but in Heaven, and that, on the contrary, \[the souls\] of the damned are not in Heaven but under the earth and are tortured there in a special place. Reason tells us \[this\] and tells us that it is impossible for anything to exist and not exist in a particular space, namely: (a) in total, infinite space, (only God exists this way), or (b) in a part of space-that is, in this part, and not some other part-and this is how all created things exist. The soul, however, exists in this bounded spaced because its substance was transferred to that place rather than to a different one either by its own innate power, (because it transferred itself to that place, and even if it is not actually active there, it is enough that it can be), or it was brought down there by the external and more potent power of something else, (for example, if it was brought down unwillingly by some good or wicked angel, or was being kept there by force, as the wicked are in Hell).
[Page 135]

This is how the various theories of scholars can be explained: St Thomas \[Aquinas\], for example, who thinks \[the soul\] exists in a place because it comes into contact with virtue; \[Duns\] Scotus who thinks it is because of the presence of a substantial existence; and Julius Scaliger, most obscurely of all, who thinks it is because this is what has been planned. This is how a soul is going to exist in a place and how it will occupy a divisible space even though it itself is indivisible. Moreover, it will be able to occupy a larger or smaller place-this being the peculiar property of indivisible spiritual things, one which is denied to indivisible things which have measurable substance if in fact there are such things which exist separately from the body for a reason other than \[because they belong to\] the intellect, which is something I find difficult to believe inside the largest sphere of action it can occupy at one and the same time, according to how much more or how much less defective is the essentiality with which it has been endowed. This means it will be able to form a body for itself, which it can assume and in which it appears, which will be at least as large as the sphere in which it is active or, (which is more extraordinary), it could even form more and different smaller bodies, assume them all, and appear in all of them, provided they were all contained within its sphere of activity, just as it is generally accepted that angels can exist in more \[spheres\] than can accommodate their perfection. \[Souls\], therefore, can and do exist in a place which can accommodate them.

So, what is the problem? Is it that they cannot move themselves from one place to another? Theologians, however, tell us this is not true. It is proved that a separated soul can move itself from one place to another, since the human mind subsists by its own power and recognises things which are not in front of it and are far away. There is also a kind of natural communion and conjunction between it and heavenly minds, and as a result \[the human mind\] is conscious of this affinity and comes closer to those \[heavenly minds\] and enjoys a pleasant fellowship with them. But what is more defective than the \[human mind\] if it aspires in vain? It would aspire in vain if it could not attach itself to \[the heavenly minds\] by local motion and would be unable to ascend on high by its own power. Moreover, doesn\'t the soul bestow on the body it is animating the power to set itself in motion? Why can\'t the separated \[soul\] also move itself since it, too, exists in a space and very much stands in need of the power not to be most wretchedly tormented if it seeks something in vain? So why, (someone may object), can a soul not return to its body or visit its own home when it wants \[to do so\]? A pupil Plato did not particularly approve of \[Aristotle\] once made this objection. My answer to it is that once the soul has been stripped of the body which acted as its covering, it cannot clothe itself again at will. It is not permitted to wander about hither and thither at will, either, because it is either being held back in the penitentiary which is the receptacle for the most dreadful of tortures, or it is willingly obedient to the supreme governor of the whole universe, by whom it was brought down and planted in this dwelling-place. It does not go back to its body because the ingress of the soul depends on the body\'s being informed and given life by Nature\'s preparations of \[the body\'s\] material, and this very close union follows very soon after those \[preparations have been made\]. So, this does not contradict \[my conclusion\], either.
[Page 136]

Some people, therefore, say that these appearances are not possible because, since spirits \[spiritus\] have nothing to do with the body, they cannot be seen by the body\'s eyes, or heard by human ears, or perceived by any physical mode of recognition we have. What Lucretius said about \[the sense of\] touch "Nothing can touch or be touched except a physical object" - applies to the rest of the senses as well, (they say), because nothing is perceived by the external senses unless it has been endowed with a body. Nothing penetrates as far as the internal sense unless it comes via the external \[senses\]. Nothing is known or understood which has not been perceived by the external and the internal sense first.

Consequently, (they say), these \'visions\', which no one has seen with the external sense \[of sight\], and spiritual things which could not have been perceived by any of the senses, are not understood. I acknowledge that this is a tough nut to crack and that one needs to look for a strong chisel to break it open. The \[solution\] which \[Girolamo\] Cardano suggests, with very little finesse, in his book, The Intricacy of Nature-that a human distinguishes the spirits \[spiritus\] of the damned and actual evil spirits naturally by sight, hearing, and smell-is worthless and silly. A person would have to have better sight than Lynceus, better hearing than that \[provided by\] the asses\' ears of Midas and Silenus, and a keener nose than Plautus\'s boozy old woman if Nature had granted him the power to hear, smell, see, and distinguish spiritual substances. But perhaps \[Cardano\] was fashioning his souls out of rather thin, airy matter. Many people made this mistake in the past, although one hopes \[they do not do so\] today.

No more credible is what Caesarius \[of Heisterbach\], a more recent author than \[Aristotle\] and more recent than any one of the Fathers \[of the Church\], claimed more than once in his Dialogues: \'The shape of the soul is like a round glass vessel seen from every angle\', and elsewhere, \'The soul is a spiritual substance and because of its spherical shape, like the globe of the moon, it sees in every direction\'. This, I say, is incredible if he means what he says. I think these expressions are allegorical and metaphorical, and that by them he meant to point out the soul\'s essential perfection and ability to recognise many things, something which is clear from what he was saying.

But Cardano-someone who had doubts about the immortality of the soul, as nearly all his published works indicate, and especially one he wrote, but did not publish, on the mortality of the soul, (although he sometimes showed it to close friends)-adds error to error when he thinks that all apparitions are imaginary, and supposes that the main sights people have of them are perceived only by their imagination. I am willing to grant that this does happen sometimes, but I am ever more willing to deny that it happens every single time. The subject needs to be explained rather more clearly because it is well worth examination, and St Thomas \[Aquinas\] declares it is extraordinary and should be counted as one of God\'s miracles.

The ancients distinguished between \'vision\' and \'an impression made on the external senses.\' Their word for vision is брама, and no matter how many times we think we see it, it appears later the way it appeared at first. They call an impression made on the external senses фávtaoua. When someone is halfawake or asleep, but thinks he is awake, some shapes offer themselves to his eyes in order to be seen. I am putting off a discussion of \'vision\' to the following Books. At the moment I have to deal with the thing which is seen, which Christians prefer to call an \'apparition\'. An apparition, as St Isidore says, has three aspects: intellectual, imaginary, and embodied, \[Etymologies 7.8.37-40\] It is called intellectual when separate substances insinuate themselves only into the mind, and not in a different shape, but in their own. This rarely happens. One kind of image is that of the beatific, eternal vision and has nothing to do with this present treatise. The aspect called \'imaginary\' is also known as \'spiritual\'. \[This happens\] not, as Cardano thinks, when the body\'s physical spirits \[spiritus\] act as servants to the senses, but when separated souls attach a number of alien forms to our internal sense and these forms induce us to recognise them. Vision is called \'embodied\' when images of this kind are received by one of the external senses.
[Page 137]

At the moment, I am discussing the spiritual and physical \[aspects\], and I am arguing that neither of them is at variance with the laws of Nature. The spiritual \[kind\] is common to those who are awake and those who are asleep. In it, \'spirits\' \[spiritus\]-I prefer to use this word for separated souls rather than \'shades\' or \'spectres\', which is what the pagans called them-give form to certain illusory appearances \[ideas\] of themselves in a person\'s passive imagination \[phantasia\], rather than concealing themselves under a quite different guise and entering it by stealth. This way, they change and affect the internal sense and do so, not by means of the guise and not all by themselves, either, but with the ministering assistance of angels or evil spirits, because such images cannot be fashioned in a person\'s imagination without some kind of extraordinary power and the knowledge of how to do it.

Spirits \[spiritus\] lack this power because they cannot, of themselves, set other things in motion. They also lack the \[requisite\] knowledge and skill because they did not have it when they left their body and did not learn it afterwards. Go on, tell me! Who would instruct them, and where would his instruction come from? So, they paint art-work with a non-physical brush-images similar to the forms of living people-on the panel of the imagination. These have a rather gloomy general appearance and expression and, for the most part, dusky, dark-coloured clothing, in the case of those who are still undergoing punishment, and, in the case of those who have been set free from their torments, a shining white garment, a cheerful, beaming face and, (to distinguish them from the others), things which will make them look very much like living people.

\'Imaginary\' apparitions, therefore, are not impossible, and this is an easy way of appearing to the living. It is how Judas recognised Onias and Jeremiah, how, (according to several writers), Saul recognised Samuel, and Constantine recognised Peter and Paul. Let me offer the same proof in the case of \'physical\' \[apparitions\]. People object that they are seen only by those who are awake and that in these cases the spirits \[spiritus\] assume bodies and make use of them. In the following Question I shall show that they can do this, and \[I shall explain\] how they do it. For the moment I am assuming that whether you pay attention to theologians or Christian philosophers, it is now certain and clear that the bodies people see appear to be ones \[the spirits\] have assumed from substances separate \[from themselves\]. Therefore, why is it surprising if living people see \[the spirits\] in the bodies they have assumed, whether the carcass has been formed from the elements or is something else altogether? Both kinds can be seen.

But someone is going to press the point, \'Are you therefore claiming that the spirits of human beings also enter corpses, (something evil spirits certainly do)?\' As far as the souls of the damned are concerned, I see no reason why they should not do this, just as evil spirits do, because they do not enter them to inform them but simply so that they, \[the damned\], can move from where they are. I absolutely believe that the souls of the blessed do not enter bodies which are not their own and that it is not seemly for them to do so. There are those who claim they sometimes enter the bodies \[which used to\] belong to them. I grant that this is not at variance with the laws of Nature, and that it is proper for them to do so. Religious and learned men make this claim and I am not stubbornly resisting it.

But I am not foolhardily agreeing to it, either, because it does not seem decent or becoming for those holy souls, now glorified, to enter the confines of their dissolving, decaying bodies again before they are resurrected. They are not obliged to do so at all and can make use of an elemental body instead. There is the suggestion that this is the way the law-giver Moses appeared along with Christ, our Lord, on the summit of Mt Tabor. I do not think \[the idea\] that Moses appeared in a body made of air for that occasion, as \[Walafrid\] Strabo and St Thomas \[Aquinas\] thought, is objectionable. One will be able to say that this was a genuine apparition in which Moses\'s real soul appeared, and one will also be accurate in saying that Moses \'appeared\', because that is the preferable way of describing it. In his Care for the Dead \[18\], St Augustine, too, hints that he thought this apparition of Moses was not unlike that of Samuel, and no one says of Samuel that he appeared in his own body.
[Page 138]

All the arguments brought against St Thomas \[Aquinas\], too, are irrelevant with the result that one cannot take the authority of the Fathers \[of the Church\], who use the word \'resurrection\' in its broad sense, and apply it to Moses, saying that he is like someone \'rising up again\' in a body he has assumed, which is an opinion to which \[Cornelius\] Jansen inclined in chapter 67 of his Harmony. But because in fact St Jerome, Epiphanius, and most of the Fathers use the word of a real resurrection-you have their words in \[Francisco\] Suárez-I shall not drag out the argument further.

I say, rather, that real resurrections have nothing to do with the subject I am discussing here, because I am talking about the appearance of the dead and Moses\'s \[appearance\] was no more going to be that of a dead man than \[the appearance\] of Elias, since Moses would have been alive at the time, and so would Elias. But what did a certain \[author\] say in The Transgressor Bound?³⁷¹ When Moses answers the evil spirit\'s question, \'Where have you come from, Moses?\' he says, From the upper air, of course. Where I, fortunate man, was carried up with Enoch and Elias, And am awaiting God\'s final Day \[of Judgement\]\'.

This must be read with caution, because if it means that Moses was carried up while he was still alive with Enoch and Elias who were not yet dead, that is undoubtedly a rash thing to say and contrary to Holy Scripture. If \[it means he was carried up\] with Elias and Enoch and will come at the Final Time, that is equally rash. If \[it means\] Enoch and Elias were carried up into the highest fiery heaven, this is clearly novel and unheard of, because anyone who has a share in that glory before death is a saint, a pilgrim who has attained his goal. More dangerous by far in the same work are Michael\'s and the good angels\' bewailing the punishment of Lucifer and even accusing God of cruelty. Plainly that was about as valuable a thing to do as transforming Aeschylus\'s Prometheus into Lucifer and the Nereids into good angels! This was how daring the poet was prepared to be.

But to return to what I said about Moses: one needs to make the same answer about those who rose and appeared with the rising Lord. They, too, had returned to life and perhaps followed the Lord as He ascended into Heaven, and had no more part in death, if they merely appeared in their own body and informed it, as is very likely. Therefore, I deny that spirits \[spiritus\] assume their own bodies, (in order to appear in them, at any rate), and that they do so in order to inform them and give them life, because under those circumstances they are not returning in order to be what they are seen to be. They are doing so in order to furnish, by means of these \[bodies\] which are well-known \[to others\], a way in which they can be recognised, for which purpose a feigned body is perfectly adequate.

The souls themselves do not construct these elemental bodies for themselves. Angels or evil spirits fabricate them for them because separated souls cannot act upon them as necessary. This would have to be done by local motion, but a separated soul cannot move other things by itself with its own power, (as the more accurate opinion has it). This is because it lacks the physical organs which it needed to set the body in motion when it occupied a body. So, who is going to be able to press the point by asking, \'If \[the soul\] cannot act upon an elemental body, how, given that there is a close association between them, can it appear or enter it?\' My reply is that souls enter these bodies not because they have decided to do so, but because it is God\'s will and command \[that they do\].
[Page 139]

Under those circumstances, God bestows upon them some property which supplies the deficiency and I think this is fairly clear, as far as the souls of the blessed are concerned, because of their sight of God, which confers many gifts on them and satisfies their desires abundantly and in every respect. In some cases, however, the bodies are set in motion by spirits which are also evil spirits, and in others the body is set in motion by the informing soul. The movement of a human being depends on his instrumental organs, because humans are stimulated by their natural longing \[for something\], and the parts of the body

move because of some property imprinted upon the tendon-the tendons are also moved so that they can make the parts of the body move-although all parts of the body do not move at the same time, but one after the other. When the mind has been released from the body, it retains its longing, of course, but is deprived of its physical organs. So, the body to which it connected itself starts moving, not piecemeal or via its \[various\] parts, as it did while it was alive, but by thrusting its whole bulk forward. (It can happen, however, that because of certain conjuring-tricks, it can look as though it is moving by means of its instrumental organs). This, then, is how \[a spirit\] enters the body it has assumed and makes it move. So, the second proof of my conclusion-that it is in accord with the \[form of\] philosophy we call \'natural-remains secure. The following argument will prove the same in relation to moral \[philosophy\].

Book 2, Question 26, Section 3

The third proof of my conclusion is that it is fitting and beneficial that God permit this sometimes because if by these means it results in many people\'s advantage, and if it is something which promotes God\'s honour, it is seemly that God allow it, and objectors need to acknowledge that it is the kind of thing I am dealing with here. It would not be difficult for me to demonstrate that these impressions made on the external senses do us a great deal of good and grant us some access to the outward vastness of God\'s glory. These apparitions result in benefit to souls which have died in God\'s grace if they do indeed appear in their own body. (I say as I find). Yet if they appear in their mortal remains, they will have found this very pleasing and very agreeable, because souls have, from the cradle, an innate love of their bodies, (as if \[those bodies\] were their home), from which they were dragged by force and to which they return of their own accord. This is why Philostratus writes that \[souls\] are separated from them unwillingly, Plato calls them філођшиátous \[loving the body\], and St Paul said we are unwilling to be undressed, but would rather be overdressed, so that everyone can see that separation from the body is a grievous thing, as Theophylact explains.

Theologians agree that this love is not lessened by the separation and that a desire for their former union and for their re-integration remains. But even if they make themselves visible in someone else\'s body, they derive not a little pleasure from it because they make use \[of it\] to do the things we do and aim to do things which have the honour of God as their goal. Mean while, they also attend to their own situation-not only those which need support and help, because even at this time they are making atonement and are getting alms, intercessions, fasts, and Masses from the living, whereby they satisfy God\'s justice and obtain a diminution of their punishments: but also the blessed themselves, full citizens of the heavenly Jerusalem, whom God occasionally orders to show themselves so that we may pay due honours to their bodies which were very pure temples of the Holy Spirit, as St Ambrose testifies of Saints Gervase and Protase. So they turn this to their advantage because they observe this in themselves and delight in paying honour to God.
[Page 140]

But, Almighty God, how many advantages, how many benefits accrue to us from this! They tell us that our soul outlives us and remains with our body. They show that the resurrection of the body, which they foreshadow, is not impossible. They proclaim that God has charge not only of the living but of the dead as well, and that these things are done at His command. They show that there are certain places where offenders are tortured incessantly and the devout enjoy immortality and take pleasure in their yoke. We are reminded of the fellowship and communion which exists between us and the spirits of the dead. They bring us God\'s commands and become the occasion of mutual love and other virtues. They inform us about their condition and tell us how we should be leading our lives. Sometimes they present themselves to the dying as companions on their journey and guides to Heaven, their homeland. Because of their warning, we turn aside God\'s anger. We get good sense from them.

They drive away misfortunes which are threatening to weigh us down, and they require that the wicked be punished as an example to the good. We are also helped by the spirits of the damned, and through them our faith is strengthened. The fact is, we give our assent much more readily to those we think we should believe \[when they tell us\] about the punishments \[meted out\] to sins, and the justice and providence of God. (A theologian of my Society, Petrus Thyraeus, has considered all these points, explained them briefly and with great skill in his treatises, and proved them to the hilt with examples and narrative accounts. His writings are very popular and give equal pleasure and profit, and while I am engaged in this discussion, I don\'t want to repeat the points he makes).

Consider, noble Reader! Aren\'t the fruits we pluck from apparitions excellent and splendid? Aren\'t these gems more precious than any gold and pearl?

Those who strive to tear the Faith from mortals\' minds are endeavouring to rip all this away from us-through wickedness and envy if they know what they are doing, through stupidity and thoughtlessness if they do not. When it comes to this subject, they thrust fear upon us, and yet they are afraid in a situation in which there is nothing to be afraid of. An enormous field of fraud, (they say), is sown because of this belief. Its furrows of afflictions are harrowed, and the broad meadows of superstition tilled. Fine nets are set out all over the place, and crafty nets are set up in which the unwary are caught and entangled. Satan has a habit of transforming himself into a human being and, indeed, into an angel of light, so how shall we distinguish him from real spirits? Discernment of spirits is a gift from God, and one not granted to everybody. Therefore, the human race has decided that if such apparitions do happen, God has not left it remedies capable of discerning them.

My reply is that the method of accurately discerning spirits of this kind is difficult and not accessible or clear to just anyone. But there is no lack of things written by learned people, which throw light on this darkness so that we may not fall perilously into error. They publish individual signs \[whereby one can discern spirits\] which are true enough in themselves-some more so, some less-and if most (or rather all) of them were to agree with each other, they would be pretty reliable and enlightening. Bertram, Bishop of Tiflis, wrote an entire treatise \[addressed\] to Euno, Archbishop of Trier, in about 1387. Johannes \[Bremer\] von Hagen, otherwise known as \[Johannes\] de Indagine, investigated the subject in a book about the apparitions of a particular dead woman, and the theologian Jacobus de Clusa dealt with it quite fully in an entire work on the appearances of spirits, how they should be investigated, and how one finds out whether their appearance is real or feigned. \[Johannes\] Weyer tries to scoff at him, as he does everything which is Catholic and respectful to God, but he reveals nothing thereby which is not respectful to God, which is not sensible, and which is not religious. He is a writer few people know. Better known are the exertions of \[Theodor van\] Pelt and the treatises of \[Petrus\] Thyraeus I mentioned earlier. From these, one can select \[observations\] which are more reliable.
[Page 141]

\(i\) If they are put to flight or revealed at the sign of the cross, the names \'Jesus\' or \'Mary\', or those of other saints, or by their relics, a wax Agnus Dei, holy water, blessed bread, salt, or wax, or other sacramentals of this kind, or symbols of the Catholic religion: if these have driven the apparition away, but \[the spirits\] still come back for no good reason and cause more distress-because when you satisfy souls they either do not come back, or do so only once in order to thank you, whereas by contrast, evil spirits string together reason after reason, either so that they can carry on haunting or, at the very least, so that they can practise serious deception-when these things happen at the same time, \[the apparition\] is the Devil.

\(ii\) If it says it is the soul of some damned person, it is lying and is, generally speaking, an evil spirit, because the souls of the damned are scarcely every allowed to come out \[of Hell\].

\(iii\) If it offers a spurious or wicked reason for its appearing-for example, it says it has been forced to appear because of the power of a magical incantation in order to reveal things about which one is inquisitive, but which one does not really need to know, or things which would be better left unknown-it is an evil spirit.

\(iv\) If its language is deceptive in other ways, superstitious, offering bad advice, manifesting and revealing the disposition of someone who is desperate, arrogant, libidinous, vengeful, or otherwise thoroughly disturbed, it is an evil spirit.

\(v\) If it appears in a shape which is not human, beautiful, and well-formed, but hideous, misshapen, or base, or \[in the form\] of a dog, a snake, a pig, a serpent, a spider, a toad, or similar creatures, or a black African, it is an evil sprite \[malus genius\]. If, on the other hand, \[it appears\] in human form, it may then be considered to be good, the work of an angel, a saint, or God. If its character is morally wicked-blasphemous, superstitious, lying, murderous, lustful, etc.-or if its behaviour is deceitful, (if, for example, during the first conversation \[one has with it\], one feels happy and calm, but later on \[one experiences\] dread, confusion, or desolation): or it goes away accompanied by a great stench, loud noise, or things being dashed to pieces, it is an evil spirit.

St Antony bequeathed this warning to his followers in something like the following words. Discernment of good or bad spirits is not difficult. If joy follows fear, we may be sure the aid has come from God. If, however, we are struck by dread, and the dread stays with us, it is the Enemy we are seeing\'. With this rule, which St Thomas \[Aquinas\], St Catharine of Siena, \[Jean\] Gerson, and others of proven experience on this wrestling-ground found, not without good reason, to be a good one, Father Ignatius \[Loyola\] armed his followers. He himself had had experience of this in a false vision furnished with eyes. While it was present it would bring him joy, but when it was absent he would feel sad.

By contrast, it is the special property of good spirits either that they preserve the feeling of happiness they bring to the mind, or remove fear from the start and make up for it with an immense feeling of peace, such as we know happened in Mamre, to Gideon, to the Blessed Virgin, and others.³⁷⁸ St Macarius was of the same opinion. Things which proceed from God and good angels, (that is to say, by grace), have been endowed with joy, love, peace, and truth. Those which come from wickedness, (that is, the Devil), are filled with perturbation and have no love or feeling of joy for God, (that is, anything which is heavenly). (I shall have more to say about this in the next Book, which deals with revelations.)
[Page 142]

I think these are not unintelligible indications of how to discern visions of bad spirits from good, and under those circumstances we ought to have no hesitation about which we should reject, because these are always either of evil spirits or the damned-if the spirits of the damned ever do actually appear. You see, even if I acknowledge that God can allow this \[to happen\], I have difficulty in seeing why He does so.

Certainly, if He did permit it, I do not see how the manifestations of those \[souls\] can be discerned from evil spirits unless, perhaps, they were to make known or reveal things which would surpass any human knowledge, or unless they were to take over a living human body, because souls or good angels never steal into the bodies of living human beings. That is the kind of thing evil spirits do, as Zoroaster noted in his Magical \[Prophecies\]. (See Pierre Le Loyer, Spectres Book 4, penultimate chapter).

So, what, (someone may say), do you say about those people whose souls, we read, have been told to go into their body again and, indeed, some \[souls\] which have been sentenced to the tortures of Hell and have been released therefrom? (An example is Andreas of Rome in 1070, whose story is well worth reading and can be found in Book 1, chapter 33 of the life of St Hanno of Köln in \[Laurent\] Sauer, Volume 6, for 4th December.) My opinion, of course, is that God has sentenced them to Hell only for the time being, because there is no relief from Hell when someone has been sentenced to it without remission. But it can sometimes happen that shortly before they seem to die, they have not actually breathed their last, but have simply experienced an ecstasy during which, because of what they see and what is shown to their mind\'s eye by God, they think they have died and have really been hurried off to judgement and to everything else they tell us about. Then, after they have told their story, they really do breathe their last, either at once or after a long time.

This is Cardinal Baronio\'s theory, as well as that of others, and \[can be found\] in Volume 11 of his Annals \[of the Church\], under the said year 1070. But although Andreas says there that he really had died, he is not actually saying he had been sentenced to perpetual tortures in Hell, simply that he had been dragged to the places of eternal damnation, (which can also be said about someone damned for the time being). This is the opinion of St Thomas \[Aquinas\], Durandus, Richardus, and Alonso Tostado. (Read \[Cardinal\] Bellarmino On Purgatory, where he discusses the soul of Trajan; \[Gregorio de Valencia, Part 3, disputation 11, question 1, point 2; and Francisco Suárez, Part 3, volume 2, disputation 43, section 3.)

It is equally difficult to distinguish the apparition of angels from that of saintly human beings, because I think that apparitions of those who are in Purgatory are, for the most part, revealed to us as substitutes for those of good angels. I know that learned men have mentioned certain signs-that human souls which are already blessed appear with a rather happy expression, \[whereas\] those still expiating their sins appear with one which is gloomy and rather sad. (This is certainly quite likely). They add that appearances of angels are distinguished from those of humans as follows: the souls are human if they look like bearded old men, children, or females, or if they are clad in mourning, because none of this is fitting for angels, just as they do not show themselves in the guise of any base creature, not even that of a dove.

Likewise, if they preach or teach things which exceed human understanding, or if they demand prayers or any other assistance. One is disposed to ask these \[learned men\], can guardian angels take the form of those whose guardians they are and who are expiating their sins in Purgatory? Certainly, it is a possibility and is what usually happens, and \[learned men\] do not deny this. So why, under those circumstances, would \[the apparitions\] ask for prayers or some similar help? Why on these occasions do they not appear in the form they had while they were alive-a child, a bearded old man, a gloomy man, or a female-so that they can be recognised? Secondly, don\'t the blessed now know many things which surpass \[earthly\] travellers\' understanding? Don\'t they also know what is going to happen, as angels do, if God has revealed it to them?
[Page 143]

These signs, therefore, are unreliable and a number of anecdotes contradict them. We find it written in the lives of the \[Church\] Fathers that an angel with the appearance and face of an old man appeared to a hermit who wanted to understand the inscrutable depth of God\'s judgements. There is a very wellknown story about St Augustine. While he was giving thought to his commentaries on the Trinity, a child appeared to him. The child was making a vain, useless attempt to pour the sea, regardless of how big it is, into a small ditch with a spoon. He could not be mistaken for an angel, any more than the other \[apparition\] in the very old story about St Dorothea\'s struggle. This one was like a very pretty, delicate four-year old child and had brought delightfully blushing roses and fresh apples to Theophilus in the middle of winter, just as the virgin, boldly trusting in God, had promised, even though everything in the area was bristling with frost and shrivelled up because it was so cold. Rufinus \[of Aquileia\] and St Bonaventure testify that Wisdom and Chastity presented themselves to St Gregory of Naziazenus\'s sight and, unless this had happened to him while he was asleep, one might suspect they were angels in female form.

St Lorenzo Giustiniani claims that a virgin, brighter than the sun, appeared to him to tell him she was God\'s Wisdom who had taken human form for the sake of reforming humankind, and that God had given her to him \[St Lorenzo as his bride. She then gave him the kiss of peace and withdrew. His biographers say this was our Lord Jesus appearing to him in that form. I should think it was an angel rather than \[Jesus\], because if Christ Himself appeared in female form, why do we not believe that angels sometimes make themselves seen in that same guise?

There is a life of St Gommar who is venerated by my fellow Brabantines, recorded either by Theobald or some other ancient eloquent writer, in which you can find that an angel, sent by God, presented itself to be seen in the guise of a dove glowing with a remarkable brilliance on top of his sleeve. It advised him against a pilgrimage he intended to make, and suggested he change to loftier studies and labours. The holy man obeyed, and in nothing which took place afterwards can one detect the mocking deception of an evil spirit. On the contrary, several things \[happened\] from which it appears that this took place on God\'s instructions.

(I am not unmindful that evil spirits have, in the past, misused the image of a dove which has been set apart as \[a symbol of\] the Holy Spirit). So I am persuaded that this apparition was that of a good angel. Another vision, conferred upon Wambert while he was awake, seems to tell us that an angel appeared to him in the form of an enormous eagle-see \[Laurent\] Sauer, the Life of St Bertulf, chapter 12, under 5th January-although if anyone were to argue that this was a real bird, (as it seems was the one Bishop Noger writes about in The Translation of St Laudoaldus, in Sauer, volume 2), I should not particularly object.³⁸¹

I recount these and similar \[anecdotes\] so that \[you may not think\] I am agreeing unreservedly with the signs recorded by the \[writers\] I have mentioned. But \[I do so\] principally because I think that the whole discernment of spirits-and \[the writers I have mentioned\] do not deny it-depends first and foremost upon divine revelation. Moreover, my judgement, (like that of St Augustine), is that it is safer and more preferable that I acknowledge my ignorance in this matter and take the role of pupil rather than, with ill-considered rashness, give an explanation of something and behave as though I were the teacher. The subject, after all, is too important for me to be able to undertake it, too abstruse for me to be able to explain it, and too deep for me to be able to wade through it. This is what I have to say about the third proof.
[Page 144]

Book 2, Question 26, Section 4

One looks for the fourth proof in divine and human authority. I call \'human\' the authority which one does not look for in the sanctuaries of theology but in the shrines of human wisdom. Let me separate these proofs. Let the fourth one be the one I drew at length from pagan opinion at the beginning of the Question. Add to this what Plato thought in relation to this in his Phaedo, \[Hermes\] Trismegistus in his Letter to Tatius, Apuleius in Defence 1, Maximus of Tyre in his book on Socrates\'s deity, Plutarch in his \[Life of\] Brutus, Pliny the Younger in his letters, and Lucian in The Lover of Lies. (I shall omit Homer, Aeschylus, Euripides, Vergil, Seneca, and the rest of the poets, and also the historians \[Cassius\] Dio, Pausanias, Tacitus, and Suetonius). One looks for the fifth proof in the writings of certain astrologers who think that the appearances of souls depend upon a particular conjunction of and influx from the stars-Ptolemy, for example, Julius Firmicus, Albumazar, and other Arabs whom Pomponazzi followed; and even if others refuted their heresy with learning and absolutely accurate arguments, it is still perfectly clear that these people, (practically atheists), recognised the appearances of souls, with the result that the heretics of our time are quite rightly adjudged to be worse than they were.

The sixth proof can be taken from the opinions of Chaldaeans, Mahometans, Kabbalists, and Rabbis, all of whom are in unanimous agreement that we should accept that souls do appear. (I am talking about a \'synagogue\', \[i.e. the witches\' sabbat\] now condemned after Christ\'s crucifixion). It is agreed that Josephus was of this opinion, as much because he thought the soul of Samuel really did appear as because he says that \[Marcus Valerius\] Catullus had been dreadfully troubled by the spirits of the men he had slaughtered. Simeon Ben Joaz thought the same in his book Machar Hasudoth; Rabbi Haccados records many apparitions in his book Galerazaya \[\'Revealer of Secrets\'\], as do others cited by \[Pierre\] Le Loyer. \[Le Loyer\] also proves the same point in the case of Kabbalists, who do not call the \'spectre\' or \'shade\' of pagans \'neshema\' \[spirit\] or \'ruach\' \[breath\], but \'nephesh\' \[soul\]. (See Servius: Vergil called it an \'image\'). They mean \[what\] is seen after death in the neighbourhood of tombs.

Muslims believe the same because of their pseudo-prophet, as Avenzoar \[Ibn Zuhr\] and Avicenna \[say\]. This was the opinion of Zoroaster in his Magical \[Prophecies\], as it was of the Indian Brahmin of Calcutta, Coloimum, and Malabar, and all the other peoples of the east; and Floridians, Peruvians, and all other westerners believe the same thing. I thought it a good idea to draw attention to these people, not because I think their opinion worth anything, but because they contradict Epicureans who reject Holy Scripture, and heretics who spurn an opinion the Church has always had: in a word, because I am compelled to go into battle with goblins \[larvas\] and buffoons, as well as witches \[larvas\] and twaddle.

Legal archives and those of courts of justice provide the seventh proof. Ulpian recognises a kind of prodigy \[ostentum\], a фабиатератидес \[prodigious apparition?\] Martianus \[Capella\] the spirits of the dead \[Manes\], and \[Herennius\] Modestinus condemns the magical sacrifices whereby it was thought that spirits \[spiritus\] were invoked. What he calls \'summoning the spirits of the dead\' was something many people tried to do, and the Emperor Constantine made it a capital offence. (In relation to this, there is a different reading \[of the phrase in this passage, which has \'when evil spirits have been summoned, instead of \'when the spirits of the dead have been summoned, because for the most part, the spirits of the dead were actually evil spirits falsely claiming to be souls). Legal experts not of the humblest reputation have held this opinion about the appearance of souls: \[Guillaume\] Budé and \[Andrea\] Alciato on the word \'prodigy\' \[ostentum\]; Cristoforo Porzio in the last paragraph of his Institutes, \'de localibus\'; Arnoul Le Ferron in his Customary Law of Burgundy; Alessandro Alessandri, Festival Days Book 2, chapter 9, Book 4, chapter 19, Book 5, chapter 23, and Book 6, chapter 21; Diego de Covarrubias y Leyva, Various Resolutions Book 4, chapter 6; Pierre Le Loyer, Spectres, the whole of Book 3. In chapter 4 he tells us that Barnabé Brisson, President of the Paris Parlement, also held that opinion. Since the general sentiment of \[Church\] Councils, holy Fathers \[of the Church\], and theologians supports these authors, I have no hesitation in placing them before you, even though all the other legal experts think the opposite.
[Page 145]

Eighthly, my conclusion is proved because this has always been how the Church has understood it. It is proved in the time of the Old Testament because Samuel\'s soul appeared to Saul, not because it has been roused by the power of a magical incantation, but because it has been sent by God. This appears to be the more accurate opinion. It has had the support, not only of rabbis of long ago-Ahia, Sandia, and Eleazar-and these are certainly to be preferred to the innovator David Kimhi-but also of Josephus and very important Fathers \[of the Church\], Justinus in his Against Typhon and St Augustine in Care for the Dead. I proved earlier that this opinion is consonant with Holy Scripture, and if we are to accept Ecclesiasticus as canonical, as all Catholics do-I am astonished that St Thomas \[Aquinas\] was inclined to doubt it, Part 1, question 85, article 8, reply to objection 2-we cannot gainsay it, as weighty theologians of the present time have observed.

The objections Le Loyer puts to us in Spectres Book 4, chapter 1, as wordy as they are energetic, are of no great importance. (a) He does not distinguish between the following proposition, \'Samuel really did appear in a body he had assumed by the power of a magical incantation or by virtue of an evil spirit\', and this one, \'Samuel really did appear in a body he had assumed by order and command of God, as a result of which Saul and the prophetess were deceived because they thought it was the result of a magical evocation\'. Tertullian, Cyril, Anastasius, and the other Fathers he adduces actually denied the first proposition, and they were quite right to do so.

None of them discussed and denied the second one which I am now defending, and so all Le Loyer\'s devices which run counter my opinion fall to the ground. (b) God, he says, has forbidden anyone to ask the dead to tell him about any subject or to consult magicians or similar misleading individuals. But since Saul tried to do this, contrary to God\'s instruction, one should not believe that Samuel, a very great enthusiast for divine law, had been willing to co-operate, so to speak, or at least to be favourably inclined to the disobedient kind and given him satisfaction. My answer is that God sent Samuel to reprove the disobedient king, threaten him with immediate punishment, and so encourage him to repent. Likewise we read that because they had garlanded the doorways \[of houses\], similar apparitions happened not infrequently to Christians in the time of Tertullian for the sake of getting these sinners to ask to be set free \[from their slavery to the Devil\].

Eusebius tells us that Natalius was scourged by angels in the same way because he had attached himself to a group of heretics, and St Augustine tells a similar story, as though it were well known to him, about a citizen of Hippo \[who received warnings\] because he was accused of breaking an oath by swearing falsely. Bede testifies that when Lawrence, Bishop of Canterbury, was thinking about running away from his flock, he was scourged by St Peter; and during the reign of the Emperor Maurice, the Blessed Virgin appeared to a landlord while he was asleep and ploughed round his knees with a switch because he had not thrown blasphemers out of his guest-house, and in the morning it was found that his shin-bones had been sundered from his legs. (See Nicephorus, History).

\(c\) \[Le Loyer\] objects that this was an evil spirit because it gave Saul no good advice at all and did not encourage him to be penitent but threw him into despair. My answer is that it is the duty of preachers and confessors to put the brevity of life before people, and to repeat the prophet\'s verse, \'Set your house in order because you are going to die and you are not going to live\', \[Isaiah38.1\] and, \'Fool, tonight they will take your soul away from you\', \[Luke 12.20\], and other things of this kind by which they can induce a holy fear. But if sinners thereby seize the opportunity to lose hope, it is wrong to blame the preacher and impute the guilt to him because \[the sinner\] has misused good medicine. One can see similarities in the case of Samuel. The things he was proposing as proper for penitence, Saul turned into bad medicine. Secondly, everyone is perfectly well aware that sometimes pieces of advice and encouragement help when they are not given directly but expressed, as it were, indirectly, because this way they have the power to console and call \[someone\] back from wrongdoing.
[Page 146]

My opponent should have considered this and not employed the argument he did. (d) With regard to the passage from Ecclesiasticus, chapter 46, verse 23, he replies that he meant merely that the writer was there recording Samuel\'s individual praises and was not particularly bothered whether the vision was false or real. The words, however, do not support this. Here is my translation: \'And after this he fell asleep and showed and made known to the King the end of his life. He lifted up his voice from the earth in prophecy to destroy the ungodliness of the nation\'. Samuel \'made it known to the King\' after his own death, or after \'he fell asleep\'. This is the Greek: xαì µετà Tò úπvwoal αútòv проеTherefore, the one who was dead prophesied and, coming out of the ground, made his proclamation-Ex MS TNV that Saul, the ávouíav \[lawlessness\] of the people would die.

\(e\) He objects that the Fathers \[of the Church\] say that the author of a book in Greek, Questions for Orthodox \[Christians\], (which he talks about with little expertise), is actually Justin Martyr, who holds the opposite opinion. Indeed, this legal expert rashly pronounces that whatever is recorded in that book agrees entirely and in every detail with Holy Scripture. (If this were the time, and the place permitted, there would be no shortage of objections to be made to this). But he also objects that St Augustine has not been properly understood because in chapter 15 of his book Care for the Dead St Augustine very plainly, (and this has been said with my opponent\'s good leave), agrees with me, as I have said elsewhere and now Jesuit theologians have clearly demonstrated.

Other Fathers take no issue at all with me, because they merely wanted to deny that Samuel could have been woken by the power of a magical incantation, and because they themselves used to attribute this apparition to the magic of the prophetess-an idea which was neither necessary nor true-they slipped away from that kind of idea and the result was that they denied that anything other than an evil spirit had appeared; and because I think that notion is untrue, when it comes to this subject I have abandoned those Fathers in favour of others. (f) After saying, at length, a good many things which are irrelevant and πapépyws \[beside the point\], he thinks that the verse, \'Tomorrow you will be with me\' Kings/Samuel 2 was a lie if it was said by the prophet \[Samuel\]. I have already suggested, at the end of Question 6 in this Book, that this verse was clearly true. So, the authority of Holy Scripture on the soul of Samuel is clear enough. A still clearer \[example\], as St Augustine says, is that of Moses who appeared with Christ our Lord on Mt Tabor, even though he really was deadSt Augustine thinks it quite likely \[he appeared\] in an assumed body made out of air-and a third example is that of Jeremiah and Onias who appeared to Judas Maccabaeus, \[2 Maccabees 15.12-14\].

But let me come to Christian times-to omit the Jews, whose opinion on this subject has been uniform, with the exception of the Sadducees-because heretics indiscriminately deny apparitions of the dead in general, whether \[the spirits\] are blessed or not. Therefore I am going to deal with these in general, whether \[the spirits\] are already in Heaven or have not yet \[gone there\], and I shall run through every epoch of the Church so that it may be absolutely clear that Catholics have really always had this perception; and \[I shall give\] examples of some people who confirmed it after investigation and others who proved it clearly and satisfactorily by providing examples of apparitions they considered to be perfectly true.

Right from the start this was the opinion of the early Church, of the Apostles, and of Christ our Lord Himself, because when He was aware that the Apostles believed it, He did not rebuke them and say the opposite, (because the best teacher and true light of the world was not going to let this pass by), but rather fixed this opinion more firmly in their minds by His silence, sanctioning it by means of this indirect reply, as we shall see in a moment. For example, after His resurrection, Christ our Lord came to the Apostles even though the doors were closed, something they did not think possible unless it was done by a spirit \[spiritu\]. They thought they were seeing a spirit and were therefore extremely disturbed and terrified. Indeed, Theophilus expressly declares that they thought it was some soul which had come back from another life. St Augustine, Eucherius, Bede, Cardinal Hugo, and many others had the same idea, but they thought it was Christ\'s soul in an assumed body \[made of air, not His own body, and that \[the Apostles\] were not yet certain He had risen again. Christ knew what they were thinking and replied that by touching His real body they could know He was not a spirit. The Apostles had had the same suspicion and fear before the Passion when they saw Him walking on water. They had been reproved on that occasion, not because they thought that ghosts \[spectra\] sometimes do exist, but because they thought it was not Christ who was then appearing to them.
[Page 147]

That others had the same opinion at the time is demonstrated from \[a passage\] by St Luke. When Rhoda told the whole Church which was in Jerusalem that Peter had been freed from prison and was standing at the gate, \[Acts 12.1415\] they said she was mad, because they were fully persuaded that Peter was alive and being kept in prison. They knew the soul of a living person could not appear outwith his body, and so they rebuked the girl\'s mistake \[in thinking\] it was the soul of a living person. They thought it was an angel disguised as Peter. Had they believed Peter was dead, they would have agreed with the girl. In this passage, the Greek word \[used\] is áγγεdos \[angel\].

So, the passage proves that sometimes souls are represented by angels, ÉTEPOTPOWNWS \[taking on a different appearance\]. But the preceding passages by Luke in the Gospels do not have but quite clearly have Tò πveûµα \[spirit\]. In Matthew, the word is фávtaoua \[ghost\]. My considered opinion about these \[words\] is this. In Matthew, the word is used as a generic term, and so we may understand that this opinion is as much about angels as it is about souls. In Luke, the phrase Tò πveûµα is used, and so we may restrict it to souls, or at least not restrict it to angels alone. In Luke in the Acts, the word \'angel\' is used, and so we may realise that when it comes to angels, the Church has had one and the same opinion. Certainly \[φávtaopa and πveûµα\] are more or less indistinguishable, and the result is that the Sadducees of old and the heretics of today attack both equally.

But who cannot laugh at \[Ludwig\] Lavater\'s argument that πveûµα simply means \'angel\'? So when the flesh resists the \'spirit\' in our bodies, it is not resisting the soul, but an angel, because St Paul uses the word πveúµatos, \[Galatians 5.17\]. So according to Peter, Christ\'s soul met angels, not human beings while they were spending time in prison, (Èv филаки πveúµαoi), contrary to the common agreement of antiquity, Peter 3.19\]. So, in the \'hymn to the three children\' \[Daniel 3.26-45\], did Athanasius incorrectly use πveúpata to signify human souls? But I fritter away my time when I take such things seriously and disprove them.

Book 2, Question 26, Section 5

Now let me come to what has been said by \[Church\] Councils, \[Church\] Fathers, and \[Church\] historians, my intention being that what \[they say\] tells us this in no unintelligible manner and convinces us of it, (unless we are shameless and obstinate, which is a special mark of heretics). Let me cut off one way of escape for them-that certain people deny all visions. Let me make it clear that these people have not written anything worthwhile because, by using the same claim of right, each of them decides for himself which of all the historical books is not part of the sacred canon, making it up today and changing his mind tomorrow, as he pleases, and so they are able to refuse to accept \[both\] faith and authority. What is more insane than this? What is more pernicious for every part of the state and more worthy of serious punishment?

Others are more restrained. They allow and accept visions of angels, but only when it pleases them to do so, (when they don\'t give them trouble during a disputation!) But these same people understand every ancient account as follows: that the souls, even of the blessed, have never appeared and do not ever appear αůtonpoowπws \[in their own person\], (as they express it), but say that whenever one reads such accounts, one must take it to be referring to an apparition actually done by good or bad spirits \[spiritus\]. The rule of St Augustine and others-that when the words of Scripture can be translated in their strict sense, one should not have recourse to metaphorical or other figurative senses-is against these people, and most of the Scriptural words dealing with these subjects are so clear that anyone who applies them in a figurative sense cannot, in accordance with that same rule, distort them in this fashion at all.
[Page 148]

Secondly, there is nothing irrational which constrains us \[to believe in apparitions\], and I have said already that nothing too little consonant with reason can happen, is appropriate to happen, or is to our advantage to happen \[if we do believe in them\]. So why can we not believe very holy, very serious people when they tell us straightforwardly and without pretence that these \[apparitions\] take place aútorpoownws \[in their own person\], but \[feel obliged\] to thrust the word éteponpoownws \[looking like someone else\] upon them even if they do not want it as though, in the very way they talk about both kinds, they have not told us \[what kind of\] apparition it is? Doesn\'t St Cyprian, (to omit others), simply use the word \'visions\' when he is talking about one which takes place in the imagination and one which is done by angels, but when he talks about an apparition of one of the blessed, he adds that \'such and such\' a person appeared? The Acts of the Martyrs makes the same distinction, and so do the Life and the Dialogues of St Gregory. (See Cyprian, Letters 10, 11, 12, and 69; the Acts of the Martyrs in \[Laurent\] Sauer under 24th February and ist May; and St Gregory\'s dialogues passim.) But angels, too, have been accustomed to make clear this very point when they appear, as did the Archangel Gabriel when he provided a vision of himself in the shape of St Marcellinus. (See Eginhard, The Translation \[and Miracles\] of St Marcellinus and St Peter, Book 3, chapter 12.)

First Century

We had the first church in Spain, perhaps in the whole of Europe, dedicated by St James, son of Zebedee, to the Mother of God, St Mary of the Pillar, near the Ebro at Zaragoza. This was reported in the annals of Celtiberia and also accepted by such a firm tradition of the church there that no Catholic in that area has any doubts about it. The tradition is that the Blessed Virgin appeared to the Apostle of Spain standing on a pillar and ordered him to dedicate a small shrine to her on that spot. It is now a rich and noble church. If she appeared in a vision to St James where he was living while she was still alive elsewhere, why can the Queen of Heaven and earth not \[appear\] these days when she pleases? In a moment I shall demonstrate that she has often been pleased to do so. Her son has appeared more than once, so why not His mother as well? Christ showed Himself unmistakably to St Peter while \[Peter\] was running away from persecution, and said He was taking Himself off to Rome to be crucified again. Peter understood what the Lord meant, turned back, and was taken to a cross. So, this is how Christ was crucified again in Peter. (St Ambrose, Against Auxentius on Handing over New Basilicas \[13\], St Gregory on the fourth penitential psalm, and Hegesippus, The Ruin of Jerusalem Book 3, chapter 2 bear witness to this). In letter 8, addressed to Demophilus, \[section 6\], St Dionysius the Areopagite tells us that Jesus, accompanied by many angels, appeared to Carpus of Crete and rebuked him because he was looking for the punishment of a lapsed brother, someone who was leading people astray; and during the reign of the Emperor Vespasian, there lived St Martial, Bishop of Limoges, a disciple of Christ. He saw the soul of the martyr St Valeria going up to Heaven like a bright flame. (This story is told in the Acts of St Martial in \[Laurent Sauer\]).

Second Century

In his second Defence, addressed to the Emperors, Justin Martyr says that the souls of the dead return and sometimes appear, and he uses the pagans\' actual evocations made by magicians to persuade them that \[souls\] are therefore immortal; and in his dispute with Trypho he claims that Samuel\'s soul really was evoked. Tertullian has no disagreement at all with what I am saying, but only with magical incantations when he says, \'But if God\'s power has called back certain souls into their bodies as proofs of His right \[to do so\], this will not therefore be shared by the self-confidence and audacity of magicians, the deceptiveness of dreams, and poets\' \'freedom of speech\', \[The Soul 57.12\]. He also makes the truth clear for us because he hints that this has sometimes been done, and he adds the reason for its having been done. He also makes an even clearer statement about the soul. \'If anyone asks about someone who has been dead for a long time as though he were still alive, the answer comes back straight away, \"He has \[life\] now and is obliged to come back\".
[Page 149]

These testimonies of the soul are as straightforward as they are true, as widespread as they are straightforward, as commonly believed as they are widespread, as commonly believed as they are natural, and as divine as they are natural, \[The Testimony of the Soul 4.11-5.1\]. This can refer not only to the resurrection of bodies, but also to the miraculous return of souls. Origen correctly thought that certain \[souls\] can return, but this is not at all satisfactory for us under the present circumstances because he restricted \[that return\] to the souls of the wicked. In Book 7, \[chapter 5\] of Against Celsus, \[he says\], \'A soul which is wicked and has been dragged back to earth by its vicious wrongdoing in such a way that it cannot even aspire to higher things wanders about and roams the earth, now flitting about in the neighbourhood of tombs where the phantoms of shadowy spirits are quite often seen, now traversing these pieces of land. What kind of spirits should we believe these are, which in every age have been confined and hobbled by acts of poisonous magic \[veneficiis\]?\' etc.

There is no doubt that in Origen\'s time, St Potamiaena appeared to Basilides, her executioner, and converted him to the Faith, and not only him, but several others, too, as Eusebius tells us in his \[History of the Church\] Book 6, chapter 4; and Origen himself, in Against Celsus Book 1, \[chapter 46\], testifies, \'Many have been brought to the Christian religion, more or less unwillingly, by some kind of spirit \[spiritu\] which suddenly changed their mind in such a way that, after they had been shown visions either while they were asleep or while they were awake, they would then not hesitate to go to meet death on behalf of the Word whom they had hated. I have seen many such instances, and if, in what I am writing here, I were to recount merely what they did, unbelievers would start to jeer, thinking I was making it up just like those they wrongly suspect are doing so. God, however, is witness to my conscience that I am trying to make people favourable towards Jesus\'s teaching, not by telling deceitful anecdotes, but by giving true examples.\' This is what he says, and I myself could undoubtedly put \[such examples\] before \[Ludwig\] Lavater, \[Johannes\] Rivius, and similar unbelievers, and cram almost every page with them.

Third Century
