St John the Evangelist, along with the Mother of God, appeared to St Gregory the Miracle-Worker, as St Gregory of Nyssa, \[Gregory\'s\] very important biographer, tells us in a style which may be accurate, but is also long-winded. When the vision is that of an angel, he usually elaborates upon it, as he does in the visions given to St Ephrem. (See Gregory\'s biography of him). St Cyprian also makes mention of night visions in Letter 12, and in Letter 69 he severely rebukes those who reject visions of this kind.

The martyred son of St Quartillosa appeared and confirmed that his mother had been martyred. The story is told in the letter of African confessors which Laurent Sauer produced in Vol. 1, under 24th February, and in an expansion to it, St Cyprian, too, is said to have appeared to the confessors Montanus and Flavianus to encourage them. Other authentic Acts of the Martyrs of Sidra, reproduced by Sauer, (Volume 3, under ist May), say that St Cyprian appeared to Marianus and stretched out his hand, and that a child-martyr was seen by Agapius just before \[the child\] received his crown \[of martyrdom\]. In sermon 90, St Ambrose says that St Agnes appeared to her parents while they were keeping constant vigil one night at her tomb. She was accompanied by a large number of virgins and spoke to \[her parents as follows. \'Please don\'t mourn me now that I am dead, Mother and Father, because I am alive in Heaven with these virgins in the presence of Him whom I loved with all my heart \[while I was\] on earth\'. \[Sauer\] adds that several years later, while Constantia was asleep, \[St Agnes\] promised her health and fulfilled her promise. Earlier, too, during the reign of Decius, St Peter appeared to St Agatha in prison, and restored her breast to her. (Acts of St Agatha, under 5th February, in Sauer.) Later, \[St Agnes\] appeared to St Lucia and freed her mother Eutychia from a bloody flux, (Acts of St Lucia, under 13th December, in Sauer).
[Page 150]

After 300AD, or Fourth Century

Christ our Lord deigned to appear to St Peter of Alexandria in the form of a child looking for its clothes which had been torn by Arius, (Sauer, Volume 6, under 15th November). In Volume 2 of his letters and decretals, \[Pope\] Hadrian says that in the prefaces to Synod VII, or the Second Council of Nicaea, Acts 2, after the blessed Emperor Constantine was converted to the Christian faith, one reads the following: \'When the day was at an end and silence fell and it was time to go to sleep, lo and behold, the holy Apostles, Peter and Paul, suddenly appeared to the King, saying, \"Since you have put an end to your disgraceful crimes and have developed a horror of shedding the blood of innocents, we have been sent by the Lord Jesus Christ to give you advice on how to recover soundness of mind. So, you have heard our warnings\", etc.

Notice they say they were sent by Christ, and that they are said to have appeared to the King unmistakably αůtonpoowπws \[in their own persons\]. They are called \'Apostles\'. In other visions which came to Constantine while he was fighting, those who appeared in them were not named, because they were angelic spirits, (Zonaras, \[Historical Extracts\] Volume 3). The following also happened. When Constantine ordered the building of a church for the Mother of God. She herself appeared to the architect and instructed him where the pillars could be erected. (Gregory of Tours, The Glory of the Martyrs, chapter 8). Spiridion and Tryphillus earlier appeared to Constantius while he was asleep and cured him, and yet they were still alive. (From \[St Simeon\] Metaphrastes in Sauer, Volume 6, under 12th December). It is as possible to manage to have a conversation with a dead person as it is to get him to appear.

Socrates, (\[History of the Church\] Book 1, chapter 8 \[12\]) and Rufinus, (\[History of the Church\] Book 1, chapter 5), say this in connection with that same Spiridion. Let me quote what Rufinus says. \'He had a daughter called Irene and when she had looked after him well, she died, a virgin. After her death, someone came along saying he had entrusted something to her keeping. Her father said he had no knowledge of this, \[but\] he searched the whole house diligently. He did not find what he was looking for anywhere, but the man who had entrusted it \[to Irene\] persisted and went on pressing the matter with wailing and tears. He kept swearing he would put an end to his life unless he recovered what he had entrusted to her. Moved by the man\'s tears, the elderly \[father\] hurried off to his daughter\'s tomb and started calling her by name. Then, from the tomb, she said, \"What do you want, Father?\" \"Where did you put what that man entrusted to you?\" he asked. She told him where it was. \"You will find it buried there\", she said. He went back home, found the object, just as his daughter had told him from the tomb in answer to his question, and handed it back to the man who had kept on asking for it\'.

Of course, it was possible for her to have replied and not appear, just as it was in the case of those who, although already dead, added their signatures to the Synod of Nicaea. Gregory of Caesarea, a priest, reported an extraordinary story. (See \[Luigi\] Lippomano, Volume 6, \'A Discourse on the Synod of Nicaea\'.) \'Two holy bishops\', he said, \'Chrysanthus and Musonius, had not yet appended their signatures to \[the Synod\'s\] decree, and it happened that, by divine providence, they departed this human life. So, the holy fathers \[of the Synod\] came to the place where \[the two bishops\] were sleeping together \[in death\], and once \[the Fathers\] were with them, \[the bishops\] heard what was being said. \"Brethren and fathers\", said \[the Synod Fathers\], \"you have fought a magnificent fight along with us, you have finished the race, you have preserved the Faith. So, if you decide that what \[the Synod\] has done is pleasing to God, (because you now perceive \[things\] in a purer way), let there be nothing to stop you from signing this decree as well\". They all said this together, put their seals on the decree, put it in the bishops\' coffin, and spent the whole of that night awake in prayer. Next day, when they came to the coffin, they found that the seals they had attached \[to the decree had been broken, and when they unrolled the holy decree, they found that the holy bishops\' signatures had been added to it\'. Nicephorus, (\[History of the Church\] Book 8, chapter 23), adds the words which had been written at the bottom. \'Chrysanthus and Musonius. We took part in the first holy ecumenical Synod \[of Nicaea\] with all the fathers, and even though we have been removed in body, we have still appended our signatures to this document in our own hand.\'
[Page 151]

From this it is clear that Spiridion and of all the fathers at the First Synod \[of Nicaea\] thought that, with God\'s permission, souls are able to return to us, because otherwise he would not have invoked his daughter and \[the bishops\] would not have asked for a signature. A provincial Council, older than that held in Nicaea seeing that it happened before Constantine while a persecution \[of Christians\] was still going on, took place in Spain in Elvira, and in its Canon 34 we read, \'The spirits of the dead should not be disturbed\' which makes it clear that the spirits of the dead survive and know what is happening near their tombs. From this it also follows that sometimes they are present in those places, and that pagan rituals, springing from error, which are concerned with souls\' flitting round their tombs for a hundred \[days\], are abominable. This is what I think the canon is referring to, and Nemesius does not seem to disagree entirely with this opinion in his Пері Yuxns \[The Soul\], a book which is wrongly attributed to Nicenus.

In 366 the Emperor Julian was killed by the spearhead of an unidentified individual. St Basil had a vision of this before it happened. St Mercurius appeared to him, along with the Blessed Virgin, and as \[the saint was leaving, he was carrying a bloody spear, as St \[John\] of Damascus, relying on Helladius, one of St Basil\'s disciples, reported in his Holy Images 1. At the same time, too, someone else, while making his way to the camp, saw, either while he was awake or while he was dreaming, several of the Apostles and prophets gathered together, looking into the wrongs Julian had done to the Church and generally reflecting upon them. Two of their number urged the rest to be of good courage and left and, returning to the company not long afterwards, informed it that the tyrant had been killed. Sozomen tells this story in his \[History of the Church\] Book 6, chapter 2, but leaves out the names of the two men. Nicephorus, however, added that the two were once the famous soldiers and martyrs, Mercurius and Artemius. (See his \[History\] Book 10, chapter 35). St Febronia, virgin and martyr, used to appear every year on her birthday at midnight, during night prayers, to everyone in the choir, occupying the very place where she used to sing the psalms. (\[St Simeon Metaphrastes in Sauer, Volume 3, under 15th June.) St Antony saw the soul of St Ammon, accompanied by angels, going to Heaven, (Athanasius, Life of St Antony; Palladius says the same in his Life of Ammon). The Blessed Virgin came down from Heaven and appeared to St Martin, Bishop of Tours, and the soul of the robber who was revered as a martyr rose from the dead \[and appeared to him\], (Sulpicius Severus in his Life of St Martin). St Athanasius in his Life of \[St\] Antony and St Jerome in his Life of St Martin tell us that the soul of St Paul, the first hermit, appeared to St Antony, borne to Heaven by angels.

Nemesius, whom I cited just now, was of the same opinion. So was St Basil in his book True Virginity, just before the middle where, after saying that even when she is alone, a virgin should always stand in awe of the presence of God and her guardian angel, he adds, \'Why should I go through every detail? There are countless numbers of angels as well as the most blessed spirits of the holy Fathers \[of the Church\]. There is not one of them who could not contemplate individual things anywhere, and even if \[these angels and spirits\] are not at all visible to the eyes of the flesh, they embrace and recognise these things with their nonphysical eyes and understand everything.\' He means, therefore, that the souls of the saints are with us when they wish to be and that, when God gives His permission, there is nowhere they cannot go on in successive stages-don\'t have idle thoughts that they are at all ubiquitous-and that there is nothing in relation to Nature and human actions which they cannot understand at any time. In his panegyric of St Mamas the Martyr, St Basil signifies that St Mamas had appeared to many people. Remember the martyr\', he says, \'as often as you get a sight of him by dreaming about him\'; and in his oration in praise of Caesarius, St Gregory of Nazianzus says his brother often appeared to him while he was asleep, and although he adds, \'whether because of my strong desire \[to see him\]\'-that is to say, those who love fashion dreams for themselves--- \'or in reality\', this is clear testimony of his opinion that it is possible for souls to appear \[to the living\]. (Several of the Fathers I need to cite live in this century. But because their deaths and the greater part of their lives fall into the following century, I have postponed \[mention of them\] until then).
[Page 152]

\[Fifth Century\]

The period between 400 and 500 should be ascribed to the fifth century and had men who were very well-known for their sanctity as well as their learning. The passing of St Martin was shown to St Severinus and St Ambrose while neither of them was present, and he also appeared in order to bring help to those who were preparing to translate his body. He appeared, not just once, elsewhere, as Gregory of Tours says in his books The Miracles of St Martin and The Glory of the Confessors. Herodias had ordered that St John the Baptist\'s head be buried very deep, and many centuries later the saint himself appeared to two monks while they were asleep and showed them where it was; and although the memory of that place was lost and people did not know where \[the head\] was hidden, before Marcian became Emperor St John pointed out, in an extraordinary way, to a monk, Marcellus, the spot where his head was buried. (Prosper and Sigisbert in Sozomen\'s History \[of the Church\] Book 8, chapter 21 and Nicephorus, \[History of the Church\] Book 12, chapter 49).

St Ambrose found out where the bodies of Saints Gervase and Protase were buried from a vision he had of them, as St Augustine bears witness, (The City of God Book 22, chapter 8 and Confessions Book 9, chapter 7.) The words of St Ambrose himself confirm this opinion of mine as well when, in the same sense as St Basil, he testifies that the spirits \[spiritus\] of the saints exist everywhere. \'All saints, wherever they may be, do good to everyone, although those who have undergone tortures on our behalf are especially intercessors for us, because when a martyr suffers, he does not suffer only for himself, but for the citizens, too\', (Sermon 77 on the feast of St Octavius, St Adventor, etc.)

In his biography of St Ambrose, St Paulinus-perhaps \'the Deacon\', not St Paul of Nola-says, \'On the day he died, as it says in a letter which Simplicianus, his successor, a venerable man, received and which was addressed to him from the east, as though \[Ambrose\] were still alive with us-the letter is held permanently in a monastery in Milan-he appeared to certain holy men, praying with them and laying his hands on them. The letter is dated the day on which it was sent, and when we read it, we found it was the day on which \[Ambrose\] died.

In Tuscany, in the city of Florence, where the holy man Zenobius is now bishop, because he had promised he would visit them quite often, he was frequently seen praying at an altar in the church of Sant\'Ambrogio which he had had built there. (I was told this by the holy priest Zenobius.) In the same house in which he stayed to avoid Eugenius when Rhadagaisus was laying siege to \[Milan\] and the men of the city had already mentally given up hope, he appeared to someone in a vision and promised he would be with them on another day. At this, the citizens\' spirits were raised, and on the second day Stilicho arrived with his army and was victorious over the enemy.

(I learned this from Pansophia, a devout woman, mother of the boy Pansophius). He appeared to Mazcezel, who was despairing of his own safety and that of the army he was leading against Gildo, in a vision at night. He was holding a walking-stick in his hand and when Mazcezel prostrated himself at the saint\'s feet, the old man struck the earth with the stick he was using to help himself along, (and this was the third time he had appeared to Mazcezel in this guise), and said, \"Here, here, here!\" indicating the spot and giving then man whom he had thought worthy of this visitation to understand that he should realise he would gain victory on the third day in the very place in which he had seen the Lord\'s saintly priest. Thus, he began and ended the war, free from anxiety. (When I was appointed to Milan, I learned all this from what Mazcezel himself had said because in the see in which I now hold an appointment, \[Mazcezel\] had given an account of it to a very large number of priests, and so I thought I could quite safely add to this book what I found out earlier from them.)
[Page 153]

In Milan we also received, with the greatest reverence, the relics of the martyrs Sisinnius and Alexandrinus who, in my time, after St Ambrose\'s death, gained the crown of martyrdom in the district of Agaunum because of persecutions by the heathen. I found out that a blind man arrived and said he had received his sight on the same day he touched the coffin in which the saints\' relics were being carried. In a vision at night, \[he said\], he had seen a ship approaching the coast, and in it there was a large number of men clad in white. While they were disembarking, he begged one of them to inform him who those men were and was told they were \[St\] Ambrose and his companions. When he heard that name, he prayed he might receive his sight, and the man said to him, \"Go to Milan and present yourself to my brethren who are going to come on\", (and he named the day), \"and you will receive your sight\". The \[blind\] man was from the coast of Dalmatia and kept saying he had never come to the city before until he made his way straight to the saints\' relics. But as soon as he touched them, he began to see\'.

This is what Paulinus has to say about \[St\] Ambrose, and his information is certainly reliable. All right! Now let me deal with someone else from this century-Prudentius, one of St Ambrose\'s colleagues, \[who wrote\] \'Against Symmachus\'. Here is Prudentius on St Eulalia.

\'The virgin, desiring a speedy death, Sought out and drank in the pyre with her mouth From which suddenly a dove sprang forth, Whiter than snow. It was seen To leave the martyr\'s mouth and make for the stars. This was Eulalia\'s spirit \[spiritus\], As white as milk, swift, and innocent.

On St Fructuosus and his friends.

\'A servant from the Governor\'s household saw The sky open to let the martyrs in And the illustrious men being carried through the stars. Yes, and he pointed it out to his master\'s little daughter And made her see the sign of her father\'s crimeThat those whom his law-court had killed were now alive in Heaven\'.

When, with devout enthusiasm, each of the brothers later tried to carry off some of the relics for himself, \[Prudentius\] adds, \'But lest they separate the relics, which were to be raised up again And soon exist together with the Lord, By burying them in different places, \[The martyrs\] were seen, clad in snow-white robes.

They gave instructions that the dust which would be treated as holy Be given back, mixed together, and enclosed in hollow marble\'.

In chapter 17 of his biography of St Germain d\'Auxerre, the priest Constantinus writes, \'The holy man was staying, undaunted, in a house infested with spectres when suddenly, right in front of a man who was reading a book, there stood a dreadful ghost \[umbra\] which, little by little drew itself erect before his eyes as he looked at it. Showers of stones were being thrown at the walls, too, so the man who had been reading was absolutely terrified and begged the priest to protect him. \[The saint\] immediately jumped up, looked the dreadful figure in the face and, first invoking the name of Christ, ordered it to say who it was and what it was doing there. \[The ghost\] laid aside its frightening lack of reality at once and spoke in a humble voice, like a suppliant, \[saying\] that he and a close friend had committed many crimes and were lying unburied, and that the reason they were disturbing people was that they themselves were unable to be at peace. He asked \[the saint\] to pray to the Lord for them so that they might deserve to receive rest. This made the holy man feel sorry \[for them\] and he told \[the ghost\] to show him where they were lying. Then, carrying a taper in front of him, the ghost led the way ahead and, in the dead of night, pointed out the spot where they had been thrown out among buildings which were in a state of complete ruin. When daylight had returned to the world, \[the saint\] called together people from the neighbourhood and, giving them a good example, applied himself to the work and encouraged them to clear away the old rubbish which had been thrown hurriedly on top of \[the bodies\]. When they had pulled it off with rakes, they found the bodies spreadeagled in a disorderly fashion, their bones still bound tightly with iron \[chains\]. \[The saint\] arranged for a grave to be dug in accordance with the law; the bodies were freed from their chains and clothed in pieces of linen; soil was thrown on top of them and they were buried; a prayer of intercession was said over \[the grave\], and the dead obtained their rest. As a result, after that day the house flourished, many people came to live in it, and there was no sign of \[its former\] horror\'. (See Sauer, under the month of July.)
[Page 154]

But let me throw in the authority and teachings of the Fathers \[of the Church\] along with all these examples which are so clear. (I shall return to other examples in a moment). In his book, Against Vigilantius, St Jerome, with a single example, slays all the innovators of our own day with the sword of sacred truth overlaid with golden plates of eloquence. \'You say the souls of the Apostles and the martyrs have a place in Abraham\'s bosom, or the place of consolation, or beneath God\'s altar, and that they cannot be in their graves and also wherever they like. They are, so to speak, of Senatorial rank, and are not shut up among murderers in a noise-some prison, but in unrestrained, honourable custody in the Islands of the Fortunate and the Elysian Fields. Will you constrain God with laws? Will you chain up the Apostles so that they are confined in custody until the Day of Judgement and are not with their Lord, even though it has been written, \"They follow the Lamb wherever He goes\"? If the Lamb \[is\] everywhere \[ubique\], it follows that they, too, who are with the Lamb are everywhere \[ubique\]; and when the Devil and evil spirits roam all over the globe and can be present everywhere \[ubique\] with unimaginable speed, will the martyrs be shut up in a box after they have shed their blood, and be unable to come out of it?\' \[Section 6\]. Substitute \'in any place at all\' \[ubilibet\] for \'everywhere\' \[ubique\], Reader-which is how I translated St Basil-and bear in mind at the same time that St Jerome\'s arguments cab also overturn those who deny that the souls of the saints can come out of Heaven.

In his book, Care for the Dead, St Augustine, a friend and admirer of St Jerome, is almost entirely in agreement that he proves that, according to God\'s usual law, souls cannot, of their own simple, unadorned natural ability, come out of the places to which they have been assigned, but that with God\'s exceptional permission, they can leave their reliquaries when they want, in order to be seen by human beings. Notice what he means when he says this. It is made absolutely clear by the contents of his argument. When he wrote in chapter 10 that the dead were said to have appeared to the living and that \[the living\] did not know where these people\'s bodies were lying unburied, and that \[the dead\] showed them the places and urged them to give them the burial they had not had, he adds, \'If we answer that these \[reports\] are not true, contrary to what a number of the faithful have written, and contrary to their own senses which confirm that such things have happened to them, people will think us impudent.⁴⁰ (Are not my opponents impudent when they boo the consensus of all ages off the stage?) But, so that we might understand that this does not happen by God\'s usual law, but by His particular ordinance and singular permission, \[St Augustine\] wrote of his mother in chapter 13 something my opponents fraudulently misrepresent to my discredit. \'If the souls of the dead were interested in the affairs of the living-and I cannot speak for other people-my mother would not fail to visit me conscientiously every night.

He also said clearly elsewhere, (chapter 15), in relation to that singular permission, something he did not say about \[God\'s\] usual law which is connected with it. \'Holy Scripture bears witness that apparitions of the dead are sent to the living and confirms this with those of Samuel and Moses\'. St Augustine therefore had every confidence in this and later, in chapter 16, he says that the confessor Felix \[appeared\] when Nola was being attacked by uncouth foreigners, and that he himself knew this not only from the benefits it brought, but also from the looks on people\'s faces, and not just because of unreliable rumours, but because there were reliable witnesses. \'But,\' he adds, \'these things are occasioned by divine providence in a way which is quite different from that in which things usually happen, which is \[the way\] which has been assigned to each individual type of created thing\'. The saintly Doctor therefore means that this can happen, but that it does not happen naturally and, in order to make this clear, he added a few lines later, \'Some things represent the limits of human experience, others are signs of God\'s power. There are some things which happen naturally, and others which happen miraculously, although God is also present in Nature so that this can happen, and Nature is not lacking in miracles.\'
[Page 155]

Miracles, of course, do indeed happen beyond the natural order, but they are never at odds with the natural character things have. What makes a louder sound than this trumpet of truth? What is clearer than this light of the Church? What do heretics dread more intensely than this hammer of falsehood? The author of the letter to Cyril of Jerusalem, (attributed, albeit falsely, to St Augustine), testifies that the spirits of the dead have taken an interest in what the living do, have assisted those who are still alive, and have been seen in our world. He tells us that St Jerome\'s soul appeared to him at the very time Jerome died and gave him a lot of good advice and learned instruction.

The authority and trustworthiness of this letter, however, are very small or non-existent, so let me turn to someone else. St \[John\] Chrysostom\'s homily 74, on John, acknowledges that the dead appear in dreams and that this was the opinion of the Apostle Jude Thaddaeus. In the homily he writes that one might think this was how Christ would appear after His death only to the Apostles and not to others. In Palladius we read that two brothers, Paesius and Isaias, were seen in the heavens by St Pambo, (in the section on \'Paesius and Isaias\'), that his dead brother was seen by Father Apollo, (in the section on \'Apollo\'), and that Anuph had seen the choirs of the blessed and that later on many people saw his soul lifted into Heaven by large numbers of angels and martyrs, (in the section on \'Anuph\').

Palladius was in the habit of explaining things clearly, and distinguishing between the vision of an angel and the vision of a soul, and he added that the angel had taken on human appearance, as you can see in chapter 86 which is about Evagrius the Deacon who was discouraged by the vision of an angel from loving a lecherous woman. In this chapter, too, you have a clear distinction between \[that and\] the apparition of evils spirits who were trying to lead him to heretical blasphemy.

Marcus of Gaza lived at almost the same time. He tells a story, of which he was an eye-witness, about the delegation of Bishop John of Caesarea and Bishop Porphyrius of Gaza to \[the Emperor\] Arcadius \[seeking permission to demolish the Marneion. In this account along with everything else he describes a very severe storm which God had allowed to rise because of the heretical wickedness of a ship\'s captain. \'We began to call upon \[him, using\] the prayers of St Procopius the Hermit, and when evening had come and the storm had not abated, we remained awake the whole night. Round about morning, however, after a great deal of suffering, the very holy bishops slept a little and, during sleep, my lord Porphyrius saw St Procopius the Hermit who spoke to them. \"Teach the ship\'s captain the catechism\", he said, \"seal him again-because he is a member of Arius\'s hateful sect-make sure he curses Arius and his wicked faith, and this great storm will abate at once\". Then \[Marcus\] adds that this happened and the sea grew calm.

Proclus of Constantinople saw St Paul helping and giving instructions to St \[John\] Chrysostom as \[John\] was writing his commentaries on St Paul\'s Epistles-this is reported, in writing, by Leo Augustus and \[St Simeon\] Metaphrastes in a Life of St \[John\] Chrysostom-and when \[St John\] was coming back from exile, St Basiliscus the Martyr, who suffered some time before in Nicomedia, appeared to him in the church in Comana, saying, \"Take heart, John, because tomorrow we shall be together\'. They say the martyr had also appeared earlier to the church\'s priest and had said, \'Prepare a place for Brother John, because he is about to come \[to Heaven\]\'. Palladius tells the story in his Dialogue⁴⁰ and adds that he confirmed this had happened, because the next day the saintly bishop died and was buried there \[in Comana\]. St Cyril of Alexandria, who was very much at odds with St Chrysostom was told in his sleep by the Mother of God to make his peace with the dead man. (Nicephorus, History \[of the Church\] Book 14, chapter 28, relying on Nicetas.)
[Page 156]

I shall pass over another vision dealing with St Chrysostom\'s glory, that of Adelphius, which was written down by Sophronius in his Spiritual Meadow, chapter 128, and quite rightly rejected by \[Cesare\] Baronio, \[Annals of the Church\] Volume 5, under the year 407. But an anecdote about Synesius, Bishop of Cyrene, and Evagrius the Philosopher, which Sophronius wrote, (relying on Leontius), in his Spiritual Meadow, chapter 195 is perfectly trustworthy. Synesius was constantly trying to convert \[Evagrius\] to the Christian religion, and \[Evagrius\] kept on objecting to Christian teaching on the resurrection of the body, the reward for good works, especially the hundredfold return after this life for one\'s almsgiving, and used to say he could not but think this was a fairy-tale. But in the end the Bishop, who spared no pains \[to convert him\], persuaded him to become a Christian and baptised him along with his children.

\'Not long after he had been baptised, he gave the Bishop three hundred gold pieces to be used for the benefit of the poor, saying, \"Take this gold, distribute it to the poor, and write me a bond in your own hand, saying that Christ will honour it in the next life\". \[Synesius\] took the gold and readily wrote the bond, as Evagrius had asked.

The philosopher lived for several years after his baptism, but at length he became mortally ill, and when he was near death he said to his sons, \"When you are making the arrangements for my funeral, put this document in my hands and bury it with me\". After his death, his sons did as he had instructed and buried him with the handwritten bond.

On the third day after the burial, he appeared to Bishop Synesius while he was sleeping at night, and said, \"Come to the tomb where I am lying and take your bond, because I have received what I was owed and have had satisfaction; and so that you can be assured of this, I have signed it in my own hand\".

Now, the Bishop did not know the bond had been buried with him, but next morning he summoned Evagrius\'s sons and said, \"Tell me, did you put anything in the tomb with your father?\"

Thinking he was asking about money, they said, \"Nothing, my lord, except the usual grave clothes. Why do you ask?\"

\"Did you, by any chance, bury a document with him?\"

The sons still did not know he was talking about a bond but, after thinking about it, said, \"Oh yes, my lord, when he was dying, he did give us some kind of document and said, \"When you bury me, put this document in my hands, but no one is to know about it\".

Then the Bishop told them the dream he had had that night and took them, along with some clerics and some of the most important of the citizens, to the philosopher\'s tomb. They opened it and found the philosopher lying there, holding in his hands the bond in the Bishop\'s handwriting. They took the bond from his hands, opened it, and found that the following had recently been added to it in the philosopher\'s handwriting. \"I, Evagrius, a philosopher, greet you, most holy Synesius, my lord Bishop. I have received what I was owed according to what you wrote in your own hand in this paper, have been given satisfaction, and have no further claim against you for the gold I have you and, through you, to Christ, our God and Saviour\", etc.

Hugo Eterianus tells the same story in his book, The Return of Souls from the Underworld, chapter 16. St Gamaliel, St Paul\'s teacher, appeared three times on different nights to Lucianus, a holy man and a priest, who was half awake at the time. (\[This happened\] not during the reign of Constantine-Nicetas makes this mistake in his oration on the martyrdom and discovery of St Stephen-but during that of Honorius and Archidius). He pointed out to him where the relics of his body were, and those of St Stephen, the first martyr, St Nicodemus, and St Abibus. They were found there and brought to the west by Orosius. There exists a letter by Lucianus, which describes this clearly, (see Sauer, Volume 4).
[Page 157]

The Spaniard \[Alcimus\] Avitus translated it into Latin from Greek, and a copy of this exists, too. Gennadius \[of Massilia\] mentions it in his Famous Men, chapter 47, and Marcellinus Comes in his Chronicles and Nicephorus in his \[History of the Church\] Book 14, chapter 9 have a favourable opinion of it. So that people would have more trust \[in the authenticity\] of this vision, St Gamaliel also appeared at the same time to Nygetius, a monk, and pointed westwards towards the location of the place, as Lucianus himself records. Evodius is witness that the first martyr himself appeared to several people as well. (See the \[anonymous\] book on the miracles of St Stephen); and one should read Sozomen, \[History of the Church\] Book 7, last chapter for an apparition of Micah and Habbakuk which happened before the ones I have just mentioned.

In Book 9, last chapter, Sozomen says the following about the body of the prophet Zacaraiah, which was discovered not long afterwards. \'Cophar is an estate in the territory of Eleutheropolis in Palestine. This place had an overseer, one Calimerus, a worker on the same estate, who was, to be sure, faithful to his master, but sometimes peevish and awkward and unfair to the neighbouring farmers. But even though he was like this, the prophet revealed himself while he was awake, appearing to him in broad daylight. He pointed out a particular garden and said, \"Please dig there\", etc. and the sacred body was found lying under the designated spot.

Terebon, a Saracen, saw Euthymius at night, although \[Euthymius\] was not in the area and was still alive at the time, and when, later on, \[Euthymius\] had cured him and Terebon had made a journey \[to visit him\], a large number of Saracens embraced the Christian faith. \[This happened\] in the year 420, as Cyril the Monk, an absolutely trustworthy historian has recorded. (See the life of St Euthymius in Sauer, Volume 1, under 20th January).

Round about the year 429 the Blessed Virgin, with St \[John\] the Baptist and St \[John\] the Evangelist, appeared to Father Cyriacus \[the Anchorite\] and said she would not come into his cell. Instead, she stayed outwith the door because, unknown to the Father, there were two books in his cell by Nestorius, an enemy of the Mother of God. (See Sophronius, Spiritual Meadow, chapter 66). Uranus tells a story about St Paulinus of Nola, (see the life of Paulinus in Sauer, Volume 3, under 22nd June). \[The saint\], he says, appeared to him before he died and St Januarius the martyr and St Martin of Tours had a conversation with him. 409 The Apostle Peter and his disciple, St Apollinaris, appeared to and were seen by Pope Sixtus in 433. They suggested that Peter, a very holy and a most eloquent man-truly \'golden of speech\' \[Chrysologus\]-should be made Archbishop of Ravenna. (This comes from the ancient archives of Ravenna in the history of the city by Girolamo Rossi).

In Book 2 of his Vandalic War, Procopius writes that St Cyprian appeared to the Libyans who had been oppressed and told them to be of good heart, and that in a short time he would be their avenger, and indeed the Vandals were routed two years later. In 456, King Theodoric \[II\] conquered the Suevi in Gallaecia \[Galicia\] and marched upon Lusitania \[Portugal\] where he was restrained from destroying the city of Emerita \[Mérida\] by an apparition of the martyr, St Eulalia. (See St Isidore \[of Seville\], History of the Goths, the Vandals, and the Suevi.)

In chapter 147 of The Spiritual Meadow, Sophronius \[i.e. Moschus talks about a letter of St Leo to Flavianus about correct faith, and Father Abbot Menas from the same monastery tells us the following story. \'\[The Abbot said\] he had heard Eulogius, the Patriarch of Alexandria, saying, \"When I went to Constantinople, I enjoyed a close friendship with Master Gregory, the Archdeacon of Rome, a remarkable man, and he told me a story about the most holy and blessed Roman Pontiff, Leo.
[Page 158]

He said that a church in Rome had a written document which said that when \[Leo\] had written a letter to Flavianus, the holy Bishop of Constantinople, against the irreligious Eutyches and Nestorius, he placed it on top of the grave of Peter, the Prince of the Apostles, and then spent time in prayer and vigils and fasts. He begged the chief of the Apostles, \"If, because I am simply a human being, I have written something here I should not have done, \[I ask\] you, to whom our Lord and Saviour Jesus Christ has entrusted both the Church and this See, to put it right\". After four days the Apostle appeared to him and said, \"I have read it and corrected it\". \[Then Leo\] picked up the letter from St Peter\'s tomb, opened it, and found that the Apostle had corrected it in his own hand. In chapter 148 he goes on to say, \'Theodore, the very holy Bishop of Dorna, which is in Libya, told me the following story. \"When I was chamberlain to holy Pope Eulogius, I dreamed I saw a man whose appearance and aristocratic demeanour inspired awe.

He said to me, \"Tell Pope Eulogius I have come\". I said to him, \"Who is it who is telling me to make this announcement?\" He replied, \"I am Leo, the Roman Pontiff\". So, I went in and announced, \"The very holy and blessed Pope Leo, Bishop of the See of Rome, wants to meet you\". When he heard this, Pope Eulogius rose from his seat and ran quickly to meet him. They greeted one another, said a prayer, and then sat down. The distinguished lord Leo said to holy Eulogius, \"Do you know why I have come here to you?\" \"No\", replied Eulogius. \"I have come\", said Leo, \"to thank you for your splendidly correct and proper reply to the letter I sent my brother Flavianus, the Patriarch of Constantinople. You explained my meaning and intention and shut the mouths of the heretics. Please understand, Brother, that you have given your holy labour and exertion not only to me, but also to Peter, supreme head of the Apostles, and to that Truth which is proclaimed to us before all others, Christ our God\". I was reassured by seeing this apparition not merely once, but two and three times, and told the holy Pope Eulogius \[about it\]\'.

In chapter 149, Sophronius \[i.e. Moschus\] says that St Peter appeared to the holy Leo on yet another occasion and told him his sins had been forgiven. Theodoret lived in this century and writes that the Apostles St John and St Philip once appeared to the Emperor Theodosius, (History \[of the Church\] Book 5, chapter 24). In the year 485, when Peter the Fuller was trying to bring the Church in Cyprus under his control, the Apostle St Barbabas appeared three times to Bishop Anthemius of Salamis on three successive night and showed him where his body was buried along with a Gospel of St Matthew written in the Evangelist\'s own hand. Both were found. (Alexander the Monk tells the story at length. See Sauret, Volume 3, under 11th June). I shall be briefer \[in my review\] of the following centuries.

The Sixth Century, that is, after 500AD

Many Severian heretics were converted by various visions-to begin with, for example, Isidore, a monk from Philoxene, \[who was converted\] by an apparition of a man who was damned because he had struck Christ our Lord on the cheek with his hand. (\'Sophronius\' tells the story at length in The Spiritual Meadow, chapter 30.) Then there was Cosmiana, the wife of a patrician Germanus, who was stopped by a visible appearance of the Mother of God and other holy women from entering a church because she was tainted by heresy. (\'Sophronius\', who adds many other examples in chapters 49, 70, 106, 188, and 213. John the Silent appeared in dreams to Regina, a woman from Cappadocia who had the office of deaconess, while he was still alive and living in Lavra, and told her what she needed to do. (Cyril \[of Scythopolis\], a monk, tells the story of this even in his Life of John the Silent. See \[Luigi\] Lippomano, Volume 6.) St Bartholomew appeared in Darae to Anastasius and pointed out where his body had been buried and promised to be the city\'s patron and protector. (See Nicephorus, Book 16, chapter 37). In the year 515 Macedonius \[II\], the Patriarch of Constantinople, who had been driven into exile by the wicked \[Emperor\] Anastasius, died and \[George\] Cedrenus has this to say about him. \'He was about to be put in his tomb, but before this happened, he raised his right hand and made the sign of the cross over it. In a vision he \[also\] said to Theodorus, one of his close friends, \"Listen to this, and go and tell Anastasius that I, Macedonius, am going to my ancestors in whose faith I have continued steadfastly. In the meantime, I shall keep bothering God until you, too, come and our case receives judgement\". (Various accounts of this can be read in Theophanes, \[Chronicle\] Book 15, Anastasius \[the Librarian\], \[Tripartite\] History, and Theodorus Lector, Selections Book 2.)
[Page 159]

But because Macedonius died a schismatic, God appointed others to accuse Anastasius, Catholics whom \[the Emperor\] had also sent into exile. God warned both of them in a vision to prepare themselves because they were going to die ten days after Anastasius\'s death and prosecute their case in front of His dread tribunal. These two were Elias, the Patriarch of Jerusalem, and Flavianus, the Patriarch of Antioch. (See Cyril \[of Scythopolis\], Acts of St Sabas in Sauret, Volume 6, under 5th December, and \'Sophronius\', The Spiritual Meadow, chapter 35). An old hermit in Lipari saw the soul of Theodoric, King of Italy, brought out with its hands bound, and thrown into Vulcan\'s crater. (Pope St Gregory tells the story in Book 4 of his Dialogues, chapter 30.)

Sometimes it turned out that while he was receiving a vision a person would be brought to a place where souls were being detained, as we are told in the exemplary story of the Nestorian monk whom St Cyriacus entrusted to God\'s care. \'At the ninth hour of the day the brother, who was in his cell, caught sight of someone with a terrifying appearance standing next to him. \"Come and see the truth\", he said, and took hold of \[the monk\] and brought him to a dark, stinking place which was breathing out fire. In those fires \[the monk\] saw Nestorius, Eutyches, Apollinaris, Dioscurus, Severus, Arius, Origen, and a number of others. \"This\", said the apparition, \"is the place which has been prepared for heretics\", etc. (This comes from \'Sophronius\', The Spiritual Meadow, chapter 26.)

When the Emperor Justinian finished building a church for the Apostles, it is agreed, says Procopius, that \'the Apostles, mindful of his good works, appeared to people in order to honour the King; and I say that when a king is devout and religious, the things of God do not withdraw from human beings, but have an earnest desire to mingle with them and spend time conversing with them\'. See Procopius, Justinian\'s Buildings Book 1 where you also see his opinion on the subject of my investigation. The following, taken from the same work, is also pertinent. \'More or less at the end of the Golden Horn, on a rather steep, high spot, a church was dedicated some time ago to St Cosmas and St Damian. Here while Justinian while he was lying asleep, seriously ill and already having the look of death on him-indeed his doctors despaired of him and \[treated him as though he were already\] lying among the dead-those saints appeared to him and cured him\'. Procopius also testifies in Book 1 of his Gothic War that a part of Rome\'s defensive walls was defended by the Apostle St Peter when the city was being besieged by the Goths.

In Book 2, chapter 6 of his History of the Franks, Gregory \[of Tours\] writes that St Stephen was seen to intercede with the Princes of the Apostles for the city of Metz where his relics were kept; and in the year 552 the Blessed Virgin showed herself in visible form to the son of a Jewish glass-worker who had been injured after working at the kiln for three days, and healed him. Later on, he and his mother were baptised. (One can look for a perfectly reliable version of what happened in Evagrius Book 4, chapter 35, because Evagrius was alive at the time. Gregory of Tours relies on him in his book, The Glory of the Martyrs, chapter 8, as does Glykas, Annals Part 4.) While Redemptus, Bishop of Ferentino was keeping watch at the tomb of St Eutychius, the martyr stood near him and said, \'Are you awake, Redemptus?\' Redemptus replied, \'Yes, I am\', and St Eutychius said, \'The end of all flesh is coming\'. (For this and what follows, see St Gregory, Dialogues Book 3, chapter 38.)⁴¹ In about 587, a vision of this kind was given to King Guntram. It was about Chilperic, the Franks\' most wicked tyrant, and King Guntram himself told the story to Gregory of Tours as follows.
[Page 160]

\'I also saw another vision which gave notice of this man\'s death. He was brought in front of me in chains by three bishops-the first was Tetricus, the second Agricola, and the third Nicetius of Lyons. Two of them were saying, \"Please release him \[from his chains\], give him a sound beating, and allow him to go\". Bishop Tetricus, on the other hand, replied bitterly, \"Don\'t do that! Let him be burned to death for his crimes\". While they were, so to speak, wrangling and exchanging high words at length for a long time, I noticed that a bronze cauldron at some distance from them, which had been placed on top of a fire, was boiling fiercely and, as I wept, they seized the unfortunate Chilperic, broke his arms and legs, and threw him into the cauldron. It was not long before he was so dissolved and melted amid the steaming waters that no trace of him whatever remained there\', (Gregory of Tours, History of the Franks Book 8, chapter 5).

Evagrius \[345-399} tells us that Narsetes, a eunuch but also a very famous war leader, left a wicked deed unpunished. \'Those who were with Narsetes remembered that while he was placating God with prayers and other acts of devotion, and paying Him the worship due to Him, the holy Virgin appeared to him and distinctly appointed the time at which he should wage war on the enemy, \[saying\] he should not get ready to join battle with the enemy until he had received a sign from Heaven\'. (Thus Evagrius, talking about Totila and Theia, the kings whom Narsetes defeated, Book 4, chapter 23. Nicephorus tells the same story in Book 17, chapter 13, but his account needs to be explained by reference to Evagrius, because he is not talking about all Narsetes\'s wars, only about the war with the Goths). A little later, while Chosroe was laying siege to Sergiopolis, St Sergius the martyr was seen, with innumerable heavenly defenders, preparing to defend it and running along the walls all over the place. (Nicephorus Book 15, chapter 17, relying on Evagrius Book 4, chapter 27.) While Justinus the Younger was Emperor, first an image of the Mother of God turned its back on Anatolius, an illusionist and an irreligious man, and then the Mother of God herself appeared to several of the faithful and roused them against that wicked man, saying her Son was being troubled by slanders which were coming from Anatolius, (Evagrius Book 15, chapter 18.)

Seventh Century

I have decided to begin the year 600 with the most blessed Gregory I, Roman Pontiff and Doctor of the Church. His authority alone ought to be enough \[to authenticate what follows\]. First, he tells the following story about St Musa. \'One night the blessed Mother of God, the Virgin Mary, appeared to her in a vision and showed her girls of her own age dressed in white garments. Musa wanted to join their company but did not dare do so. The voice of the blessed Virgin Mary asked if she wanted to be with them and live in her service. The girl said, \"I do\", and immediately received an instruction not to behave in a light-minded and girlish fashion and to refrain from laughing and joking, knowing full well that in thirty days\' time she would be entering \[the Virgin\'s\] service along with the virgins she had seen.

After seeing this \[vision\], the girl changed her behaviour entirely and wiped away her girlish light-mindedness with the heavy hand of seriousness. Her parents were astonished at the change in her and when they asked her about it, she told them what the blessed Mother of God had instructed her to do, and on which day she told her she would be entering her service. On the twenty-fifth day she was seized with fever and on the thirtieth, when the hour of her death drew near, she saw the blessed Mother of God coming to her with the girls she had seen in her vision. She began to reply to \[the Virgin\] who was summoning her and, with her eyes respectfully lowered, she called out in a loud voice, \"Look, Lady, I\'m coming\"; and with these words she gave up her spirit\', (Dialogues Book 4, chapter 17). He speaks about St Probus, the Bishop of Reate. The martyrs St Juvenal and St Eleutherius appeared to him and to a child who was with him. They were wearing white stoles and carried Probus\'s soul with them into Heaven, (Dialogues Book 4, chapter 12.)
[Page 161]

These \[examples\] are about celestial \[apparitions\]. Gregory supplies other examples in chapters 11 and 14, and also examples of apparitions of the damned, such as that of Theodoric\'s soul which was seen to be thrown into Vulcan\'s pot, \[and example\] I cited earlier. Hugo Eterianus confirms this in chapter 7 of his book The Return of Souls, and \[St Gregory\], Dialogues Book 4, chapter 51 \[talks\] about a nun who was seen cut in two in front of the altar. \[St Gregory\] adds more about souls which were still expiating their sins. \'Pascanius, a deacon, was seen in Rome, standing in the baths and ministering to the bathers as a punishment for the favour he had shown towards a schismatic, as we are told by the man to whom he appeared-Germanus, Bishop of Capua, by whose prayers he was soon set free from his place of punishment. He tells a fairly similar story about the soul of the owner of a bath-house. Because of his sins, after he died, he was assigned to the hot rooms of the bath-house and ordered to the unremitting service of those who were going to wash. After he had performed this duty more than once for a priest from Civitavecchia, the priest, as a sign of \[fraternal\] affection, brought him two pieces of blessed bread, but he, full of sorrow, said, \"Why are you giving me these, Father? This bread is holy. I can\'t eat it. This person you are looking at was once the owner of this place, but after my death I was assigned \[to work\] here because of my sins. But if you want to help me, offer this bread to Almighty God on my behalf and intercede \[for me\] on account of my sins. You will know He has heard you when you come here to wash and don\'t find me at all.\" With these words he vanished and he, who was a spirit,\' etc. (Op.cit. Book 4, chapter 55). In the same chapter, \[St Gregory\] adds another story about a monk, Justus, who appeared to his brother Copiosus and told him he had escaped punishment because of the offering of the sacrifice of salvation \[the Mass\]. (Glykas gives a slightly different version in part 4 of his Annals). Thus far, then, St Gregory, a man of such merit, holiness, and good sense that no one except a heretic can reject his testimony.

In the same century lived Gregory of Tours and St Isidore of Seville, whose testimonies I have adduced earlier. \'Sophronius\', author of The Spiritual Meadow, was also alive then-and I have already cited several of his testimonies-as was St Ildephonsus \[Hildefuns\], Bishop of Toledo, to whom the Blessed Virgin gave a dazzling white garment when she appeared to him with a large number of virgins. (This garment is still preserved today.) St Leocadia rose from her grave and appeared to him and the King and the whole clergy and people and seemed to be holding out a large cloth in her hand. In order to test whether this apparition was genuine, the King handed St Ildephonsus a knife and \[Ildephonsus\] cut off some of the cloth. (This piece of cloth, along with the knife, has been deposited among the treasures of the Church). Julianus Pomerius, a bishop of the same church, testifies to both \[stories\], (Sauer, Volume 1, under 23rd January.) In 696 a blind man heard Ebroinus being carried off by evil spirits to Vulcan\'s pot, as Ado of Vienne tells us in his chronicle.

Eighth century

After the year 700 there flourished Julianus Pomerius who, in my opinion, lived a little before the laudatory things he said about St Ildephonsus, as did St Suibertus who appeared after his death to sick people whom he \[then\] cured. (See St Marcellinus in chapter 30 of his biography of St Suibertus in Sauer, under the month of March). Likewise, the Venerable Bede, who makes clear he had the same opinion about apparitions in his History of the English \[Church\] Book 4, chapters 14 and 25, and Book 5, chapters 14 and 15, and in other passages of the same book and of the Lives of the Saints he wrote. \[At this time\] too, lived St John of Damascus. When his hand was cut off on the orders of an iconoclast Emperor, the Blessed Virgin Mother of God restored it, as St John, the Patriarch of Jerusalem, tells us in his biography of him.
[Page 162]

At this period lived Paul the Deacon who tells us that Arnulf the Langobard was protected in battle by the martyr, St Sabinus, and by a barbarian \[Arnulf\] after he had seen and recognised a painting of the martyr, (History of the Langobards Book 4. Chapter 5). What Bishop Constantine says is entirely worthy of astonishment. He was an eyewitness of the translation of the relics of St Euphemia the Martyr, (see \[Luigi\] Lippomano, Volume 6). Blinded by the splendour of her miracles, Leo Isauricus secretly removed the martyr\'s relics and put dry bones in their place, and threw the genuine relics, along with their reliquary, into the sea waves. Some sailors came upon the reliquary but did not know what it was and, thinking some kind of treasure had been put in it, they took it and opened it and, drenched in the sweet scent which breathed miraculously from it, realised these were holy relics, and although they remained ignorant of whose \[relics\] they were, that same night they saw lights and candles and men shining wonderfully white and praising God.

\'When they landed in Levinum and had gone to sleep, during their repose they saw the martyr Glyceria meet the virgin Euphemia, embrace her, exchange a kiss with her, and congratulate her on her arrival. Then they realised whose the relics were. Three times they set sail from Levinum, and three times they were driven back by contrary winds. Finally, Euphemia stood in front of them in their dreams and told them who she was. She said she wanted to stay there because of what had happened to her after she had been thrown into the sea by the irreligious Leo.\'

Thus, Bishop Constantine who is more believable than Zonaras who attributes these events to \[the reign of\] Copronymus. A little later Zonaras writes of Leo the Armenian as follows. \'These things disturbed the Emperor and a dream his mother had also frightened him. She thought she was in the Church of the Mother of God in Blachernia and saw a lady accompanied by young people who were wearing white garments. The floor of the church was awash with blood, and she heard the lady giving instructions that a dish filled with that blood was to be given to the Emperor\'s mother. When the Emperor\'s mother objected, the lady said, \"But your son fills those who venerate me with blood and does not realise that he is moving God and my Son to anger\". (These words make it clear that the lady was the Blessed Virgin.) Zonaras adds, \'St Tharasius, too, was now translated from this wretched life. Someone saw him, in sleep, calling upon a certain Michael and urging him to seize hold of Leo and kill him\'; and actually Michael Balbus \[Emperor Michael II\] did kill him not long after in 787. St Andrew the Apostle appeared to Aonghus, King of the Picts, and promised him victory, a promise he fulfilled. While \[the King\] was fighting, a red saltire appeared in the sky. (Even though he is a Calvinist, \[George\] Buchanan recorded this in Book 5 of his History of Scottish Affairs, under the sixty-fifth king.) At this time lived St Gertrude, an apparition of whom is mentioned by Louis of Blois more than once in his Spiritual Instruction. Not long afterwards the Roman Empire was transferred to Charlemagne and St James, son of Zebedee, appeared to him and urged him to seize the kingdoms of Spain from Saracen control, as a number of more recent writers have recorded, (Lupold von Bebenburg,

The Zeal of the Ancient \[Kings of France and the German Princes.) Apparitions of St James to Kings of Spain were quite reliably witnessed during this and the following centuries. He promised he would bring them help in battle and was seen to put the enemy to flight during the fighting. The Moors, too, said they saw him and our men boasted of it. (I shall talk about this in a moment.)
[Page 163]

The Ninth Century began with the year 800

In that year Ramirez, King of Spain, fought very bravely for a whole day with a very large number of Saracens and then, under cover of night, retreated with a tiny band of his own men to a particular hill. While the King was staying awake and praying to God, the saint stood near him and told everyone to cleanse themselves by confession and receive the eucharist, and they would win the battle. In the morning everyone to a man complied with the saint\'s instructions, and then they picked up the standards and cut sixty thousand of the enemy to pieces. The Apostle was seen mounted on a white horse during this battle, waving a white flag with a distinctive transverse red cross on it. (See Bishop Rodrigo; the General History of King Alfonso; \[Juan de Mariana, Book 7, chapter 13; and other authors.)

In this century lived Theophylact \[of Ohrid\], the Bulgar, who says in the fourteenth chapter \[of his commentary on St John\'s Gospel that the spirits of the dead appear to the living. I think Julian of Toledo, the Younger, author of Foreknowledge \[of the World To Come\], lived at the same time. In Book 2, chapter 29 he says that \'the dead are able to know what the living are doing\' and, relying on St Augustine\'s opinion in Care for the Dead, he closes the following chapter in almost the same words, \'The authority of Scripture clearly proves that the dead can appear to the living\'. During the reign of Charles the Bald, King of France, someone called Numonis, a usurper of the Church\'s goods, saw and felt St Maurilius who appeared to him, upbraided him loudly, and struck his head with his holy crozier. Nor was the vision in vain. Numonis died a little later. (Annals of Anjou).

Likewise, one reads in Book 2, chapter 62 of the Chronicles of \[Monte\] Cassino that a child caught sight of Pandulf, Prince of Capua, bound with iron restraints, dropped into a muddy, stinking lake right up to his throat. He was being dropped into the deep \[water\] and immediately hauled out again with ropes by two jet-black spirits \[spiritibus\]. The child asked him why this was happening, and he replied, \'Because I stole a golden chalice from St Benedict\'s monastery and failed to put it back \[before\] I was in the throes of death\'. Chapter 84 \[says\] a hermit saw Aethiopians, who said they were evil spirits, laden with straw and preparing a pyre for that same Pandulf: and it was found he had died at that very hour. (I have written this about Pandulf at this point because it has a close bearing on my argument, although of course it happened in the eleventh century.)

After the year 900

While Ludger was looking in vain for the body of St Lebuinus, at length in sleep he had a notion that the very holy priest of Christ was talking to him and telling him where he was buried: and parts of his body were discovered in the place the spirit \[spiritus\] indicated, (Hucbald de Saint-Amand in Sauer, Volume 6, under 12th November). When \[Pope\] John X sent an army into Apulia against the Saracens, the Apostles St Peter and St Paul were seen during the battle, procuring a very great victory for the Christians, (see Liutprand on those very recent events, Book 2, chapter 14). St Theodore the Martyr, by command of the Blessed Virgin, was seen by many and brought help to Emperor John Zimisca while he was fighting the Russians, (Zonaras, Volume 3.) In the same century there took place the well-known dreadful tragedy of Udo, Bishop of Magdeburg, which I shall tell you about later. But please, Reader, bear in mind that \[the tragedy\] would have been happening at this time.
[Page 164]

\[Tenth century\]

In the tenth century lived Euthymius, a Greek, and Hugo Eterianus, a Latin, both of whom thought that the spirits of the dead sometimes appear to the living and are seen by them, (Euthymius on John, chapter 14; Hugo Eterianus, The Return of Souls, chapters 7, 11, 14, 15, and 16). In the year 914, Fernán González, Count of Castile, an illustrious proof of both good and bad fortune, a man noble in both word and deed, relying upon God\'s counsel overthrew Al-Mansur\'s enormous force with only three hundred men. Sixteen years later he also fought an unbelievable number of Arabs for three continuous days with one thousand five hundred infantry and four hundred and fifty cavalry beneath his standards, and on the final day St James was seen to be with them in the fighting. Innumerable men were slaughtered, but \[González\] was triumphant, (\[Jan\] Waes, General History, and other authors.)

The Eleventh Century followed the year 1000

In this century there was holy war under the leadership of Godfrey of Bouillon. During the expedition to Antioch our men almost died of famine and nearly ran out of horses but were strengthened by a vision and the admonitions of St Andrew who also showed them the Lord\'s holy lance. They obtained more than one victory and during the battle with Kerbogha, too, lines of white horses appeared, which put the Persians to flight, (William of Tyre, who took part in the holy war, Sacred Histories Book 6, chapters 4 and 9).

During this same war, when the Christians captured the holy city, Bishop Adhemar, who had died long before, was seen leading the soldiers, scaling the walls, and encouraging his men to follow, (Johannes Naucler, Volume 3, generation 37). Shortly before that, Christ our Lord, together with the Blessed Virgin, St Peter, St John, St Stephen, St Martin, and many other angels besides, showed Himself to the Emperor Henry II at night while the Emperor was keeping vigil in a church in Rome, (Naucler, generation 34). At that time one of St Dunstan\'s dead friends appeared to the saint and predicted that many things were going to happen, which did indeed take place, (Osbert in The Life of St Dunstan in Sauer, under 19th May.) St Odilo, Abbot of Cluny, was seen by friends more than once after his death, (Peter Damian in his life of Odilo in Sauer, under the month of January.)

Peter Damian, a very learned and most eloquent man, flourished at this time and lived into the next century. He changed his life of a cardinal for that of a solitary. His works-and there are many of them-are filled with various kinds of apparition, such as those of the two noble Faventi who appeared out of Hell, (second letter to Hildebrand): or the souls which appeared in the form of small birds, (Letters 5, chapter 3): or the soul of Benedict VIII who appeared, sitting on a black horse, to the Bishop of Capua. Not only Damian (chapter 4), but also Martin of Poland, St Antoninus, and \[Bartolommeo\] Platina recorded an apparition of Benedict IX, and Damian (chapter 11) one of St Andrew and St Gregory flogging an apostate monk. He also has other examples which it would take a long time to mention.

If you look at this sequence of time, one would have to include here what I said about Pandulf of Capua, (which Leo of Ostia confirms, \[Chronicle of Monte Cassino Book 2, chapters 62 and 82). It is worth noting the story of the foundation of the Carthusians. The occasion is well known and in the public domain. On the first, second, and third day during vigils for the dead. An apparition \[was seen\] while they were singing the verse \'Reply to me\' \[Job 13.22\]. The dead man raised his head three times and called out that, by God\'s righteous decision, he had been accused, he had been judged, and he had been sentenced. (Francesco de Puteo tells the story in his life of Bruno in Sauer, Volume 5, under 6th October). In this century lived Bishop St Hanno of Köln and Bishop St Benno of Meissen. St Hanno\'s predecessors as bishop appeared to him, pointed out a stain on his garment, and in this notable fashion impressed on him that he should entirely get rid of his bitterness of mind towards those who had unjustly done him wrong, and forget about it. Of St Benno it is recorded that after his death he appeared in sleep to the Margrave of Meissen and dug out one of his eyes because he had inflicted damage on the Church. (Lambert Schnaffnab tells the story about Hanno in his History of Germany, and Jerome Emser that about Benno in his biography of him, chapter 32, in Sauer, under July).
[Page 165]

\[Twelfth century\]

The twelfth century does not lack its visions of the dead. Notice if you will that in 1112 or rather 1139 Alfonso, King of Portugal, was about to join battle with five Saracen kings when he was encouraged, during his sleep, by Christ to be of good heart and to use a standard on which was embroidered an image of the Five Wounds during the battle. He gained a memorable victory, and this was the origin of the royal arms of Portugal. Fourteen years later Alfonso, King of Castile, was besieging Baéza. St Isidore appeared to him and encouraged him to join battle, and there has scarcely been a more famous victory than this. (\[Juan de\] Mariana, \[Inquiry into the Affairs of Spain\] Book 10, chapter 15, \[Jan\] Waes, \[Esteban de Garibay y\] Zamalloa, and others).

In the year 1117, along with a very large number of other extraordinary events which were agitating Italy, \'In Cremona a tiny child was lying in its cradle, wrapped up in its bed-clothes. Contrary to what happens naturally, it opened its mouth and began to speak, interrupting its mother while she was seeing to his elder brother\'s physical needs and preparing food for him because he had been crying and asking for something to eat. \[The child\] told her he had seen Mary, the Mother of God, standing in front of Christ\'s judgement-seat, supplicating Him with her most earnest prayers because of the judgement which He had laid upon the world because of its sins. After this, \[the child\] did not speak again until it was the appropriate time in the course of human nature for it to do so\'. (See \[Abbot\] Dodechin, \[floruit mid thirteenth century\]. \[Continuation of History \[of Marianus Scotus\].)

An apparition of Pope Benedict X appeared first of all to João \[Peculiar\], Bishop of Porto, in which \[the Pope\] confessed he had been saved from the danger of everlasting death by the prayers of Abbot Odilo. He then appeared to Edelbert and also confessed he had been set free from the pains of Purgatory. (Peter Damian tells the story in his life of Odilo in Sauer, under ist January.) St Norbert, too, appeared after his death to his household. (The story is told in chapter 42 of his biography.) One night, St Bernard saw his uncle Gualdric who had died several days earlier.

\'How are you?\' he asked. \'I\'m fine\', replied his uncle. Then Bernard asked what was the reason he was bellowing in agony \[just before he died\], and why his body was being wracked in a dreadful manner. \'At that very moment\', said Gualdric, \'two vile spirits \[spiritus\] were doing their best to hurl me into a very deep pit, but St Peter came to my aid and I escaped\'.

(See the author of the Life of St Bernard Book 1, chapter 10). Rupert of Deutz and Honoré d\'Autun have the same opinion \[about apparitions\]. Rupert tells us that the Blessed Virgin made herself visible to him and urged him to write, (Rupert in the prologue to his Commentary on the Song of Songs), and in chapter 1 of his Sign of Mary, Honoré mentions Mary of Egypt to whom the Mother of God appeared and drew to repentance, and Theophilus whom she snatched from slavery to the Devil, and the children of Israel whom she protected in the furnace. (Likewise, Peter of Cluny in his book on miracles.) St Martin, a priest and canon regular, lived at this time in León where he wrote various books. He was suddenly transformed from an uneducated man with no Latin to a very good scholar by St Isidore, in whose monastery he was living. \[St Isidore\] appeared to him while he was asleep and offered him a book which he read through eagerly from beginning to end, as Juan de Mariana testifies in his Inquiry into the Affairs of Spain Book 2, chapter 16. \[St Martin\'s\] books are kept there in the large monastery. Those who have seen them say they deal with the prophets, and that some are on Daniel and the Apocalypse. When I was in León there were not many books to be seen, \[but\] one can see and kiss St \[Martin\'s\] hand which remains intact with its skin and nails, although it has dried up and shrunk and is very small.
[Page 166]

\[Thirteenth Century\]

The thirteenth century follows after the year 1200. \[Juan de Mariana, (Book 12, chapter 15), \[Esteban de Garibay y\] Zamalloa, and other writers on the annals of the history of Spain tell us that St James appeared to Alfonso, King of León, at Mérida, and that St Isidore appeared to others at Zamora at the same time and brought them help while they were fighting. Vincent de Beauvais, the author of The Mirror of History, and the German Cistercian, Caesarius of Heisterbach, author of a very large book of miracles or exemplary stories, both lived in this century, and both recorded the appearances of spirits \[spirituum\]. There is no time to copy down any of them here because the subject is too big, and I say the same when it comes to Hélinand de Froidmont\'s Chronicle, Thomas of Cantimpré\'s book \[entitled\] The Bees, and the Life of St Lutgard Book 2. They also lived in this century and compiled a very large number of examples which were very well known to them. St Thomas Aquinas lived at this time. His opinion on the subject is clear, (\[Summa Theologiae\] part 3, question 69, article 2), where he examines the subject and stands on my side \[of the argument\]. So did Guillaume d\'Auvergne in the whole of the last part of his book, The World; and for the purpose of this discussion, one should refer to the example of the apparition of St Ursula and St Gereon who defended Köln along with their companions. (See \[Tilmann\] Bredenbach, Book 4, chapter 22).

\[Fourteenth Century\]

More or less in the following century, the fourteenth, came other theologians with a great reputation, all of whom agree with me-such as Richard of Middleton, in IV, distinction 45, question 3; Petrus de Palude there, the same question 3; and \[Duns\] Scotus, question 1, article 4.

Fifteenth century

Antoninus, Archbishop of Florence confirms this and supplies many examples throughout his History, and so does \[Jean\] Gerson, passim, in his works; Jacobus Chusanus in the book he wrote on this subject; Denis Ryckel, the Carthusian, in Commentary on Book IV \[of the Sentences of Peter Lombard\], distinction 45, question 1, and his work on the four last things, and his book on the novices of his order, where you also find many apparitions; and Alonso Tostado in \[his commentary\] on the First Book of Kings.

\[Sixteenth century\]

In our century, 23rd January 1534, in answer to the Orléans case about which it had been consulted, the Theological Faculty of Paris said, \'It is perfectly clear that, by divine providence and with God\'s permission, the souls of the dead do sometimes return to the living.\' You can find this written answer in Volume 2 of the various legal consults published in Frankfurt and, (to omit any others), \[the following\] openly defend it: Domingo de Soto in his Commentary on Book IV of the Sentences \[of Peter Lombard\] 45, question 1, article 4; \[Theodore\] van Pelt, Purgatory, chapter 5; Peter Canisius, The Incomparable Virgin Mary Book 5, chapters 20 and 21; \[Juan\] Maldonado, chapter 14; Juan Gregorio de Valencia in Part 3, distinction 6, question 11, point 1, and disputation 11, question 1, part 1; \[Roberto\] Bellarmino, Arguments About Purgatory Book 2, chapter 8; José Anglés, A Discussion of Free Will Book 4, art. 1 at the end; Petrus Thyraeus, published treatise on this subject, especially chapter 11; Marko Marulić, The Institution of the Monastic Life Book 6, chapter 14 where, for example, there are a good many examples; and Alfonso Mendoza in Scholastic Disputations, question 5.

A number of historians have also supported this \[opinion\]: \[Battista\] Fregoso, \[Marcantonio\] Sabellico \[Cocci\], and Ignatius in a very large number of passages in their books by way of example; Paolo Giovio, Life of Azzone Visconti and History \[of His Own Times\] Book 30; Carlo Sigonio on the Empire in the West, Books 3 and 10 of The Kingdom of Italy Book 1; Albert Kranz, Saxony Book 7, chapters 16 and 14, and Metropolis Book 9, chapter 13; Francisco \[López\] de Gómara, History of Mexico, chapters 20 and 104; Jerónimo Osório, The Deeds of Manuel, \[King of Portugal\]; \[Giovanni\] Pietro Maffei, History of India; and Tommaso Bozio, Signs of the Church, passim; and to close this list I have put in last place a very bright torch of the history of the Church and a most doughty fighter against the assaults of the innovators, the unconquered footsoldier Cesare Baronio, whose individual volumes of The Annals of the Church, of which he has already published seven volumes, to the benefit of the public, are like seven golden cups of the Catholic candelabrum.
[Page 167]

I shall add at least some of the more notable examples from these last centuries, because my opponents make little of more recent \[examples\]. First, therefore-and I cannot be sure of the century-I shall relate here what Glykas \[says\] in part 4 of his Annals. \'In the history of the Church by the historian Philo, one finds that at a particular moment during the ancient persecutions, a bishop banned a rather elderly ecclesiastic from \[holding\] church services. But when that elderly man went elsewhere to do something he had to do, it happened he was seized by natives and gave witness in his own person to the truth. In consequence, a church was founded on the spot and the ceremonies of consecration carried out. When the Bishop started to pronounce the words, \"Peace be to everyone\", the bier went out the church, and this happened not once, but two or three times. From there the elderly man addressed the Bishop from the sleep \[of death\]. \"I\'m going\", he said, \"but please intercede for me with my bishop \[and ask\] him to come here and release me from my bonds, because he excluded me from my sacred office and so I can\'t be present with you at religious services. I have obtained the crown of my witnessing, but I have not yet seen God\'s face. But if \[the Bishop\] comes here and does not release me, no one else will be able to do so\". Accordingly, when \[his Bishop\] complied with these \[requests\], the bier ceased leaving the church.\'

This is an appropriate place to record the revenge taken by divine providence upon a wicked bishop. It happened during the tenth century but was omitted from my earlier account. \'When Udo, Bishop of Magdeburg, was not moved even by signs and prophetic voices to refrain from lewd behaviour, the clergy asked God either to amend or to remove him. One of their number, Canon Frederik, was praying after this fashion in the Cathedral of St Maurice at night, when he noticed that all the lights in the church were extinguished by a rather strong gust of wind, and not long afterwards that two youths carrying two candelabra with lighted candles came in, followed closely by Christ, His mother, and the Apostles. They summoned the holy people whose bodies were lying at rest in the church, and \[St\] Maurice was seen to come along with them. In a long, serious speech he laid an accusation against Bishop Udo and not long after that Christ ordered the two \[youths\] to fetch him, stripped of his clothes. He passed sentence against him, but the bishop put up a fight and one of the two who were bringing him struck him a violent blow in the midst of his kidneys, \[whereupon\], into a chalice which had been placed on the altar, the Bishop vomited up the Host of Christ which he had taken in communion the previous day. Then Udo was struck down by an axe and the vision and everyone in it vanished. Frederik was therefore absolutely terrified when he went up to the altar, looked in the chalice, \[and saw\] that it contained the Host of Christ and that the Bishop was lying dead on the ground. He woke the other clergy and they removed the Bishop\'s body and buried it in a field\', (\[Battista\] Fregoso, \[Memorable Deeds and Sayings\], Book 9, chapter 12, and Johannes Naucler, \[Chronicles\] Part 2.

\'Two wealthy merchants who came from not undistinguished families were making their way to France via the Mont Genèvre Pass when they met a man of larger than human appearance and received an instruction from him which went as follows. \"Speak to my brother Lodovico Sforza and give him this letter on my behalf\". They were rendered speechless and wondered who he was. He told them he was Galeazzo Sforza, and not long afterwards vanished from their eyes. They hastily returned to Milan and from there went to Vigevano where \[Ludovico\] \'The Moor\' was staying and delivered the Duke\'s letter whose contents were as follows.
[Page 168]

\"Oh, oh, oh, Ludovico! Watch out! The Venetians and the French are about to come and destroy you and overthrow your House, but if you give me three thousand gold pieces, I shall help you to mollify the spirits \[spiritibus\] and get them to avert your hapless fate: and I promise this will be the result, provided you agree and don\'t oppose me. Goodbye\", signed, \"The spirit of your brother, Galeazzo\".

Some people may laugh at this as an invention, but not long afterwards the prince was ejected from his principality by Louis XII, King of France, and taken into captivity\', (Bernardino Arluno, an eye-witness, History of Milan, section 1.)
