\'Not long after he died,\' (says \[Marc Antonio\] Sabellico), \'Lodovico\'s father, the lord of Imola, appeared to a secretary who was being sent to Ferrara by his son, Lodovico. \[He appeared\] while the man was on his way and told him to tell his son to meet him there the next day because he had something of great importance to say to him. When Lodovico heard this, he sent someone else in his place because he did not believe it and because he was afraid it was a trap. The same ghost \[umbra\] who had appeared before met the man and was deeply pained that his son had not come, because he said he had a good many things to tell him. On this occasion, however, he wanted to say just this-that after twenty-two years and one month had passed, (and he added a specific day), Lodovico would lose the control of the city which he now enjoyed; and when the time predicted by the ghost arrived, even though great care had been taken on the very night his father\'s spirit \[manes\] had cast suspicion, the confederate army of Duke Filippo of Milan got as far as the walls, since most of the ditches were frozen solid, set up ladders, and captured the city along with its prince,\' (Sabellico, Exemplary Stories Book 1, chapter 4.)

In Book 1 of his History \[of Italy\], Francesco Guicciardini says that a rumour spread around that the spirit of Ferdinand, King of Naples, appeared on three different nights to Jacopo, a surgeon \[in the household\] of his son King Alfonso, and ordered him, in mild and gentle language at first, but later with threats and reproaches, to tell Alfonso, in the words he was actually using, that there was no reason for him to flatter himself with the worthless hope that he could keep French power in check, because it had been decided and determined by the law of Fate that the House of Aragon was to be shaken by an endless number of misfortunes and, in the end, deprived of its kingdom.

Guicciardini, however, does add, \'One is, however, at liberty not to disparage such things entirely\', and he added it, not in relation to the apparition, but to the trustworthiness of the prediction. He was quite right to do so, because we see it was not true. Count Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola writes in his biography of his uncle, Giovanni Pico, that not long after his death, Giovanni\'s ghost \[spectrum\] appeared to Girolamo Savonarola and told him he was being tortured by the pains of atonement because he had not multiplied, with sufficient diligence and fertile results, the talent of learning and cleverness God had credited to him, and because he had not made a proper return on God\'s inward suggestions and inspirations which kept urging him not to break off the honourable labours he had begun and which he had told himself mentally to undertake in earnest and bring to a conclusion. Not long afterwards, Girolamo told people about this in one of his public addresses and commended Pico to everyone\'s prayers.

Cesare Baronio testifies of the elder Michele Mercati that Michele and Marsilio \[Ficino\] were very close friends because they were deeply interested in the same things and were closely bound together by \[their belonging to\] the School of Plato. One day, however, it happened \'that as usual \[they were discussing\] Plato\'s opinion on which part of a human being survives after death and, not without consternation, they came to the conclusion that \[his arguments\] were unstable and needed to be propped up by the mysteries of the Christian faith. (A learned letter from Marsilio to Michele Mercati about the immortality of the spirit and God, \[with a reference\] to this deduction still exists).
[Page 169]

But when they had argued their differences for quite a long time, they brought their discussion to an end and closed it by agreeing to make a pact that whichever one of them should die first would, if he were permitted, tell the other about his state in the next life. Having made this agreement, they both swore their oath and then went their separate ways. After only a brief lapse of time it happened that Michele the elder was engaged in philosophical studies in the middle of the morning when he suddenly began to hear the racket made by a horse galloping at speed. It stopped at his door and Marsilio\'s voice cried out, \"Michele, Michele, it\'s true, it\'s true!\" Surprised by his friend Marsilio\'s voive, Michele got up, opened the window, and saw the man he had heard riding away again, mounted on a white horse. He shouted after the white figure, calling him by name, \"Marsilio, Marsilio!\" and pursuing him with his eyes. But he vanished from them. Astounded by this strange event and feeling anxious about Marsilio, Michele went and made careful inquiries-Marsilio was living in Florence at the time of his death-and found out that Marsilio had died at the very time he had heard and seen him\', (Baronio, Annals \[of the Church\], Vol. 5, under the year \'412\'.)

I shall pass over what \[Tilmann\] Bredenbach says in his Collection \[of Holy Stories\] Book 7, chapters 11 and 46 and Book 8, chapters 12, 14, 23, and 40. \[But\] I do include one \[example of an apparition\] which one can rely on because of the evidence of many perfectly serious soldiers who solemnly swore they had seen it. Some years ago, when Alessandro Farnese was conducting his remarkable siege of Antwerp, in order to help the besieged the Dutch and English made a sally, and so did the besieged at the same time with a view to keeping the enemy occupied. They fought very fiercely, seized, and took possession of the royal army\'s earthwork, and appeared to be free from danger. At that moment, the more distinguished of the King\'s soldiers started to see Pedro de Paz near the earth-work-a Spanish commander and a man very much praised for his military qualities and the attention to duty which marked him out. He had died a few months earlier and on this occasion was seen fully armed as he used to be when leading his regiment. He signalled to his former soldiers and gave them the order to follow him. The front rank pointed him out to the second, the second to the third, and the third to those behind them. They all saw the same thing and, full of astonishment, plucked up their spirits and followed the leader they knew so well. He went in front of them and led them straight towards the enemy, and the fighting started. The Dutch, however, were driven back to their ships, leaving the earthwork to the King\'s troops, and at that moment the town, which was very well fortified, lost its hope of victory and of defending itself.

A quite dreadful thing to read is something which happened in Pavia earlier this year (1601) in April, in the diocese of a small town called Corretto. A notary\'s funeral was taking place in the church, when suddenly the corpse raised himself from his bier, turned to one of his relatives who was attending the funeral, and told him to hurry to his house, and to take a number of \[hand-\]written documents which were there and put them straight back in a holy place. Because he had concealed them, the dead man \[said\], God had justly sentenced him to the torments of Hell, having said which, he laid his head down as before. God permitted this so that mortals might realise what serious penalties await treacherous clerks of this kind who embezzle the legacies of people who are devout. I shall also write down a second anecdote, one worthier of being remembered, about an apparition \[spectri\]-I do not know whether it was of an evil spirit or a damned soul-which I did not recall I had read. I hope the pleasure and use \[the reader will gain\] from reading it will overcome its length. It has been taken from a letter from Peru, dated 1590, and is told in the actual words either of Francesco Benci or Gaspar Spitilli.
[Page 170]

\'An extraordinary thing, well worthy thinking about, happened to this same Fr Samaniego during the mission\', (the one to Itata), \'and the inhabitants of the city where it took place have known nothing like the one which has happened to him. It is confirmed by so many testimonies, too, that there is no room left for doubt. It was experienced by people who saw it with their own eyes and heard it. This event, unfortunate in its outcome, was allowed \[to happen\] -and one can believe this about similar things which people have confessed have happenedso that a population newly converted could learn that the sacrament of penance, in which one finds it difficult \[to confess\] sins which are not inconsiderable, must be treated with good faith and integrity. Consequently, the Indian woman who, everyone could see, was violating the strict rule that confession must be complete, was punished and chastised in sight of everyone, so that the salvation of many souls could be procured by the downfall of one. What happened was this.

In the house of a distinguished lady of this locality lived a young girl aged about sixteen, who had been captured during war and baptised. She was called Katarina. As she grew older, so did her lack of self-restraint and dissolute behaviour, even though she was not infrequently rebuked and punished by her mistress. The girl came to such a pitch of wickedness that she began secretly to frequent \[the company of certain profligate individuals. Yet she did not fail to go to confession, although she kept her mouth shut about this sin in case people would think she was a little tart, or someone whose morals were past recovery. On 1st August 1590 she fell ill and sent for a priest, to whom she laid bare her soul\'s offences. She did not do so entirely, however. The priest came and went nine times during her illness, but she would laugh scornfully \[and say\] something like this to the other servants who were there. \"That other sort of thing I was doing-it wasn\'t what I\'d confess as one of my sins\", and she used such shameful, disgraceful language as she said it that all the other servants were offended and reported it, word for word, to their mistress. She quite rightly shouted at Katarina, but then changed her expression and tone and asked in a friendly way what were those \[sins\] she was unwilling to confess to the Father. Katarina had no problem in telling her, and said that every time she sent for the Father during her illness so that she could cleanse herself in confession, someone looking like an Aethiopian stood on her left and kept telling her not to confess because those \[particular\] sins were very slight and of no importance, and if she confessed them the Father would be able to think she was more dissolute \[than she was\]. But on her right St Mary Magdalene appeared and urged her to purge herself frankly of whatever it was.

The Father came again, and the lady acquainted him with everything that had happened, and he tried in every possible way to get her to make a full atonement for \[the benefit of her soul. He did so, however, in vain. The more he exhorted, the more stubborn she became-to the extent that she was unwilling to pronounce the word \"Jesus\". On another occasion, too, they offered her a holy picture of the crucifixion so that she could contemplate it and reflect that Christ was nailed to the cross and died on the cross for us. But with the greatest disdain and emotional agitation she replied, \"I already know that. What do you want me to do about it?\" Her mistress said to her, \"We want you to turn to Christ so that if you acknowledge \[the sins you have committed, He can forgive them\". Katarina answered, \"Please stop bothering me\", and when her mistress had gone away, she started to talk in a sing-song voice about her loves and her shameful behaviour. She kept this up for several days, \[talking about\] the things she did and said, until one night her mistress came along with \[some of the female servants, and Katarina burst out, \"I\'m on the rack and in agony because I have profaned confession; and from that very moment until midnight her whole body became stiff.
[Page 171]

They thought she had died and began to think about digging her grave and giving her a funeral. But she came to, and the priest was sent for. She, however, made no change at all from the way she had usually spoken before. Three hours later, not long before she died, the servants urged her to take a cross and a holy candle in her hand and invoke the name of Jesus. But she replied, \"Who is that Jesus? I don\'t know the name of Jesus\", and she turned back to the end of the bed, sat on it, and was heard having a conversation with someone they could not see. Another female servant who was lying sick in the same room earnestly begged her mistress to have her moved to another bedroom because, she said, she could see a number of black hobgoblins \[larvas\] and she was absolutely terrified of them. On the night Katarina died, the whole house was pervaded by such a foetid stench of corruption that the corpse had to be put in a place open to the air.

The lady\'s brother was dragged out of bed by his arm; a female servant was hit on the shoulder by a small stone and carried the marks of her injury for several days; stones rained against a wall, and a horse which in other respects was perfectly tame ran hither and thither and went mad all night long; and dogs did the same, running about and barking. After the body had been buried, one of the female servants went into the room in which Katarina had lain ill, and even though she saw no one, she had the experience of having a dish, which had been put on the wooden floor, being thrown at her. The greater part of the town saw a very large number of slates and roof-tiles hurled about, with a great deal of crashing and noise, up to a distance of two miles-although there is not any slate or roof-tile on the big house because, for the most part, all this city\'s buildings are roofed with palm leaf. Another female slave was dragged by the foot a very long way while many people looked on, and yet they could not see who was dragging her.

On 7th October, when a female servant went into the wardrobe to fetch a dress for herself, she saw Katarina slowly rising to her feet to seize hold of a plate, and when she ran away, Katarina threw the plate at the wall so violently that it broke into a thousand pieces. Next day a paper crucifix was pinned to the wardrobe, but it was torn down from the wall in front of everyone\'s eyes and ripped into three pieces. On the same day, while the lady was having her dinner in the garden, a slate was thrown into the middle of her plate, all the food fell on the ground, and her four-year old son started shouting, \"Mummy, Mummy! Katarina\'s strangling me!\" They hung holy relics round his neck and he was free from that torment. These \[events\] forced the lady to change house, and she took herself off to her cousin\'s house, leaving her own house in the charge of some of her female servants. On the tenth of that month, one of the servants went into the housekeeper\'s room and felt herself being called three times by Katarina.

She went to pieces in an excess of fear, and the rest of the female staff urged her to call upon our Lord for help and to go back into the room with a lighted holy candle. She chose two of the women who seemed to be bolder than the rest and went back, feeling secure. But the dead woman told her to send the other women away, get rid of the candle, because it was causing her pain, and stay there alone. Katarina then started to discharge flames, along with an unbelievable stench, from every joint in her body. She was burning from head to foot and had a band of fire, as it were, round her, eight or ten fingers wide, which extended right down to the ground. At the sight of this apparition, the servant began to tremble and turn white, and the unfortunate dead woman said to her, \"Come here! How many times have I summoned you!\" Almost half-dead \[with fear\], the servant replied, \"Holy Jesus, who would not be terrified at the sight of you?\" The moment she said this, there came down into that place a very beautiful child, dressed in white, who urged the servingwoman to lay aside her fear, take courage, and note carefully what Katarina was \[going to\] say to her, so that she could tell other people and atone for all her sins by \[going to\] confession as soon as she left that place.
[Page 172]

Then Katarina spoke as follows. \"Please be aware that I have been delivered up to Hell and subjected to the heaviest of punishments because when I went to the priest to confess my sins, I told him only those which were not very serious-that I was a chatter-box and talkative, prone to anger, and other things of that kindbut I said absolutely nothing about my wanton behaviour and my habitual sex with young men. Learn, then, to make a good confession and not keep silent about anything you have done wrong. I am impressing this on you because I have been commanded to do so, and I am forced to be frank about this as an example to others.\"

Then they heard the bell ringing for the Angelus and the dead woman quickly drew back into a corner \[of the room\] and vanished. But the angel-because the serving-woman was convinced the child she had seen was an angel-told her to go away home, and that is what she did.\'

I decided to leave this well-attested anecdote till last because of kinds of apparition come into it: angels, St Mary Magdalene, an evil spirit looking like an \'Aithiopian\', and a damned soul in an assumed body-not, I think, its own, but one made of air in the likeness of its own body. Atheists usually make fun of the pains of Hell and the immortality of the soul, unless they are willing to come to their senses. Calvinists do the same in the case of blessed candles, the relics of the saints, and the soundness of and necessity for auricular confession. What are they going to say? \'It\'s all made up by the Jesuits!\' Who can say it has all been made up if everyone these days can disprove that \[by referring\] to the passages I have quoted? Let them go and listen and see. Are they going to say these are the inventions of silly women? \'I believe that the slates and roof-tiles which were removed and scattered in places far away\', (which the whole population saw), \'are the results of women\'s imagination\'. Men dragged out of bed, a woman struck by a piece of earth and dragged along the ground while others are looking? \'Dreams!\' The plates dashed against the wall and broken into innumerable pieces? \"They were dreams!\' Older people could be malicious enough to make these things up and spread them, but an innocent four-year old child? That is not an age capable of evil intent so that \[a child\], in the presence of relics. Lies that he is being strangled by a dead woman he scarcely knew, or feigns fear when relics are hung round his neck. I\'m afraid Katarina\'s death is a threat to those who praise and imitate her offence and either turn the holy mysteries of confession into a theatrical hypocrisy or think the penalties of Hell are no more than fairy tales!

You have a cloud of witnesses, a veritable hailstorm of examples with which the wild vines and nettles of the innovators have been ripped \[out of the ground\] by their roots, torn to shreds, and trodden underfoot. I make this one suggestion. Think carefully whether it is more reasonable and safer to believe the Fathers of the First and Second Council of Nicaea, the Greek and Latin Doctors of the Church and six hundred other very weighty theologians and historians of proven credibility, or a number of insolent individuals of the last century-and very few of them at that!-from the stage-play of a de-cowled Saxon and a branded criminal from Noyon. These people say that the dead cannot appear to the living at a particular time. They deny that God ever permits it, and they contradict both \[propositions\], as I have told you, either in explicit terms or by producing examples they would never have quoted had they believed them to be true. Here you clearly have the Scaurian judgement. You are not unaware of the splendid, high-minded thing Scaurus said. You read it in Book 3 of Valerius Maximus. \'Varius \[Severus\] of Sucro says that Aemilius Scaurus has been corrupted by a royal bribe and had betrayed Roman imperial rule. Aemilius Scaurus\', (he was the Leader of the Senate), \'denies that this crime has anything to do with him. Which man are you going to believe?
[Page 173]

I am now going to make a similar argument. The buffoons of our time, the satirical Momuses and plagues of the Church, deny the apparitions of spirits \[spirituum\] and mock them. The saints, the doctrinally correct Fathers \[of the Church\], and the world\'s learned Asian, Africans, and Europeans acknowledge some \[apparitions\] and hold them in honour. Which side are you going to believe? The centuriators of Magdeburg and the croaking frogs who live in Lake Geneva maintain there have never been any genuine sightings of the dead. The captains and heralds of the Gospel, who sucked their knowledge of eternal wisdom from the teats of the Church Militant, which is the pillar of truth, include a very large number in what they write and unanimously agree that they are genuine. Which side are you going to believe? We have apparitions of saints, we have apparitions of the damned, we have apparitions of the blessed, certified by documents from the Church\'s history, by the teaching of \[Church\] Fathers, by acts of the Councils \[of the Church\], and by the records of divine Scripture. Isn\'t it ill-advised to fight against these, shameful to resist them, and irreligious and blasphemous to disparage the good faith of all of them? To accuse these and their authors of lying or stupidity? I am at a loss to know what to call \[such people\]. Understand thoroughly how vile this situation is, Reader, and when you have understood it, cast the thought from you: and when you have cast it from you, crush it underfoot and curse it. To accuse them of lying-unless perhaps they are pretending that one should swallow untruths for truths? To ascribe stupidity to them, if they are not pretending that so many very weighty, very wise, very holy men were stupid enough to allow themselves to be deluded by these inane nursery songs, and to accept old women\'s tales as historical fact? This is the becoming conduct of one\'s opponents.

Book 2, Question 26, Section 6

But perhaps they are impelled to believe this by some rational process of thought or effective argument. I shall set out their objections honestly so that you may examine them. It is for you to judge and determine. \'In Italy\', says Philipp Melanchthon on Manilius\'s Calendar, \'they have the most filthy and obscene fairy tales about Mary who appeared to some monks\'-a fine saying, and one worthy of a buffoon entirely given to lying. Asia has apparitions of the most holy Mother of God. So does Africa, so does Spain, and so does Germany, as I have said, not just Italy, and these are as far removed from all obscenity and filthiness as Melanchthon himself is from purity of faith and Luther from chastity and holiness of life and morals. These cannot be called fairy tales, either. In every age the most weighty writers and most holy men have ratified them by their public testimonies, and the traditions of the Church have confirmed them.

But Melanchthon and Johannes Rivius have felt honour bound to consider that apparitions of the saints, which have been proven by so many centuries, are pieces of old women\'s nonsense; and even though they themselves might be very silly people, they keep calling Pope St Gregory silly, and add falsehood to impudence by childishly, without discernment, and deceitfully, without integrity, heaping up a large number of untruths and inanities, and taking the private abuses of some individuals and the laughable conjectures of fools, and twisting them into public contumely of Catholics, as though \[belief in the appearance of spirits\] did not arise from things which are perfectly good and true. In consequence, \[apparitions\] are explained as subject to deceit and the tricks and malice of human beings and evil spirits and tend to the destruction of those who misuse them. What is more useful than bread? But an abundance of it did harm to Sodom. What is healthier than wine? But drunkenness has killed several people as though it were a sword. What is better than the worship of God? But how many have turned it into the veneration of idols? We need discrimination and common sense, therefore, in order to separate true from false. It is not permissible, however, to disparage faith in things which are true on behalf of things which are false.
[Page 174]

Johann Marbach denies that any \[apparitions\] are genuine unless they turn out to be of Christ and of angels. Why, then, have the Fathers \[of the Church\] and historians been in the habit of adding this so carefully when \[the apparitions\] have been occasioned by angels? Why does the new Vigilantius chain the saints up? Why does he bind them with shackles and manacles? Why does he lock them up in this prison, a man entirely worthy of being imprisoned himself because of his manifold blasphemies, who should be put in shackles because of his desertion to the enemy, a four-footed creature who should be chained up because of his madness and raving?

The centuriators and Lavater add arguments little different \[from his\], by which they try to prove that Purgatory does not exist-I pass over these here because they are irrelevantbut I shall make certain replies which are adapted to the \[present\] discussion. \'What answer\', they say, \'can you give to Augustine who quite clearly denies that the souls of the dead have any interest in what the living are doing, even though, (said Augustine), his mother would be a very welcome sight to him on any night?\' (See The Care of the Dead as cited earlier). \'What answer,\' (they say), \'can you give to Athanasius, Chrysostom, Theophylact, and Tertullian who thought these were the deceptions of evil spirits who need our prayers and pretend they have come from Purgatory by a difficult far-off path?\' They add \[passages\] from Scripture. \'Someone who has gone down into Hell does not come up again any more,\' (Job); and the Psalmist, \'A human is a spirit which goes and does not return. He is dust, and after he has bloomed once, it is said he no longer recognises his place\'. In what way can he return? Elsewhere we are told, \'Everyone dies, and like water slips away\'. Who will call back to its source \[water\] which has slipped away? St Peter and St Paul say the dead are sleeping. Why should you wake them before the end \[of the world\]? Abraham says that the dead cannot cross the chaos which lies between him and them, so that means they cannot be sent to tell the living what is happening where they are. If they cannot come by their own power or because they are sent by God, in what way will they appear to mortals?

This is a summary of those of their arguments which have any importance. But actually it is an abuse of the Scriptures and misrepresents what the Fathers \[of the Church\] say. In Luke, the patriarch Abraham does not deny that the dead can be or are habitually sent by God. He was denying that God was sending them at that particular time, and his word \'chaos\' signifies that enormous, uncrossable gap which none of the dead can cross without God\'s permission, because it has been constituted by God\'s immutable law that souls cannot leave their places of refuge without His permission.

God\'s law has also established it as inviolable that the blessed have no pity for the damned-St Gregory said the road back here is impassable-and that there is no change of destiny or any community of merits between them. (Ambrose and others have replied to this, on which see St Thomas Aquinas, The Golden Chain). The princes of the Apostles testify that the dead are sleeping. This I acknowledge, but the word \'sleep\' signifies \'death\' and \'to sleep\' is \'to be dead\'. This sense comes from the Greeks and is why they call cemeteries коциптуріа, that is, \'dormitories\'. Homer, too, when talking about Mercury\'s wand with which they used to think he roused the dead, said Úπvwvtas Éyeípei, \'he wakes up those who are asleep\', i.e. the dead. (Vergil renders this more obscurely and less accurately as \'he unseals the eyes in death\'.) The Jews also use the word \'to sleep\' for \'to die\', and the Syrians call the dead \'those who are sleeping\', because they hope to be resurrected. As Angelo Canini has observed, if \"to sleep\" means, as far as Lavater is concerned, that \[the dead\] are never seen by anyone, where is faith in the resurrection? Moses, Samuel, Jeremiah, and Onias were \'sleeping\', yet they appeared.
[Page 175]

But \'we all slip away like water. We are dust,\' and we are dispersed into the air. Well now, Reader, whoever you may be who leans for support on these pillars, I\'m telling you, I\'m giving you a good shake-beware of using arguments of this kind, because if they prove anything, they prove that there will never even be a resurrection. \'Once water has slipped away, it never returns\'. Look at rivers! Surely we are not \'never\' going to return? One should therefore say, rather, that in these and similar passages Holy Scripture simply intends to indicate to us how weak and fleeting our life is, as St Augustine and Angelomus \[of Luxeuil\] noted. The royal Psalmist says that a dead person does not recognise his place any more. Here notice that not one of the Fathers \[of the Church\], and no one before Luther, explains this passage the way Lavater does, so that you can recognise an innovator as quickly as you can accurately when it comes to such a novel fabrication.

I find that earlier \[commentators\] had four explanations. I omit the two Jewish ones in the Midrash Tehillim: one, because it is irreligious and deduces from this that the soul is mortal, and the second, which has very little to do with the text \[we are discussing\], is about original sin and that does not arise \[when one is talking about\] a person\'s rising from the dead. These interpret \'velo yashub\' \[nor will he return as \'yatsar haras\' \[breaking down something which has been formed\]. Two of the \[Church\] Fathers\' expositions are clearly figurative, such as that by Arnobius who talks about wealth, and another by Basil who talks about repentance and says that once someone has fallen into sin, he cannot, without God\'s grace, rise up again by his own power. The third belongs to those who say that this passage is not to be interpreted as relating to the body, but to the whole sublunary world to which the soul cannot return, once it has left its dwelling place of the body, without God\'s particular permission. (Thus, an anonymous, but very learned interpreter of the psalms). The fourth is that of Apollinaris and Euthymius. Their meaning is that God knows and recognises the state of the human condition. Death hastens after us-we who begin to perish soon after we are born. Our brief light dies as the same time as we do and the flower of life withers. The web of life, once broken, cannot be joined together again. The bond of soul and body, once untied, cannot be tied together again, and once our soul has flown away, like the wind, it cannot recognise its body any more in order to come to it and take possession of it. During the resurrection itself, the blessed will discover that \[the body\] is shining and incorruptible, the damned, that it is foul, frightful, and liable to corruption. But the damned as well as the blessed will realise that it is, by God\'s command, a work worthy of an angel.

Why? Is it because several people realise that in the case of their friends, once the soul has retreated, they do not recognise their body any more, but hate it? These people are interpreting \'its own\' for \'its\', the way Hebrew does. Father Manuel de Sá seems to be of this opinion. Why? Because St Jerome followed Hebrew usage and translated \'it will not recognise its place any more\'. This is how most Jews understand it, and the Chaldee \[Aramaic\] paraphraser and \[François\] Vatable follow suit. St Job had said this earlier in chapter 7, and the Hebrew, Chaldee \[Aramaic\], Greek, and Latin \[versions\] agree exactly with this interpretation-that memory of the dead person will die as though he had not existed, and that survivors will never know, unless they are informed through revelation, what happens to the dead, along with their life, or where they are being detained. It is perfectly clear that none of this has any force in the face of generally held opinion. What the royal prophet is told-that a human being is a \'breath of wind going and not returning\'-is less of an obstacle because the words which preceded it, \'because they are flesh\', clearly indicate that they are referring to the corruption and wickedness of human nature, and since this always inclines to what is worse, it cannot come back from evil to good on its own.
[Page 176]

A better interpretation is that they are referring to the human being who is liable to decay, who hastens towards death at the speed of Pegasus, and who cannot by his own power come back to life from death. St Job\'s words, \'Someone who has gone down to Hell this way will not come up again. He will not return to his own house, nor will his place recognise him any more\'. \[7.9-10\]. These \[words\] clearly mean the same as those of David which I explained a moment ago. Denys \[van Leeuwan\] also explains in fine style that the soul which has gone down to Hell does not return in the ordinary course of Nature to its former way of life, to its riches, its estates, and its palaces, \'although\', (the words are his), \'it may be able to happen miraculously and often does\', and St Thomas \[Aquinas\], older than he, says in relation to the same text that Job was talking about what happens in the natural course of things, not about what is done miraculously.

So, there you have our opponents\' weapons from the quiver of Scripture! Perhaps one will be able to have more confidence in their use of the Fathers \[of the Church\] Perhaps \[they will get\] more support from them? Not less, at any rate. These people\'s whole attestation is undoubtedly a swindle, and if they allowed themselves to be bound by any law, the Lex Rhemnia would not let them escape branding. I am surprised that St Augustine is offered to me as an objector. You have heard his opinion in this Book, and he convicts these stubborn individuals in no uncertain manner. He says, in fact, that for the most part these appearances are those of angels, but he adds that spirits \[spiritus\] are also sometimes sent to us autorpoownws \[in their own person\]. He denies that they can return because they decide to do so without regard to the will of God, even though \[in other respects, he says,\] his mother would always be present to him; but he maintains that sometimes they do come back at God\'s command, and he establishes this with examples.

\[The objectors\] produce Athanasius to fight \[for them\], but he is wearing a mask, because the man they are referring to is Anastasius of Nicaea, not Athanasius. Athanasius\'s authority must be kept separate. The reason for this and I throw back at my opponents with careless ease the kind of things he says in his Question 11, is that, relying on David\'s testimony-and I have already dealt with this at length-he denies that spirits appear and adds these words. \'There can be no doubt in the case of the souls of the saints, because they will recognise everything both after death and on the Day of Judgement.\'⁴ From this we can make the following inferences. If the souls of the blessed recognise everything after they are dead, they therefore also recognise their own place. If \[they recognise\] their own place, they can return to it, with God\'s permission. Therefore \[Anastasius\] has denied that the souls of the damned can return, because they do not recognise their own place, and he has taken David\'s words to refer only to these souls and not to all souls, as Lavater does. His Question 8 does not deprive them completely of any return, (because this contradicts Holy Scripture), but it does deny that they are sent back by God to satisfy human curiosity and tell people what is happening in Hell.

One needs to be aware, too, that a particular heresy was spreading and flourishing in those days: that the souls of the dead, especially of those who had died a violent death, become evil spirits and are then forced by wicked incantations to obey humans and answer their questions. The Fathers \[of the Church\] opposed this madness as forcefully as they could and unremittingly confuted that insane doctrine. Isidore \[does so\] very well in Etymologies Book 2, chapter 8, and so does John Chrysostom of Constantinople in his second homily (on Lazarus), and in his fourth homily (on Luke) where he explains why God does not allow many people to return to life. It is so that evil spirits may be deprived of material for their deceptions.
[Page 177]

When he says \'many\' are not permitted, isn\'t he acknowledging that some are, even if only a few? He also removes their ability to return in order to tell interesting little anecdotes but does not entirely take away their liberty to come back at God\'s command. On the contrary, in his twenty-ninth homily (on Matthew), he clearly says \'they cannot come back of their own accord\'. Why would he add \'of their own accord\' if they could not return in accordance with God\'s will? This is what Theophylact was dealing with in chapter 8 \[of his commentary on\] Matthew, when he testifies that evil spirits wanted to fabricate a doctrine about the wandering of souls in this world, with which, \[he says\], one cannot agree at all because when souls have come out of the body, they already have a place determined for them and do not go wandering about. With these words he denies that spirits \[spiritus\] \'Èv TW koope πhavátas\' \[wander about in the world\]-these are his words-by their own decision, because they are immediately assigned to specific places. He and Chrysostom are opposing the same error, and both agree with me, as I said.

Finally, Septimius Tertullian also rejects that plainly poetic, and perhaps Platonist, pagan fiction about those who have died before their time and tells us that their souls \'do not wander until the rest of their allotted years have been completed, and he rightly rebuts this poetic licence, along with the audacity of magicians and their meaningless \'power\' to draw forth the soul of those lying \[in their graves\]. But he also adds that God\'s power has called back certain souls as proofs of His right to do so. (You can find this written at the very end of his book on the soul). So neither Holy Scripture, nor the Fathers \[of the Church\], nor reason support \[the objectors\]-only foolhardiness, the malignant disease of trying to be novel, and a desire to traduce something which has a long history, and to revile Catholics, support the objectors.

Book 2, Question 26, Section 7

My second conclusion must be that magicians cannot make use of evil spirits to show us the real souls of the dead. This is what Tertullian thinks in the penultimate chapter of his book on the soul, St Isidore in Book 8 of his Etymologies, Chrysostom and Augustine whom he cites and agrees with, St Thomas \[Aquinas\] Part 1, question 117, article 4, objection 2, Guillaume d\'Auvergne in chapter 24 of the last part of his book on the universe, and \[Francisco de\] Vitoria, Magic, number 17; and a recent writer, Alfonso Mendoza, discusses this at length in question 5 of his Scholastic Questions for Discussion.

The reason is that the souls of the saints are in the hand of God and obey God alone. The Devil cannot allow \[the souls\] of the damned to come out \[of Hell\] unless God commands it, and unless He does, the Devil has not the power to do it, since he too has a place in those things for which \[the damned\] are making atonement. So evil spirits take their place, and are still wandering through the world, and love to cheat these souls, as Iamblichus, relying on Porphyry, explained in his book on the mysteries of the Egyptians. One should not find this surprising, since they transform themselves into an angel of light, and into the blessed Virgin Mother of God, as happened in Bordeaux-I know this happened because I was living there at the time⁴⁵²-and indeed into Christ the Lord Himself, which is the form in which the Devil appeared to St Martin.

From these examples it follows: (i) that the ridiculous notion of some people, reported by D\'Anania, that when you stretch out your hand to the dying and the dying stretch out theirs and take it, their souls will appear to you after they have died, is utterly superstitious, and there is no authority either in the written or the unwritten word of God, or the narratives of various histories, which lends support to this idea.
[Page 178]

\(ii\) It follows that one should not heedlessly imitate something which several people once did and something which Johann von Freiburg says, after mature reflection, in his Manual for Confessors, Book 1, title 11, question 23, should not be censured on the ground that it is a sin. He and St Thomas \[Aquinas\] do not deny-and \[Jan de\] Beets follows them-that it is dangerous, that for the most part such people hand themselves over to be made sport of by an evil spirit, and that they have succumbed to the threatening remark that \'He who loves danger will perish in it\', \[Ecclesiasticus 3.27\] Consequently, in Book 3, chapter 11 of his book on superstition, \[Pedro\] Ciruelo sensibly rebukes those who strike a mutual bargain with someone, intending that, (if it is allowed), the one who dies before the other will appear to the one who is still alive and give him information about his condition.

I know that God has sometimes permitted this, as I told you in the case of Marsilio Ficino. One can also read about St Lutgard, (in Book 2 of her biography in Sauer, Volume 3, under the month of June); and this once happened to a monk in the Dominican house in Zamora. He had entered into this pact with a Minorite, and after a number of days \[the Minorite\] appeared to his fellow-covenanter, who was laying tables at the time, and said he was carrying a fire of atonement around with him. As evidence of his torture, he pressed his hand on a thick wooden table and made a very deep burn-mark on it. I have been told by eye-witnesses that the scorch-mark of a hand still exists on the table and, as a perpetual reminder of what happened, has been covered with a small piece of iron lattice-work. (Father Antonio of Siena writes in his history of the Dominicans that one can have no doubts about this incident at all).

When God allows \[a soul\] to return, He does so for the greater good, and for the most part Behemoth takes on this role. It is scarcely possible for this covenant to be made and remain free from the sin of overinquisitiveness, and it is always responsible for discharging its wish for one\'s welfare accompanies by fear and hope. When St Lutgard told a nun that if she were to die first, she would visit her, she added that she would first say the Benedicite, the Lord\'s prayer, and the angelic greeting \[Hail Mary\], \'so that\', she said, \'the Devil cannot introduce any deception, as he usually does.\' (See op.cit. Book 2). If this saint, who was so enlightened, was afraid of this, how much more should we be afraid?

\(iii\) It also follows from what I have been saying that one can easily tell apart the illusions of the various and diverse shapes taken by the apparitions which evil spirits cast in one\'s path, and so now let us examine these further.

Book 2, Question 27, section 1

The appearances of evil spirits, or the apparitions which evil spirits cast in our path.

I must omit two things-the various causes of the illusions, and the various types of evil spirit. The first is obvious, because many things which are natural are considered to be apparitions. Likewise, when they are not what they seem, such things turn out to be caused by a defect of the senses or the weakness of a faulty passive imagination \[phantasiae\]. Let us look at some individual \[examples\], since in the case of most of them \[people\] have followed Pierre Le Loyer. The ignesfatui dealt with earlier are natural, but very liable to be deceptive. I have seen several such things at the shrine of St Audomar in St-Omer. They also exist in Italy, and ancient authors have told us about them in different places-on Lake Vadimo, on the ager Caecubus, and in Rieti.
[Page 179]

There are also trees and woods torn up by the roots, which provide a kind of fleet in the sea, as Pliny the Elder testifies; and rotten pieces of wood which shine at night; and a small Indian creature, a member of the glow-worm family, but larger, like a scarab-beetle, which shines with its whole body, but especially with its eyes which are enormous in comparison with its little body, and pour out so much light that people are in the habit of reading and writing by it. \[The natives\] call it \'coculium\'. It shines with the light of four little stars together, and if anyone smears his hands and face with them, he looks like a burning, frightening ghost \[spectrum\]. (See \[Pedro\] Cieza \[de León\], History of Peru Part 2, chapter 30). Likewise, vapours form themselves into different shapes, such as those looking like goats\' heads, which were seen by Sulla and happened at Epheus, and similar phenomena which, writes Diodorus Siculus, happen for the same reason at Syrtis Major. (He is worth reading on the subject).

Certain sounds-if you don\'t mind my turning from sight to hearing-are remarkable examples of this kind of thing, such as the echoes in various places, which I have also noted elsewhere in my commentary on Seneca\'s tragedies. In Book 18 of his Intricacy \[of Nature\], \[Girolamo\] Cardano says they deceive several people. Likewise, there are the sounds of the statue of Memnon, (which I dealt with in my commentary), those of the wall of Megara, on which see Pausanias in his book on Attica, \[Geography 1.42.2\], the cavern in Britain about which Clement of Alexandria asks, in Book 6 of his Patchwork asks, was it perhaps St Patrick\'s cave? Likewise, the voices of battle and victory on three mountains in Persia, on which see Clement.

Sometimes unskilled physicians are tricked by the extraordinary sounds made by a child from the suffocation of the womb, \'when\', says Cornelius Gemma in Book 1, chapter 7 of his Cosmocratic Art, \'the arteries have been inflated and enlarged by a poisoned gust of air and are then contracted along their length, the result is that one hears extraordinary noises coming from deep within the intestines, now like the croaking of frogs, now like the hissing of snakes, now like the cawing of crows, the crowing of cocks, the barking of dogs, etc. But this tells one there is an emergency because of the relative size of the passages and the \[amount\] of air which is bursting out\'. Just as natural things sometimes deceive, so also do those which are manmade and taken to be ghostly apparitions. This is what happened to Daedalus\'s aútópata, (on which see Aristotle, The Soul Book 1 and The Statesman Book 1), and others by Archimedes, Archytas, and Boethius which I dealt with earlier. In our own times, too, Charles V was presented with a bronze eagle which flew through the air, and in Paris there was a silver trireme which was driven forward, stopped, and driven backwards by oars of the same material. Then there were the water-organs of the ancients and those which cause astonishment in the gardens of the D\'Este family in Tivoli. Claudian says of these instruments,

\'Let him \[the organist\] make a sound with his wandering finger, And let him rouse into song the hard-working waters deep within by means of the wooden lever\'.

(The surviving books of Hero \[of Alexandria\] talk about these \[machines\]). There are man-made apparitions, and in The Variety of Nature, \[Girolamo\] Cardano talks about how they work, as does Battista \[della\] Porta in his book on natural magic, although in fact the character of the things they use adds a great deal of power to the artefact; and in the case of the natural \[apparitions\] I mentioned just now, the engagements of human beings with them brings artificial \[elements\] to them. To this class \[of phenomena\] more properly belong certain people\'s \'artificial\' voices. When others are present, these individuals pretend they are the voices of people who are not there, and although it is their mouth which is speaking, the words seem to emerge from their belly. This way, they counterfeit the voices of angels and evil spirits.
[Page 180]

Likewise, those who speak through tubes at night pretend these are the voices of angels or souls. Some such story about Boniface VIII went the rounds, according to \[Johannes\] Naucler, Massei, and others. Likewise, there are those who pretend they are the souls of dead husbands and in this way persuade widows to marry them. (I know there is someone who frequently does this in Leuven, and \[Pierre\] Le Loyer tells a story in chapter 7 about someone who used to do this in Anjou.)

According to \[Hector\] Boethius \[Boece\], this is how King Kenneth of Scotland conquered the Picts by whom he had been conquered. While the commanders of his army were sleeping at night, he sent servants to them, dressed in shining scale-armour and holding in their hands spears made from rotten wood which glows in the dark, in order to encourage his \[commanders\] to engage bravely with the enemy, saying that they themselves had been sent by God to guarantee victory. The commanders believed they were angels, enthusiastically attacked the Picts, and massacred them.

Several people have made use of crabs and tortoises by fixing little candles on them and thus, more than once, providing the sight of glowing ghosts \[spectrorum\] in cemeteries. This is why a number of people suspect that Homer gave the name \'tortoise\' to a piece of military equipment intended to deceive \[the enemy\] \[a spearhead of magic\]. Stupid youngsters, too, tried to frighten Democritus with a kind of ghost, as Lucian tells us in his Lover of Lies \[32\], and there are very many examples in more recent books, such as Boccaccio\'s Decameron and so forth.

I am more taken, however, with ancient examples in stories such as the one invented by the young slave in Plautus\'s Ghost: or the one the orator Aeschines tells us tells about what Cimon the Athenian did when he pretended to be Scamander in order to corrupt Callirhoe: or the one Josephus tells about Mundus who pretended to be Anubis in order to deceive Paulina: or the one invented about a heathen priest, Tyrannus, who lied about being Saturn so that he could be free to commit adultery with well-born married women. (Rufinus \[of Aquileia\] tells us about this, and St Cyril of Alexandria mentions this same villain in Against Julian Book 7). Nectanebo, an Egyptian magician, transformed himself into the likeness of Jupiter Amun and deceived Olympias, Philip \[of Macedon\'s\] wife, and from this adulterous union was born Alexander the Great-which is how he got the idea that Jupiter was his father. Joseph ben Gorion tells the story in Book 2, chapter 6 of his History of the Jews, and praises the shrewd historians of Egyptian affairs, Titus \[Livy\] of Rome, Strabo of Cappadocia, and Nicolas of Damascus.

The debauchery which takes place in these pieces of fiction is a more serious sin than in the case of straightforward debauchery and should be punished more severely. When the case is one of theft, it should be punished by the penalty for sneak-thieves: the law relating to pick-pockets, Digest, \'On criminal offences which are out of the ordinary\' \[47.11.7\]. If nothing more than fear is inflicted, the matter should be judged in relation to how much danger was involved. \[An offence\] of this kind is, I think, a mortal sin. The penalty is discretionary-a whipping, for example-and if it is followed by deathperpetual exile. (Digest 48.8.4.1, \'lex Cornelia de sicariis\'; Digest 47.2. 68 and 92, \'lex de furtis\'; Digest 47.11, \'lex de extraordinariis criminibus\'; Digest 48.19.30, \'lex de poenis\'; Licinius Rufinus in a comparison of the laws of Moses and \[the opinions\] of \[Roman\] lawyers; and Le Loyer, op.cit. supra, folio 173). I do not think love provides a suitable reason for letting someone off, and Oldradus\'s legal opinion, no. 101, is not sufficiently safe, even though in other respects he is a very good legal counsellor.
[Page 181]

In \[Book 1\], chapter 8, Le Loyer tells us that change or defect in the senses is not an infrequent cause of people\'s seeing apparitions because their sight, or one of their other senses, is deceiving them. He gives the example of drunks about whom St Ambrose gives a very good account. \'This is why meaningless images come to drunks, whose sight is untrustworthy and unsteady. They often jump over shadows as though these were little pits; the ground shakes, along with their face, and seems to rear itself up and lean away from them, and thinking it is whirling around them, they rush straight ahead in fear and clutch at the earth with their hand-or they think mountains are racing together and imprisoning them. They suffer from a roaring in their ears like the crashing of sea waves and

the thunder of the surf on the sea-shore. If they see dogs, they think they are lions and take to their heels. Some dissolve into confused laughter; others wail in inconsolable grief, and others see things which make them irrationally afraid; and they dream while they are awake and quarrel while they are asleep. Before him, Pliny also declares there is no doubt that dreadful sights appear to drunks.

Lavater distorts Holy Scripture, the way his sect usually does, when he interprets the following verse of Solomon as advice against drunkenness: \'Your eyes will see strange women and your heart will speak wickedness\'. \'Strange women\' \[extraneas\], he says, are extraordinary visions and apparitions, and in this he has as his guide \[Santes\] Pagnino who, along with the Chaldaean \[Aramaic\] paraphraser, seems to have accepted \[the Hebrew word\] zaroth as if it were a neuter noun and meant extraneous things.

This, although it did not displease \[Cornelius\] Jansen, is not a good \[interpretation\] because it should be understood in its feminine sense and thus as referring, not to things, but to women. The sense, accepted by St Jerome and Bede, is that wine is the cause of one\'s lusting after foreign women, and therefore, in order to avoid the ambiguity, the Septuagint translates \[the word\] by a singular noun, &Motpiav, \'different\' or \'foreign\' woman. Neither Holy Scripture nor the Fathers \[of the Church\] accept that this word simply refers to another thing rather than another woman, or \'tart\'. See, for example, how it is explained by \[St\] Salonius, Bede, Jerome, and others \[when it occurs\] a little earlier in the same chapter, \[verse 27\]: \'A foreign woman is a deep well.

A cataract makes one see things like flies or jumping ants flying about in front of one\'s eyes, and I have experienced these, not without inconvenience to my studies, for many years already. Ibn Zuhr, (Book 1, chapter 1, discussion 8), mentions a disease called halahol which makes people affected by it think they are seeing double, and to \[eyes\] which are bow-shaped, everything appears reddish-yellow. Aristotle talks about someone who, because of a defect in his eyes, kept thinking he was seeing an image or likeness of himself in the air, as though \[he were\] looking in a mirror. He also testifies that if anyone looks at the sun for a long time and then quickly turns his eyes away to something else, everything he sees will be yellow at first, then reddish, and finally black, until the change in his vision recedes.

Damaged hearing is also accustomed to deceive, such as happens in those people who suffer from the \'fanciful\' disease, as they call it. Learned men tell us there are two types of this sickness. One is better known under the name фреVÎTIS \[inflammation of the brain\], during which spectral images are cast in front of the mind in visible form. Cornelius Celsus and Caelius Aurelianus should be consulted on this type, which belongs to a defect in the passive imagination. The other type is xopußavtiaaµóç. This removes sleep from the vision and imposes a defect on the ears, because of which \[sufferers\] think they are hearing a ringing and loud noise. On this, see Varro in his satire, Prometheus:
[Page 182]

\'The lightly sleeping mind speaks sounding images; The pupils are not overspread with sleep.\'

Damaged taste also deceives sick people in a remarkable way, as does the sense of smell because of a polyp, catarrh, or xópuça \[runny nose\], and that extraordinary catarrh which was prevalent in Castile in 1581, affliction which did away with many people. A more frequent and greater deception, however, flows from the corruption of the passive imagination. This is sometimes conjoined with \'phrenitis\', which is what it is called when it exists alongside a strong fever. Sometimes \[it is conjoined\] with madness. (This is free from fever and lasts longer). Sometimes the fever is insignificant and not enough to lead to phrenitis. The physician Theophilus-in other respects a sensible man-used to think that when someone had a fever, flautists and cornet players should stand near his bed and play in his ears, and he maintained that this \[illness\] had happened to him persistently and he was now cured. Still, one person finds these details boring and another enjoyable. As Horace puts it in his Letter to Florus:

\'At Argos there was once a man, not undistinguished, Who used to believe he was listening to wonderful tragic actors,

And sat happily and applauded in an empty theatreSomeone who would perform all the other duties of life in a perfectly straightforward manner, Undoubtedly a good neighbour, an amiable host, Courteous to his wife: someone who could forgive his slaves And not go mad if the neck of a bottle had been damaged: Someone who could avoid a rock or an open well. This man was restored to health by his kinsfolk\'s help and care. But when his sickness and its bile had been driven out of him by a strong dose of hellebore And he came to himself again, he said, \"Oh dear, oh dear, my friends, you have killed me, Not saved me, because you have taken away a pleasure, And removed by force a delusion of my mind which gave me much delight,\" \[Epistles 2.2.vv.129-140\].

In his book on the extraordinary things one hears, Aristotle tells us that a similar thing happened in Abydos and, in connection with the pleasure a mad false notion \[can give\], in Book 4 of his Motley History, Aelian gives evidence at length about someone called Thrasyllus who thought that every ship which came to Piraeus belonged to him.

But let us return to imaginary apparitions. I don\'t know of anything which has happened less frequently than an incident related by Timaeus of Locri. \'There was a house in Agrigentum which was called \"The Trireme\" because of the following incident. Some young men\', (from Tauromenium), \'were attending a drinks party there. They started to become somewhat merry and, as usually happens not long afterwards, when they had become well and truly flushed, they were at such mental cross-purposes and so beside themselves that they thought they had been deposited in a trireme, and that a dreadful storm was tossing them on a sea full of swelling and plunging waves.

So, as though they were afraid that the ship would be wrecked because the things in the house were being thrown around, in order to lessen \[the effects of that shipwreck, they started to throw all the plates and anything else they came across in the fuddled state, into the road, or \"sea\", and people ran from all over the place to plunder what had been thrown away. The next day, when they came before magistrates who charged them with what they had done, they replied, (because they had not had enough sleep), that the fear of death had made them prefer to destroy what was in the ship rather than be devoured, themselves, by the waves. Everyone was amazed at their stupidity, and one of their number who seemed to be somewhat older, said, \"Sons of Neptune, I was so afraid, I threw myself under the beds and lay down in the bottom \[of the boat\]\".
[Page 183]

Since they were seen to be even more out of their wits, they were dismissed by the examining magistrate, who quite seriously impressed upon them that they should be wary of any other brutish invitation of this kind. They, however, pretending to thank him, said, \"If we are preserved from such stormy waves and make our way back to harbour, we shall raise statues to you in our own country as our saviours, along with \[those\] of other gods of the sea, Sons of Neptune\". (Thus Caelius Rhodiginus, deriving \[the story\] from Athenaeus.)

People suffering from hydrophobia run away from water because in it they see the dog which bit them. The Devil is in the habit of frequently adding himself to these natural illnesses and abusing humans\' tendency \[to catch them\]. \'This evil, says St Augustine, \'which comes from the Devil, creeps through every entrance of the senses, gives itself to shapes, adapts itself to colours, adheres to sounds, underlies smells, pours itself in by means of flavours, and fills all the pathways of understanding with little clouds. Sometimes it tortures cruelly with grief and fear; sometimes it distracts by means of jokes, or mocks by means of stageplays,\' \[Book of Eighty-Three Questions\].

Book 2, Question 27, Section 2

Just as evil spirits have various ways of working and various wilful caprices, so there are various kinds of them, as I noted in \[Book 1, chapter 2\]. They are all foul and wicked and hostile to human beings, as Porphyry, who lived after Chrysippus, Plutarch, and Philo, rightly says in opposition to Iamblichus. Minucius \[Felix\] makes a better job of it than he in his Octavius. Marcus, in The Way Evil Spirits Operate, by Psellus, divides them into six kinds, as Orpheus before him had done when he wrote to Musaeus:

\'Evil spirits of the sky, the air, the waters, the earth, and beneath the earth, or \"summanes\".

In Eusebius\'s Proof of the Gospel, Book 4, Apollo himself recites the following:

\'They are partly rulers of the earth, partly rulers of the heavens, and sometimes of the upper air, And of the lower air which penetrates damp places, And of the seas and everything below the earth, Because they are in control of everything by means of the abundance of these natural things.\'

He calls celestial \[spirits\] \'ethereal\' or \'fiery\', as Marcus does. Plato also knew of aery \[spirits\] and attributed the discovery of magic to them. The Chaldaeans, along with Zoroaster, call terrestrial \[spirits\] \'wild\', or \'earthly dogs\', (as Psellus explains), because in Zoroaster\'s Magical \[Prophecies\] we read: \'because wild animals will inhabit your vessel of the earth\'. Psellus adds to this, \'\[evil spirits\] who roam around the earth\', are wild animals of the earth\'. I much prefer this version to that of Pletho: \'worms and all kinds of fouler creeping things.\' This same magician later adds: \'they undoubtedly produce earthly dogs from the bosom of the earth and never show a human being a genuine sign\'.

Pletho means they usually appear in the shape of dogs or some other loathsome type of thing to those who are being initiated in sacred rites, and even though Zoroaster accepted this in connection with magical ceremonies, it is also true that these apparitions are also in the habit of disturbing those who have forsaken a wicked course of life and devoted themselves to any monastic order. (Le Loyer confirms this with evidence from the Jews in a work they call Holam Abba). Athanasius says the same in his biography of St Antony, and Abbot Everwinus relates things quite like this about St Simeon of Trier, (Sauer, under ist June), and one can read other accounts elsewhere in the life of St Norbert, about an evil spirit which disturbed the ceremonies at the holy foundation of the Premonstratensians. (See chapters 19, 26, and 27 of St Simeon\'s biography in Sauer, under 6th June.)
[Page 184]

I can confirm this, because it actually happened at the start of my own Society, when a college was founded by Fr. Ignatius \[Loyola\] in Loreto. Its rector-in charge was Fr. Olivier Manare, who was still with us at the time, and who gave me an absolutely accurate account of it, which I shall add now. In 1555 malicious spirits \[spiritus\] were disturbing first one place, then another with loud crashing and banging, causing a remarkable amount of trouble, and depriving a very large number \[of brethren\] of their rest, among whom several were novices in the religious life. They also used to appear in broad daylight in different shapes. An evil spirit appeared to one brother in the form of an \'Aithiopian\' with many enticing and persuasive words to make him leave the religious life. But when he saw that his effort was in vain, he went away, accompanied by very heavy breathing and a foul stench which lasted the whole day.

He manifested himself to another brother in the likeness of St Paul and began to urge him to a devout reading and study of his Epistles so that, on this specious pretext, he could draw him away from his attention to his liberal studies and thus push him into disobedience and out of the religious community-which he was successful in doing. He met Olivier \[Manare\] himself, in the shape of a very large black Molossian hound, jumped up at him and nearly threw him to the ground. He would thrust and push in the form of a mewing cat against the bench on which brethren were resting while they prayed, and rub up against them more than once, in order to divert or destroy their concentration.

There was no peace while the brethren were asleep, and meal-times were not allowed to pass without disturbance. Those who were resting would repeatedly feel little cats climbing on to the bed and getting under the covers, and this filled many brethren with fear and would deprive them of sleep and make them get out from under their blankets. While he was having breakfast, one brother was struck so hard on his side by the fist of an unseen assailant that he turned very pale at once, and almost fell backwards off his bench.

This molestation of his flock caused the rector a lot of annoyance. He tried \[those of\] the Church\'s remedies which would meet the case-Masses, prayers, exorcisms, holy water, holy relics, and other such things. But God did not want the evil to be removed entirely by these. Therefore, Olivier had recourse to the father they all had in common, St Ignatius, and asked for his prayers and help in this molestation. Because he was clearly depressed, Ignatius wrote him a letter, exhorting him to patience and urging him to use those remedies the Church usually employs in similar situations. \'We have already looked to these and tried them all\', said Olivier in a letter, \'but the evil is still with us and is becoming worse\'.

When he received this letter, Fr. Ignatius replied, \'Put your trust in the Lord, and in His goodness. He will set you free, and I shall pray earnestly for you.\' When he received this letter, the rector read it to a circle of Fathers and brethren who by chance had gathered together at the time and, (marvellous to relate), that infestation of apparitions and phantasms instantly stopped, with the result that nothing of the kind was heard or seen again afterwards in Loreto. From a letter written later, we are left in no doubt that the best of Fathers began to pray for his sons and kept on praying as long as letters were carried from Rome to Loreto.

Zoroaster, therefore, calls these \[spirits\] \'earthly dogs\', and \[he does so\] either because of the form they have taken, (which is why Pletho, too, calls them dogs), or, (as Psellus does), because they are the executioners of souls. \'They are earthly\', he says, \'because they fell from Heaven and flutter round the earth\'.
[Page 185]

But what Psellus, or \'Marcus\', \[noted\] briefly about the \[different\] kinds of evil spirits, which he inferred from where they live, Trithemius wrote in much greater detail, and I shall be happy to quote his words from question 5 of his Questions to the Emperor Maximilian, with many additions which I judge to be relevant to an explanation and discussion of magic. \'Just as evil spirits\', he says, \'differ from one another in the power and cunning of their subtlety, so also the power of female workers of harmful magic \[maleficarum\] is not one and the same in all of them, but different in each individual, so they turn out to be in evil spirits which are more refined in disposition or more gross.

There are, you see, many kinds of evil spirit and there are particular degrees of difference between them because of the places into which they fell after they were initially driven away \[from Heaven\] The first kind of evil spirit is called \'fiery\'? It wanders around the upper air and will not be thrust to the lower regions at any time before the Day of Judgement. Even though \[these spirits\] remain continually in regions below the moon, they have no traffic with human beings on earth. That they really do stay beneath the moon St Augustine also testifies in his book Christian Combat, where he says that evil spirits do not live in the sky where the sun and stars are, but that they all remain beneath the region of the moon. That apart, I think Apuleius said that living creatures are made of fire. I think he did so particularly because Aristotle maintained that certain tiny living creatures have often been seen fluttering round, with little wings attached to them, in burning furnaces. These pass their whole life in fire, since they are born with fire and are extinguished along with it, vanishing and returning to their own place from which they originally came\'. (St Augustine\'s reference is from chapter 3.)

St Augustine simply says that evil spirits do not live in Heaven, from where they fell, or in the firmament. He adds nothing about a sphere of fire, so his words have nothing to do with proving \[the existence of\] this first kind. \[Trithemius\] was mistaken in his reference to Apuleius, because he actually did attribute fiery bodies to evil spirits. Finally, those living creature were not evil spirits as far as Aristotle was concerned, but a kind of ephemeral flying insect usually found in furnaces on Cyprus.

If Apuleius thought they were evil spirits, how could he have imagined they are extinguished along with the fire, or are produced along with the fire, since it was thought, in accordance with the principles of his sect, that evil spirits are immortal and not subject to death? Perhaps he meant that when a fire has been lit, one begins to see evil spirits in the flames under the form he describes, and that they cease to appear when the flame is extinguished. But \[Trithemius\] goes on, \'There are no traits in common between these and workers of harmful magic \[maleficis\] because when they linger in the fire they cannot, by reason of the refinement of their \[bodies\] put on a gross or aery body\'. Worthless reasoning!

If there is no sphere of fire, as many people think, this whole fiction falls to the ground. If, by chance, such a sphere does exist, it still does not follow from this that evil spirits of this kind do not traffic with workers of harmful magic, because evil spirits do not have a body of their own, but are simply substances of spirit which, even when thickened as earthly, fiery, or aery substances, are not like a body at all. Therefore, either they do traffic with workers of harmful magic, or one must look for another explanation of this phenomenon, and I have not yet been able to find one. Why, too, is it necessary to enclose these \[spirits\] in fire, that prison outwith Hell, which is in the centre of the earth, as if they do not carry their punishment around them wherever they are? I believe, therefore, that this kind of evil spirit is fictitious and was called \'celestial\' by Orpheus because he thought they lived in the heavens. Others call them \'fiery\', perhaps because they have remained in the higher regions of the air.
[Page 186]

\'The second type is called \"aery\", \[says Trithemius\], \'because it wanders about and lingers in the air which is near us. These are certainly able to descend to the lower regions and, having assumed bodies made of this grosser air, sometimes appear to human beings. With God\'s permission, they quite frequently throw the air into confusion and stir up thunder-claps and storms. All plot the destruction of the human race. Just like humans, they are moved by strong emotions, especially pride and envy, and are affected by continual disquiets. They are not clothed in a solid body and do not stay in one place. Nor do they have a single form, but very many, and change them quite often according to alterations in their mood; and when they have been evoked by the incantations of female workers of harmful magic, or when they have been driven to do so by their disquiets, they come to these women in these \[forms\] in order to do harm\'. One should take note of this error, too, and avoid it-that evil spirits have any body of their own at all, or are born with one, or are endowed with any form visible to humans\' physical eyes. They can be seen only in the form of a body they have fashioned from outside, or of a corpse they have assumed.

He goes on: \'They have a character which is entirely violent, full of rage, and filled with perturbation, which is why these extremely mischievous and thoroughly disturbed \[spirits\] contrive ambushes, (for the most part unexpected); and while they are carrying out their assaults, some of them want to remain hidden and others carry on with the violence. Female workers of harmful magic are propped up by the work of these evil spirits, and the more the women have attained a higher concurrent authority in their order, the more power they have to work evil. \'I think all this is true.

In his biography of St Antony, St Athanasius said that the air is full of evil spirits and, of course, before him Hermes Trismegistus said that there is no part of the world which is without the presence of evil spirits. This must be understood as referring to the sublunary world, otherwise what St Augustine says in chapter 3 of his Christian Combat is not true. Now listen to what \[Trithemius\] says. \'Bishop St Ambrose said, \"The world is full of holy powers because it is full of evil \[spirits\] Be careful of what follows because, in his usual fashion, \[Trithemius\] mixes falsehood with truth. \'Consequently, Platonists have proposed that evil spirits fly in the air like very thick snow which has been set on fire, and that they can be seen by those who want \[to see them\] if people will keep looking steadily at the sky while the sun is shining. But I do not know whether an object is in the air because it actually is there, or whether in fact a blocked movement of the eyes causes one to see false images. Not without slight misgiving, I am satisfied with the experience of those people I have just mentioned.\' A deceptive experience indeed, good for nothing, and worthy of a greater misgiving-its mother being Inquisitiveness and its father Ignorance! It was an illusion of the sense, with the result that you thought you were seeing something which was not, in the nature of things, visible. At least children can notice that the brightness of the sun blinded \[those people\'s\] eyes like that!

\'We call the third kind of evil spirit \"terrestrial\" and have no doubt that they fell from Heaven to earth because of their moral failings\'. Catholics consider there is no doubt that evil spirits of the air, too, fell from Heaven because of their moral failings. \'The most holy bishop and martyr of Christ, the late Ignatius \[of Antioch\], wrote about these as follows in a letter to the Ephesians. \"There is nothing better than to have a peace in which every weapon of the spirits of the air and of the earth as well is rendered null and void\". You see that St Ignatius did not know the spirits of fire \[Trithemius\] mentioned, and in my opinion, he classed the spirits of water under the heading \'terrestrial\'. I have already said several things about terrestrial spirits.
[Page 187]

Trithemius, rather lengthily, adds a number of points by way of illustration. \'Some of these evil spirits live in woods and groves. They set traps for hunters. Others, however, dwell in wide open countryside and cause travellers to go astray at night. Several linger in hidden places and caverns. The rest, less frenzied and disturbed than the others, take pleasure in staying with human beings in the dark\'. All the types-fourteen of them-which I shall adduce in a moment from various authors, belong to this group. \[There is\], for example, the \'faun\', also called \'the prophesying faun\' and \'the little prophesying faun\', and satyrs who are also called \'noises\' and \'females with asses\' legs\', about which Sozomen tells us in his \[History of the Church\] Book 8, chapter 6, where he mentions Gerontius, the deacon, who was punished by St Ambrose and discharged from his service by Nectarius because he kept on insisting he had got hold of \'She with asses\' legs\' at night by her shaved head and thrown her into a mill. The holy men thought, not without good reason, that this meant he had been trafficking with the demon \'She with asses\' legs\', although they did not condemn him for maintaining his belief in evil spirits of this name. This is how one should interpret what Nectarius and Ambrose did.

In talking about this type of spirit, \[Marco\] Stellato imagined they had attached Sarracilus, Sathiel, and Iana to him as companions during a journey by moonlight, but poetically, (that is, mendaciously), he supposes them as saying that their home was on the moon. In his Festive Days, \[Alessandro\] Alessandri also tells us about a spirit such as this which appeared to the monk Thomas. Spirits such as these are \'lamiae\', which the Jews call Lilith, \[deriving the name\] from \'night\'.

Such a kind are Ceres, Diana with the members of her entourage, \'nymphs of the small valley\', dryads, hamadryads, Trophonius, and similar pseudo-deities. Such are the prophesying fauns, the Furies and their mother Furina, (so the tale goes), Mana, the mother of the Summanes, Mantua, Mania the bugaboo, Manuana, and spirits like these, believed to be masculine-the \'swarthies\', the Summanes, and the \'deluders\', about which Martianus Capella talks in Book 2 \[of The Wedding of Philology and Mercury\], Isidore in his Etymologies, and Arnobius in Against the Heathen. In a short while I, too, shall say several things about the cave-dwellers. \[Trithemius\] adds, \'They are guided, not by a single emotion, but by many different ones, because some are less depraved than others, although they are all filled with wicked emotions.
