Certain of them take pleasure in frightening human beings simply with illusions, but others want to draw them to listen to them and be astonished by their predictions of future events. Several, however, direct their greatest effort to frighten, harm, or kill human beings who have been thrown into confusion by an irrational madness, or are out of their minds because of their melancholy; and this we know from experience has happened quite often\'.

Consequently, in the third Book of his God\'s Providence, addressed to the monk Stagyrius, St John Chrysostom says, \'The great ability of grief to do harm becomes more powerful with each action by the Devil, because the evil spirit overwhelms everyone, overwhelms everyone through grief. If, however, one removes that, the evil spirit will not be able to harm anyone at all\'. All this is perfectly clear, and what follows straight away is sensible, although not equally clear. This kind of evil spirit is rarely a close companion of workers of harmful magic because it is emotionally unreliable and fickle. These creatures enjoy terrifying large numbers of people rather than being at the beck and call of a foolish woman\'.

This argument is utterly trivial and is based on the false presupposition that they can be near at hand or lie concealed. That is not true. They cannot be near one person and still terrify large numbers. But they sometimes hang about and make promises to mad people in a glass vessel, or a crystal, or a mirror. When roused by magical incantations, they give replies to silly women, and they tell them if anyone who is about to come their way has cursed them. Anyway, after they have done that, evil spirits do not spend time on these activities, but carry out other functions for them. \'This kind of female worker of harmful magic\', says \[Trithemius\], \'is very different from the rest because when they make someone ill through harmful magic, they promise to cure them. But because for the most part they repel harmful magic by means of harmful magic, the Church forbids all Christians to come near them\'. This is perfectly true, and because of that it appears that Satan\'s kingdom is superhuman, and you have the reason for the disreputable hatred which exists between prophesiers \[ariolos\] or \'truth-tellers\' \[veratores\] and magicians who deal in poisons \[magos venenarios and vampire witches \[striges\] The former try to expose and betray the latter, the latter in their turn secretly lay traps for the former.
[Page 188]

\'The fourth kind of evil spirits is called \"watery\" because they immerse themselves in liquids and live round streams and lakes. They are full of anger, agitated, restless, and deceitful. They raise storms at sea, plunge ships into the deep, and take away the lives of many in the waters. Whenever these evil spirits assume a visible body, they appear quite often as females. They are more rarely seen as males, particularly because they live in wet places, live a more delicate kind of life, and cannot easily take on the masculine shape of the others.\' False reasoning from a false fundamental premise about their physical constitution. If they are spirits, (as they are), why do they compose a female form from water mixed with the other elements rather than a male form from air? Why can they not as easily assume a male body as a female one? \'

Hence antiquity gave Naiads, Nereids, and nymphs of the waters female rather than male names.\' But the same antiquity gave Neptune, Nereus, Proteus, Phorcus, Glaucus, Palaemon, the Tritons, Achelous, Alpheus, Tiber, and so on male names. His next remark is beside the point as well. \'But our \[people\] call them \"water-women\", that is \"Wasserfrauen\" in the vernacular\'. But Germans in the Spanish Netherlands call them by the masculine name \'Neckers\'. \[Thomas of\] Cantimpré calls them \'Neptunes\' in an anecdote about a particular man in his Bees Book 2, chapter 57, page 11.

There were watery evil spirits who, on the very day Syracuse was captured by the Saracens, gave the news to Peloponnesian shepherds who were near their swamp. (See \[Tomasso\] Fazello, Two Decades on Sicilian Affairs Book 6). Because of the attentions of this kind of evil spirit, a person who perjured himself was plunged into the lakes of the Palisci, (a place which is now called Naphthia), as Aristotle, Diodorus, and Macrobius tell us. Near the town of Ihomisum \[Comiso in Sicily\], there is the \'spring of Diana\' which flows into Camarina. In ancient times, if any with dirty hands diluted wine with this water, the wine and water would not mix. In the territory of Alesinum, today known as Caronia, there was once a spring which was usually calm, but when pipes were played harmoniously, it would start to swell and overflow its banks. (See Fazello, first decade, Book 1, chapter 5).

These things were either fairy-tales, or they happened because of the cooperation of evil spirits. The evidence is that they no longer happen. One comes back to what Trithemius says. \'Whenever those which live in rather dry, parched places appear in visible form to human beings, they transform themselves into men\'. Not even this is believable. He tells us that witches \[striges\] also have dealings with this kind of tart and he \[gives them the names \'Naparaeae\', \'Dryades\', and \'Desert-dwelling Lamiae\'. \'These types also assume \[the forms\] of various kinds of brute beast in accordance with the way they are moved by various emotions. But since holy angels\' emotions never vary, they always appear the same way, in the shape of a man. Nowhere have I seen it written that a good spirit has been seen in the form of a woman or any animal.

They have always had the appearance of a man\'. I have already pointed out that this last remark about angels\' apparitions is not particularly well-established, (on which see this Book, question 26, section 3, about the end of the letter I). The argument about the unchangeability of their emotions has little to support it, because just as an angel is unchangeable with respect to evil, so is the Devil with respect to good; and just as the Devil is disposed to evil now in this way, now in that, so also is an angel to good. So, just as an evil spirit adopts one \[form\] in order to satisfy one state of mind with respect to a particular evil \[deed\], and adopts another \[to satisfy\] a different \[state of mind\], what is there to stop an angel from adopting various forms not unworthy of itself as well, in order to undertake various good purposes? Finally, one must make sure that \[Trithemius\] is not saying anything which is inconsistent with an angel\'s freedom to make up his own mind.
[Page 189]

\'But evil spirits have often been seen in the neighbourhood of rivers and springs, sometimes looking like women dancing, sometimes spreading out their hair the way women do, but on occasion speaking with human beings and jeering at them. This type does not usually have dealings with workers of harmful magic\'. Stories told by the ancients, however, indicate that they have been accustomed \[to have dealings with\] human beings the way strumpets do, although to some extent they have been confirmed in the case of Morgana, Melusine, Alcina, and the like, which are popularly known as \'fates\' or \'fairies.\' (See more about these elsewhere in this chapter, about section 14.)

\'The fifth kind is called \"subterranean\" because they dwell in caves, caverns, and remote hollows in mountains. These evil spirits have the worst temperament, attack those in particular who dig wells and mines and look for treasure hidden in the earth and are fully prepared to destroy the human race. They form clefts in the earth, raise up winds which belch forth flames, and shake the foundations of the earth\'. In Eusebius, Book 4, Apollo, by way of description, calls this kind uπoxdovíous and veptepíous, \'subterraneans\' and \'undergrounders\'. Did Satan, Job\'s tormentor, belong to this kind of \[evil spirit\] I think so, and so did those who were still teaching the superstitious techniques of harmful magic in Salamanca and Toledo within the memory of our grandfathers. \'Sometimes at night, adds \[Trithemius\], \'troops of them come out from the mountains and lead wonderful, amazing dances on the plains \[below\] and, as though they are in fear of their single leader, at his signal they suddenly vanish and return to their lurking places.

Occasionally one hears the sound of little bells in the midst of them, and sometimes they pretend they are the spirits of dead human beings.\' Pliny tells a memorable story about their dances and the different sound \[they make\] on Mt Atlas, (Inquiry into Nature Book 5, chapter 1.) So does Solinus, The Wonders of the World, chapter 37. He calls these evil spirits \'Aegipanes\', and Saxo the Grammarian describes the dance and gives the names of every kind of evil spirit in Book 1 of his history of Denmark.

\'The savage troop of dancing ghosts is carried forward and runs to and fro along the wind, And makes harsh noises in the direction of the stars. Fauns come together with satyrs, and a body of Pans Mingled with spirits of the dead fights with a wild look on their faces. Swarthy \[spirits\]⁴⁷⁸ come together with those who dwell in forests, and hobgoblins who do harm Are eager to share the stony footpath with vampire witches. Furies leap forward and the hobgoblins crowd together with them; And a prophetess, together with sub-nosed \[spirits\], jostles them\'.

I shall give many examples of warlike squadrons in a moment when I discuss the household of Hellequin, which is also mentioned by Thomas of Cantimpré, (Book 2, chapter 49, page 4); and perhaps in connection with this I should refer to an emendation by Peter of Blois (letter 14)-\'soldiers of Herkelin\' or \'of Herleurin\'-when he is talking about court officials or courtiers who often wander about late at night. All the codices have different readings and there does not seem to be any satisfactory emendation, as Fr. Jan Buys has diligently signified in his notes on that passage.

I do not so much reject his conjecture as put forward something which has come into my head-that, in connection with this, one should refer to the ghosts \[spectra\] belonging to the night in Frisia, about which Cornelius Kempius \[writes\] in Book 3, chapter 31 of his Origin of the Frisians. (In Book 6 I shall quote what he says). Perhaps, too, the armies which appeared in Spain to someone called Sanchez belonged to this category. Peter of Cluny, a writer of that period, wrote about them, but I shall aside what he said, even though it would be appropriate to insert it here. (I found the passage some time ago in Book 3, part 1, question 7, section 1, versicle 11). Now for Trithemius. \'They seek nothing more than to frighten human beings and astonish them. Consequently, we have found that sometimes they have led rather disingenuous people into their hiding-places in the mountains and shown them, much to their astonishment, the most extraordinary sights and pretended they are friends of the living, as though these \[places\] were the mansions of the blessed\'.
[Page 190]

From wanton nonsense of this kind arose stories about the Hill of Venus, which Pius II mentioned in his letters, the Sibyl\'s cave in Ancona described by Antoine de la Sale, the hill of the white women in Kempius\'s book, (the Alvinnem berg in Brabant), the Cave of Nursus in Italy, and the Sibyl who lived there who is mentioned by his Holiness Pius II in letter 46. There are also the amazing things reported from court records by \[Pierre\] Crespet, from which magicians\' extraordinary blindness becomes apparent. In his book, The Hatred of Satan, discourse 6, \[Crespet\] refers to a Domenico Mirabelli from Arpino. He, along with his step-mother, Marguerite Garnier and other accomplices, was arrested in Mantes la Jolie in France and brought to Paris, along with books on magic which they were taking to be consecrated by the presiding sybils of magic.

This Domenico confessed in court that an associate of his, known as \'the Scotsman\', who had lived in France for a long time, had the reputation of being a necromancer and had carried out remarkable trials of his skill in front of various princes. Quite a few of them had become his students, \[although\] he had taught them nothing worthwhile. This Scotsman had made his way to and met the famous Sybil who, people said, lived in the Cave of Nursus in Italy. He reported that she was a tiny woman who sat on a low stool, and that her hair was loose and hung down to the ground. She gave him a consecrated book and an evil spirit enclosed in a ring to wear on his finger. The book and the evil spirit could be made to work anywhere he might wish, as long as an adverse wind was not blowing on him as he was about to go there. (Do laugh, Reader! The wind is stronger than the evil spirit? Do laugh, too, at the following pieces of fiction regarding magic).

He also said that the supreme Pontiff had placed guards who guard the cave with the utmost care so that no one can take himself off, go there, and consult the sibyl, and that no one can reach her except those who have been initiated into magic and know how to make themselves invisible. This is because when anyone is speaking with her, whether he is a magician or not, dreadful storms and lightning-flashes disturb the whole surrounding countryside.

This is what \[Marco\] Stellato meant to imply in these verses from Book 2:

\'Either the gods of the air or the stars send these winds. Indeed, often when a magician wants to remove Treasure which is lying hidden in the earth, Or to consecrate a book, Or to bring any deity under his control by means of a magic ritual, I have been told he raises a wind and a sudden storm\'.

(Crespet adds that these Sibyls willingly guard flocks of sheep and live among cattle. This is not the reason. It is because shepherds and herdsmen are usually rather simple-minded and somewhat dull-witted, and have plenty of time on their hands, and so are more exposed to being ambushed by these evil spirits.)

The following comes from Trithemius. \'Among these evil spirits, too, there are some which guard the treasures the greed of mortals has hidden in the earth, and so that \[these treasures\] do not come again into human use, \[the spirits\] remove them, steal them, and keep an eye on them, and every so often take them from one place to another\'. The dreadful dog and the half-woman half snake are examples of this kind of spirit. (I have deal with them in Book 2, question 12, at about 10, letter G, and see below, question 28 at the end). \'This kind of evil spirit does not usually have anything to do with women who work harmful magic\'. (Whether one believes this depends on who is saying it). I add to these treasure-guardians their governor, Mammon, whom the ancients called \'Plutus\'. Enchanters almost always invoke him to no effect, for the most part accompanied by the loss of both their physical and spiritual lives, and never without the loss of the better of the two.
[Page 191]

\'The sixth kind of evil spirit is called \'light-shunner\' because they have an absolute horror of light and hate it. In consequence, they never appear during the day and cannot assume a body in any fashion other than that of the night. This is \"the terror which walks by night\". This kind of evil spirit is inscrutable, utterly gloomy, driven by cold passions, malicious, restless, and thoroughly disturbed. It violently stops people who meet it during the hours of night from going further and quite often, if God permit, kills some of them with a breath or a touch\'.

I am not at all sure whether Asmodeus, about whom one reads in the Book of Tobit, belonged to this type. But I think the pagan idea that Cerberus and ghosts wander about at night and are imprisoned again once the light has risen had its origins in this type of spirit. (You can read what I have said \[about this\] above in question 26, section 1, at letter B). The Jews say the spirit which wrestled with Jacob was one such, and Origen follows them in First Principles Book 3, but the context quite clearly indicates, and the fairly general tradition of the Greek and Latin Fathers \[of the Church\] tells us, that it was a good angel, who was representing God. The Targumatic commentators on Psalm 90, verses 5 and 6, acknowledge \[the existence\] of light-shunners, and in Book 2 of his Persian War, Procopius gives a sign that he does, too. I think Hecate belongs to this type. (See what I shall say about her a little later in subsection 8, at letters G and H).

The Abbot of Spanheim \[Trithemius\] goes on to say, \'This kind of evil spirit does not meet workers of harmful magic and cannot be confined by incantations because it runs away from the light, the voice of human beings, and also any kind of disturbance\'. This is not a good enough reason for us to say that lightshunners do not have any dealings with human beings, because the real reason for this traffic is to offend God and destroy human beings, and this is how they have their place among these and the other kinds \[of evil spirit\]. The power of their predisposition to hate God and humans-\[: a predisposition\] they and this type which, as Trithemius said, excels in malice, all have-is both great and strong and increases every day. But it can happen that this \[sixth kind\] find it more difficult to make pacts and that they do so less frequently than the others because of a kind of peevishness and aversion from all social intercourse.

\[Trithemius\] goes on to say, \'The aery kind is most particularly accustomed to fall in with women\'s acts of harmful magic because it is more full of guile and the most presumptuous foolhardiness\' I think he did not want to say they fall in with men\'s acts of harmful magic as well. What reason can there be, if they are very foolhardy and very full of guile, for them to stand in awe of men, quite a few of whom, it is generally agreed, end up ensnared? Why should they love to trap and drag to their destruction only women and not men? It looks as though the Abbot mentioned the \[case\] of women only because far more women are always being arrested for this offence. (I am talking about witchcraft \[strigatione\], because perhaps you can find more men who are enchanters or fortune-tellers.)

\'Several of the terrestrial \[kind\], too, it is said, humour workers of harmful magic, although they are of an inferior rank and cannot perform the more powerful acts of harmful magic at all\'. I acknowledge that evil spirits do not all have the same power, but I think there is not one of them that cannot, with God\'s permission, destroy crops, raise storms, produce rain, and kill human beings with acts of poisonous magic \[veneficiis\]. I also think that not a few familiars and household manikins belong to this terrestrial type. But God and the angels know that we human beings cannot be sure about any of this. So, I have set out this demonology in Trithemius\'s words and added a few things which the doctrine of correct philosophy suggested to me as I was writing. Now let me continue and, in as far as my reading of a large number of books will be of assistance, I shall confirm my individual points with examples and the authority of historical accounts.
[Page 192]

Evil spirits are called malachim ra-aim in Hebrew, \'wicked angels: rasha\'galioth, \'heads or leaders of captivity\': satanim, \'slanderers\', (from which Arabic satainim is derived): el-elim, \'deceptions\' or \'phantoms\', (from which is derived Greek ÉXEXEÚS, the surname of Bacchus): and shedim, \'evil spirits\'.

In Greek they are called πveúµata πháva and πveúµata πóvnpa, \'deceiving spirits\' and \'morally worthless spirits\', and δaínoves áπò TOÛ Saluoveîv, \'evil spirits, derived from \[the word\] Saluoveîv,\' because they strike terror \[into people\]. All around the world, many whisper silently the true name of these sprits -- the shelby, or, in some circles, Elbysh.

In Latin the ones we are dealing with are \'manes\' \[the souls of dead ancestors\], \'lares\', \[spirits of the household\], \'genii\' \[spirits of a particular place or person\], \'larvae\' \[hobgoblins\], and \'lemures\' \[ghosts of the dead\]. In French they are called \'gueliers\' \[hunters\], \'lutins\', \[hobgoblins\], \'gobelins\', \[evil gnomes\], \'moynes bourez\', \[spirits of the countryside\], and loupsgaroux\' \[werewolves\]; in Italian, \'farfarelli\', \[goblins\], \'mazzaruoli\', and \'mazzapengoli\', \[spirits which knock you on the head\]; and in Spanish, \'trazgos\', \[goblins\] and \'duendes\', \[house-spirits\].

In times past they appeared in more forms than they do now, and from them came a large number of the ancients\' gods of the underworld. I have now spoken about the spirits of a particular place \[geniis\], ghosts of the dead \[lemuribus\], spirits of the household \[laribus\], and the souls of dead ancestors \[manibus\]. Let us have a look at others.

1\. First one comes across Hecate who presides over the places where three roads and four roads meet. She has three faces. The one on the right is that of a horse, the one on the left that of a dog, and the one in the middle that of a woman. \[The ancients\] used to think that this \[goddess\] despatches ghostly terrors, that at her arrival the ground would tremble, and that one could see brilliant flashes of lightning, which Seneca calls \'sacred fires\'. She would come surrounded by many dogs whose dreadful howling and barking would be heard. According to the scholiast on Apollonius Book 3, when she changed this appearance, she was called \'Empusa\', but other people distinguish between the two. She was the wife of Pluto and was also known as \'Proserpina\'. I learn about Hecate\'s dogs from Eusebius: \'Yaia δ\' ÉµWv σкилánwv Svopepòv γévos nvioxéúel: \'but the earth carries the tribe of my black whelps\'. By these words she signifies that she is command of the ethereal and aery spirits, and she adds that the terrestrial \[spirits\] also obey her. Zoroaster wrote verses which say that these appear to the Magi when they are making sacrifices. These words accompany those I quoted earlier at the beginning of this section.

2\. Pluto: the Greeks call him \"Aions because he brought death into the world. \[His name is derived\] from the Hebrew ed. He is also called \'Plutus\', the god of riches, which is the equivalent of Hebrew or Syriac Mammona. By the Egyptians he is called \'Serapis\', that is, \'burning\', from the Hebrew word saraph. Others maintain it means \'the sun\', and this receives confirmation in Macrobius, Saturnalia Book 1.1.20 and, somewhat less clearly, in Eusebius, Proof of the Gospel Book 5.

3\. Charon, a word of Hebrew origin, denotes someone who is filled with anger and frenzy. Vergil\'s Aeneid Book 6 and Seneca\'s Hercules Furens provide us with examples.

4\. According to Le Loyer, \'Cerberus\' can be derived from celeb ha aretz, that is, \'dog of the earth\', because Zoroaster has told us that this is how evil spirits are called.

5\. There are also wrestling evil spirits which used to force human beings to wrestle. The radapvaios \[murderer, avenging spirit\] Temesius which used to trouble Temesa was one of these. He pretended he was the soul of Polita who had been murdered by the Bruttii. The Greek have a great deal to say about this one. He would ape the angel who wrestled with the patriarch Jacob.
[Page 193]

6\. Those which are called \'alastores\' \[tormentors\] were extremely ferocious. Perhaps they were the same as the alastor Nemesis, because \[the ancients\] used to imagine that Nemesis was the avenger of crimes. In Against Celsus, Origen attributed this duty to \'Alastor\', Zoroaster called him \'the executioner\', and the scholiast to Euripides\'s Medea thought he was so called because no crime at all remained hidden from him-öv oux \"OTI hateiv, \[it cannot escape his notice\]. I think, on the other hand, that no crime escapes him or lies hidden from him because God allows him to get rid of them and destroy them.

Perhaps this \[spirit\] is the destroyer which appears in Apocalypse \[9.11\], the king of locusts, the angel of the abyss, whose name in Hebrew is \'Abaddon\', in Greek, \'Apollyon\', and in Latin \'the one who destroys\', (\'Abaddon\', áπoMúov), where Primasius \[of Hadrumetum\] misreads \'Armageddon\', and \[Ambrose\] Hansbertus and Haymo \[of Halberstadt\] \'Labadon\'. Like me, however, Andreas of Caesarea and Arethas of Cappadocia read it correctly. But Tychonius disregards \[the word\] entirely. Perhaps that was not long after the Donatists decided to mutilate Holy Scripture. Both \[Theodore\] Beza and similar innovators today delete \'the one who destroys\'. The Church of Rome recognises it and retains at as something which was added by someone who understood Latin very well. In all three languages it signifies an evil spirit which corrupts and destroys everything.

7\. There were also \'Fones\' \[deities of the woods\], or satyrs, whose leader was Faunus. \[The ancients\] used to attribute terrifying and unexpected sounds to him and, to remove these delusions, would sacrifice to Jupiter Faunus to whom a shrine was dedicated on the Aventine \[hill\]. Psellus calls them \'donkey-footed\' from the \[Greek\] form \'Onoscenes\' or \'Onoscelides\' \[she who has asses\' legs\]. (See Sozomen, \[History of the Church\] Book 8, chapter 6). Holy Scripture calls them \'shaggies\'-the Hebrew is sahirim-in Leviticus 17, verse 7 where \[Latin\] translates \[the word\] as \'evil spirits\', but the Septuagint as \'deceptive things\' or \'idols\', with the same sense. (You can find the same in 2 Chronicles 11, verse 15). Evil spirits are also called moles and bats by Isaiah, and also \'shaggies\', \'asscentaurs\', and \'vampire-spirits\', undoubtedly from the diversity of their appearances.

They are also called \'aegipanes\', that is, \'goat-footed\'. Pliny writes that creatures such as are described by poets, and their dances, were often seen on Mt Atlas. \[Pomponius\] Mela recounts what he has been told \[about them\], and Julius Scaliger confirms that they have been seen and heard in our time. In his biography of St Antony, St Athanasius says that St Antony met one of these in the desert. Plutarch in his \[Life of\] Sulla and Diodorus Siculus in his Library make mention of these same spirits\' having been seen; and, in his commentary on Genesis, Rabbi Abraham ben Ezra has many anecdotes about them, which are not worth reading.

8\. Holy Scripture also makes mention of an evil spirit in the following words: \'You will not be afraid of the terror at night, of the arrow flying during the day, of the hobgoblin walking in darkness, of the attack and the evil spirit at midday\'. Here are enumerated four perils or baneful things, all of which \[the Psalmist\] means are types of evil spirit. As the Aramaic paraphraser puts it, \'The fear of evil spirits walking in the night, the arrow of the angel of death shot during the day, death walking in darkness, and a tumultuous crowd of evil spirits going about at midday\'. I think one can count two perils or damages by human beings and two threatened by evil spirits-in the case of humans, done by overt force and cunning machinations, in the case of evil spirits \[done\] both secretly and openly, during the night and at midday especially, sometimes merely by \[causing\] fear of spectres and terrors, sometimes by an evil spirit\'s breathing forth a noxious sickness or causing some other injury.
[Page 194]

In his homily on holy baptism, St Gregory Naziazenus, of course, conjoins the terror at night and the evil spirit at midday, missing out anything in between. I also think that he puts the two damages \[caused by\] evil spirits in first and fourth place so that he can signify that the damages inflicted by human beings are not done without the help of evil spirits. As he says elsewhere, God inflicts \'what He sends\', (that is, His blows), \'by means of wicked angels\'. I know that Rabbi Salomon and others refer the last two to evil spirits, but instead of dabar, (with two kamatz), which signifies \'business, cause, thing, contrivance\', these people read it less correctly with Masoretic dots as deber by which they mean a kind of evil spirit. The word, however, signifies death or disease, (Exodus 9, verse 3), as St Jerome translates it in that verse and is followed \[in this by François Vatable. Our Vulgate is not alone in reading dabar, of course. The Septuagint also translates it as πpâyµa \[troublesome business\].

As for the evil spirit at midday, even if I like St Jerome\'s explanation that it means the palpable insults of evil spirits and, clearly, the harms they bring, I still think that what St Basil and Theodoret recorded is perfectly true-that the Psalmist spoke according to an opinion held by many and which later prevailed, and that sometimes the word refers to evil spirits at midday, which is also the meaning given in a commentary on the Psalms attributed to St Jerome. Let me say what I think about this. First, the phrase \'from the attack\' signifies a violent, fierce, overt, and savage infestation. The Hebrew word is mikketeb, that is, \'from ruin\', (as Arnobius says), \'overthrow, destruction, devastation\'.

The Greek word is oúvπtwµa, which some people translate as \'bite\', others as \'destruction\', and others as \'the ruin of those who die and fall together.

Secondly, I accept perforce the verse\'s \'from an attack and an evil spirit\' as a way of saying in common parlance \'from an attack of an evil spirit. This is how Aquila and Symmachus correctly translated the phrase. Thirdly, I think \'the evil spirit at midday\' is said for two reasons: (i) because of the place. \[Evil spirits\] were believed to live in the desert, something which meant \'midday\' to the Jews. (\'Midday\' and \'desert\' often refer to the same things in the Old Testament and so I think \'the evil spirit at midday\' and \'the evil spirit of the desert\' are one and the same). Hence, we read that the garden which contained the tomb of Jamnes and Mambres was the dwelling-place of a very large number of extremely ferocious evil spirits, (as Palladius tells us in Macarius), and we gather from Dio\'s History of Libya that the kind of evil spirit \[known as\] \'lamiae\' \[vampire-spirits\] lived in the deserts of Libya where there are still many apparitions today.

Procopius, and Cyril \[of Alexandria\] in his \[commentary\] on Isaiah, testify that evil spirits delight in living in forests and deserted places; and, according to Eusebius Preparation of the Gospel Book 5, \[chapter 6\], the oracle of Dodonian Apollo of Branchidae replied that, with the high-pitched sound of his incantation and the dreadful appearance of his body which was contorted in frenzy, Pan killed nine peasants who were cutting wood in the \'forested mountains\', údnevta xαì ойреа, and that he would have killed more had not \"Apteuis åypotépn \[Artemis of the countryside\] restrained him, (that is, a stronger evil spirit holding back a weaker).

In his book The Way Evil Spirits Operate, Psellus explains that this kind of evil spirit is connected with the sun and is therefore leonine in character, which is the appropriate characteristic for them. Raphael binds Asmodeus in the desert and releases him, and Christ our Lord is tempted in the desert. According to several \[commentators\], David calls these evil spirits \'beasts of the forest\' in Psalm 103, verse 20. Likewise, the violent wind which destroyed Job\'s house and crushed his children at the same time, (Job 1.19), came from the desert at noon, (this is \'the midday pestilence\'). It undoubtedly happened while they were having a meal, as either Origen or another author puts it very well in the Job attributed to Origen.
[Page 195]

The Hebrew word ruah means \'spirit\', (πveûua in the Septuagint), as well as \'wind\', (Aquila, áveuos), and this is correct because the evil spirit raises this wind. On this see Origen, (if he is the author of the foresaid book). \'The saints know these \[spirits\] and are mindful of them because at noon, the time of their meal, Job\'s children were seized by disaster and died. They begged God to save them from the pestilence which walks in darkness and form destruction and the evil spirit of midday, because more attempts are made by evil spirits in the darkness of the night and in the middle of the day than at any other times.\' Later on, he introduces Job as saying that that wind was not sent by God, but that the evil spirit himself had accomplished \[the destruction with a counterfeit wind: namely, \'You and a wind coming from the desert, have touched the four corners of my house\', etc. Jarrius writes that there are two evil spirits, one of which should be called \'deber\' \[pestilence\] and the other \'qeteb\' \[destruction\]. One of them goes about at midday, the other at night.

\(ii\) Therefore the midday \[evil spirit\] gets its name from the time of day because this type of evil spirit usually appears at noon and molests human beings cruelly and severely, on the one hand by spiritual temptation and strife, especially spurring them to lechery and idleness, both of which sins drive a human being strongly when he is full to bursting point with food-as Nicetas correctly says in the homily by \[Gregory\] Naziazenus which I cited, and Euthymius \[Zigabenus\] and Theodoret on the Psalms-and also by physical afflictions, which one can gather from the opinion of ancient pagans who believed that Pan, (in this case one of the evil spirits of midday), was extremely irascible and the source of a good deal of fear, as Theocritus testifies in Idyll 1, and as one gathers from historical anecdotes.

In eastern Russia, an evil spirit had the habit of roaming the countryside at noon during harvest time, dressed like a grieving widow, and would break the arms and legs of labourers unless they immediately fell flat on the ground at the sight of the apparition and worshipped it. Books also provide written testimony that frightening apparitions are accustomed to appear as often as infectious and contagious diseases or other evils of this kind arrive, as I have proved satisfactorily in my commentary on Oedipus, verses 172 and 174.

I add something which is entirely relevant to this point. In the salt-pits of Peru, behind the River Consata, an apparition of gigantic size appeared to the Indians. Its stomach had been cut open and it had been disembowelled. It was carrying two small children in its arms, made dire threats, and then vanished. The ignorant people made light of it because it appeared while the sun was shining, but a dreadful, fatal plague followed its threats, as \[Pedro\] Cieza confirms in Part 1, chapter 24 of his history of Peru.

I add another \[example\] from Procopius. He writes that it was recorded that during the widespread, extraordinary plague which depopulated the whole world at the time of Chosroe and Justinian, evil spirits looking like human beings were seen busying themselves in public and private places. They struck down those they met, and those whom they had struck down were immediately wasted by disease, (\'a particularly virulent, burning blast which is why the Jews call these evil spirits Mererim and Rhessaphim, \'deadly\' and \'burning\' or \'fiery\'\'). \[Procopius says\] this happened to some people while they were awake in broad daylight, (there you have the evil spirit at midday), and to some while they were asleep at night, (there you have the evil spirit of the night). Aristophanes\'s scholiast confuses the evil spirit at midday with Empusa, explaining that \'Empusa\' is Saluóviov ueonußpivóv \[the midday evil spirit\]. I think Empusa was also an apparition of the night, but that when she appeared at night, she was Hecate, and when she appeared at midday she was \'the evil spirit of midday\'. She would change herself into different shapes. She would become, (as Epicharmus says in The Marriage of Hebe), a twig, an ox, a viper, a stone, a fly, and a woman. Aristophanes adds a mule and an enormous monster.
[Page 196]

Others ascribe \[to her\] a single bronze leg, others an ass\'s leg or member, and from the latter she is called óvoxóxos \[\'ass\'s hoof\'\], and from the former óvokévtaupos \[ass-centaur\]. In his commentary on Isaiah, Theodoret says, \'certain ancient \[authors\] call ass-centaurs \'Eunoúoas, but the modern name for them is óvooxeríSES \[\'women with ass\'s legs\'\]\'. Aristophanes\'s scholiast agrees with this. A final point: Empusa would become anything she wanted, whatever was advantageous to her, as Epicharmus says.

This has been about the evil spirit at midday. When it comes to evil spirits of the night, there is general agreement that night is, as it were, the proper battle-ground of apparitions and evil spirits, and that this is why they are called \'princes of darkness\', and why we read that the night belongs to them and the power of darkness, (Ephesians 6.\[12\] and Luke 22. \[53\]. See Augustine on Psalm 103). Asmodeus in the story of Tobit chapter 6, \[verse 14\], is one of these nocturnal spirits, as is the exterminating angel of the Egyptians, which was the evil St Jerome talks about in \[his commentary on\] Isaiah, chapter 24, and Rupert \[of Deutz\] in his commentary on Exodus 12. (\[François\] Feu-Ardent follows them in \[his commentary\] on Ruth, chapter 3, verse 9.) Seneca said that the pestilence was seen walking about during the day, and that apparitions \'Wander through the groves in the likeness of men, \[But are\] larger than men\'.

At night, however, (the two \[spirits\] should be kept separate like this), \[there is\] the one I have been talking about: \'\[They say\] that, yes indeed, the dog has burst its chains of Taenarian iron, And is wandering through our lands\', \[Oedipus 171-173, 174-175\].

I have learned elsewhere from very many authors that most of the apparitions and terrors of evil spirits are clearly in possession of their strength at night and vanish in the morning. Psellus also says that evil spirits recognise certain people as inhabitants and lovers of darkness. The patriarch\'s Jacob\'s opponent, however, was a good angel, not an evil spirit as certain people have thought according to Procopius and a particularly headstrong, rash individual, Francesco Giorgi in Problems Relating to Holy Scripture.

Among these \[spirits\] of the night, the leader of the \'succubi\', \[evil spirits who cause nocturnal emissions is an apparition the Jews call Lilith, (from \'laila\', that is, \'night\'). This type of evil spirit is not usually dangerous to men except at night. Hence the Church, along with St Ambrose, offers a prayer against them.

\'May dreams go far away from us, And fantasies of the nights. Tread down our enemy So that our bodies may not be polluted, \[Compline hymn\].

Incubi are evil spirits which roam around during the day. The ancients made Pan their leader. The Jews call him \'Haza\'. Christians give him the far better name of \'incubus.\' In the Glossary he is called \'the incubus Пâv\' and \'Inuus\'not, I think, \[derived\] from Hebrew anas, \'to afflict, which is far-fetched, but from the Latin verb \'entering\'. The ancients, you see, thought that this evil spirit and those subject to him are on fire with a shameless love of women, not because they take pleasure in copulation, but because they delight in humans\' offending God and in the human race\'s being so loathsomely defiled. This is how one should understand Abbot Serenus in \[John\] Cassian when he says, \'Some evil spirits are more ferocious and more savage. They cannot get enough of wars and disasters, attack those who cross their path, and try to kill them by means of a cruel death\'. (This seems to be referring to the evil spirit at midday and to those about which St Matthew \[speaks\] in his Gospel, chapter 8, verse 28, and pseudo-Abdias in Book 3 of the Life of St Andrew). \'But there are others called \"fauns\" which mislead and poke fun. They haunt certain places or roads, and while they take no pleasure at all in the sufferings of those passers-by whom they have been able to deceive, they are happy to tire them out merely by mocking and jeering at them rather than doing them harm. Sometimes, too, they have sex with human beings in harmless copulations.
[Page 197]

Two things need to be noticed in this passage: (i) they take no pleasure in the sufferings of human beings must be understood comparatively, not as though it applied in all cases, and (ii) the others, who are crueller, do take pleasure \[in harming people\], because it is truer to say that absolutely all evil spirits want to inflict very serious ills upon human beings, if God allows them \[to do so\]. According to Athanasius, St Antony says that all evil spirits have an inborn hostile hatred of human beings, more particularly against Christians, and one which is especially savage against monks and virgins dedicated to God.

But they harm individuals only to the extent that God permits. If they are not prevented from going further, they take pleasure in mocking and deceiving people and in enticing them into some sin which is at least venial, such as letting their eyes wander idly, or talking to no purpose, laughing without much restraint, being distracted during prayer, and other \[sins\] of this kind into which even good people are very easily led astray. The second point about evil spirits\' copulations, which he put last, does not claim that all oppression and, as it were, stifling of this kind is produced by evil spirits, because it is agreed that sometimes it arises from that kind of melancholic illness which the Flemish call \'mare\', \[phantom\], the French \'cauchemar\', \[a phantom which presses down on one\], the Spanish \'pesadilla\', \[a small heavy weight\], Themison \'πviyahiwv\', \[\'a throttling spirit\'\], and \'ephialtes\', \[the one who jumps on you\], and is described at length by the physician Paul of Aegina. Sometimes, however, it is not an injurious disposition of the body, but an undisguised, straightforward attack by an evil spirit, as I said earlier in question 15. Now let us go on to other things.

9\. Pausanias describes for us an evil spirit \[called\] Euronymus. It has long, rather prominent teeth and a fearsome, bluish-black, cadaverous body, and wears a fox-skin. It usually feeds on dead bodies and devours them right down to their bones. To some people this seems to be a description of Death. My opinion, however, is that some apparition appeared to the ancients in some shape or other and straight away they gave it a particular name and instituted sacrifices or ceremonies of atonement to drive it away. Hence arose the following names and fairy-stories about female apparitions or portents.

10\. In the tenth spot comes the Sphinx of Thebes about whom Strabo \[wrote\] in \[The Geography of Greece\] Book 9, Apollodorus in Book 3 of his Library, Hyginus in Stories chapter 151, Natale Conti in Mythologies Book 9, chapter 8, and Giglio \[Gregorio\] Giraldi in Obscurities. If she existed, she was an evil spirit which took the form of that type of monkey called sphinges, on which read \[Johannes\] Pierius, Hieroglyphs Book 6 and Solinus, \[The Wonders of the World\], chapter 30. Secondly, \'Harpies\', on which see Hesiod\'s Theogony; Apollonius, Argonauts Book 2; the Orphic Argonautica; Apollodorus, Library Book 1; Eustachius on Odyssey Book 12; and Izacius on Lycophron. Thirdly, three \'Gorgons\', on whose form and kind I have written at length in my commentary on Seneca\'s Medea, verse 831.

Fourthly, three \'Sirens\', concerning which I made the necessary points in my Adversaria on Seneca\'s Medea. There is also a mention of these in the Latin version of Isaiah 13, verse 21. This is how the Septuagint translated \[the word\] and, according to Eusebius, so did three translators, although they did not use the same word for the Hebrew as Latin does. Theodoret thinks the Sirens were evil spirits inciting \[people\] to physical pleasure. Fifthly \'Erinyes\' or \'Eumenides\' which Latin \[writers\] called \'Poenae\' \[spirits of vengeance\]. There were three of them too. I brought together a lot of information about their appearance and nature in my commentary on Medea verses 13 ff. and verse 960.
[Page 198]

Fifthly, \'nymphs.\' Some of these are associated with air, such as the Sibyl about whom Plutarch writes in his book The Delay of Divine Vengeance. Some are associated with earth, such as the Oreads, the Napeae, the Dryads, and the Hamadryads. Some are associated with water, such as Nereids and Naiads, the former of the sea, the latter of rivers. It is agreed that all these were apparitions of evil spirits, and if it were not generally agreed, it would be proved because the sight of those enraged women would drive \[people\] out of their right mind, (which is something evil spirits do), as the scholiast to Theocritus and Hesychius, among Greek writers, tell us, and Varro and Festus among Latin writers. The Greeks call those who have been driven mad this way \'Lymphatos\', \[affected by the water goddess Lympha\], as though to say \'nymphatos\', vuuporýπtas, \[seized by nymphs\]. Those who have seen Ceres are called \'ceriti\', \[affected by Ceres\]; those who have seen a faun at midday, \'confounded\'; and those terrified people who have heard Pan and are distressed by a groundless fear, \'panic-stricken\', although this can be applied, broadly speaking, to those suffering from hydrophobia, and so on and so forth. (See what I have said in my commentary on \[Seneca\'s\] Hercules on Oeta, verse 246.)

The eleventh kind of apparition is the one with which wet-nurses usually quieten small children by striking them with fear. The main one of this type is \'Pytho\', (Lucilius, Satires \[Book\] 30). \'Acco\', \'Alphito\', and \'Mormo\' are, I think, names taken from characters in tragedy or comedy, who cause terror because of their ugliness. Theocritus and Aristophanes\'s scholiast tell us about Mormo. Acco originates in the elderly woman who drives people insane when they see her in a mirror and makes children terrified. \[Lodovico\] Ricchieri tells us about her in Book 17 of his Ancient Readings, as does the writer of Adages \[i.e. Erasmus\] under \'Accisare\'.

Plutarch gives the same information about Alphito. Next there is \'Gello\', about whom there is an adage, TEMA \[a woman over-fond of children\]. They say she was a young woman who died and that her ghost went wandering about on Lesbos and brought premature death to children. They note she was mentioned by Sappho. Another was \'Gilo\' about whom Nicephorus tells us the following while, among other things, he is talking about the things which foretold Maurice\'s becoming Emperor. \'When his mother was giving birth, she said that a strange, constantly changing, sweet smell was given off by the earth. She added-and this sounds like an old wives\' tale, and I have grave doubts about it-that very often a female called \"Empusa\", (but someone else said was \"Gilo\"), took the child out of its cradle as if she intended to eat him, but that she was unable to do him any harm at all\'. Whatever the story, however, it is more likely that this vampire witch \[strigem\] or \'lamia\' was someone who was unsuccessfully ambushing a child which was well protected by an angel. The more sensible Evagrius, (Book 5, chapter 21), added nothing about Gilo or about old wives\' tales, leaving the whole thing to the reader\'s judgement.

This was partly a bit of old women\'s nonsense, partly not nonsense but actually true. It was nonsense and out of step with reason to think that these women\'s bodies became so small that they could come through closed doors and push the bodies they had been born with through them. (See what I said in question 17.) \[But\] it was true that, with the help of an evil spirit, these wicked females were sometimes in the habit of entering the bedrooms-regardless of the fact that the size of their bodies could not change and penetrate solid objects-and once there making every effort to destroy the children. Consequently, the judge who is unwilling to be ensnared by credulity or incredulity when it comes to these matters must distinguish carefully between them, and he must examine very carefully indeed all the circumstances relating to the judgements he is going to make, because it can happen that a judge acquits the accused of one of the charges of deception which have been brought against him, although he would have been found guilty had the charge been brought in a different way. For example, suppose some vampire witch \[strix\] is accused of having entered \[somewhere\] through closed doors, having changed her body into spirit form, and thus, no longer in physical form, has killed the children. No judge will be stupid enough to believe this accusation, and if the accused denies it, there is no agreed evidence from someone other than \[the accuser\] that the children have been killed or harmed, and the accuser cannot prove his case other than by referring to the story in \[Ovid\'s\] Metamorphoses, she will undoubtedly receive a verdict of acquittal.
[Page 199]

If you read carefully the biography of Tarasius, written by the monk Ignatius, you will have no difficulty in understanding that this was the situation in the case of the women acquitted by George, (not Philip, as some people call him), the city governor, and that it was the cruelty and ignorance of Isaurus Leo which had put them in that position because, in his incredible stupidity, he believed that the women had changed themselves into spirit form.

Listen to what Ignatius says. \'Some poverty-stricken women had been accused of murdering infants at the breast by coming through a hole in the house \[walls\]⁵ or through closed doors, and killing the children before anyone realised it was happening. They were hauled into court by those who believed fairy stories\'-he is talking about the story of Gillo which had been made up by pagans in ancient times-\'and were unwilling to follow the teaching of Christ, our God, which is not in the least deceived by ghosts \[spectris\] and mental apparitions \[phantasmatibus\]. In the fairy stories told by the Greeks, you see, there is a woman called \"Gello\" who is said to have died before her time and to have come in various spectral forms to newly-born infants and killed them\'. (There\'s a fairy story for you! A dead woman came back and committed infanticide!) \'Having been enticed and deceived by the wicked spirit \[improbo spiritu\] of this story, those who were making a plausible job of saying these things tried to transfer this cursed crime to the women by saying that they had changed themselves into spirit form and that they were the reason \[the children\] were dying before their time\'.

You see the basis of the charge, Reader. They single out \[the notion\] that the dead Gello had killed the little children; it is discovered that many of them have been carried off suddenly; therefore, it is probable it was done by these women who got rid of their body\'s bulk, changed themselves into spirit form, and in this fashion inserted themselves through solid objects or thin cracks and murdered \[the children\]. Isn\'t the writer justified in exclaiming against such accusers in the following terms? \'What stupidity! What blindness of the eyes of the heart! If the body, which has been put together and moulded by length, depth, and breadth were dissolved into spirit form, and allowed to do this, then Christ, who is truth itself, says, \"A spirit does not have flesh and bones\", \[Luke 24.39\]. It is those who make this assertion who think it is a phantom, namely, \[those who have said\] that these women were not physical beings, but spirits, or had been endowed with this spiritual power of penetrating \[solid objects\].

Moreover Christ, who took real flesh and rightly told His disciples that a spirit does not have flesh and bones, will not be limited by any object He meets to being a figment of the \[passive\] imagination. Thinking along these lines, therefore, and making a judgement without much hesitation, George acquitted these women of the crimes.\' Ignatius is not sufficiently worldly-wise when he takes the \[words\] \'apparition\', \'spirit\', and \'appearance\' as referring to the same things, that is, a mental image or something which merely appears to be a physical object, and does not touch upon or deal with the crime committed by real vampire witches \[strigum\] at all. His proof has not force at all in combatting those women and what I have said about them, as those who understand the subject easily perceive at first glance.

In the third place come \'lamiae\' \[child-killers\], about whom the prophets Jeremiah and Isaiah say they were genuine stunts by devil spirits, or apparitions, as Apollonius of Tyana, St Jerome, and other Fathers \[of the Church\] have told us. I have dealt at length in my commentary on Seneca\'s Medea, verse 732 with these and \'striges\' \[vampire witches\], (which I think ancient authors in the Glossary call \'lestrigones\' because they eat human flesh); and so that it does not look as though I am going over old ground, I had very much rather you skipped this, Reader, than make me repeat myself. Lower Germany calls them \'Alven\' and \'Alvinnen\', and the Goths \[Swedes\] call them \'elves\'. St Jerome seems to mention them when he says that two and three month old \[babies\] are often killed by an evil spirit while they are feeding at their mother\'s breast. There was an ancient narrative or fancy to this effect, as Lucilius mentions, because in Book 20 he calls them \'lamiae who dwell on the earth\', and in Book 30, too, he attributes ravenousness to them with that very epithet, alluding in the following verses to their feeding on infants\' flesh: \'Because sharp-toothed Lamia and Pytho, Those wicked, absurd old gluttons, are coming.
[Page 200]

Twelfth kind. When this appears in the form of a goat, it is therefore called \'hairy\' by Isaiah and Moses, or zahir \[= sair, \'hairy\'\] by \[Solomon\] Jarrhi and \[David\] Kimchi; or a lion, which is why lions\' heads used to be nailed to pagan temples, \[a practice\] Arnobius makes fun of in Book 6 of his Against the Heathen; or a snake such as the one Asclepius brought to Rome; and as a hermit, on which see St Nilus, Prayer to God, chapters 102 and 103; or a night-owl, (see Vergil in the last Book of the Aeneid); or a fly, which is how it appeared in Laon in the cases of possession by the Devil, and how it usually appears to Anabaptists. This is why it is called Baalzebub, \'the lord of the fly\'. \[It also appears in the form\] of a pig, a wolf, or some other living creature of some kind. In almost all these forms it attacked St Antony, St Macarius, and all the other holy monks who lived in the desert.

A more recent example, (but you will scarcely find one like it), was recorded for posterity by \[Robert\] Gaguin in his History of the French. During the reign of Philippe le Bel, a lay-brother, (or \'convert\' as the French call them), from the Monastery of Vallis Sarnarii, by the name of Adam, was riding along the road very early in the morning with a serf-attendant a little before Christmas on monastery business when an enormous tree, covered in frost and rime, presented itself to him on the way, hurrying towards him as though to join its pace and bulk to his. His terrified horse jerked its head back and tried to go back the way it had come. With an immense effort Adam restrained the animal and spurred it forward. When it was quite close to the tree, the tree suddenly vanished from sight, leaving behind a dreadful smell of sulphur.

From this Adam deduced that the apparition was an evil spirit, and fell to prayer, commending himself to God. But he did not interrupt his journey. A little while later he looked back and saw that a black man, sitting on a chestnut-brown horse, was following him. \'How is it\', he said, \'wretched, damned \[creature\], that you dare attack me with these tricks of yours? Don\'t you know that my brethren back in the monastery are pouring out prayers and supplications to God for my safety? Go away and be quick about it. I have nothing in which you can be interested and possess nothing which belongs to you\'. The wicked \[spirit\] went away but, lo and behold, returned a moment or two later in the shape of a man with a long, slender neck. Adam, sick of being bothered so often, drew a sword which had been fastened to his saddle, and waved it in the air two or three times.

It split the air so quickly that it made a whistling sound. He tried to drive the apparition away, but it suddenly changed its appearance and stood where it was in the form of a man of middling height, wearing a hood on its head and a monk\'s scapular. Its eyes sparkled like steel or bronze caught in the sun\'s rays. Adam tried to stab his enemy several times, but his blows had no effect or force and merely parted the air. Then, in greater contempt and derision, the evil spirit took the form of an ass. The servant was terrified and persuaded his master to draw a circle on the ground with his sword and to make a cross with arms of equal length in the middle of it. Adam followed his advice, but the old fox did not stop. It changed its ass\'s ears into horns and in this shape, and in a most threatening manner, pretended it wanted to burst into the circle. Finally, it had had enough of the game and turned itself into a wheel which then rolled forward and attacked the monk. Carried by its continuous rolling, it crossed over the circle, leaving the monk completely unharmed, and disappeared from sight. The long sickness which followed his servant\'s terror, the long-lasting stench of Adam\'s clothes, the hoarseness of his voice and his difficulty in breathing from which he found it difficult to recover, assured people of the truth of what he and his servant had been through. So did the sudden change in his horse which, after this vision, \[ceased to be\] tame and tractable and started to be fierce, to throw its rider to the ground, and to kick out.
[Page 201]

Now let me add some less frequently found kinds of apparition.

The thirteenth kind \[is seen forming\] cohorts, battle-lines, and military encampments, because just as battle-lines of good angels appear twice in Holy Scripture, so also devils, who ape God, often show themselves to human beings in the form of soldiers in close array. In the Life of St Norbert, chapter 26, (and also in the life of St Guthlac the Hermit, in Sauer, under the month of April), one reads that evil spirits under the appearance of armed soldiers attacked a number of monks. In Trithemius\'s Chronicle of Hirsau, we read, under the year 1168, that a very large number of cavalry and infantry, drawn up in battle-lines as though going to fight, appeared near Worms to a monk \[called\] Limpugius. When the monk was dating enough to ask who they were, they replied they were not real, living soldiers, but the souls of soldiers, and that long since they had died in this very spot in the service of a certain prince.

Their uniforms, weapons, and horses, \[they said\], which had been tools of sin while they were alive were evidence of their punishment, now they were dead. Everything he saw round them was merely the fire in which they were being burned and he could help them with his prayers and supplications. Le Loyer scoffs at this monk because he was rash enough to believe that they were the souls of dead men, but his reasons and arguments against it are not convincing. \'Evil spirits pretend they are souls\', he says. Often, (but not always), \'they have produced a crashing din as they leave\'. So, too, can the spirits of the dead. \'Their clothes and weapons surrounded by flames were proof that they were evil spirits\'. The historical record does not say this, but the very things which looked like clothes and weapons and horses to the onlookers were, as far as \[the ghosts\] were concerned flames which were actually on fire. Secondly, they will \[actually\] have been flames. Didn\'t \[the ghosts\] say they were spirits who were still atoning \[for their sins\]? Thirdly, there is the verse by Vergil:

\'The care \[they take\] in looking after their sleek horses Is the same after the horses have been laid to rest in the earth\', \[Aeneid 6.654-655\].

This merely proves that evil spirits also imitate the decisions taken by God, whereby frequently \'a sinner is punished in accordance with the way in which he has sinned. This example, therefore, could be added to the preceding question about souls.

I certainly think that this story is the same as the one about something which happened in the same place, but in the year 1123, and on folio 281 \[of his chronicle\], the Abbot of Ursberg tells a story with the same general sense. I think the conclusion to this story is pertinent because \[in it\] people ask for a number of useless, over-inquisitive things, and not for prayers. Johannes Caesarius tells us that the night after the Duke of Brabant\'s army was slaughtered by the men of Liège, round about the beginning of the night, one of the Count of Loon\'s servants saw a very big tournament held by evil spirits near Montenaken, which was where the slaughter had taken place. A Spanish priest, too, while walking from one village to another at dusk near the Count\'s castle, saw an immense tournament of dead people screaming and shouting, \'Sir Walter of Milene! Sir Walter of Milene!\', this Walter being a well-known knight who had recently died. \[Caesarius\] adds that this vision quite often vanished that night and then appeared again.

Johannes Nider tells the following story. \'When the kingdom of Bohemia was being shaken by insurrection and slaughter on account of heresies, not far from the borders of the kingdom, next to a particular valley, people heard shouting at night and quite often saw presses of horsemen who were dressed in different colours. On the occasion I am describing, there were two rather bold knights in a castle not very far from the spot where these prodigies were happening. The knights wanted to know if there was any truth at all in these visions and hastily went at night to a boundary wall from which people had been accustomed to see them.
[Page 202]

But before they dared come near \[those visions\], one \[knight\] had pangs of doubt and said to the other man, \"We\'ve seen them. Let that be enough. I\'m not going anywhere near them. What\'s the old saying? \'One shouldn\'t make a joke of this kind of thing\". The other \[knight\] rebuked him for being timid, spurred his horse on, and man and horse drew near all the foresaid armed men. One \[horseman\] came out \[from the crowd\], cut off \[the knight\'s\] head, and rejoined his companions, and when the timid knight saw this, he fled and told everyone the dreadful things which had just happened. The headless body was discovered the next day, along with its head, in the valley where the armed men had been seen, but no trace of any human being or horse was found. However, certain signs and traces of something ill-omened were seen in the mud\'.

\[Nider\] adds that he was told this story, and another one, by the Archbishop of Mainz, a very hard-working man and a person worthy of being believed.

\'He told me\', (says \[Nider\]), \'the name of a soldier from the Rhine whom he knew very well, and whose son was alive at the time. This soldier had always been a prominent figure among almost all the nobles of Lower Germany because in warlike situations he was completely fearless. Because of his high-spiritedness and courage, he used to carry out serious raids and sometimes suffered them in his turn at the hands of other people. In pursuit of these \[raids\] he was in the habit of riding out-not always during the day, but also at night-to places where he would have the advantage. So on one particular night, with some hand-picked servants, he wanted to ride through a forest near the Rhine and entered it where it started.

But before he came to the end of the forest, beyond which lay a broad open space, just as people do when they fear an ambush, he sent a servant ahead to where the forest ended to see whether there was any ambush \[waiting for him\] on the open space, because \[such an ambush\] could be seen under the light of the moon and the twinkling of the stars. So when the servant peered through the branches of the trees and saw a quite extraordinary army strung together the length of the open space and approaching on horseback. When he had seen this, he reported the results of his inspection to the soldier who said, \"Let\'s stay where we are for the moment, because it\'s likely that others are following close behind as a rear-guard for this army.

We\'ll make a dash towards them, and we\'ll not be afraid of \[their\] small numbers, whether the advance troops turn out to be hostile or friendly\". Then, after a brief delay, the soldier and his men left the forest, came into the open space, and saw no one except a man sitting on a horse, following his people at a distance, and leading another horse. The soldier came towards him and asked him who he was, saying, \"Aren\'t you my cook?\" (because, from a distance, that is who he thought it was. The soldier\'s cook had died not long previously.)

\[The man\] answered, \"Yes, my lord.\"

The soldier said, \"What are you doing here, and who are the men who came before you did?\"

The dead man replied, \"My lord, those who preceded me are the nobles and knights such and such.\" (He gave many of them their own names, you see.) \"They and I have to be in Jerusalem tonight, because this is our punishment.\"

The soldier asked again, \"Why do you need the horse you are leading when there\'s no one sitting on it?\"
[Page 203]

\"It will be at your service\", came the reply, \"if you want to come to the Holy Land with me. Don\'t be alarmed. I shall bring you there and back alive, on my word as a Christian, if you heed my warnings.\"

Then the soldier said, \"I have attempted many extraordinary things in my time. I\'ll add this strange \[experience\] to them.\"

While his servants tried to persuade him not to, the soldier jumped down from his own horse, mounted the dead man\'s horse, and both riders were carried away from the servants\' eyes. The next day, however, the servants waited in accordance with their instructions, and the soldier and the dead man returned to the place where they had first met. Then the dead man said to the soldier, \"So that you don\'t think this vision was an entire fiction, I am giving you two unusual things. Keep them in memory of me.\" Then he produced a small piece of cloth made from asbestos and a little knife in a sheath. \"When the \[cloth\] is dirty\", he said, \"clean it with fire. When you draw the knife, do so carefully because if it cuts you, it will poison you.\" In this there are many things which indicate it was a vision of evil spiritsespecially the gift of the knife which would contrive the death of many people including the man to whom it had been given. (In Book 11 of his Annals, the very eminent \[Cesare\] Baronio provides another anecdote relating to the year 1004AD, which he has taken from a codex from Montecassino. I shall reproduce it in Book 6).

\[Marcin\] Kromer, a careful writer, says, \'The King of Poland, Vladislaus I, was besieging Naclum, a very well-fortified castle in Pomerania. The sentries there quite often saw lines, as it were of soldiers, in the moonlight, galloping towards the Polish camp and assaulting it from the open fields. This happened fairly frequently and so the Poles became angry and restless, and since the enemy nowhere got as far as open fighting, one night, when they were told the enemy was at hand at that very moment, they gave a great roar and charged out of their camp in a great mass and, in a fruitless attempt, pursued them as they fled in different directions. The besieged \[Pomeranians\] heard the noise the Poles were making and suddenly burst out from the fortress in a hostile charge. They worked quickly to spread \[fire\] round in several places while the few who had stayed behind in the fortress defended themselves, and easily set the siege-engines and a large part of the camp on fire. They claimed that the perpetrators were ghosts of the night who, under the appearance of a hostile army, attacked the Poles this way with God\'s permission, the particular reason being that they had violated the Lenten fast which should be observed by all Christians, and had rashly and heedlessly eaten flesh and food containing milk in defiance of the usages and ordinances of the Catholic Church.\'

He declares that these were apparitions of devils, just like the ones \[mentioned\] by St Antoninus \[of Florence\] in part 3 of his History. Within our grandfathers\' memory, they used to be known as the \'family of Hellequin\'. Guillaume d\'Auvergne told us some time ago that all instances of this kind were illusions of evil spirits. (See the last part of his Universe, chapter 24). Magicians could put on displays of many armies such as these if they could evoke evil spirits subject to their will because of a pact they have with a more important evil spirit, but one reads that God has never allowed such a militia to go beyond striking \[people with\] meaningless terrors, because if they were directed to do harm, to murder, to capture fortifications, or to attach an enemy\'s camp, no human power could resist them, as Guillaume sensibly \[observes\]. (In connection with this, I draw your attention to Olaus Magnus\'s \[account of\] engagements between the kings of the peoples of the North and the victories they have won in spite of troops of magicians and evil spirits drawn up ready for battle.
[Page 204]

The fourteenth kind. Evil spirits classified under this type of apparition show themselves in groves and other pleasant places in the form of girls or mature women dressed in white. Human beings sometimes find them in stables, along with wax tapers from which the wax has dripped down. Later on, they are usually found in the horses\' manes which they have very carefully combed and braided. Such is the well-known Russian widow who prowls about at midday. I mentioned her not long ago. So, too, are those called \'sibyls\', or \'white nymphs\', \'ladies of the night\', \'the good ladies\', and their queen Habundia. Superstitious old women think their arrival brings a great deal of prosperity and an abundance of everything to the houses they frequent, and so they lay out feasts for them in the house-plates of food and bottles of wine, all open and uncovered so that \[the spirits\] can start eating and drinking without having to wait.

This \[practice\] is clearly idolatrous, as the Bishop of Paris says in the book to which I referred, and so does Nicholas of Cusa in Book 2 of his sermons. Here I draw your attention to the three women who appeared to Machabaeus Bancho \[Banquo\] from whom the Stuart line \[is descended\] and, having appeared to him and his party while they were hunting, proclaimed he would be king. (\[Hector\] Boethius, History of Scotland Book 12). These \'good ladies\', whom the pagans called \'Parcae\' \[Fates\], and the feast prepared for them are mentioned in the life of Bishop St Germain and in St Antoninus \[of Florence\], History, title 2, paragraph 5, page 2. Those of this kind, too, \[called\] \'the deadly sisters\' or \'nymphs\', were wrongly thought to preside over wars and confer victories and were the familiars of King Hother. (See \[Olaus Magnus\], History of the North \[Book 2, chapter 10\]). The Italians called them \'Fates\' and the French \'fairies\'. The fictional Melusine, Alcine, and Morgana belong to this type. (I shall say more about these in Book 6, chapter 2, section 3, under \'remedy 1\'.)

Fifteenth kind. Through this kind of apparition God sometimes makes known future wars, plague, changes of kingly rule, and things such as that. I shall give a few examples. On the night before a rebellion by the people of Antioch, an enormous woman with a frightening expression on her face was seen lashing the air so forcefully with a whip that the whole city could hear the noise. A ghastly apparition appeared to Gennadius, the Patriarch of Constantinople, while he was approaching an altar in the church in order to beg God to keep the people safe, and when he addressed it in holy fashion it replied that as long as Gennadius was alive, it was not permitted to inflict the damage which was threatening the Church, but that once he was dead, it would attack it with great savagery. Before \[King\] Hunnericus initiated his persecution of Catholics in Africa, an immensely tall man was seen on the summit of Mt Ziquenus and heard to cry out, \'Go away! Go away!\' In Faustus\'s church, which is in Carthage, a large number of \'Aithiopians\' was seen chasing away the church\'s saints and driving them in front of them. (\[Girolamo\] Cardano, The Variety of Nature Book 14, and \[Battista\] Fregoso Book 1, under the heading \'Extraordinary Things\', give many other examples of this kind.)

In the sixteenth place one meets those apparitions which, at certain times and in certain places or houses, are in the habit of causing upsets and making nuisances of themselves in various ways. I shall not give examples of this kind because it is very well-known and one can read about them in both ancient and more recent authors: for example, the evil spirit Temesius in the passages of Strabo and Pausanias I have cited, and Aelian, Motley History Book 8.\[18\]; the black children with curly hair, St Augustine, City of God Book 22, chapter 8; \[the story\] about Datius, Bishop of Milan in St Gregory, Dialogues Book 3, chapter 4; the voices and noises heard in Marathon after the battle with the Persians, Pausanias in Attica \[= Description of Greece 1.32.4\], and before the battle of Leuctra in the temple of Hercules, Cicero, Divination \[1.34 (74)\] before the battle with the Cimbri, Pliny \[Inquiry into Nature\] Book 2, chapter 59 = 2.58 (148)\] before the Sullan civil war in Italy, Appian, The Civil War Book 1; in Antioch before the first battle of Pharsalus, Caesar, The Civil War Book 3. \[105\]; before the death of the dictator Caesar, in his own house, \[Cassius\] Dio, \[History 44.16\]. More recent \[examples\] are in \[Johannes\] Nider, The Ant-Hill, last Book; Guillaume d\'Auvergne, The Universe, chapter 24; \[Antonio de\] Torquemada, A Garden of Strange Flowers, dialogue 3; and \[Alessandro\] Alessandri, Festive Days Book 5, chapter 23. The practice of the Spanish courts confirms \[the existence of these spirits\]. \[Diego\] Covarrubias, following Portius, thought that a tenant who had no prior knowledge of it was permitted to leave the houses he had rented in the case of their being disturbed by apparitions. Disturbances of this kind are usually approved or permitted by God as a punishment for sinners, a moral encouragement to good people, or for some other reason hidden from us.
[Page 205]

Sometimes evil spirits carry out these things at the instigation of workers of harmful magic \[maleficorum\], as happened in the case of St Antony and a noble from Aquitaine who had robbed a learned Parisian of his right by force. Quite often they act of their own accord, simply in order to deceive human beings by means of some kind of false revelation or deceptive belief just, as, (so he tells us), they kept on dissuading St Augustine from being baptised. Sometimes they are content simply to harass and disturb without damaging a person physically, as in the case of the jesting spirit \[spiritus\] about whom Guillaume d\'Auvergne \[tells us\]-it used to disturb his sleep by clashing plates together and throwing stones, and would pull the mattress from underneath him and throw him out of bed-and Torquemada\'s \[spirit\] in Salamanca which used to let fly with big rocks at everyone, but whose blows were harmless and ineffectual.

Marcus the magician in Psellus\'s \[The Way Evil Spirits Operate\] says that this is the nature of underground evil spirits, and I shall deal with these in a moment. Scholars disagree, however, about the kind of pact which allows evil spirits to throw stones and do other things of this nature which seem to require hands and physical means \[to be able to do them\]. I agree with those who think that when an evil spirit is present in his own person in a particular place and has not assumed a \[physical body\], these things happen because he moves the air next to the stone by commanding and willing it \[to move\], and thus drives it forward. If, on the other hand, he were to create and assume a body, or assume that of a corpse, there would be no difficulty \[in his being able to throw a stone.

In seventeenth place let us put those apparitions from Borussia \[Prussia\] about which Jan Malecki writes in a letter. \'In Sarmatia they revere certain spirits one can see which are called \'coltri\' in Ruthenian \[Old Ukrainian\] \[and\] \'Kobold\' in German. They believe \[these spirits\] live in hidden places in the house, or in the wood-piles, and feed them sumptuously on all kinds of food because \[the spirits\] are in the habit of bringing them corn which they have stolen from other people\'s barns. When those spirits want to live somewhere and get fed, they make their desire known to the head of the household in the following way. They bring slivers of wood into the house at night and put various bits of animal dung in pails full of milk, and when the head of the household notices this and does not throw out the slivers of wood or the dung from the pails, but he and his whole household consume some of the defiled milk, then \[the spirits\] are said to appear to him and stay in his house\'.

People have the same idea about the apparitions of little men or little women with the short stature and the appearance of a pigmy or a dwarf which, in olden times, used to frequent many people\'s homes and attend to almost all the household chores, look after the horses, sweep the house, bring wood and water, and tasks such as these. Olaus Magnus \[writes\] about them. The French call them \'gobelins\', the Germans Rabbautermanneken\' and \'Gulkens\', and the Swedes \'trullas\'. Whether the \'Eros\' and \'Anteros\' who appeared to Iamblichus at his command in the form of small children while he was in his bath, I leave to you to think about.

Undoubtedly, \[however\], the mountain-dwellers of whom Georgius Agricola published an account are relevant at this point. \'They are found\', he says, \'in metal-mines and are undoubtedly fearsome or dreadful simply in the way they look. They cause trouble mainly in metal-mines and are unfriendly. Such was the Aunaberg evil spirit which killed more than twelve miners with its breath in a pit which is called \"Rosenkrantz\", and for this reason \[the pit\] was abandoned, even though it was extremely rich in silver. When \[the spirit\] appeared in the likeness of a horse with a long neck and savage eyes, it would discharge its breath through a mouth gaping wide. The \"Schneeberg\" was the same kind of \[evil spirit\] and wore a black cowl.
