It once lifted a miner off the ground in the Georgenthal mine and placed him, not without scraping his body raw, in a higher spot in that immense cavern which was rich in silver.
[Page 206]

A Jew in Turkey was forced to abandon a very profitable mine because of an evil spirit of the mine, which would frequently appear to people in the form of a she-goat with golden horns. Some Germans, however, like the Greeks, call those \[spirits\] which are quiet \"Kobali\" because they imitate human beings. They laugh as though they were in transports of joy, and appear to do many things even though sometimes they actually do nothing at all. Others call them \"little mountain men\", because for the most part they appear in the form of dwarfs two and a half feet tall. They look like old men and are dressed as metalminers, that is, in a vittetum, a shirt, and a piece of leather hanging round their loins. These are harmless to metal-miners, even though sometimes they exasperate miners \[by throwing\] gravel at them. But they very rarely hurt them unless they have been provoked by loud derisive laughter or cursing. They seem to work mainly in those pits in which metals are already being dug out, or there is hope that they can be dug out\'.

He describes everything correctly, although less so when he includes evil spirits in the category of \'living\' things. I know not a few ancient \[authors\] spoke this way, but I also know they imbibed this from the milk of Platonists and that they were unsure whether they were composed of a soul and a body or not. These days, because it has been satisfactorily defined for Catholics that those distinct, fully completed substances are not compounded with a soul or a body, we should avoid this way of talking, which is not circumspect enough and not accurate enough.

One must utterly condemn Paracelsus\'s error of reducing these kinds of evil spirits to types of human beings. In his Letters to the Athenians, or The Secret Philosophy, he has not been afraid constantly to assert, as though it were a perfectly reasonable axiom, that in addition to human beings, the descendants of Adam, God created four other kinds of human, endowed with flesh, bones, and reason, and put them to live in the individual elements; and that in these elements, each one of them would have his own role and provide himself with food and clothing by his own activity and endeavours. \[He says\] that those humans who live in the bowels of the earth are called \'pigmies\' and \'gnomes\'; those who live in water are called \'nymphs\' and \'undines\'; those who live in air are called \'sylphs\' and \'melusines\', and they alone have dealings with Adam\'s human posterity and give birth to their children after having sex with them; and those who live in fire are called \'vulcans\' and \'salamanders\'. So far, \[he says\], it is uncertain which kind of these humans has been given true faith in God and an open road to salvation. (\[Johann Gödelmann and others cite him as their authority for these \[ideas\]).

In a book of extracts I find that Paracelsus is represented as speaking hesitantly, but I think his Latin translator patched him up with this so that he could hide his master\'s irreligiousness, because there can be nothing more frivolous than these words of his, nothing more blasphemous, and nothing more alien from correct faith. What, therefore, will a mutilated, maimed Holy Scripture have taught us about just one kind of human being? Is our salvation therefore in doubt? Is our redemption therefore in doubt? Did the second Adam therefore not exist so that we, who died in the first, might be brought back to life in the second? If this is in doubt, don\'t the promises of the Old Testament come to nothing? Doesn\'t the message and revelation of the New \[Testament\] come to nothing? Are there really people who translate into Latin books such as these which are crammed with nonsense, lies, and blasphemies? Are there people who hammer them out and forge them and make them look beautiful? Are there really people who like being called \'Paracelsians\'? Still, let me return to the matter in hand.
[Page 207]

18\. In the final place one is presented with the apparitions of evil spirits who guard treasures. When Kavadh \[I\], King of Persia, accepted a very large treasure consisting of very precious stones, gold, and silver in the very well-fortified stronghold of Zudader which is on the boundary between India and Persia, it was being guarded by evil spirits in order to prevent all mortals from gaining access to it. When \[the King\] came with an army and tried to seize the castle by force, the evil spirits rush out and fought bravely and \[the King\] was driven back. So, he had recourse to the magic of the Jews and the Magi, but this too turned out to be fruitless. \'So\', says Kedrenos, \'he was persuaded that he could get hold of it with the help of Christians\' prayers to God, and asked the bishop of the Christians living in Persia for help. The bishop ordered the Christians to fast, pray, and take communion. He celebrated the divine mysteries, came to \[the fortress\], invoked the evil spirits who were dwelling there, drove them out, and handed the castle over to Kavadh without any problem\'. (Glykas, Annals Part 4 and Kedrenos in his biography of Anastasius.)

Such an evil spirit was the one in Basel about which I spoke earlier in question 12, and after this it came about that the guardians of treasures were said to be snakes-for example, \[those\] watching over the garden of the Hesperides and the golden fleece. After this, too, it came about that people used to add a snake made of gold to their treasures, as Ascanio Centorio says in relation to a treasure which was found near Deva, a city in Transylvania in about 1550. (See Book 4 of his Commentary on the Wars in Transylvania.)

Note, too, the story of Germanicus, a magician who went into a cave where a treasure had been hidden, while his companion stayed just out with the entrance. \[Germanicus\] saw a dog, which was entirely black, lying on the chest. Cupidity, however, drove away fear and he stepped forward a little. The earth gave way, and he was buried in the cave. (See more in question 12.) I pass over certain \[examples\] which are already well-known, such as a story the Indians tell, lying that the souls of those wives who voluntarily die at the same time as their husbands carry on being alive with them. (Pedro Cieza testified to this more than once in part 1 of his History of Peru.)

Now that we have looked at the \[various\] types of evil spirit, we could ask by what means evil spirits can be driven away and overcome, but because all wicked things done with the help of evil spirits come to a common end, I therefore thought I should discuss this point in the question about remedies against acts of harmful magic in the last Book of this work. I shall now investigate in greater detail the means whereby evil spirits are able to appear, a question St Augustine left undecided in the enchiridion \[handbook\] he addressed to Laurentius, chapter 59.

Book 2, Question 28

How can an evil spirit make itself visible to the eye of the flesh when it has no body?

It is generally agreed that he cannot do so physically unless he has assumed a body. What St Ignatius \[of Antioch\] said in his letter to the people of Smyrna is undoubtedly true-that non-physical beings cannot be seen by physical beings because non-physical beings do not have an outward appearance, or a shape, or the smallest part of any living creature which has a shape, because they are naturally constituted as something unmixed and uncompounded. It is also generally agreed that they either make a body for themselves out of the elements, or enter into and assume that of a corpse. I shall give plenty of examples of the two ways I have said one should distinguish between them, and I used the word \'physically\' because they can appear in an imagined form, once the imagination has been thrown into confusion.
[Page 208]

Book 2, Question 28, Section 1

Sometimes, then, they assume the dead body of a human being or animal and make it move. This they do, not by a motion which confers life upon it \[motu vitali\], but only by a motion restricted in its area of operation \[motu locali\], the same way they are able to stir the air. I also think that if God permits it, they can make adjustments to this body, (especially one which is not too far decayed), in such a way that they can completely deceive the sense of touch, and I see nothing out forward in argument by any learned or religious person which persuades me otherwise. Take, for example, what the Lord says in St Luke \[24.39\], where He proves He really does have a body and is not a spirit produced by the imagination: \'Handle me and see. A spirit does not have flesh and bones as you see I have\'.

Now, this does not add much to our discussion, and certainly does not move it forward. The Lord merely wished to prove they could feel Him and come to a decision by touching Him: that their eyes were not deceiving them, because He actually did have a body which was real, since it could be touched: and that it was not merely a phantom clothed with a counterfeit body. There was no question in this case of His having assumed a body. He had flesh and bones, and by this Christ proved He was not a spirit-something the disciples believed He was because they had seen Him enter through closed doors, which a body cannot do, whether it is alive or dead: and because they could recognise simply by touch that it was a body, although \[that did not tell them\] whose body it was. He told them to use their eyes and see His hands and feet-\'see\' is the correct word, as the text indicates-and from the wounds which were still there, they were quite certain that this was the man who had been crucified three days before. Hence it is clear that the Lord\'s words are true and credible, even if we were to say that the body had been assumed by an evil spirit and could be so modified by him that the sense of touch was deceived.

Well now, here is a question. Does not an evil spirit do things every day which are far more astonishing than simulating the softness of flesh, the hardness of bones, and the warmth and gentle heat and all the other qualities associated with touch which one notices when one touches a body? I say he is able to simulate those qualities. He cannot produce real ones, those genuine vital characteristics which belong to a living body and proceed from its informing soul. Simulated characteristics, and those which approximate to the real thing, do not require an informing soul and can be constructed from the contents of the mind in such a way that they appear real to the external senses. I agree entirely with those who say that even in the corpse of someone who has died so recently that one can detect it is still warm, those properties of gentle, life-giving heat and the ability to move parts of the body are missing, and a kind of rigor has taken its place.

But I deny their conclusion that therefore a malign spirit has taken over \[such\] corpses. Grant that the substance of the body be the same in the first instance as it is in the second and grant that more of the same properties be present, in as much as the heat I mentioned still remains behind. Even so, this heat is still lacking in the corpse \[the evil spirit\] has assumed, because he counterfeits the properties I mentioned as having been left behind. The way I see it, the evil spirit is inside the corpse, counterfeiting all of them. It is easier to make a skilful counterpart than it is to create the real thing. So, it would not follow that he is unable to create a body which is still warm and provide it with those properties all by himself, and that therefore an evil; spirit cannot cleverly and ingeniously counterfeit those same properties in the corpse he has assumed in such a way as to deceive \[the sense of\] touch. Why can someone who is able to deceive the principal sense, (sight, of course), not deceive touch, the most earth-bound, the lowest, and the least spiritual of the senses?
[Page 209]

Certain people, however, have observed that these assumed bodies, such as those made from air, are cold and extremely unpleasant to the touch, such as those described by \[Alessandro\] Alessandri in Festive Days Book 2, chapter 9, and \[Girolamo\] Cardano in The Variety of Nature Book 16, chapter 93, and others. (I shall discuss these later.) From this one deduces that either the evil spirit does not want to counterfeit warmth or that God does not allow him to do so. This is the meaning of what \[Cardinal\] Cajetan says-that when someone asks about this coldness and extreme unpleasantness of the body, the evil spirit replies that he cannot do better.

In other words, God is standing in his way or at least not giving him permission. If anyone wants examples of these apparitions, he will be able to read in the last part of Guillaume d\'Auvergne\'s Universe that on one occasion \[an evil spirit\] assumed the corpse of a brute beast and deceived a soldier who thought he had possessed a beautiful young woman, only to find he was lying in the embrace of an animal\'s putrefying, stinking corpse, and I have written in more than one place about the apparition of Polycritus as a ghost which ate his son.

There is an example similar to this which Phlegon of Tralles related, but he says he relied on the testimony of an eye-witness. His story runs as follows, \[although\] I shall give it in abbreviated form. Philinnion, the daughter of Damostratos and Charito, fell in love with Machates, her father\'s guest. Her parents did not take kindly to this. She died of grief and had a public funeral. Philinnion came to \[Machates\] and, after they had talked for a long time, she had sex with him more than once that evening. They had dinner together and Machates gave her an iron ring and a gilded cup.

She, in her turn, gave him a gold ring and an enchanted pectoral. Her nurse went solicitously to make sure the guest did not lack anything and saw the girl she had nursed lying in bed with him. Delighted, she told \[Philinnion\'s\] parents and they, full of astonishment, ran \[to the room\] and, on the second day, took the two of them by surprised. With a wail, they embraced their daughter who was sleeping alongside their guest. But Philinnion looked at them grimly and said, \"Father, Mother, you were cruel to your daughter, and because of your prejudice against me I have been enjoying my Machates in my father\'s house for fully three days. Whether it was over-anxiousness on your part or lack of feeling, it will stay with you and cause you great sorrow. You will grieve all over again and bury your daughter a second time\".

She finished speaking, turned very pale, and her corpse collapsed like a dead person again-or rather, it actually was dead-and her parents, overwhelmed with grief, collapsed at the same time. The people and magistrates found out about this incident and ordered that \[Philinnion\'s\] tomb be opened. They found no body there, only the iron ring and gilded cup Machates had given her. Philinnion\'s corpse was found in the bedroom, on the bed, and because it was a monstrous portent, on the advice of Hyllos, an augur, it was thrown outwith the city bounds to be devoured by birds and wild animals. Machates himself, realising he had been deceived by an apparition and unable to bear the shame, killed himself not long afterwards.\' Phlegon tells the story at greater length and Paul, the legal expert, and Origen, relying on him as their source, use him to demonstrate \[the truth of the eclipse which occurred at the time of Christ\'s death. In the story of Nicole d\'Obry, the possessed woman of Laon, too, we read that the devil Beelzebub assumed the body of a hanged man and tried to drive the exorcist away from her.
[Page 210]

Agrippa\'s villainy was not dissimilar, (see below question 29, section I shall now what Caesarius, the monk of Heisterbach, writes about the same subject in Book 12, chapter 3 of his \[Discussion of Miracles. After the body of a dead German prince had been buried for a whole year, an evil spirit got hold of it and, as though he were giving it life, exercised control over it. (This is how one should interpret \[Caesarius\] when he writes, \'the spirit quickened the \[body\] in place of its soul\'.) \[The spirit\] could not have done this by informing it or by penetrating its essential being, but only by manifesting itself \[in the body\] and imitating \[the prince\'s\] living actions through the \[corpse\], as \[Alonso\] Tostado tells us in many passages of his \[Commentary\] on Exodus, chapter 7, question 13 and what follows. Caesarius adds similar things, (which one can read in his biography of St Patrick, the Apostle of the Irish), about someone whose body a devil inhabited for many years in the place of the man\'s soul.

At length, \[the devil\] came out of him at the command of a holy man and the body fell down and was reduced to dust. In chapter 4, too, he writes that a clerk had such a pleasant voice that \[people\] thought it was a pleasure to listen to him, until a monk came along and warned them that this was not the voice of a human being but of a devil. The monk spoke earnestly and solemnly to the evil spirit, and when it went away, the corpse fell down and stank most dreadfully. Lastly, I should refer here to the many false resurrections \[recorded\] by pagan \[writers\]. It is generally agreed that in most cases witches \[sagis\] were involved, along with a devil-whether incubus or succubus-which had clothed itself in the corpse. Consequently, in this kind of human being, too, one detects the ghastly, foetid stench of a dead body.

Book 2, Question 28, Section 2

Sometimes the Devil surrounds himself with a body made from the elements, and once it has been formed by the power he has to do such a thing, he makes it one with him, just as a lifeless body capable of being moved is united to the mover who is inside it. I doubt whether he can choose his material simply from the air, and I do not think he can condense air alone to the point where he produces a solid object. But whether he can do so or not, he usually finds it quite easy to mix together parts of air, (the element he uses most), and the earthy and aqueous parts of clouds, vapours, and exhalations, with the result that he quite easily produces colours from this mixture and quite easily condenses that \[various\] parts and makes them stick together. This is Luis de Molina\'s opinion and that of the ancients, and the argument certain people use to prove that \[evil spirits\] assume a body made simply out of the element air does not constrain me since it proves merely that they make use of the most efficacious part of air. This is why other people think that this body will never be of such a kind that someone cannot recognise it is not a real body simply by touching it. But I have shattered these arguments during the course of this investigation. I have demonstrated that what Christ our Lord said can be understood differently from what \[these people\] think.

I add that in the book of Genesis \[18.2-4\] we read that angels appeared to Abraham and he washed their feet and did not recognise \[what they were from touching them. They deceived him rather than letting him know \[who they were\]. \[People\] say the Patriarch was deceived because he had made up his mind in advance that they were men. So, my question is, why is it that anyone who thinks \[the angels\] were men has not formed in advance the opinion that they were men? Was Abraham alone less perceptive than anyone else? \[Cardinal\] Cajetan himself, on whom these people rely for support, says \[the angels\] showed something like real bones and flesh, and that these were not only visible, but palpable and solid. \'Solid\' is what he says, not \'something like a flux of air\' or \'having some kind of hardness\'. But when the devil in Caesarius Book 3, chapter 6 says that the bodies assumed by evil spirits this way have no back to them, he was lying through his teeth and making fun of the girl. What I said about \[the body\'s being made\] from some mingling of elements was known by Vergil when he wrote,
[Page 211]

\'Then the goddess \[made\] a finespun shade without strength, Which looked like Aeneas, a remarkable sight to behold, And adorned it with Trojan weapons\', etc. Aeneid 10. 636-638\].

Not made of air alone, you see, but of a cloud mixture. In The Hammer of Women who Work Harmful Magic, Jakob Sprenger tells us about vapour. He says that women have been seen in the fields with their dress pulled up, moving their body about and thrusting with their haunches, and that not long afterwards an evil spirit came into view, withdrawing himself from them in the form of a black vapour or exhalation.

Magicians from Mans, Mirabellius and his associates, who were burned in Paris, said in their judicial confession that two things were necessary for an evil spirit to assume bodies-a favourable wind and a full moon, because if the wind is against him he cannot get the body to coalesce, and if there is a waning or a new moon he cannot form a body because he has only enough material for one which is very small indeed. The bigger the moon, the bigger \[the body he can make\]. \[Pierre\] Crespet agrees with this, (The Hatred of Satan, discussion 27), but to me it seems ridiculous.

As though, (provided God does not stop him), an evil spirit cannot calm or cut off a wind and made good a failure in the moon\'s influx! That is to say, the air and the clouds belong to the murex family which has the habit of following the moon\'s waxing and imitating her waning! Dionysius of Halicarnassus tells us that the Tarquinii often saw an apparition of an evil spirit in a body made of the elements, and the ghost which appeared to Athenodorus in Pliny \[the Younger\] was of this kind, whatever Gianfrancesco Pico \[della Mirandola\] says in Book 1 of his Foreknowledge of Things.

In the case of apparitions of pagans and the damned, because they are without the Faith it was not the soul of the deceased which appeared, (except by special permission from God, and in this case, what does one say about what Athenodorus saw?), but some evil spirit \[cacodaemonum\] in its place. Such, I think, are all apparitions of spirits which take part in many, different, and useless conversations with human beings, such as those mentioned by \[Girolamo\] Cardano in connection with his father, Fazio; and, quite rightly, they all make one suspect that someone is trying to foretell the future \[sortilegii\] or has a pact with an evil spirit.

In his book on the soul, \[Philipp\] Melanchthon testifies that he had arranged conversations and disputations with several apparitions. Was this a morally acceptable thing to do, unadorned inquisitiveness, or \[the result of\] a secret pact? I make no judgement at all. I leave it without further comment. Undoubtedly the apparition of the incestuous Persian, whose corpse kept on being thrown out by the earth, was of the kind we are discussing. There is a lengthy account of it by Agathias at the end of Book 2 of his Persian War. Such, too, were the sightings of pagan apparitions such as those of Castor and Pollux at the fountain of Juturna: of Lucius Domitius Ahenobarbus: of Amys, the Persian: the one which appeared to Dion of Syracuse: to Curtius Rufus: to the Emperor Pertinax on the Capitol: to the Emperor Tacitus, according to Vobiscus: to Constantius and Julian, as recounted by Ammianus \[Marcellinus\]:

to Valens, according to Zozimus who also records \[an apparition\] to Serena, Stilicho\'s wife; and Petrus Thyraeus adds other examples in addition to these in Book 1, chapter 9 of his Apparitions of Spirits. Such apparitions always vanish suddenly because \[an apparition\] constructed from an elemental substance can remove itself from one\'s eyes by making itself extremely thin all of a sudden.

At this point one must make a distinction. When \[evil spirits\] appear in a real corpse, they cannot surround the corpse with a second, living body or assume any other body than the one they are wearing \[at the time\]. Physical laws are against it because, without a genuine miracle, \[the spirits\] cannot simultaneously penetrate \[more than one\] dimension relating to size. But when they appear in an elemental body, the rule is that either they cannot assume another body or corpse at all, or that they can do so, but only with very great difficulty. (I incline to the former opinion.) They are, however, able to surround a real, living body with this elemental body, and the result is that when they are clothed this way as a human being or some other living creature, \[the body\] seems to be much bigger \[than normal\]-indeed gigantic. I see no reason to prevent their doing this, because the argument that air has the ability to expand and become less solid, or to thicken and become dense, is persuasive, which is the reason Psellus gives to explain why apparitions seem to grow and shrink in such a short space of time.
[Page 212]

I find that a third kind of apparitions of evil spirits takes place, very infrequently, through certain images or statuettes, and I shall tell you what Thomas of Cantimpré said about these in Book 2 of his Universal Good, chapter 57, paragraph 21.

\'Gunerchena is a remarkable and well-known village in Brabant. Here a young man fell in love with a young girl, but when he spoke to her parents about marrying her, they refused. It was not long before the girl fell into an acute fever, and when she grew worse everyone thought she was dead. There was much lamentation, and the bells were rung as though for someone who had died. Without delay the girl\'s young lover left the village at twilight and was making his way to another \[village\] when, as he was passing some thickets, he heard a noise like that of a woman weeping. He ran to and fro, anxiously trying to find \[the source\] of the sound and discovered the girl he thought was dead.

\"Your family is mourning you because you\'re dead\", he said to her. \"Where have you come from?\"

\"Oh, a man went ahead of me and showed me the way\", she said. The young man was astonished at this, since he could see no one else except the girl, so boldly he hurried her away and immediately hid her in a house outwith the town. From here he came back to the village and spoke to his friends, then he and his friends went to the girl\'s father who was sitting by the corpse. \[The young man\] asked him if he would let him have his daughter whom he was mourning as though she were dead.

Her father was amazed and said, \"Surely to God you are not saying to me that you will raise her from the dead and take her as your wife?\"

The young man said, \"Only if you willingly swear I can have your daughter as my wife if I give her back \[to you\] alive and cured\".

Her father agreed and confirmed his promise in front of everyone. A moment later, the young man pulled aside a linen cloth which they thought was covering \[the girl\'s body\], and found a remarkable statue, the like of which could not have been made by human hands. Those who have taken a close look at devilish statues of this kind, however, say that inside the wood appears to be rotten and that the outside is wrapped in thin skin. After this the girl was brought back and restored to her father, and a few days later, now fully recovered, she took the young man as her husband and lived, safe and sound, right up to the present day. An almost similar instance is that of a man in the territory of Flanders. His sister fell ill and people thought she had died. On the day before she was to be buried, \[her brother\] found a statue in a thicket of reeds next to the sea-shore. He took \[his sister\] back to his own house, then entered the house where his friends were mourning her as if she were dead. He drew his sword, uncovered the statue, and cut it to pieces. Everyone was frightened and cried out, \"Why would you behave with such savage barbarity towards your sister\'s corpse?\" He smiled \[and said\], \"Do I look as though I am being cruel to my sister? That is not my sister\'s body, but an image \[of her\], an illusion \[created\] by evil spirits\". So saying, he took them all with him, brought them to his own house, and showed them his sister whom he had brought back. She, too, lived right up to the present day\'.

If you ask me what I think about these \[stories\], I shall say, as Albertus Magnus and \[Thomas\] of Cantimpré did, that I have my doubts. These things could be done by an evil spirit, with God\'s permission, but my opinion is that the girl\'s suitor in the first one and the young woman in the second were in league with Satan.
[Page 213]

Book 2, Question 28, Section 3

There is a popular belief that there is always something in bodies assumed by evil spirits which strikes the eye and makes one realise that this is an apparition \[created\] by an evil spirit. I can easily believe this is true, but I think that if evil spirits are not prevented by a greater power, they can appear in whatever shape they want and present it as a complete human being, excellently and very beautifully done in every respect. Such \[a form\], perhaps, showed itself to St Albert \[of Trapani\], the Carmelite, who caused it to run away by making the sign of the cross at it. (See the narrative by Giovanni Maria \[de Poluciis\] of Compostela at the start of his biography of St Albert).

But God\'s will, which is always present and uncovers irreligiousness and deceit, usually puts a stop to \[the evil spirit\], and so mortals cannot offer ignorance as a reasonable excuse. \[God\] has not yet allowed evil spirits to make use of certain forms of living creature, such as doves, young sheep or lambs. This may be because Christ, the good, true shepherd, is the true lamb, and the Holy Spirit has been accustomed to appear in the image of a dove, and because these creatures lack guile and are not in the habit of doing harm, as \[Petrus\] Thyraeus, whom I cited earlier, tells us. An evil spirit is more willing to clothe himself in the forms of creatures in which you can quite rightly find some kind of defect. He has used the form of a male or \'billy\' goat since very ancient times, and because the goat is an Egyptian deity, Moses Maimonides thinks the Jews were commanded to offer a male goat in their sacrifices, (Guide for the Perplexed \[3.46\]).

Jupiter Ammon was once worshipped in the form of a he-goat. He was called \'Ammon\' as though this was \'Chammon\', \[derived\] from Cham, the discoverer of magic. Even today, when \[people\] make an overt pact \[with an evil spirit\], they offer him a sacrifice and he appears in the form of a man. But after the pact \[has been made\] and they are supposed to worship him, he always \[appears\] in the form of a male goat, as Jean \[del Vaulx\] from Stavelot, whom I have mentioned earlier, confessed happened in his case. This is also intimated more obscurely in Dr Edelin\'s confession in \[Nicolas\] Jacquier, \[Scourge of Heretics\], chapter 4, and is given quite open expression in the summary of the sentence passed at Avignon, which I shall quote word for word in Book 5, more or less at the end of section 15.

Sometimes \[the evil spirit\] is not seen by those who are paying him homage, as \[Jacob de\] Meyer says in his Chronicle of Flanders, under the year 1459. But when \[the evil spirit\] displays himself to be worshipped, he always does this in the form of the husband of she-goats and, moreover, in the form of one which is ugly, black, and stinking. Various reasons can be given for this, one of which, of course, is that out of respect for the incarnate Word, God does not allow him to assume a human shape, and in annotation 10 of his Pneumatology, Sébastien Michaëlis thinks that one should accept the literal sense of Apocalypse 13, verse 4-that the wicked will worship a beast, that is, the Devil in the shape of a brute beast. But although, according to St Hippolytus, St Methodius, St Irenaeus, St Aretha, and others, Antichrist himself is called \'the beast\' in that passage, it is generally agreed that this is not the literal sense. It is more accurate to say that because people once worshipped the Devil in the form of a he-goat, evil spirits are therefore called \'sairim\', that is, \'hairy\' or \'shaggy\', as St Jerome sometimes translates the word. (See Leviticus chapter 13, and Isaiah chapter 13, verse 21 and chapter 34, verse 14).
[Page 214]

In Hebrew, the word \'sair\' means \'he-goat\', as hircus does in Latin, derived from \'hairs\' and \'bristle\', which may also be the derivation of the word \'satyr\'. \[David\] Kimchi confirms this in his Book of Roots when he writes that sair is a name of the Devil because he is accustomed to present himself to people\'s sight in the likeness of a he-goat. \[Sébastien Michaëlis notes that as well. In Leviticus chapter 17, verse 7 the Septuagint carelessly translated Mataíous \[meaningless\] as though it meant fatuellos \[\'buffoons\'\]. (The Longobard word matous is derived from \[the Greek\], with the omission of \[two\] letters. The French call them folletons \[\'scatter-brained\'\]others abbreviate the word to follons-because they cause laughter by laughing, stamping and jumping while they dance, and whistling while they sing, etc. and it is generally agreed that this is what the evil spirit did in the Life of St Antony, and \[see also St Thomas \[Aquinas\], first part of the second part, question 89, article 4, objection 3. I myself prefer to translate Mataíous as \'worthless\' which looks back to \'Elelim\' \[divinities\] a word for \'idols\', because in that passage sacrifice to idols is prohibited, that is, to the evil spirits who were being worshipped under the worthless guise of idols).

Another reason \[for the Devil\'s appearance as a goat\] can be given-the lust and arrogance and other vices which evil spirits are eager to imitate in their characters and actions. (\[Nicolas\] Rémy gives many examples in Book 1, chapter 24). Finally, as we read in the Life of St Radegund Book 2, written by the nun Baudomina \[Baudovinia\], her contemporary, he clothes himself as a male or female goat because perverse individuals confer upon him the leadership of goats and he is undoubtedly worshipped for the most part under this form, as \[Robert\] Gaguin proves by the example of the scholar from the Sorbonne, Edelinus \[Adelmus\], and \[Antonio de\] Torquemada by others. Rémy thinks this has been so since the very ancient times of Theseus, and I think it can be deduced from the sacred testimonies of the prophets that it was even earlier since, as I said, they call \[evil spirits\] sairim.

When \[an evil spirit\] wants to provide a symbol of close friendship and loyalty, he takes on the likeness of a cat or a dog. (\'Cat\' is what witches \[lamiarum\] assure us they are in the confessions they make every day, and Cornelius Agrippa and Simon Magus were given dogs as their familiar spirits). If someone needs to be transported somewhere, \[the spirit\] clothes himself as a horse, as Olaus Magnus tells us in the case of Hadingus, and Peter of Cluny and Hugh de Fleury in that of the Comte de Mâcon. If he is going to come through a narrow place or a crack, or fool sentries, he turns into a bat, a small mouse, or a weasel. If there are people present who are going to hamper his conversation \[with a witch\] and he wants to escape their notice, he clothes himself as a fly and occupies the space next to her ears and whispers what he wants to say.

If he wants to frighten animals or humans and throw them into confusion, in order to be able to mutilate the flock and savage it, he clothes himself as a wolf. He appeared to Pachomius as a large, raging cockerel, (\[Simeon\] Metaphrastes in Sauer, under the month of May); to St Romuald as a raven or vulture, (see Damian\'s biography of him), but to Hilarion as a little fox. \[He appeared\] to Dunstan as a dog, (see his biography in Sauer, under the month of May); to Léonard de Corbiac as a snake; to St Margaret and to Fernández Gonzalvo, Count of Castile and his army, as a serpent. If he wants to lead someone to evil under the appearance of good, he appears in the form of Christ, as he did to St Martin, (see \[Sulpicius\] Severus\'s biography); or in the form of God, as he did to the widow Theodora; or in the form of an angel, as he did to St Juliana. Finally, he takes the form which is most suited to what he intends to do, and because the human form is the most appropriate for almost all his purposes this, for the most part, is the one he counterfeits, although it is not the same every time.
[Page 215]

An old author writes \[as follows\] in his life of Abbot St Robert. \'One night the holy man saw that something malign was standing at the entrance to the choir and was, in fact, making attempts to enter, although these attempts came to nothing. It had the form of a peasant with his tunic pulled up over long bare legs. He was carrying a little basket on his back and had a piece of wood fastened to his chest with cords. He started to walk round the choir with his neck thrust forward, casting his eyes intently on the brothers to see if he could perhaps notice something in one of them which would be pleasing to him. The \[holy\] man, however, started praying more earnestly to God and rousing the brothers from their listlessness, and the impudent spy was kept waiting for a long time to no avail. When he saw he was achieving nothing, he grew angry and took himself off to the lay-brothers\' choir. There he also observed everyone inquisitively.

If he saw they were drowsy, he laughed and rejected them; if they were occupied with harmful thoughts, he broke into applause and started dancing in an extraordinary fashion. At length, he found a young man among the novices, whose mind was wandering off towards forbidden things. He was present only in body and, indeed, having secret thoughts about running away. Noting, therefore, that this man had captured his interest, the peasant\] seized hold of him with a small three-pronged fork, thrust him into his little basket, and quickly rushed away from there. The holy man noticed this and was very anxious and concerned for the young man\'s salvation. In the morning he made diligent inquiry after him, but the youth with his wandering thoughts had fled before dawn, shaken off Christ\'s gentle yoke, and followed His enemy.

He joined a band of criminals, took to thieving, and not long afterwards was arrested and executed, ending his life in a most wretched fashion\'. An evil spirit with crisply curled hair appeared to St Robert during a wedding procession, busying about with an insolent expression on its face. (See Sauer, Volume 3, under 7th June.) It appeared to Maximus of Rhegium as a ship recently landed, with its crew; to Evagrius as an heretical clerk, and to Macarius on one occasion as a reaper, and on another as a druggist; to Nathanael on one occasion as a driver riding a donkey, and on another as his servant, Jorarius, as Marulus and Sabellicus, from among the older writers, testify. It appeared to Apelles as a beautiful, long-haired, seductive woman, but he was sensible enough to drive it away with a glittering sword which he turned on it.

But however much \[an evil spirit\] wants to present itself in the likeness of a human being, which is a privilege belonging to God, he usually does so only in the form and manner which God permits, as St \[John\] Chrysostom bears witness in homily 4 on Lazarus. Consequently, we see that either \[the spirit\] does this unwillingly, since those who are not too stupid to do so can always recognise the deceit if they pay attention, or evil spirits reveal themselves in a human body which is black, filthy, stiff with dirt, stinking, hideous, and frightful-or at least with a grim, ill-omened, horrible expression on their face. Their nostrils will either be misshapen, like those of monkeys, or excessively dark; their mouth will gape open wide; their eyes will be deep-set and the colour of flame; their hands and feet hooked and vulture-like; their arms and legs lank and very hairy; and their shins like those of a goat or a donkey. One foot, or both, will be cleft and hard as horn. Sometimes the hoof will be solid; sometimes one foot or both will be maimed. Their physical stature will never be the way it should. One will always be able to note something unusual about it, and the deformity and crookedness which reveal the evil spirit underneath. Some people prove these points by means of ancient and more recent examples, and I have already told you many of these.
[Page 216]

\[The evil spirit\] is certainly in the habit of appearing to the dying under an extremely frightening aspect as, according to Severus Sulpicius, he did to St Martin, and to a well-known Florentine to whom he appeared, pouring forth a huge flame from his mouth and sulphurous smoke from his nostrils, (see Blaise Melanesius\'s life of St Giovanni Gualberto,) and similarly to St Odilo, (according to Sauer in the biography of the saint, under ist January.), To St Opportuna he appeared raging in the likeness of a very black \'Aithiopian\', with his hair and beard dripping as though \[made\] of hot, flowing pitch, and with eyes like glowing red-hot iron, \[promising\] tortures, (Bishop Adelelinus in his biography of her in Sauer, under the month of April). An angel gave an explanation of this to St Aichadrus. It is permitted by God, \[he said\], so that the very terror of such a frightening vision can purge saintly people \[of their sins\], if there is anything left in need of purging in them. (See what Fullelus writes in his biography of Aichadrus in Sauer, under the month of September).

The whole of antiquity testifies that \[evil spirits\] could and were accustomed to speak in a body which they had assumed, and \[antiquity\] records the oracles they poured out from speaking caves, oaks, and statues. (I have dealt with these elsewhere). To this type also belong the skulls or heads of dead pagans in which evil spirits spoke, such as the head of Polycritus\'s son, about which Phlegon \[wrote\], taking his story from Hieron of Alexandria; the head of PubliusPhlegon again, from Antisthenes; the head of Gabinius, described by Pliny the Elder; the skull of a magician, about which \[Gianfrancesco\] Pico \[della Mirandola\] tells us, and that of another magician, on which see Melchior Flavinus, the Minorite.

One should be aware that Nature has rejected all actions of evil spirits which relate to the business of a living thing because, strictly speaking, they have no body and therefore should not be thought, any more than angels, as really eating or drinking a physical drink or any physical food; and to have doubts about this-as Origen seems to do in his third dialogue which deals with the human side of Christ-does not, in my opinion, accord either with natural philosophy or Catholicism, but follows the madness of more recent Rabbis. All natural philosophers know it does not accord with natural philosophy, and \[Alonso\] Tostado proves this most convincingly in question 11 of his \[commentary\] on Exodus, chapter 7. Holy Scripture confirms it is not Catholic in Tobit 12, verse 23.

That it is a rabbinical \[idea\] is clear from the Avot of \[Rabbi\] Nathan where the madman writes that evil spirits have three things in common with human beings-they eat and drink, they have sex and beget offspring, and they are subject to death. Who doesn\'t smile that these ideas are being stuffed into people today by a Calvinist to whom the Book of Tobit is not part of Holy Scripture? So as speaking is an action connected with a living being, it follows that when evil spirits are seen to speak, they are not actually speaking by means of an action connected with a living being but, as \[Nicephorus\] Gregoras notes, by producing a sound in the air similar to the human voice, which sounds like \[a voice\] to the ears of bystanders, almost the way someone skilfully expresses the sound of a human voice by means of some instrument such as a pipe or trumpet: or the way hollow valleys catch hold of a voice and throw it back very distinctly: or air-organs separate sound into various accents and tones by the numerical arrangement of \[teeth\] on a comb \[like that used to crisp the hair\].

(Please read \[Alonso\] Tostado, op.cit. chapter 7, question 15). For the most part \[evil spirits\] make use of someone\'s regional accent, as Psellus recorded. Butand you should note this, because from it you can recognise their deceit-they use a voice which grates and hisses, is difficult to understand, and is feeble and very indistinct, as though it were coming out of a large jar or through a cracked pot. Very many of the confessions made by witches \[sagarum\] say this, according to \[Nicolas\] Rémy.
[Page 217]

But when a devil appears in an assumed body, he can be seen, and when he speaks, he can be heard, only by those he wants \[to see and hear him\]. He remains hidden from everyone else, and that is in accordance with his character. If, of course, in the case of some people and not others, he causes some impediment in the instrument or object, and if he impedes the medium in such a way that it cannot bring visions to the eye or make them heard: or if he stops the images themselves: or wants to hide the people he carries off in a trance, he allows images and sounds to pass freely to and fro and abducts people\'s imagination so that they think of nothing else.

Final Section

Finally, it is in no way licit to get evil spirits to appear to us, or to bring images of the souls of the dead before us, (something they cannot actually do), and in fact \[trying to do so\] is a mortal sin. St Augustine and St Thomas \[Aquinas\] tell us that we cannot summon evil spirits as allies, and all familiar intercourse with them is strictly forbidden. The Apostle \[Paul\] is against our \'becoming allies of evil spirits\', Corinthians 10.20\]. To ask and beg something of them in friendly fashion, or to receive something likewise, is to take oneself off into the Enemy\'s camp and desert the Church of Christ. We cannot compel \[evil spirits\] by giving them a command and making them obey it, unless we have to some extent been delegated by God to do this by \[holding\] the office of exorcist, during the exercise of which we are making use of God\'s authority. Finally, even if it were allowed, it would be a stupid thing to do because one can expect nothing good or advantageous from an evil spirit. He confers nothing good at all on human beings, unless \[the good\] is mingled with a greater evil. He tells us nothing unless in answer to mere inquisitiveness and worthless \[questions\]. He does not obey unless it is so that he can destroy and bring under his dreadful despotic rule those who have been swept away by everlasting ruin.

As a result of these apparitions of evil spirits, someone could raise the question, why did they appear in visible form so frequently to our ancestors, but do not do so to us these days in similar fashion? St John of Bridlington once asked St William of Sleightholme this question, and St William replied, \'Our old ancestors\' minds were not illumined by deceptive splendour and worthless glory, but by divine grace, and so they easily saw through the lying tricks and machinations of the Enemy.

Like undefeated boxers they fought him bravely. The arrogant \[spirit\] did not put up with this, of course, and began to throw in their way temptations which were very difficult to struggle against, and present them with monstrous shapes, and make really appalling attacks on them, in order to thrust them into sin by force. But us, who easily obey him, he easily floors with the darts of secret temptations, even if he does not show us any loathsome image\'. (Thus far, a writer of that time, in Sauer, under 10th October). I add that God permitted it at that time because there was less inquisitiveness in those days and people were in less danger of being deluded.

Book 2, Question 29

Can the Devil really make a human being rise from the dead?

In \[Diogenes\] Laertius, \[Lives of the Eminent Philosophers\] Book 8, Empedocles and the rest of the magicians concede that there are certain herbs with the power to bring a dead person back to life. The historian Xanthus says that one Tillo and a snake were brought back to life by the herb Balis, and Juba tells us that someone was resuscitated by an Arabian herb. The arch-magician of our time \[Agrippa\], however, says that an evil spirit cannot do this in the case of a human being, \[although\] he can in the case of any other living creature. Drowned flies can be brought back to life naturally if they are put in warm ashes, and so can bees if they are sprinkled with catnip juice. Eels which have died because of a lack of water \[revive\] if they are thrown whole into vinegar which has vulture\'s blood mixed in with it and are then stored away under thyme. Within a few days they all come back to life. The pelican calls its chicks back to life with its own blood; a dead lion cub, too, is resuscitated by its parent\'s voice and breath; and a weasel can do the same for its pups. (I know many eminent ancient authors are in agreement about the pelican and the lion). Indians call a small bird which has been resuscitated \'vicicilinus\' because they think it dies in the winter and comes back to life in spring, (see Francisco Gomara, History of Mexico, chapter 23), but their mistake is pardonable.
[Page 218]

\(1\) I think that living creatures which are born from putrefying matter can, once they are dead, be revived by an evil spirit using natural remedies. This is because he knows the power contained in the heat produced by putrefaction, and everything else which is necessary for his purpose. One should note, however, that this living creature will not be exactly the same \[as it was before\] because, although its basic matter can still be the same, provided it has not yet decayed, the soul which will be produced afresh by \[the spirit\'s\] power will not be exactly the same as the one \[the creature\] had before it perished. God alone has the prerogative of being able to reproduce exactly something which has perished, and I think this opinion-which is that of everyone except \[Duns\] Scotus, who does not agree-is fairly accurate. Scotus and Richardus \[Rufus of Cornwall\] think that this can also sometimes happen through natural causes. On the other hand, Durandus \[of Saint-Pourçain and Marsilius \[of Padua\] think that God cannot do this.

More recent writers refute Scotus, and quite rightly so since, according to Aristotle, Natural Philosophy Book 5 and The Generation of Living Things 2, and St Dionysius, The Names of God, chapter 6, one cannot come back from a state of not-having to a state of having. Ambrose, Lactantius, and other Fathers \[of the Church\] always speak about this in terms of its being a genuine miracle. \[Domingo de\] Soto, \[Fausto\] Socini, and Francisco Suárez, and others I have cited, rebut Durandus at such length and so forcefully that anyone who still tries to defend him certainly cannot escape the reputation of being obstinate and having ill-considered opinions.

Hence, I further deduce that, since resurrection requires that what is rising again be exactly the same as it was before both in regard to their form and their matter, and at the very least their specific combination, only a human being can properly be said to rise again since, because he is immortal, only a human being\'s form remains the same. If any other living creatures are brought back to life, it is incorrect to say they have been resuscitated. Therefore, (to put it simply), Satan will not even be able to restore insects to life.

\(2\) I think \[an evil spirit\] cannot, in the way of Nature, restore to life more perfect living things and things not born from putrefaction because \[creatures\] which are more perfect require particular arrangements \[of their basic matter and a rather careful preparation of their individual organs, since these cannot be created except by the power naturally inherent in semen. This is acknowledged, in the Life of St Silvester by Simeon Metaphrastes, by Zambre, a Jewish magician, who had killed a bull by whispering words in its ear and could not bring it back to life again except with God\'s \[assistance\]. So if it happens that such creatures have sometimes been seen to revive, they have not been entirely dead, but simply looked as though they were, as in the case of living things which lie motionless for part of the year and seem to be dead, and then rouse themselves once spring arrives and appear to have come back to life-dormice, for example, and bears, and the vituline sparrow, (i.e. the \'vicecilinus\' in Mexico), as \[Girolamo\] Cardano tells us.

\(3\) I think an evil spirit cannot make the soul of one brute beast animate the body of another. This is because individual souls of this kind, produced from the potentiality inherent in matter, die if you separate them from their own \[basic\] matter. But what happens in the case of humans?

\(4\) I think that an evil spirit has very little or no power to effect \[such a thing\]. My explanation is this. He cannot make a person rise from the dead, nor can he make a human being\'s soul enter its body and bring it to life and inform it.
[Page 219]

This part of my conclusion has to be taken on trust. It rests upon natural philosophy which explains that nothing can return to its natural condition after it has suffered a complete, total deprivation \[of life\]. Therefore, if such a thing happens it is a miracle and comes from God alone. The evil spirit cannot inform that body, because he himself is a perfect, complete substance, and putting together another substance as a part \[of something else\] is incompatible with his \[capabilities\]. I prove that an evil spirit cannot reintroduce to the body a soul which has been separated from it so that \[the body\] can inform the soul, because there is only one natural way of joining a soul and a body, and that is by begetting and creating the body and pouring a soul into it. In the case we are discussing, however, the body is not begotten and therefore in this case the natural way of joining \[soul and body\] does not exist; and the supernatural way of the kind which takes place in a resurrection belongs to God alone. If God were to give His permission, an evil spirit could force a damned soul to enter a body in order to give it movement and let people see it performing some actions, because he himself can enter it and could, by means of torture, compel an unwilling soul to do this.

Magicians, too, with the help of evil spirits of a higher rank, (because of their mutual pact), can compel evil spirits of lower rank to enter a corpse, wear it, move it, and do everything else for the time being which make it seem to be alive. Cornelius Agrippa did this in Leuven. He used to have an inquisitive member of his household and on one occasion, when Agrippa went on a journey and left the keys of his library with his wife, (whom he later divorced), with the instruction that she was not to let anyone enter it, this ill-considered young man kept badgering the poor woman in and out of season until he succeeded in getting from her the means of entering \[the library\]. He went in, came across a small book of invocations, and read it.

Well now, lo and behold he was disturbed by a knocking at the door! He continued reading. Someone knocked again. The young man, who was inexperienced in these things, did not answer at all and an evil spirit came in. \'Why have you summoned me?\' it asked. \'What are your instructions?\' Fear stopped the young man from speaking. The evil spirit blocked his throat, and thus the wretched \[youth\] paid the price of his wicked curiosity. Meanwhile the arch-magician returned to the house and saw evil spirits jumping about all over it. He made use of his customary magic, and they answered his summons and told him what had happened. He ordered them to enter the corpse of a murderer and go for a walk every so often in the market-place, (where the rest of his students were accustomed to meet quite frequently), and leave the corpse there. The body walked there three or four times and then fell down as the \[spirit\] which was directing it left it. For a long time, people believed the corpse fell down because \[the man\] had died all of a sudden, but the signs of suffocation started to give rise to suspicion, and later on time revealed everything and Agrippa fled to Lotharingia and began to spew out the heresies had had been concealing.

Book 2, Question 29, Section 2

We are presented with many stories about those resuscitated by the power of Aesculapius-Tyndareus Glaucus, Admetus, and so forth-not only by pagan poets, but also by weightier writers. Plato tells a story about Er, Pliny about Gabienus and others, Apuleius about Zaclas the Egyptian, Philostratus (Book 4) about those resuscitated by Apollonius \[of Tyana\], Clemens and Hegesippus about those resuscitated by Simon Magus, and Prochorus, in his apocryphal \[Acts of John\] about others \[resuscitated\] by the magician Numadius. There is an extraordinary story in Plutarch who tells us that Enarchus was left for dead by his doctors and was seen not long afterwards to come back to life. He said he really had risen again from the dead and that the evil spirits which had taken him away from life had been severely reprimanded by their prince because they had carried him off instead of Nicandas, a tanner, who had been seized by fever the same day and hour while lying in bed and had died of his illness.
[Page 220]

Not content with this, in order to prove he had come back from the dead, he predicted to Plutarch, who was not well at the time, that he would soon be fully recovered-and that is how things turned out. Ranulf \[Higden\], the author of Polychronicon, tells us that one night Earl Richard went into a church all by himself to offer a prayer, and there found a bier on which a corpse had been laid out. \'All of a sudden, while the Earl was praying, the dead man jumped off the bier with a loud cry and came towards the Earl with his arms outstretched, as though to knock him down in his embrace. The Earl made the sign of the cross on his own forehead and somewhat forcefully adjured \[the dead man\] to go away. This he did not do, so \[the Earl\] drew his sword and cut him down, and thereafter gave instructions that watch was to be kept over corpses throughout the whole region\'.

More extraordinary than either of these \[stories\] is one told by \[Robert\] Gaguin in his description of Muscovy, and I shall now add it \[to my account\]. \'Certain people living in Lucomorye, a province of Russia\', he writes, \'die every year on 27th November, as swallows and frogs are in the habit of doing, before the cold of winter sets in. Then, when spring returns, on 24th April, they come back to life again\'. He adds, \'When these people sense that death is near, they put goods they want to sell in certain places and their neighbours, the Grustintzi and Serponovtzi, take them away, leaving behind in the same spot payment of equivalent value. When the people of Lucomorye come back to life, they take this payment if they think it fair. If they do not, they ask for their goods back. It is said that this is why quarrels and wars very frequently arise between them\'. Sigismund \[von Herberstein\] tells the same story, but \[Cesare\] Baronio thinks it is a fairytale, (see his book on Muscovy, folio 86).

Consequently, I must briefly reply to all these and similar examples by saying that evil spirits usually \[create\] many illusions in connection with this kind of \[phenomenon\]. Sometimes \[a spirit\] steals the bodies of the dead and substitutes others made from air which move as though they belonged to living people. We know from Livy that \[a spirit\] removed Romulus\'s body this way, and we know \[about\] Cleomedes\'s \[body\], and that of Alcmene which \[spirits\] fashioned and gave to Rhadamanthus as his wife. We also know from Plutarch and Herodotus about Aristeas of Proconnesus, and from Antoninus Liberalis about the virgins Aspalis and Cresylla. Likewise, \[a spirit\] substituted other people for Glaucus, Tyndareus, Er, and others, or her himself secretly entered their skins.

Sometimes, in order to make things which are actually corpses come to life, he secretly goes into them and, like a sailor making a ship move, he makes them move and forces them to act as though they were alive and imitate the gestures \[of living people\]. Now, because people raise objections, at this point I must refer you to what was done by Simon Magus, to what happened in the case of Earl Richard, and to what happened in the case of Er of Pamphylia or Armenia, (unless that instance was actually made up by Plato in order to frighten people away from wrong-doing by making them afraid of the underworld, which is what \[pseudo-\]Justin Martyr thought in his Exhortation).

There are also the cases of Gabinius, Polycritus, and Publius whose heads predicted the future, and the example from Apuleius about Socrates whose head was cut off by a female inn-keeper who was also an aged sorceress \[veratrix\]. Having cut it off, she joined it to the body again by sticking a sponge \[under the cut\]; but next day, when the unfortunate man, contrary to her instruction, drank from a river, the sponge fell out and he lost his head because it really had been cut off and its restoration was counterfeit, \[Metamorphoses 1.16-18\]. Relevant here is the story I told about Agrippa\'s boarder, three others which \[Nicolas\] Rémy tells in Book 2, chapter 1 of The Worship of Evil Spirits, and one told by Philostratus in Heroic Deeds dialogue 1. \[18\] about Phrygian shepherds.
[Page 221]

When their cattle were being killed by murrain, they gathered at the tomb of Ajax, son of Telamon, in Sigeum, and heaped various curses on the dead man. One \[shepherd\], more forward than the rest, began to recite verses from Homer in which Ajax is described as running away-\'Aïas δ\' oùxéti µíuvev\' \[Ajax stayed no longer, Iliad 14.727\]-and a voice burst fiercely from the tomb, \"AMou \"piliov\' \[But I did stay\], and the shepherds, thoroughly frightened, took to their heels. The origin of the voice, you see, was an evil spirit. Finally, also relevant here is the story about a German youth which I shall tell in the next question, which would be remarkable but for the fact that he pretended to be killed, even though he had not been killed, but had simply fainted away and then thought he was decapitated. Everyone else thought the same, since their eyes had been blinded, but his head had not been cut off, nor had it been restored. It was all a conjuring-trick.

At this point, let me add one example of a fictitious resurrection. Antiquity had produced no story more convincing or more wonderful than this one. It took place in Dalheim in Lotharingia in 1581, and involved Petronius, a herdsman, and Abrahel, a succuba. In his summary of Book 2, that remarkable man, Nicolas Rémy, had shown most picturesquely and in very elegant verse, how Abrahel seduced Petronius into criminal embraces, then into the murder of his own small son, and finally, because of his grief and despair and a false resurrection of his child, into idolatry: and how the evil spirit presented him with a likeness of the living \[child\] for a whole year and then abandoned it as a corpse. I shall transcribe this with greater pleasure than equanimity because, as the result of the insupportably bad practice of its recent printer, the verses in \[Rémy\'s\] Worship of Evil Spirits have been removed and the work published in mutilated form. \[Thus\] they will be preserved unharmed, and in consequence will be read in more places.

\'There is a country in the middle of the bounds of an enormous kingdom Between the winding streams of the sandy Moselle And the straight courses of the Saar with its undisturbed river-bank. There is a village here called Delheim-not a well-known nameAnd while a herdsman was grazing his flock in a distant valley there And pondering the various cares which were rolling around in his heart, Perhaps sick of the unfairness attendant on his straitened circumstances, Or contemplating, with a degree of passion, revenge and anger Because of a loss he had suffered, or high words exchanged in a quarrel, A succuba presented herself quite unexpectedly.

She was most seductive. Her face had a maidenly expression, her gestures were charming, Her head was decked with false sapphires, And \[her hair\] was twisted into glistening curls on each side \[of her face\]. She comforted the unhappy man and gave him hope of becoming master of his situation, And promised him the wealth which the needy have so often prayed for in vain, Or which the long-eared king \[Midas\] was willing to demand of Iacchus \[Dionysus\] As long as he did not object to what he was saying. Just as when a fire which is quickened by a pair of bellows Licks the iron while the puffing renders \[the metal\] ductile, So that the hard \[substance\] is persuaded to be compliant And is prepared \[to receive\] all its instructions, And does not disregard the arts of persuasion: \[The herdsman\] is happy to submit to \[the succuba\'s\] alluring command, And on both sides trust is strengthened by a \[mutual\] pact. He made his way into her dreadful embraces. Their unequal love was cemented by senseless coition. Irreparable, freedom Died at once amid those unspeakable passions. And, just as the parts of a body wounded by the dangerous bite of snakes Spread the consequent poison throughout the whole body, So that health itself cannot provide what health requires, Even if it spreads itself as far as it can, So Abrahel took over her lover entirely.
[Page 222]

And broke him by filthiness-this very wicked woman had designated it thus so that she could bend the vanquished man to any of her stipulations, and he could have absolutely no will \[of his own\] to say yes or no. Straight away and without hesitation she demanded an initial recompense for her violated virtue. \'Play your part\', she said, \'so that your love May be the equal of mine\'. There could be no greater proof of this Than if he conquered Nature and carried out a crime Any by this fresh deed subordinated a father\'s love to his passion. Therefore, since it was already late, he drove his whole flock Into the sheep-folds and finally settled himself in his little house.

First, so that his dear son would put his weak arms round his neck To support himself \[to receive his father\'s\] kisses, he drew out of his bagWhich his son was keen to search, although he had often found it empty When \[his father\] came back from the countryside-an apple and held it out to him. \[The boy\] had scarcely taken his first bite, grinding his teeth because he did not like the sour taste, Than he turned pale and sucked it hesitatingly with the tips of his lips, And lay stretched out on the ground which fell away behind him. If \[his father\] could look at this with equanimity While carrying out his orders with this pledge, It would be strong evidence that his was still cleaving to his new love.

Such an unspeakable crime horrified him to begin with And Nature and passion fought each other in a battle whose outcome was uncertain. But he who introduced a powerful enemy into an empty citadel Hesitated in vain to carry out his instructions, because if he did nothing in response to her first command when she entreated him seductively, The insolent woman immediately passed to violent anger And her will, ever present, was like statute law to him. Now the evening which was obliged to be privy to so great a crime Had brought back to the threshold of his cottage the father entangled in these dreadful things. When his son met him and spontaneously greeted him with a big hug as usual, He groaned, and shifted from foot to foot, and stammered. Yet this did not stop him at all from \[fulfilling\] his wicked promises.

The cruel man Dared to continue to reach for the deadly gifts of murder. Let his son be the victim of an abominable love! His father did not shrink from slaughtering him with his own hand. Straight away Nature, which had allowed herself to be concealed And had withdrawn because of his feigned excuses, came out more ardently and rose up powerfully with grief; And while \[this grief\] fed on the feeble advantages \[he had wanted to gain\] And his ill-considered weeping, the woman stood nearby to give him her advice. The moment he killed \[the boy\], she consoled him And, at the same time, promised to restore \[the boy\] to life and make him live again, If \[the father\] would willingly worship her and, as a suppliant, beg this of her; And he, who had \[morally\] bankrupted himself earlier, Readily agreed to this turn of events, for which he would pay an extremely heavy rate of interest.

And while the fickle man wanted to appease, in any way he could, The gods of his household whom he had dishonoured by the foul murder of his son, He disgraced them even more and polluted them more repulsively Because first of all he threw himself on his knees before the evil spirit And worshipped it forthwith as a divinity. Then he promised the boy to her as payment, If only, with her help, his last-born child could feed himself upon life-giving air once more, \[Begging her\] just as when winds join battle and cast themselves An angry sea, and a sailor trembles in every limb quite uncertain of his life, and is lavish in his pleas to all the gods, And tires them out with his prayers.
[Page 223]

The corpse lay on its back on the mound where it had been thrown When life had finally fled and abandoned it, And its mother, very late in the day, began to make haste to bury it. But look! \[The child\'s\] body seemed to stir itself between her hands as she touched him And gradually once again to overcome \[death\] with \[living\] heat. The kind of light those who are half-alive, half-dead show in their downcast eyes, Or which, with difficulty, releases those who have been plunged deep in sleepThese signs of health restored, beyond what people hope, Seize them and give them joy, and with as much eagerness as they can The vigorously warm the powerless limbs And recall now the arms, now the legs, to their living functions.

The boy rose to his feet straight away, then Took a step, can called to his parents in the voice they knew well; Only he was different in appearance from \[the boy\] he had once been, Because he had a face \[rendered\] ugly because the skin was drawn tight over it, And because a gloomy stupidity was in possession of his receding eyes. A foreign life, therefore, quickened the senses which had been deserted And in this apparition restored the breath which had once been there, (As Virbius once obtained the light \[of life\] which had been cut off from him). But the mind was not the same, and the breath Which used to warm his life did not now exercise that same office. An evil spirit provided the mechanism to drive the sluggish bulk, Give it the appearance of life, and imitate the actions of \[human\] behaviour, Breathing \[into it the actions\] which Nature used to carry out for herself. At length, the moment a year had run its whole course And completed its circuit of the dense globe, \[The evil spirit\] abandoned its stopping-place and left the dwelling empty.

It betrayed its flight by a sulphurous smell and a noisy din, And pushed itself out, bursting forth into stinking gusts of air. Then the corpse fell down, thrust away by this second death. There followed a foetid stench and \[the body became\] very heavy. A kind of numbness \[caused by\] unhindered emotion covered the motionless \[child\] Completely with earth, and cut off the widespread strength of the stench So that it could not gain entrance to human nostrils. \[The body\] was carried away from the house by a hook firmly attached to it, And the dishonoured remains were laid under a clean sod cut from the earth, While the father, who had been mad enough to shame Nature this way With his disgraceful act, shuddered at what he had done\'.

Finally, (1) \[The evil spirit\] fashions dead people who are not actually dead, and for this purpose makes use of the not infrequent convenience of natural illnesses known as ¿φóvous \[undead\], as in the case of the apoplectic, the planet struck, or the thunder-struck. These people\'s brain is very badly impaired at the point where all the nerves begin, and this damages feeling and movement in every part of the body; and so, because feeling and movement are impaired this way, \[the people\] fall down and lie as though they were dead.

\(2\) If their xapâ \[brains\] are attacked in the case of the head\'s being beaten, as it were, by a debilitating fever, the humours injure the brain in such a way that the consequence is a stupor and immobility of the whole body; and when deep sleep accompanies this, the human lies with closed eyes, looking like a dead person, sometimes for a very long time, as though he \[actually\] were dead.

\(3\) In the case of those who are afflicted by \[swooning\], that is, a syncope or collapse of the soul, this illness sometimes adds to \[the symptoms\] I have mentioned a disturbance of the artery and a lack of pulse. This affliction makes the human being very much like someone who really is dead and lasts until the excess and attack of cold humours is dissipated. According to \[Paolo\] Giovio, John Duns Scotus suffered from this kind of illness and was buried alive as though he were dead. I should think the Euarchus mentioned by Plutarch was suffering from an illness of this sort, and that the prediction which came from human foresight and medical judgement could not have had any importance.
[Page 224]

\(4\) In the case of women who are putting up with suffocation of the matrix and suffering from jaundice, sometimes what they are suffering makes them lie as if they were dead. Such, we understand from Philostratus, and Eusebius\'s book against Hierocles, was the girl \[resurrected\] by Apollonius \[of Tyana\], and such, too, the girl \[resurrected\] by Heraclides \[of Pontus\]. In the case of Aviola and Tubero, the power of the fires broke up the cold humours of the syncope.

With regard to the inhabitants of Lucomorye, I should perhaps be correct in replying that the author himself tells it simply as a story he has heard from other people, and therefore keeps repeating the phrase \'it is said\'. But if the story he tells is true, it should be put down to conjuring-tricks by evil spirits. \[Such tricks\] are very common in that part of the world, as lycanthropy among the Nervi makes clear, and meanwhile one must presume that the inhabitants have been numbed by sleep after the usual manner of dormice and bears.

From those deceptions by an evil spirit has arisen a ridiculous conviction held by witches \[strigum\]. They believe that sometimes the oxen or rams they have killed, boiled or roasted, and then eaten are instantly brought back to life later on by an evil spirit, and it is not difficult to account for the various ways in which an evil spirit is able to do this.

\(1\) He can do it by binding up the eyes of onlookers and then, of course, sending \[pictures\] into the organ by means of a humour or some other way in which they can be imprinted, or by agitating the \[physical\] spirits in the imagination and bringing them in this state to the common sense and the organs of the external senses to which images and semblances which have been impressed on them by agitation \[of the physical spirits\] and by the object of the senseimpression normally descend. These semblances are usually retained there, but the very process of recognition often influences them in such a way that the external sense thinks it has been equally affected as though it really were being changed by the external object. It is the kind of thing which usually happens to a sleeper in his dreams, but the Devil can also make it happen to people while they are awake, because physical organs obey non-physical substances as far as local motion is concerned. (This is his method according to St Thomas \[Aquinas\]. Read his Evil, question 16, article 11).

\(2\) He can do this by substituting one thing for another, because what prevents an evil spirit from removing the dead ox, along with its skin and bones, and putting in its place a live OX which looks like the dead one, and doing this so quickly that the speed of the exchange escapes the notice of the bystanders? In the meantime, while he is taking away the corpse and replacing it with another ox, by inserting a cloud or \[performing\] some other conjuring-trick he can make sure the bystanders do not see that the place is empty and the OX has been taken away.

\(3\) Several witches \[nonnulli striges as \[Bartolommeo\] Spina says, confess that he can bring it about that when the king or queen of the journey or meeting⁵⁵² takes a stick and strikes the hide in which the bones have been collected and put in a pile. Then some evil spirit immediately gets underneath the skin, joins things together and assembles them, fits them into the shape of an ox, makes the large bulk stand upright, and then takes control of it so that it looks as though the original OX has been resuscitated.

\(4\) He can form a complete ox\'s body from air, with a new hide and new bones; or he can put together another aery body along with the bones left behind \[by the first ox\] and attach it to the rest of the hide and, when the time comes for him to do so, he can keep it intact under the hide, and make it move and bellow. Then later on, when he has to, he withdraws from it, and it will look as though the OX has collapsed and died. \'This\', says Spina, \'is why witches \[strigibus\], as a group, maintain that after such oxen, (which they believe have been resuscitated), are brought back to their masters\' byres, they die within three days, by command of the Lord or Mistress of the transvection, as though some wasting disease were shrivelling them up entirely in a matter of hours, and that after quivering for a short while, what seemed to be flesh quickly breaks up into the material it was before\', (in other words, air). (See Bartolommeo Spina, Inquiry into Witches, chapter 7.)
[Page 225]

But, (to return to the illnesses I was talking about), the miraculous resuscitations of Christ our Lord and of saintly men are easily distinguished from the cessations of those illnesses because, according to the evidence of the Faith, they were accomplished by a simple command or by a prayer poured out to God, not by an intervening period of time or delay. The body had been dissolved by putrefaction, too, and was stinking, as happened in the case of Lazarus.
