But in the illnesses we are discussing, the body continues to exist without decay and stench, and whenever the body has been restored \[to life\] one can easily see that it has been mangled and has fallen to pieces, sometimes after a very long time, to the truth of which St Maurilius in the case of a child dead for seven years and St Stanislaus in the case of someone called Petricus \[who had been dead for\] three years, testified in the case of the Church against Boleslaus. (See more about this in Miguel Medina, Correct Faith in God Book 2, chapter 7).

Book 2, Question 30

What power does magic have over the evil spirits \[cacodaemones\] themselves?

Books on the subject, written by some of the ancients, have perished. Battiades had written one about familiar spirits and Campester one about people possessed by spirits and spirits of the underworld. (They are cited by Fulgentius in his Moral Exposition of the Content of Vergil according to Philosophers). So, we have to look for help in other \[authors\], especially that touchstone of truth, sacred theology, and in proven histories. I think it is evident to any Catholic that magicians have no power at all over good angels or the souls of the blessed, and that they have no power at all over wicked \[angels\], either, unless they have made a pact with them. Therefore, evil spirits pretend that magicians are compelling them, and \[pretend\] to obey them of their own accord, in order to trap more people and keep hold of them once they have been ensnared. This now needs to be explained a little more clearly.

If you ask about good spirits or angels, I think they naturally have some power to compel evil \[spirits\], or at least to restrain them, but not indiscriminately. They can do this naturally in the case of evil spirits of inferior rank and order, but they cannot exercise this power unless God tells or allows them to do so. In the case of evil spirits of an equal order or rank, however, they have no natural power to compel them, but simply receive is sometimes by God\'s special concession and, so to speak, delegation. The same must be said about the power of evil spirits over evil spirits.

(I find that these are divided into diviners, that is, fortune-tellers: familiars, that is, personal servants or accessories: possessors, that is, those which afflict terribly those whom they besiege and dash to the ground-such was the one the Evangelist talks about-and paraders of dreams\', as Irenaeus calls them more than once, that is, removers of bad dreams or displayers of good ones). All these types pretend to obey magicians, and they all have something in common-some are of higher rank, some of lower. But whereas good angels willingly and eagerly obey their superiors, evil spirits, on the contrary, do not obey their superiors, just as vicious slaves \[do not obey\] harsh masters or a weaker \[person does not obey\] a more powerful tyrant except unwillingly and because they have been tamed by evil.

Consequently, it is also reasonable to say that among evil spirits, the subordination of one rank from which they fell to another is certainly not genuine and not one which is natural to them. The reason is that it lacks the formal structure and perfection of an orderly chain of command which is arranged, via stages of subordination, for one ultimate purpose. In this sense, Job was perfectly accurate when he said that no order is found in Hell. There is only a kind of imperfect or, (as they call it), \'material\' distinction of orders left there, which will last until the end of the world. In this sense theologians, including Cassian, recognise the hierarchy of evil spirits which the Apostle unambiguously confirms in his \[Epistle\] to the Ephesians, last chapter, verse 12, \'Against principalities and powers\', where you see him name two large groups while he is dealing with evil spirits.
[Page 226]

From this natural strength and pre-eminence among the order of evil spirits is also born the strength and power of certain magicians over others, whereby they either impede what the other \[magicians\] are doing or, in their turn, (with God\'s permission), badly injure or kill them by means of their own magic. This strife between them is, as it were, a struggle between the evil spirits with whom the contending magicians are allied by a pact. I told the story of one struggle in Burgundy in Book One.

Here is a second I came across when members of the Faith told me about it. Two magicians came together at the court of the English queen in order to put on an entertaining show and made a remarkably friendly agreement to obey each other in one \[of their tricks\]. The first one told the other to look down from a window, but when he did this, all of a sudden stag\'s horns sprouted from his head and for a long time everyone wanted to pull faces and mock him. Consequently, rendered powerless by the insult and wanting to avenge himself in a more savage fashion, \[the second magician\] drew a human figure on a wall with charcoal, then ordered the first magician to go up to the figure. As he was about to do so, he noticed it was devising instant death for him and, filled with horror, he warded it off with prayer.

The other \[magician\] reminded him forcefully of their agreement and so, under this compulsion, \[the magician\] went up to \[the figure\]. The wall seemed to make room for his body as he approached, but once he had come right up to \[the wall\], he was never seen again anywhere afterwards. In other words, an evil spirit of higher rank had killed him and taken his body away to an uninhabited place or a cave somewhere else.

I shall transcribe a third \[example\] in the words of C, a German, who tells the story as something he heard from someone else. \'In N\', he says, \'an illusionist \[praestigiator\] who came from a good family decapitated their host\'s servant, in a public guest-house, at the request of the other guests. But when he wanted to put the man\'s head back on again, he noticed there was another magician in the room who was stopping him from carrying out his intention. \[The illusionist\] asked him not to do that, but when \[the magician\] would not comply after being asked more than once, the illusionist made a lily grow on the table, and when he cut off its head and flowers, the magician who had been impeding him immediately fell \[from his seat at\] the table minus his head; and straight away, without hindrance, the illusionist put the magician\'s head on the servant and then fled the guest-house and the city to avoid being arrested by the magistrate on a charge of murder.⁵⁵ In this case, the illusion lay in cutting off the first head and replacing it, and also in producing the lily and cutting its head off. The context took place between the magicians\' evil spirits, the one of lower rank being strong enough to hinder the power of the illusion but, compelled by the more powerful \[spirit\]-not unwillingly, in my opinion-to comply with the murder of his client, who was of very little importance to him and who really was killed.

A fourth \[example\] concerns Zyto of Bohemia, a well-known illusionist, who was engaged in a contest with Bavarians. Jan Dubravius tells us what happened. \'Wenceslaus, Emperor and King of Bohemia\', he says, \'was a relation by marriage of John, the Prince of Bavaria, because he had married his daughter, Sophia.⁵⁵⁷ His father-in-law knew that his son-in-law took pleasure in stage-plays and magical illusions, and so he filled a wagon with actors and illusionists and took them with him to Prague. There, while the most remarkable of the performers was displaying his professional stunts to delight people\'s eyes, among the spectators was Zyto, Wenceslaus\'s magician, his mouth gaping open in a great yawn. He came nearer the Bavarian performer and suddenly swallowed him and all his equipment, with the exception of his shoes which he spat out because they seemed to be covered in mud. Looking for a way to get rid of \[what he had swallowed\], he unburdened his stomach, which was heavy with its unusual food, into a bathtub filled with water and restored the illusionist, still wet, to the spectators\'.
[Page 227]

In this case, Zyto\'s evil spirit was the more powerful. The business of swallowing was merely an illusion, but the unfortunate \[performer\] was genuinely snatched away and thrown into the tub of water by the evil spirit, not discharged via the pathways of \[Zyto\'s\] bowels. My fifth \[example\] will be taken from Olaus \[Magnus\] Book 3, chapter 20. Here a magician called Gilbert was arguing with Catillus, his instructor, about superiority in the practice of magic. \[Catillus\] pointed a tiny stick inscribed with Gothic or Ruthenian characters at him, so \[Gilbert\] took hold of him. \[Catillus\] became rigid at once and was carried away, trussed up, to an underground cave on an island in a lake called \'Veter\' by the Ostrogoths.

Saxo Grammaticus mentions a sixth \[example\] in Book 1 of his history of Denmark. It is about an old man who was a very eminent magician, as is proved by the fact that this one man surpassed all the men of Perm who are fierce in their reliance on magic. The story is a long one, so if anyone wants to read it, my indication is that it occurs in Saxo at the point where he describes a battle between Hading and Thuning. In addition to this, I am happy to refer to a story told by the Greeks about a contest between the soothsayers Calchas and Mopsus, which ended in Calchas\'s defeat and death. This, therefore, is the power of evil spirits over evil spirits and of magicians over them.

Book 2, Question 30, Section 2 \[sic\]

Holy people have often received from God an extraordinary power to keep evil spirits under control, as is attested by the innumerable miracles whereby those \[saints\] have got the better of them and overthrown them. This same power has always invested the sacraments, sacramental, and exorcisms of the Catholic Church, and this power is not found outwith her. Certain \[practices\] similar to our exorcisms, instituted in earlier times by Solomon, seem to have flourished among the Jews. This is proved not only by Josephus, Origen, and St Epiphanius, but also by the words of our Saviour when he confronted the Jews on the grounds that they were saying He was expelling evil spirits in \[the name of\]

Beelzebub. \'In whose name do your children expel evil spirits?\' He said \[Luke 11.19\] and learned men have been right in thinking that this is more correctly referred to the Jews\' usual exorcists than to the Apostles, because when this exchange was taking place, the Apostles had not yet been sent to preach, and this power was granted to them after \[they had been\]. The disciples also suffered the same malicious charge as their teacher, as Jesus predicted they would in Matthew \[10.17-18\].

Consequently, the Lord had not used the example of His Apostles and disciples to sufficient effect because the Jews replied straight away that they accomplished these things in the same name as the Master. In my opinion, Solomon had instituted these exorcisms at a time when he was still cleaving to God, and therefore God\'s power made them work. Perhaps, too, they continued to have some power from their \[original\] establishment by God, but not, I think, as much as there is these days in an exorcism \[carried out\] by the Catholic Church. If, however, you argue that they were instituted by Solomon when he was an idolater, you will be forced to attribute the, (as St Thomas \[Aquinas\] and \[Francisco de Vitoria do), to magicians\' incantations, all of whose power rests upon a pact with an evil spirit. But I prefer my first explanation.

Therefore, since the whole power of magicians over evil spirits depends on and arises from the covenant they have made with them, it undoubtedly follows that if a magician has a pact with an evil spirit of higher rank, he cannot exercise this power of this higher-ranking spirit. But through that higher-ranking spirit which is acting as his servant and assisting him because of their pact, he can compel evil spirits of lower rank to do something, or not to do it, and to prevent them from carrying out something they could otherwise have done. This is proved by the complaint of the Chaldaean magician in St Augustine who \'kept complaining that the great effort he was making to be successful in purging his soul was rendered ineffectual because a man who had power over this and was touched by envy had bound the Powers he had invoked with holy prayers, the result being that they could not grant what was demanded of them.\'
[Page 228]

Therefore one evil spirit bound what another could not undo. Eusebius tells us the same thing, and quite clearly it applies to the magician Cynops in Prochorus, (whoever he was), \[who says\] that when \'St John was questioning evil spirits about why they were in the habit of obeying Cynops, one of them replied, \"Because the entire power of Satan resides in him, and he has a treaty and pact with every one of our princes. We, too, on our part, have a pact with him and so Cynops obeys us and we him\". In the absence of such a pact, every attempt made by magicians against evil spirits is useless, since these work only because of the pact, (as I said in question 2.)

Hence first of all comes Apollo\'s lie when he answered the magician: Listen to me. I am not willing, because you have bound me by force\', (Eusebius, quoting Porphyry).

He was replying of his own accord, but he was pretending that he was being compelled by a contract which had been renewed seven times, \[a contract\] he himself had drawn up, as it clear from another passage in Eusebius\'s Proof of the Gospel Book 4, which begins KAnišelv \'Epuñv, \[They call Hermes\], etc. Then there is also the fiction, as if said by a suppliant: Furthermore, release the king. A mortal does not hold a god captive any longer\'.

The utterly vile evil spirit is speaking about himself and is demanding that the magician release him. In addition to this, magicians\' boasting about their ability to conjure up evil spirits and apparitions is worthless. Nor is there any power in the herb \[called\] \'Dogshead\' or \'Osiris\'s herb\', as Apion was silly enough to claim. Worthless, too, were Numa\'s boasting and ceremonies, and other dissembling visions produced-merely as the result of a pact, regardless of the power they had to compel them-by the magician Sicidita, (see Nicetas), Theodorus Santabirinus, (see Zonaras), and John the German.

Let me write down what one reads about that last one in the author of the German adages, because it is appropriate, and not unpleasant, to blunt the boasting of certain people who are themselves morally degenerate, and prattle about the smoky images of their ancestors, and \[say\] they did things they themselves have not done. Hence, they write that although \[John\] was illegitimate, he had a great reputation for learning and was promoted to a canonry at Halberstadt, a post not usually granted to anyone unless he came from a good family. The fault common to good families-pride-made his fellow-canons despise him. He was irked by this and finally decided to remedy his annoyance.

He invited his colleagues to dinner and received them in most elegant fashion; and among all his other pleasantries he included mention of a magical performer and suggested that each man would surely like to see his own parents. They were indeed extremely keen to see them and after a while, by means of a wicked incantation, \[the magician\] evoked the misshapen shadowy forms of a cook, a pub landlord, a professional jester, and a peasant. \[The canons\] admitted they had sometimes seen these faces in their parents\' homes. \[John\'s\] own father was shown, too, dressed as a canon, with a fat paunch. The shadowy forms were dismissed, and then \[John\] said, \'Well now! Tell me honestly, is there anyone\'s father here you think is better born \[than the others\]?\' Thoroughly frightened and filled with shame, each of them went back home and from then on did not accuse John of any imperfection in his blood-line.

Likewise, it follows that one should come to the same conclusion about the ritual evocation of the gods of the ancients from \[besieged\] cities, the form of words for which was recorded by Sammonicus Serenus in Macrobius, \[Saturnalia 3.9.6-7\]. I know God has not infrequently granted this power to saintly people, and I pass over anyone else. St Stephen, Bishop of Perm, \'told the evil spirits, who were turning people away to prevent them from listening to him, to show themselves openly in visible form. They did as they were told and appeared-immensely tall, completely black, with horns \[on their heads\], and breathing out stench and flames\', (Sauer in his biography \[of St Stephen\], under the month of September).
[Page 229]

Book 2, Question 30, Section 3

Secondly, one concludes-and on this point \[Paolo\] Grillando seems to have made a mistake in question 3, number 23, when he talks about compulsion-no words and no physical objects have the power directly and of themselves to compel evil spirits to appear, come, go away, do something, or not do something. No physical objects can equal the strength of substances which are spiritual and different. Therefore, evil spirits cannot be affected by them naturally, although they can be affected supernaturally. Consequently, I do not agree with magicians when they produce various natural remedies against evil spirits, such as Hermes \[Trismegistus\] who said that evil spirits are brought by a suffumigation of aloe-wood or whale-sperm and driven away by one of sulphur. Porphyry and Proclus talk a great deal of similar nonsense, as do some people in Hippocrates who talk even greater nonsense. I do not agree with heretics who disparage all supernatural power in exorcisms and anything which has been consecrated and blessed, contrary to what other people do.

On the other hand, one cannot approve of the credulousness and superstitious behaviour of certain Catholics, either, who ascribe supernatural power to things which have not been consecrated, such as St John\'s wort, rue, horn, goat\'s dung, baths, and whippings, and think that an evil spirit is adversely affected by these and therefore is annoyed and driven away by them. When one whips the body of someone who is obsessed \[by an evil spirit\] too much, the illness becomes worse and intensifies, and so the sick person is made worse.

What is more, anger and sadness serve to increase those states of melancholia which the evil spirit wants to encourage; so while these stupid people want to harm the evil spirit by doing things such as this, they are actually helping him. Nor can you find anything here which is irksome to an evil spirit other than that he thinks he is being whipped in someone else\'s \[body\] and suffers pain in his pride. But this is one thing which should be of no importance at all to these floggers. The saints have sometimes done this, but their \'whips\', such as their voices and the soles of their shoes to help them, would miraculously torture the evil spirit. Baths are useful for purging the melancholic humour, and the leaves of rue and St John\'s wort cure problems with breathing and are wonderfully good for calming the humoral disorder caused by breathing problems.

Dung and goat\'s horn dispel the same problems with their acrid fumigation, and that proud fellow is angry, too, that he is being laughed at and made a fool of in someone else\'s \[body\]. It has therefore been very well observed by Konrad Wimpina and Francisco Vallés that evil spirits more frequently attack those who have too great a supply of black bile, because that humour provides a particularly suitable way of making people frenzied and insane, and evil spirits like it very much indeed. Consequently, they lay siege to melancholics and, for the most part, are the cause of illnesses stemming from black bile. Hence it follows that because they cannot change physical matter unless they connect active things with passive, they treat people badly if, once they have made use of physical things of this kind, the inclinations those people have to malice or deceit are expelled and their opposites introduced immediately.

Therefore, whatever physical things introduce into the sufferer\'s body an inclination contrary to the one the evil spirit needs for his active purpose \[also\] cleanse the physical humours directly and expel \[the sufferer\'s\] physical illness naturally. Indirect causes alone, however, are the reason the spirit runs away and deserts that body, and in the absence of these, he does not do so. On this understanding, it will be permissible for those under siege by an evil spirit to possess and carry on their person stones or herbs which are naturally effective \[in these cases\] without, however, any \[accompanying\] incantation. This is what is generally thought.
[Page 230]

Certain objections are usually raised against this. (1) In Josephus, Antiquities Book 8, chapter 2, \[section 6\], Eleazar draws out an evil spirit by using a particular root. Someone may respond, perhaps this was instituted by Solomon at the same time as his exorcisms, and so it could have been possible that God worked supernaturally in the herb. That is scarcely likely to be true after the introduction and promulgation of Christianity. My opinion is rather that Eleazar was a magician and did what he did as a result of a pact \[with an evil spirit\], and the circumstantial details added by Josephus will easily convince the careful reader of this. The power of the root he dug up in such a superstitious fashion seems to be of the same kind as the one \[Josephus\] writes about in The Jewish War Book 7, chapter 25.

\(2\) There is a passage in Tobit which runs as follows: \'if you put a small part of the heart\' \[of the fish \"Callyonimus\"\] \'on top of charcoal, its smoke will clear out every kind of evil spirit\', and we read later that this was done. My response is that this suffumigation drove the spirit away indirectly. The angel Raphael was the one who expelled the evil spirit directly and despatched him to an uninhabited place. In consequence, the whole action was attributed to Raphael afterwards, \'He (Raphael) chased away the evil spirit from her\'. The power as a natural property, however, is attributed to the \[fish\'s\] heart not in a literal, but in a prophetic and symbolic sense, because it must be referred back to Christ, our Lord, who freed the Magdalene from the evil spirits which had possessed her, and has also freed us from the tyranny of the Devil. This is not my comment, but that of St Prosper \[of Aquitaine\]. At some length, and in many words, he says the following, among everything else: \'He feeds His disciples on the shore fully from Himself, and offers Himself to the whole world as an ixdúv \[fish\], and because our ancestors explained \"fish\" in Latin by reference to sacred letters which they deduced from the Sibylline verses that \"Jesus Christ, Son of God, the Saviour\", is a fish cooked in His own suffering, by whose inward aids we are illuminated and fed every day\'.

You will understand this somewhat obscure saying if you read St Augustine from whom he took it. Certainly, Christ is called a fish by Tertullian. \'But we are born in water as little fish, according to the example of our ixdúv, \[Jesus Christ\], and we are not saved in any way other than by remaining in the water\'.

\(3\) They beckon us in the direction of David\'s harp but do so in vain because in that instance it was not the pleasant tune which was the direct cause of putting the evil spirit to flight. When the King had been soothed by the harmoniousness of the music, as long as David was playing the harp, at any rate, the humours which were agitating him started to settle themselves down to some extent, and in the meantime were in a less suitable state to be affected by the evil spirit\'s stirring them up. This is why the wicked \[spirit\] began to retire at that moment-not because it was pleased or offended by the music, but because it would find it difficult to put up with the fact that its possession of its dwelling-place was being thrown into disorder, so to speak, by this improvement in the mingling of \[the King\'s\] humours. Many Catholics agree with this opinion of Rabbi Moses of Egypt \[Maimonides\]. The notion some people have about the evil spirit\'s inclination for harmony is, of course, worthless. This is where magicians get their nonsense that one should offer Saturn singing which is mournful and the rest of the planets singing which is cheerful and happy. (Guillaume d\'Auvergne refuted this a long time ago). Others take refuge in symbolism and maintain that the evil spirit really was put to flight by the sound of the harp, but that this was done by the power and merit of Christ\'s cross of which the harp was a symbol.
[Page 231]

In connection with this third point, one\'s conclusion is that poets\' beliefsee the heretic \'Palingenius\', \[The Zodiac of Life\] Book 10 or \'Capricorn\', and the Catholic Pierre de Ronsard\'s Hymn on Evil Spirits-that magicians can bind evil spirits fast in small jars, mirrors, or rings the way criminals are locked up in the stocks or tied fast to a board is not true. This is either because a more powerful \[spirit\], or one of higher rank, always compels them to be there and keeps them there by his command, or because they are there of their own accord and pact \[with the magician\] and keep themselves there. Consequently, one must strongly disapprove of the treatise by Hermes \[Trismegistus\] in which he published instructions for binding evil spirits, and the lies told by Apollo and Hecate in Theodoret\'s book on oracles, in which they say they have been constrained to appear by \[certain\] forms of words.

As well as these two authors, Porphyry makes the \[same\] lying claim. This, as I have said, has been attributed to the opinion of the Fathers \[of the Church\] and the Schoolmen. What Heinrich of Hesse said on this subject in his \[commentary\] on Genesis is very well worth noting. \'\[The evil spirit\] pretends he has been taken prisoner in order to take you prisoner: that he has been vanquished in order to vanquish you: that he is subject to your orders in order to subject you to his: that he has been imprisoned by you so that, in the end, he can imprison you. He pretends he has been bound fast in an image or stone by your magic so that, once he has bound you fast by the ropes of your sins, he can lead you to Hell.⁵⁷⁰ (You can read more in confirmation of these points in Gianfrancesco Pico \[della Mirandola\], Foreknowledge of Things Book 7, chapter 4.)

\[Alonso\] Tostado has provided a closely argued, though verbose, argument on this topic as follows. \'It is not possible for a lesser power to subject a greater power to itself, and there is no power on earth which is comparable to that of evil spirits, (Job chapter 40). Therefore there will not be anything in the way of Nature or ritual by which human beings can compel evil spirits to come, to reply to questions, or to do anything \[for them\], unless you object that the Church\'s exorcisms \[do just that\], in which case it has to be said that \[the spirits\] are compelled to do something, namely, to leave the bodies of those they have been besieging, or not to do them harm.

But this is done by God, in as much as He Himself instituted what is done by the Church and therein lies hidden the operative power of God, just as in the working of the sacraments. God, however, is not working in the operations of workers of harmful magic whereby they want to compel evil spirits, because these \[operations\] are contrary to God\'s honour. Therefore, workers of harmful magic cannot compel evil spirits \[to do\] anything. The evil spirits, though, lie and pretend they are being compelled, because herein lies their greatest power of persuasion. If we could not compel them to come or answer questions or do anything, but whatever they might do they would do voluntarily, human beings would stop having recourse to them, on the grounds that they could achieve nothing with their assistance and withdraw themselves from them.

But since human beings think they can compel them, because \[the spirits\] pretend \[to be compelled\], they are very much influenced to acts of harmful magic, as if by these means they can obtain everything they want; and evil spirits are keen that they do this so that, through this kind of error and influence, they can keep humans\' minds in chains. Yet evil spirits cannot really be compelled by human beings through acts of harmful magic. Indeed, if they could be compelled, they would not teach human beings how to do it because if humans really could subject evil spirits to them, they would thereby make the spirits more wretched \[than they are already\]. So \[the spirits\] pretend they have been subjected, (although in fact they have not), so that they can deceive \[the humans\], and are content to act as their servants for the time being, although actually evil spirits would not be willing to be subjected to human beings for any money.
[Page 232]

Nothing is truer than this. But how dangerous it is to imagine one has imprisoned this kind \[of spirit\] and has them under one\'s control is proved by various examples. One, recounted by Johannes Nider as follows, will be sufficient. \'A little before the present General Council, a man of wicked life had a house in Kleinbasel and, as it happened, and had come under reasonable suspicion of performing acts of harmful magic \[maleficiis\]. He had a daughter and married her to a young man in his house, and when at length the old man fell ill, he pointed out a box to his daughter and son-in-law and said, \"Don\'t move this little box, because if you do, you\'ll regret it. Let it stay where it is after I am dead\", and thus not long afterwards the old man expired.

A long time went by. Neither his daughter nor her husband paid any great heed to her father\'s instruction, and when they moved from their former home to another little house which was big enough \[for them\], they carried the box to their new home. During the journey, however, it started to weigh so much that \[the husband\'s\] strength failed him, and he called on his wife to help him. Whether his wife opened the box later in the house, or whether somehow or other she forgot \[what her father had said\] about the box, people agree on one thing-the two of them came into their new house with a small child they had begotten. Well now, all of a sudden, the wife rushed at the cradle like a raving mad woman, with the intention of killing the child. Her husband saw this, forcibly seized hold of his wife, and realised she was under the control of an evil spirit. While the evil spirit was being exorcised, it threatened it would not leave unless \[her husband\] killed her, which he did in spite of the exorcist\'s trying to prevent him. Next day her husband was making his way along a pavement in the town when, quite unexpectedly, he was struck by a stone falling from the upper part of a water-channel which was coming away \[from the building\]. It struck him, apparently with the co-operation of an evil spirit, in the face and rendered him so ugly that he scarcely seemed to be a human being.\'

\[Pierre\] Crespet has much to say about these imprisoned \[spirits\], because sometimes they appear to their masters. Some have to be worshipped with a particular ritual because they are connected with Saturn, others because they are connected with Jupiter, others because they are connected with Venus, and others because they are connected with Mercury, etc. At fixed times in the night, they rouse their owner and, on occasion, talk to him, unless they prefer to do something wicked. In France and Italy, they are for sale and always ruin those who buy them. (Crespet goes into greater detail in The Hatred of Satan Book 1, discourse 12).

Holy men, however, have not infrequently imprisoned or bound up evil spirits in certain things: St Conon, for example, about whom the Jesuit Matthias Rader gives the following account in The Succession of the Months among the Greeks, under \'March\'. \'He exercised such great force and power over evil spirits that he would put some of them in charge of the fields and make them guardians of the crops. Others he would imprison in sealed-up buildings and bury in the foundation of a church he intended to build. Likewise, St Lupus imprisoned one in Sens, afflicting him with thirst and inserting him, wailing and proclaiming who he was, in a small vessel of cold water he had told someone to bring. (This is what his ancient biographer says in Sauer, under the month of September.)
[Page 233]

So, we read that Asmodeus was bound by the Archangel Raphael in an uninhabited place, (Tobit 8.2); and that four angels, bound in the great River Euphrates, were released by God\'s command, (Apocalypse 9.14 and 15); and that the Devil was chained up by an angel for a thousand years and sent into the abyss, (Apocalypse 20.2). No one denies Asmodeus is an evil spirit. Tichonius in Homily 7, Andreas of Caesarea, and Bede quite correctly maintain in relation to that verse that these four angels were evil spirits; and by \'binding\' one means \[being bound\] to a particular place and not just in a mystical sense to the hearts of the wicked, as Ambrose \[Autpert\] finally proved conclusively in these words: \'I think one must interpret the binding of the Devil literally to mean he is being held in bondage by the power of God in some place in the abyss, or certainly in Hell, until the day of his release\'.

St Gregory sticks to the letter \[of the text and says the same thing in his commentary on Job Book 4, chapter 12, and Andreas of Caesarea quite correctly adds that in Catholic interpretation \[of the text\], the more powerful operation whereby the malice of an evil spirit is constrained is called a \'chain\', because these means \[of restraint\] are bonds and ropes and are called this elsewhere, (2 Peter 2.4, and Vol. 6 of the Jewish canon). Caesarius \[of Heisterbach\] says that an evil spirit imprisoned in the body of demoniac was forced by exorcism to say that there are three phrases in the canon of the Mass whereby Lucifer was bound up in Hell as if by chains, namely, \"Through Him and with Him and in Him\'. (Read Book 5, chapter 13 of his Discussion of Miracles.)

Saints can vanquish evil spirits as follows: (i) as servants of God, by injecting fear and making threats which an evil spirit knows are not usually ineffectual; (ii) by withdrawing the divine agreement obtained from God which means that evil spirits cannot be moved from a place, (which, were it not for that, they could do); and (iii) by imprinting on evil spirits some spiritual condition and detaining force-\[exorcism\] being an example of this-whereby these non-physical spirits are detained unwillingly in Hell and tortured by physical flames.

The practices and forms of words used in exorcism, with which an evil spirit is sometimes excommunicated and, as it were, despatched to the lowest dwelling-place in Hell and fettered there as I have described, tell us about the first of these methods. Consequently, St Augustine, made use of the language of Honorius\'s edict and proclaimed an interdict. (See The City of God Book 20, chapter 7). Palladius reminds us of the second method in his life of St Macarius in which he deals with Jamnes, Mambres, and their tomb, (Lausiac History, chapter 20). The third method is more frequent and is commonly \[employed\] against all evil spirits because \[it pertains\] to their eternal punishment, on which read St Thomas \[Aquinas\] Part 1, question 64, article 4, contrary 3, Summa Against the Pagans Book 4, chapter 90, and the Bishop of Avila, \[Alonso Tostado\], in his \[Commentary on\] Matthew, chapter 25.

I have found a few \[examples\], however, which seem to attribute this power even to workers of harmful magic \[maleficis\]. But when one takes a closer look at these, they indicate merely that because of the pact the magician \[magum\] has made with a more powerful evil spirit, he can impede the activity and power of an evil spirit of lower rank, and so \[the spirit\] can sometimes be coerced by the magician, whether he is willing to be so or not. This, however, is done by the magician via the vicarious power of a stronger evil spirit, or rather, by making use of his power.
[Page 234]

Thus, in St Jerome\'s \[life of\] Hilarion, an evil spirit has to be held fettered by a piece of string and a metal plate; this is how Jamnes and Mambres, in the passage to which I have referred, bound many evil spirits. This is how one should interpret St Augustine when he writes that evil spirits are bound, put to flight, removed, and transferred from certain places and objects on the orders of \[evil spirits\] of higher rank, (The Divination of Evil Spirits, chapter 7), and this is how I interpret Eusebius when he says that evil spirits can be restrained by human beings, (Preparation for the Gospel Book 5, chapter 6). This is why Albertus \[Magnus\], \[Francisco\] Victoria, and every other Catholic theologian thinks they are enclosed, (according to popular belief), in rings as a result of a pact and kept there forcibly by a more powerful evil spirit, (if they actually are enclosed and kept there), or they pretend of their own accord that they are being kept there when a more powerful spirit has not been involved.

One infers that the spirits popularly called \'familiars\' or \'little Martins\' or \'little teachers\' act as servants to human beings entirely of their own accord. The Greeks call them πapéδpous \[those who sit beside you because they are constantly present. (Irenaeus and Tertullian have dealt with these.) Some people thought that Socrates\'s spirit \[daemonium\] was one of this sort. St Clement attributes one such to Simon Magus, Prochorus one such to Cynops, and Aristotle one such to a person from Thrace. In his Chronicles, \[Jean\] Froissard attributes one called Croton to a learned man. Cornelius Agrippa\'s dog was one such, and even if his pupil \[Johann\] Weyer denies it, everyone else quite rightly confirms it. I remember reading in Johannes Caesarius the exemplary story-well worth remembering-of a familiar spirit \[paredri\].

In this story, what one finds astonishing is that the evil spirit demanded five gold coins as his price and gave orders for a bell to be purchased with them for a poor church so that it could summon the faithful to divine worship every Sunday. There was undoubtedly some deception lying hidden here, and the hope of a larger sum of money was blindingly obvious. It is also remarkable that he caused the soldier no harm. Familiar spirits are scarcely ever in the habit of going away without doing harm to their masters since, for the most part, an evil spirit makes a contract for a particular period of time \[which stipulates that when that time is completed the evil spirit can either find another master, or \[his master will submit to being killed by his evil spirit, as Nicolas Rémy tells us in Book 1 of his Worship of Evil Spirits, illustrating his remarks with an example.

But this is enough about magic \[in general\]. Let us proceed to harmful magic.
